An Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of the Young Men’s Business League’s Sunshine Camp
Infrastructure Development Project located on LCRA’s Turkey Bend Property, Travis County, Texas

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

With Contributions by
Daniel J. Prikryl
Ted Reiley
Geoff Saunders

TAC Permit # 4779
Interim Report No. 3.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Community and Natural Resource Services
February, 2008

38

Management Summary
LCRA proposes to allow development of a 21.04 acre section of land by the Young Men’s Business
League (YMBL) for Sunshine Camp, designed to provide outdoor education for underserved inner-city
youth. The land is located in western Travis County on Lake Travis, within LCRA’s Turkey Bend park
(Figure 8). Primary development elements include a 54 x 80 ft enclosed pavilion, a 20 x 54 ft restroom
and shower facility, and six 16 x 28 ft cabins. There will be an on-site well, two sets of septic tanks, an
approximate 1-acre drain field that will hold a volleyball court, underground electric utilities, both high
and low ropes challenge courses, and floating canoe and swim docks. Two 10 x 10 ft storage sheds and a
small office building/medical station will be placed on piers. Other buildings will be placed on slabs built
to grade. Ground disturbance will include bulldozing, trenching, and drilling of holes for piers and ropes
structures. Access will be along an existing park road. Total disturbance will be approximately 2 acres.
There are five known archaeological sites located either completely or partially within the project area
(Figure 9). These are prehistoric sites 41TV1849, 1853, 1860 and 1882, and historic site 41TV1835.
Only site 41TV1849 is within an area that will be directly impacted by construction (Figure 10). This is
an open camp with chipped and ground stone tools, fire cracked quartzite, chert and sandstone and
weathered mussel shell in sandy, gravelly soils at depth of up to 50 cm. Portions of the site have been
heavily impacted by ongoing looting, which appears to have been occurring for decades (Figure 11).
41TV1849 is closely associated with 41TV1853, also an open camp, but located below the ridge that
holds 41TV1849. The majority of this site is normally under the waters of Lake Travis. Sites 41TV1860
and 41TV1882 are both lithic acquisition locales within gravels exposed on slopes on either side of the
ridge. These sites impinge only slightly into the lease area at the south and north. Site 41TV1835 is the
location of the Burton House, as determined by a 1936 contour map, on which four structures are seen.
This homesite has been severely impacted by past clearing activities, but is not within an area proposed
for impacts associated with the present project.
Although site 41TV1849 has dense amounts of debitage and time diagnostic artifacts dating from Middle
though Late Archaic times, there is little potential for separating time periods vertically. There appear to
be trends suggesting site use varied horizontally, but a lack of intact features and organic preservation
coupled with the extent of previous impacts makes any substantive interpretations a tenuous prospect.
Site 41TV1849 is not considered to be eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or
nomination to the National Register of Historic Places.
It is recommended the project be allowed to proceed.
Artifacts collected during this project will be permanently curated at an accreditated curation facility.
The background sections from the following report are taken from an internal LCRA resource assessment
document for Turkey Bend.

Environmental Background
Geology
Surface geology results at Turkey Bend were derived by visual inspection of the property during 2005 and
by research primarily based on NRCS data. The Turkey Bend Resource Area was found to be evenly split
between Cretaceous-aged Cow Creek Limestone and Hammett Shale Undivided on the western part of the
property and Quaternary-aged deposits in the east. The Cow Creek Limestone is a white, honeycombed

39

Figure 8. Project location on Pace Bend USGS quad.

40

1853

1835

1849

Figure 9. Project area in relation to nearby archaeological sites.

41

Floating docks

Restroom/showers

Figure 10. Detail of project elements and shovel test location in 41TV1849.

42

limestone of about a 60-foot thickness, while the Hammett Shale is greenish-gray shale that includes
oyster beds, cobbles, and pebbles. The limestone and shale outcrops occur in the upland physiographic
zone.

Figure 11. Relatively recent looting at 41TV1849. Clipboard for scale.
The Quaternary deposits consist of Pleistocene-era fluviatile terrace deposits, which coincide with the
upland margin physiographic zone, and more recent Holocene alluvium, which occurs on the
lowland/floodplain zone. The fluviatile terrace deposits are noteworthy for their large volume of gravels,
which are visible on terrace slopes.
Although the site has geologic characteristics capable of developing subsurface solution cavities (karst
features), no known caves have been identified to date.
Soils
The soils at Turkey Bend Resource Area were surveyed in 2005 using a combination of on-site visual
inspections and reviews of secondary sources. Seven soils were identified. The research found that the
U.S. Department of Agriculture’s NRCS’s soil surveys of Travis and Burnet counties define the soil in
the project area as Travis and Tarrant series.
The soils formed from several kinds of parent material: in the western part of the county, the parent
material was principally limestone dolomite, interbedded limestone and marl, and clay, but along the
Colorado River, the parent material was alluvium. The surface layer is light brownish-gray gravelly loam
or clay loam about four inches thick. The next layer is pale-brown clay loam to a depth of about 15
inches. The underlying material is interbedded limestone and marl.

43

Erosion problems were noted during the course of field investigations. Those observed in the western
section appear to be due to the steepness of slope. This is indicative of Cretaceous-aged Cow Creek
Limestone and Hammett Shale and is also attributed to previous overuse of the property.
Closer to the water, increased erosion and scattered rock deposits are attributed to the bend in the river.
The more easterly soils, associated with Quaternary-aged deposits, have areas of erosion which appear to
have been caused by frequent flooding and overuse.
Approximately 30 percent of the entire Turkey Bend property is bare ground. This is standard for rocky
Hill Country soils.
Extended overuse has deteriorated the condition and diversity of biomass on Turkey Bend. Using best
management practices, it is possible to restore this property to near pristine condition. Decreased canopy
and increased grass and forb production will boost infiltration rates, yielding increase quantity and quality
of overland flow.
Physiography and Landforms
The Turkey Bend Resource Area is located within the Edwards Plateau section of the Great Plains
physiographic province (Carr 1967:3). In the general area around Turkey Bend, land surfaces consist
mostly of plains with high hills (Hammond 1964:Map 4). However, the landscape at the resource area
differs from that of the surrounding region because it flanks the Colorado River. Through time, this river
has dissected the landscape to create a deep valley while simultaneously depositing sediment loads from
upstream areas within the valley and adjacent areas. As a result, the Turkey Bend Resource Area includes
floodplain/lowland acreage that is associated with the largest stream channel in Travis and Burnet
counties.
This property is a northeast to southwest trending terrace that is situated within a bend of the Colorado
River. LCRA’s Turkey Bend landholding can be divided into three gross environmental/topographic
zones: lowland/floodplain, upland margin, and upland.
Lowland/Floodplain Zone
The lowland/floodplain zone extends from the top of the original bank of the Colorado River at an
elevation of 620 feet mean sea level (ft msl) up to 700 ft msl. Presently, approximately 60 percent of this
zone is normally inundated by the conservation pool level of 681 ft msl of Lake Travis. A pre-reservoir
map indicates that expanses of a lowland/floodplain zone were present at the far south central end of the
terrace landform at Turkey Bend, and that at its maximum, this zone was about 3,270 feet wide. It
consists of deep alluvial soils that have a surface veneer of fine sandy loam. From this south central area,
the width of this zone progressively decreases on both the upstream and downstream sides so that at the
far northwest and southeast ends of the current recreation area, steep cliffs descend from the uplands
straight down to the former river channel. LCRA acquired Turkey Bend for the development of Lake
Travis and maintains the property, in part, to serve as a buffer for flooding, so inundation is expected.
Sections of the western portion and major parts of the eastern portion lay within the floodplain.
Upland Margin Zone
The upland margin zone extends from about 700 to 800 ft msl. At the northwest and southeast ends of the
resource area this zone lies on very steep slopes with exposed shale, gravelly conglomerate, sandstone,
and limestone bedrock often visible on ground surfaces. Elsewhere, the terrain varies from slightly to
moderately sloping, and in these areas the generally shallow topsoils vary from gravelly clays to gravelly
fine sandy loams. The presence of a spring-fed tributary creek in the east-central part of the property and

44

a smaller tributary near the far eastern edge of the property add to the environmental variety of the upland
margin zone. The data suggest that, prior to modifications resulting from historic land use patterns, the
upland margin area may have supported diverse floral and faunal communities.
Upland Zone
Topographically, the upland zone extends from 800 ft msl upward. Upland surfaces have little
topographic relief. Limestone bedrock and/or patches of loose limestone rocks cover about one-half of
the land surface. Elsewhere shallow, cobbly, clay topsoils are present. Overall, this zone lacks the
environmental variety that is evident in the upland margin.

Cultural Background

Prehistoric Background
The Turkey Bend Resource Area lies within the Central Texas prehistoric cultural region (Prewitt 1981).
Prehistoric site types in Central Texas consist of camps, caches, isolated artifacts, interments, cemeteries,
kill/butcher locales, quarry/workshops, lithic scatters, and rock art sites (Collins 1995:363). The
numerous excavations of major campsites conducted along the larger streams and rivers (cf. Peter et al.
1982; Prewitt 1982) have demonstrated that Central Texas was occupied for at least 13,000 years prior to
the coming of Europeans. Generally accepted prehistoric chronological divisions are as follows:
Paleoindian (pre-6800 B.C.), Early Archaic (6800 B.C. - 4000 B.C.), Middle Archaic (4000 B.C. - 2000
B.C.), Late Archaic (2000 B.C. - A.D. 700), and Late Prehistoric (A.D. 700 - 1600) (Collins 1995).
Historic Background
The Turkey Bend property is carved from three original patents: the C. Lane Survey, the H. Phillips
Survey, and the H. Vickers Survey. These were granted in 1845 by the Republic of Texas (Lane), and the
state in 1846 (Vickers) and 1850 (Phillips).
In 1865 the Henry Thomas Masonic Lodge was founded there, and for a time the area was known as
Lodge Valley (Debo 1979). The lodge moved to Smithwick in 1876, and at some point the community
was renamed Turkey Bend, presumably in recognition of the numerous wild turkeys that were present.
Debo refers to Turkey Bend as a “pioneer hamlet”, and a sketch map in Thompson and Frasier (1996)
shows a community located far down on the bend, near the river, and just west of the Burnet County line.
Debo describes the Spanish Oak School as being in the Turkey Bend community, and states that it was
more commonly referred to as the Turkey Bend School. The school was in operation prior to 1899 and
operated at least until 1942, and possibly as late as 1948, when it was consolidated with the Smithwick
school district (Smyrl 2001). A photo from 1902 shows the students in attendance.
A USGS map from 1900-01, with “culture revised” in 1906, shows a dispersed community with perhaps
six structures across a portion of Turkey Bend. A school is not shown as such, but Spanish Oak Church is
clearly mapped just west of the Burnet/Travis County line, and is almost certainly the location of recorded
archaeological site 41BT322. No mention of a church has been found in historical records, but it is likely
that church services were held in the school building at this location on what is now LCRA property. It is
also unknown where the Masonic Lodge was located. A lodge might have been a communal property and
may have sustained such status as a school or church after its departure.
It is unclear when the first settlement on what is now LCRA property occurred, but it almost certainly
predates 1865, when the Henry Thomas Lodge met “in Lodge room (sic) near the residence of Bro. John

45

F. Burton” (Debo 1979:264). Charter members included Burton, M. Cox, J. Vickers, H. L. Hensel, and
Calvin Hall.
Following the various chains of title becomes quite complicated, largely due to intermarriages between
closely associated families, often after the death of a spouse. Surviving children were not necessarily
adopted, and retained their original surnames as new children were added to recombined families. It was
not uncommon for there to be 10 or 12 children under one roof. Various families apparently consolidated
land holdings through time, but also seemed to make land sales based on perceived future inheritances.
There are four structures seen on a map commissioned by LCRA in 1936 that have family names
associated with them. These are, from west to east, Phillips (TN-35), Boultinghouse (TN-34), Hall, and
Burton TN-32. The map did not extend above the 740 amsl contour, so any potential additional structures
at higher elevations may still have been present at that time.
The main economic pursuits at Turkey Bend were clearly farming and ranching, perhaps with an
emphasis on the former. On the 1936 map, the lower terraces are all marked as “cultivated fields.”
Photographic evidence shows a number of the Halls, along with Lewises and Dennistons, as members of
the Farmers Union, a collective bargaining organization that was an offshoot of the Grange (Thompson
and Frasier 1996:46). Statements made during probate hearings describe fences, dwellings, houses, barns,
outbuildings, and “appurtenances” being occupied continuously by families and tenants for cultivation
and pasture. The estate of H. S. Phillips, in 1935, listed 10 cattle, one horse, and 85 goats.
Economic diversification was attempted. Interestingly, in 1919, Brown Boultinghouse leased his property
to J. R. Yett, a member of a prominent local family, and a J. W. Hunnicut for oil and gas exploration. It is
unknown whether any wells were actually dug, but it is assumed that the venture was not successful. In
1925 Brown Boultinghouse leased 225 acres for $400 to a Tom Heine for timber cutting. Heine was
allowed to build gates and roads and permitted to camp. Timber cutting apparently was a successful
enterprise. In 1927, Hall traded timber rights to J. H. Shaffer in the amount of $500 for 350 acres.
Numerous cedar stumps bear witness to these activities. One of these “cedar chopper” camps is probably
represented by site 41TV536.
The properties were all conveyed to LCRA between 1938 and 1939. This includes the Wilson Tract (TN36), which was part of the original Vickers Survey, and is usually underwater. The 1906 map shows a
structure very near where the tract boundary should be, and clearly shows a portion of a road that led to a
river crossing known as the Phillips/Rod Lewis Swiftwater crossing to Post Oak Bend. These crossings
were certainly used, and photos show Burtons attending school with Boultinghouses, Grelles, DeSpains,
Krauses, Hollingsworths, Carpenters, and Coxes in 1910 at Haynie Flat, near Muleshoe Bend. As these
families grew and dispersed, many left their names on pieces of the landscape, such as Boultinghouse
Mountain, Shaffer Bend and Krause Springs, and in geologic descriptions such as Hensel and Smithwick.

Previous Investigations
Scientific archaeological studies in Central Texas can be traced to J. E. Pearce’s early 20th century
investigation of burned rock middens. A number of large-scale excavations later took place as the
Marshall Ford Reservoir (Lake Travis) was under construction. Investigations in Turkey Bend began in
the early 1980s when LCRA instituted a series of fence-building and brush-clearing projects. Numerous
sites were recorded, the majority of which would probably have escaped the attention of the earlier
researchers. Of interest was that several sites which had been recorded as isolated finds of a single
artifact, when revisited during recent surveys, were found to be relatively extensive scatters of prehistoric

46

materials, at times with fairly complex inventories of artifacts. Most recent work has been during the past
10 years as part of ongoing inventory on LCRA lands, and occasionally for minor development projects.
A total of 67 archaeological sites have been recorded, including 47 previously unrecorded (new) sites
documented during recent archaeological reconnaissance and survey. Fifty-three were prehistoric, 11 had
both prehistoric and historic components, and three were strictly historic. There were therefore a total of
76 archaeological components. These 76 components consisted of 10 lithic procurement sites, 23 lithic
scatters, 20 mixed use locales, 11 open camps, three historic house sites with cisterns, four foundations
(two with cisterns), and four historic artifacts scatters (one with an apparent nearby root cellar).
Of the 67 recorded sites, 44 appeared to warrant preservation or additional work if they cannot be avoided
by ground-disturbing activities. These 44 sites consist of 47 individual components: three historic house
sites with cisterns, four foundations (two with cisterns), three historic artifacts scatters (one with what
appears to be a nearby root cellar), two lithic procurement sites, 10 lithic scatters, 15 mixed use areas, and
10 open camps.
Sixty-six percent of the known archaeological sites at Turkey Bend have sufficient preservation to suggest
that their interpretive and research potentials are not exhausted. Sites buried along the lower terraces may
be in excellent condition. Some exposed or shallowly buried prehistoric sites in upland settings also
appear to have intact activity areas. Archival research indicates that some historic sites have the potential
to predate the Civil War, and are associated with persons who were instrumental in early settlement of the
area. Additional field work may help to date both the mapped and unmapped historic sites that are
present, and to place them in association with past residents. Additional artifact analyses and archival
work may clarify the economic status of the residents, especially in regard to the tenant farming system as
seen at nearby Muleshoe Bend.
The interpretive potential is high for both the historic and prehistoric sites. Groups such as the Burnet
County Historical Commission have an ongoing interest in the region and are connected with descendents
of the original settlers. Large areas containing relatively undisturbed prehistoric sites are becoming
increasingly rare. Such site distributions can be the focus of many different types of studies based on
varying theoretical perspectives. For example, the majority of site areas, both historic and prehistoric,
tend to face southeast, presumably taking advantage of both summer and winter climatic variations. The
effects of intermittent inundation on archaeological sites have been addressed in various studies (e.g.,
Lenihan et. al 1981, Will and Clark 1996, Malof 1999), but little research has been conducted on how site
stabilization can best be addressed or the effectiveness of such efforts (Ware 1989:31, Thorne 1988).
There is potential for long-term studies in this area, as well as site preservation in general.

Research Design and Methods

A files search indicated that site 41TV1849, the prehistoric open camp, was the only known site within
the construction impact area. Site 41TV1853, another open camp, is normally submerged under Lake
Travis and has no impacts associated with it, although portions of it will provide access to the water.
Historic site 41TV1835 is within the lease property, but no improvements or land use is planned for its
area. Field work was therefore designed to more fully investigate 41TV1849 and other areas which will
receive project-related impacts and to determine if there were areas potentially eligible for formal listing
as State Archeological Landmarks or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. This was
accomplished by placing shovel tests systematically within areas to be developed, and judgmentally in at
least one spot thought to be of high probability of containing buried cultural materials. Shovel tests
averaged 40 cm in diameter, were excavated in 20 cm levels and matrix was passed through standard 1/4

47

in wire mesh. Total project impacts will be approximately 2 acres within a six area portion of the larger
24 acre lease. The most substantial impacts will be construction of an approximate 1-acre septic drain
field. Other impacts will be placement of piers and grading in preparation for building slab foundations.
In total, 26 shovel tests were placed within this six acre area, well above minimum survey standards as
suggested the by the Texas Historical Commission. Materials recovered from shovel tests were collected,
except in a few cases where recovery was extremely limited. Project maps, aerial imagery, and survey
stakes were used for ground orientation, and a GPS receiver was used to help document shovel test
locations. GPS shots were typically within a 3 m error range. Investigations took place on January 30
and February 1, 2008, by a crew of three archaeologists.

Results of Investigations

As a result of the investigations, the boundaries of 41TV1849 were enlarged considerably, primarily in
the septic drain field to the north (see Figure 10). Shovel testing indicated a very consistent soil profile
across the project area, consisting of reddish-brown gravelly to very gravelly sandy loams that would
contain increasing amounts of cobbles with depth, and typically terminated in reddish clays at about 40
cm. In no place were soils seen to exceed about 55 cm in depth.
Site 41TV1849 occupies a linear ridge at the southern edge of a broad lobe overlooking Lake Travis
(Colorado River), and a low interfluvial divide between two steep drainages just to the north, both
mapped as Travis gravelly soils. This is at the junction of an unnamed spring-fed tributary and the
Colorado River.
Materials observed on the surface during previous reconnaissance and recording included chipped stone,
ground stone and mussel shell. A Fairland point was observed in 1999. The site has been severely
impacted by looting, both in the past, presumably for decades, and recently.
Despite this, it was possible to gather additional information. Flake counts from shovel tests along the
ridge nearest the river had very large numbers of artifacts in the form of lithic debitage. Most of these
were small decorticate flakes. This contrasts with flake counts on the northern half of the ridge, and even
more pronouncedly, with recovery from the lobe located to the north in the septic drain field, where
recovery was very low. A number of time diagnostic artifacts were recovered. These consisted of:
Nolan – ST 2, L 2
Bulverde (?) stem – ST 6, L 2
Castroville – ST 9, L 1
Marcos – ST 26, L 2
Ensor – ST 21, L 2
Fairland – ST1, L 1
Scallorn – ST 20, L 3
Arrow tip (Perdiz?) – ST 14, L 2
Arrow (?) medial – ST 24, L 1
One mano was recovered from the ground surface near ST 5. Interestingly, there appeared to be few
flakes that had been used as tools, with only three or four recognizable as such. No formal unifaces were
recovered. Cores were occasionally found, but hard hammer flakes were rare. Burned rock was not
uncommon, but it was never found in high numbers. It was primarily quartzite, followed by sandstone,
including possible recycled metates. There was almost no burned limestone recognized. The only
organic material recovered was mussel shell, which was fragmentary and highly weathered, and a very

48

small amount of charcoal. Only at ST 6 did there appear to be development of soil with increased organic
content. The soil here was noticeably darker than elsewhere, although there was no distinct difference in
the burned rock count.
Site 41TV1849 is considered to be fully investigated. Although it undoubtedly contains additional time
diagnostic artifacts, there is little to suggest vertical or horizontal patterning (e.g., a Scallorn point was
found below 40 in gravels, a Nolan was found adjacent to the location of a Fairland) and the broad time
range so far represented merely indicates this was a highly favorable location for a long period of time.
The lack of organic preservation (the small amount of charcoal may be of recent origin) and lack of
recognizable burned rock features make any meaningful data recovery a highly tenuous prospect.
The site is almost certainly associated with 41TV1853, located at lower elevations past the tip of the
upper ridge. It extends to near the present river channel. Previous inventory level investigations found
burned rock, debitage, and bone at these lower elevations. A local avocational archaeologist also reported
a human burial near the tip of this lobe, exposed during very low water conditions (30 – 40 ft below
normal pool of 681 amsl). Portions of the site above normal pool are primarily gravels exposed along
slopes that have evidence of lithic procurement activities. This site is also subject to vandalism during
periods of low water. This site will have no direct impacts. When lake levels recede there will certainly
be foot traffic, but at expected lower frequency than at present, and under more controlled conditions.
Site 41TV1835 is recorded as the S. S. Burton House. Deed records and a site description conducted by
Dan Utley indicate the house was built around the turn of the century, perhaps 1909. Little remains but
some period fencing and low mounds of rubble and scattered artifacts generally typical of the period. An
April 1, 2005 obituary was published in the Austin American Statesman for Tiny Burton Naumann, born
in 1902 as the daughter of Silas Sylvania Burton and Mary Ellen Boultinghouse, whose “families farmed
and ranched along the Colorado River as early as the 1850s in an area called Turkey Bend…” The
unpublished Utley files indicate that 41TV1835 is fully recorded and ineligible for formal SAL or
National Register listing. However, no shovel testing was conducted at the site, and should future
development threaten it, minimal shovel testing is warranted in order to confirm there is no earlier
occupation. In addition, adjacent prehistoric buried lithic scatter 41TV1881 may extend into the lease
area and may overlap 41TV1835.
Site 41TV1860 and 41TV1882 are both lithic procurement locales located in cobbles along sloping
surfaces at the southern and northern edges, respectively, of the lease area. No impacts are planned for
either of these two areas, and neither site is felt to contain additional research potential, regardless.

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA has agreed to lease approximately 21 acres of land located in its Turkey Bend property on the
northern bank of Lake Travis in western Travis County to the Young Men’s Business League for
development of the Sunshine Camp for underserved inner-city youth. Construction will involve the
building of six cabins, a restroom/shower facility, and an enclosed pavilion on cement slabs, an
office/medical hut and some small storage buildings as well as a ropes course on piers, and an
approximate 1-acre septic drain field that will serve two septic tanks, one at the shower and one at the
pavilion. Total project impacts are about 2 acres within a six acre portion of the larger lease area.
Development will directly impact site 41TV1849, a large open campsite that was occupied minimally
from Middle Archaic through Late Prehistoric times. A total of 26 shovel tests was excavated that
demonstrate that despite the wealth of chipped stone tools and debitage, there is little additional

49

information that can reasonably be obtained from the site. Extensive looting and relatively shallow,
gravelly sandy loams have resulted in mixed horizontal and vertical components. Site 41TV1849 is
therefore considered ineligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the
National Register of Historic Places.
Site 41TV1853 is normally submerged by Lake Travis, and no direct impacts are planned. Historic Site
41TV1835 is outside any area presently planned for use. Site 41TV1860 and 41TV1882 are minor
procurement locales located on the edge of the lease, and no impacts are planned for these areas either.
A standard clause in LCRA lease agreements states that the agreement is defaulted if the lessee engages
in knowing or reckless destruction or misappropriation of archaeological or historical resources, known or
unknown. This is particularly relevant to site 41TV1853, the SAL status of which remains unknown.
Increased presence and control of the property will help minimize impacts that are presently abated only
through chance encounters with law enforcement personnel. Additionally, any possible future
improvements to the camp will require an LCRA environmental review, which includes a cultural
resources assessment.
It is recommended the project be allowed to proceed as described.
