An Intensive Cultural Resource Survey of the Lower Colorado River Authority’s
Proposed Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area Capital Improvement Plan Project,
Travis County, Texas

By
Daniel J. Prikryl and Cody M. Kiker

Daniel J. Prikryl, Principal Investigator

Texas Antiquities Committee Archeology Permit # 4779
Interim Report #2.08
Project # 5.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
February 2008

56

Abstract
During the period from mid November 2007 through January 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff
conducted an intensive cultural resource survey of the LCRA’s new Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area
Capital Improvements Plan. Four proposed development areas totaling about 160 acres were field
surveyed through a combination of pedestrian survey and subsurface testing that consisted of both
mechanical auger testing and shovel testing.
As a result of the survey, four new cultural resource sites consisting of 41TV2287-2289 and 41TV2291
were discovered and assessed. Six previously recorded sites consisting of 41TV498-500, 41TV505,
41TV507, and 41TV508 were also revisited and assessed.
Prehistoric materials were found at 41TV498, 41TV500, 41TV505, 41TV508, 41TV2287, 41TV2288,
41TV2289, and 41TV2291. All of these sites can be classified as lithic scatters with lithic procurement
activities also evident at 41TV500, 41TV508, 41TV2287, 41TV228, and 41TV2289. In general, the
prehistoric materials at these sites are sparse and lack diversity. No diagnostic artifacts or evidences of
intact features were found at any of these prehistoric sites. Further, the archaeological materials at these
sites either occur in sandy loam deposits that show evidences of bioturbation or occur on upland surfaces
in shallow gravelly sandy loam deposits that are vertical mixed. Given these facts, all eight prehistoric
components examined during the field survey are assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the National
Register of Historic Places and not meriting formal designation as State Archeological Landmarks.
Sites with historic remains consist of 41TV498, 41TV499, 41TV505, 41TV507, and 41TV508. The
recovery of eight hand-forged square nails from two shovel tests placed adjacent to the presumed former
house location at 41TV499, represent the earliest identified artifacts found at any of the historic sites.
Avoidance of an approximately 40x40 meter area centered on these finds is recommended (see Figure
38). This avoidance area is assessed as potentially eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and potentially
meriting formal designation as an SAL. If avoidance of this portion of 41TV499 is not feasible, then
additional investigations will be needed.
Historic remains at all other historic sites and at other portions of 41TV499 all appear to date from the
very late 19th century to as late as the mid 20th century. The dating of these components fits well with
the historic archival and oral history research conducted by Utley (1996) at Muleshoe Bend. His research
indicated that sites 41TV498, 41TV499, 41TV507, and 41TV508 were sharecropper house sites that were
a part of the Turner Farm. The tenant farming operation probably began in the 1880s or 1890s.
Production of cotton and corn on the lowland/floodplain terraces adjacent to the river channel was the
principal economic pursuit at the Turner Farm. Although the fifth historic period site, 41TV505, was not
researched by Utley, it is probable that it was associated with the tenant farming operation.
Except for the potential earlier component area at 41TV499 recommended for avoidance, the other
historic period archaeological remains at these five sites do not appear to have any potential in providing
new information about rural lifeways in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Utley’s historic archival
and oral history research has already provided a more detailed story about the history of the Turner Farm
and its tenants. Since Utley interviewed many individuals who were already quite elderly in 1996, it is
probable that very few of them or other individuals who were not interviewed are still living. Because of
the very limited potential for additional oral history research and lack of the archaeological potential to
provide new and interesting data, the historic components at 41TV498, 41TV505, 41TV507, 41TV508
are all assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not worthy of formal designation as SALs.
Similarly, the portion of site 41TV499 not recommended for avoidance is assessed as not NRHP or SAL
eligible.

57

Aside from the avoidance of a portion of site 41TV499, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff
recommends that the proposed Muleshoe Bend CIP project be allowed to proceed without any further
cultural resource investigations. As always, standard emergency discovery provisions are applicable to
this project. Should the construction personnel uncover any presently unknown buried archeological
features during construction work, then work should cease in the immediate area of the discovery and the
LCRA Archeology Services staff should be notified at 1-800-776-5272, ext. 6714 so that the find can be
assessed in consultation with the THC. In such a situation, work can continue in other areas where no
archeological materials are present.

Introduction
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing a set of park improvements in new Capital
Improvements Plan (CIP) for its Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area, which is located on the south side of
Lake Travis in Travis and Burnet counties, Texas (Figure 12). The improvements will include a multi-use
campground where overnight camping pads will be constructed along with a pavilion and restrooms with
showers at Development Area 1 (Figure 13). Three new day use areas that are proposed are shown as
Development Areas 2-4 on Figure 13. Facilities proposed for these day use areas include restrooms,
picnic tables, pavilions, special event areas, volleyball courts, playscapes, small floating docks, a disc golf
course, and interpretative kiosks. In total, about 160 of the 787 acres currently open to the public will be
developed during the implementation of the Muleshoe Bend CIP.
From November 2007 through January 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted an
intensive cultural resource survey of the areas slated for development. The survey consisted of pedestrian
survey, shovel testing, and mechanical auger testing, Personnel who conducted the fieldwork consisted of
LCRA staff members Daniel Prikryl (Principal Investigator) and Andrew Malof and contracted
archaeologists Cody Kiker, Patrick Hatten, and Glenn Goode. The investigation was conducted under
TAC Blanket Permits #4412 and #4779, which were issued to the LCRA by the Texas Historical
Commission (THC) for Calendar Years 2007 and 2008, respectively. All work was completed in
accordance with methods for fieldwork and reporting as proposed by the Texas Council of Archeologists
(1992).

Environmental Background
Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area is located within the Edwards Plateau Section of the Great Plains
physiographic province (Carr 1969:3). The Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area is a part of northeast to
southwest trending terrace that is situated within a giant bend of the Colorado River. The recent in-house
Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area Land Management Plan (LCRA 2006:14-16) provides information on
the three environmental/topographic zones present at the park. Data on these three zones –
lowland/floodplain, uplands margins, and uplands – is summarized in the next three paragraphs.
The lowland/floodplain zone extends from the top of the original bank of the Colorado River at an
elevation of 620 ft mean sea level (msl) up to 700 ft (msl). Most of this zone is normally inundated by the
conservation pool level of 681 ft msl of Lake Travis. However, the eastern portion of Development Area
2, the northern edge of Development Area 3, and almost all of Development Area 4 lie within the
lowland/floodplain zone. Detailed mapping of the river basin undertaken just prior to the construction of
the lake indicates that within the park area, the lowland/floodplain terrace is as wide as 950 to 1,950 ft in
some areas. The lowland/floodplain zone consists of deep alluvial terrace deposits (Barnes 1981).

58

During times of low lake levels due to drought, staff examined cutbanks that indicate that this terrace has
40 ft alluvial deposits in some areas adjacent to the river channel.
The upland margins extend from about 700 to 800 ft msl. All of the Development Area 1 and the majority
of Development Area 3 are situated within this zone. In many parts of the park, base of the upland
margins are marked by moderately steep slopes that rise abruptly from the inner edges of the
lowland/floodplain. The surfaces of the upland margins are draped with Pleistocene-era

Figure 12. Section of the Pace Bend, Texas 7.5’ USGS map showing the LCRA Muleshoe Bend property
boundary outlined in red.

59

fluviatile terrace deposits (Barnes 1981). These terrace deposits are generally less than 4 ft in thickness
and include large volumes of lag gravels (Figure 14.
None of the proposed development areas are located in the upland zone. Topographically, the upland
zone extends from 800 ft msl upward. Upland surfaces have little to no topsoil. Outcrops of the Cow
Creek Limestone and Hammett Shale are frequently evident on ground surface in upland areas as are
surface exposures of Pleistocene lag gravel deposits (Barnes 1983)

Figure 13. Enlarged section of the Pace Bend, Texas 7.5’ USGS map showing the four proposed
Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area Capital Improvements Plan development areas outlined in black.

60

A variety of soil types is present within the four proposed development areas (Werchan et al 1974). The
Travis soils (TrC) cover the majority of the upland margins ridges and side slopes of Development Area
1. The representative soil profile of the Travis soils has an approximately 36 cm thick surface layer of
brown and light brown fine sandy loam that is slight acidic and contains a few rounded pebbles. The
underlying B21t and B22t horizons are usually a red to light red to reddish brown sandy clay of about 91
cm thickness.

Figure 14. Examples of chert cobbles in lag gravel deposit exposed on slope of upland margins at
Development Area 3.
The Doughtery loamy sand (DoA) is found across much of the lower elevations of Development Area 2
and in a small part of Development Area 1. The surface layer of pale brown to light brown loamy sand
ranges from 60 to 100 cm thickness. This deposit is well-drained and acidic in nature. The B horizon is a
red to light red sandy clay loam of approximately 90 thickness.
The Travis gravelly soils (TsD) are the principal soil type in higher elevations at the north end of
Development Area 2. This soil also covers small portions of Development Area 1. Where not eroded
from previous land use, this soil usually has a surface layer that is an approximately 46 cm thick brown
gravelly fine sandy loam. The upper part is brown in color while the lower part is light reddish brown.
The next layer is the red gravelly sandy clay B horizon that is typically 80 cm in thickness.
The Tarrant soils and Rock outcrop (TdF) occupy the entire Development Area 4 tract. In some areas,
limestone outcrops and/or smaller loose limestone fragments cover the ground surface. Elsewhere, the
surface layer is an approximately 18 cm thick dark grayish brown stony clay or stony clay loam. This
deposit is underlain by limestone bedrock.
A final minor soil type is the Volente complex (VoD) which is a calcareous soil that occurs in the
bottomlands of several narrow valleys in Development Area 3. In representative profile, Volente
complex soils have a surface deposit of dark grayish brown silty clay loam of about 56 cm thickness. The

61

next layer is usually an approximately 36 cm deposit of dark brown silty clay. Deeper, the B horizon is a
dark brown silty clay of 25 cm thickness.
Vegetation found on the alluvial terraces such as the eastern part of Development Area 2 and northern
edge of Development Area 3 consists a predominantly herbaceous community that includes
bermudagrass, switchgrass, and eastern gammagrass with some buttonbush and mesquite trees also
present. Post oak, live oak, and Buckley’s yucca, cow pen daisy, Texas croton, and wooly croton are also
found on these terraces.
In the upland margins in remaining development areas, dense thickets of Ashe juniper are the
predominant vegetation along with some live oak, post oak, and mesquite trees (Figure 15). Shrub
species including agarita, hog plum, prickly pear, brasil, Texas persimmon, twist leaf yucca, and
mountain laurel are very common. In some areas, especially at the locations of former historic house
sites, white brush, tassajila catci, and prickly pear catci are very dense (Figure 16).

Figure 15. Typical view of Ashe juniper trees in Development Area 4.

Figure 16. Example of dense white brush vegetation at site 41TV508 in Development Area 3.

62

Culture History
Prehistoric Background
The project area is located within the Central Texas archeological region (Prewitt 1981; Ellis et al. 1995).
The Central Texas archeological record has typically been divided into four main subdivisions. These
periods, in chronological order from most ancient to most recent are: the Paleoindian, the Archaic, the
Late Prehistoric, and the Historic periods. These periods are differentiated mostly by broadly defined
subsistence patterns. The prehistoric part of this chronology has been subdivided further into phases
based largely on changes in material culture, especially projectile point styles. Combined, this
information begins to describe the 11,500 year history of human occupation in Central Texas.
A longstanding chronology of Prewitt (1981, 1985), has recently been revised by Johnson and Goode
(1994) and Johnson (1995), who utilized new lines of evidence based on climatic and geomorphological
data to redefine period divisions. This can be contrasted with Collins (1995), who indicates climatic
variables at times were somewhat different than those of Johnson and Goode. Though these chronologies
are invaluable to our knowledge base for Central Texas archeology, they must be used as a stepping-stone
to expand our understanding of the cultural history of an area by addressing the question of why changes
occurred in various regions and cultures when they did.
Prehistoric site types in Central Texas consist of camps, caches, isolated artifacts, interments, cemeteries,
kill/butcher locales, quarry/workshops, lithic scatters, and rock art sites (Collins 1995:363). Central
Texas is perhaps best known for the many burned rock midden sites that occur on the Edwards Plateau.
Numerous excavations of major campsites have been conducted along the larger streams and rivers (cf.
Peter et al. 1982; Prewitt 1982). Such excavations have demonstrated that Central Texas was occupied
for at least 11,500 years prior to the coming of Europeans. These also show that throughout these
millennia, prehistoric peoples were nomadic hunter-gatherers who moved across the landscape exploiting
seasonally available plant and animal resources.
Historic Background
Most of the historic background information in the following paragraphs is taken from Utley’s (1996)
previous historical study of the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area. A number of local area residents,
including some who lived within Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area on the historic tenant farms as children,
were interviewed by Utley. Their recollections of life on the tenant farms as well as descriptions of the
farmsteads are found in Utley’s (1996) report entitled Through Boundary Waters, which was published by
the LCRA.
Although some undocumented historic period activities may have occurred earlier, it was not until the
1870s that sustained, intensive settlement of the immediate Muleshoe Bend area began. Muleshoe Bend
has traditionally been considered to be a part of a dispersed, rural community called Haynie Flat. While
this community did not have a commercial center, there was at least one cotton gin in operation in the
early 20th century. It was located about one-half mile south of the project area. The real focal point of
the community was the local school and nearby cemetery, which are located about 2 miles south of the
park. The one-room school was started in the 1870s and offered only seven or eight grades. The school
operated until 1949, when it was consolidated with the Spicewood school.
The first settlers in the vicinity of Muleshoe Bend were subsistence farmers. They planted small patches
of corn and vegetables, raised a few cattle and hogs, and hunted wild game. Although the rocky, shallow
soils west of Austin made farming difficult, by the late nineteenth century there appears to have been
increased economic emphasis on cotton farming area on the broad floodplain terrace expanses adjacent to

63

the Colorado River at Muleshoe Bend. Specifically, the economic system of land tenancy, that was
prevalent across the eastern half Texas at that time, was found on much of the property that now
comprises Muleshoe Bend Park. Utley notes that this was an economic system “where cashless tenants
worked for landlords who lacked sufficient capital to pay wages.” Basically, the production of crops took
the place of rent, and tenants worked on shares, typically referred to as “thirds” or “fourths,” with the
fraction referring to the amount of the tenant’s crop that was owed to the landlord for use of his property.
Tenant farm families were provided houses on the land they worked by the landowner. Sometimes, too,
the owner provided farm machinery for crop production.
At Muleshoe Bend, George S. Turner, a prominent rancher, started a sharecropping farm operation in the
late 19th century. Following his death in 1903, his granddaughter, Lura Penelope “Nella” Turner Evans
inherited this land and oversaw the farm operations until she sold the land to the LCRA in 1939. Today,
the descendants of some of the sharecropper families who were tenants on the Turner Farm still live in the
vicinity of Muleshoe Bend Park. The locations and family names of the occupants of five tenant farm
houses are shown on a 1936 era contour map made by LCRA land surveyors of the then proposed Lake
Travis basin (Figure 17).
The archeological remains of six tenant farmsteads and one unrelated owner-occupied farmstead are
among the cultural resource sites within the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area. All houses and
outbuildings at five of these sites were deliberately removed by LCRA when it purchased the Turner
Farm over 70 years ago. LCRA allowed one of the farmsteads to remain occupied into the 1950s, and
then demolished that house and its associated outbuildings. In some cases, underground cement-walled
cisterns, root cellars, trash dumps, portions of barbed wire fences, and remnants of house foundations are
still discernable at these sites. Limited numbers of artifacts such as metal fragments and broken glass and
ceramics are also visible on ground surface at the sites.
Cotton farming and its accompanying tenant farming system waned in the 1930s and 1940s across Texas,
at approximately the same time that the LCRA was purchasing the Turner Farm to become a part of its
Lake Travis lands. In adjacent areas not bought by the LCRA, cattle ranching became the predominant
economic activity in the following decades along with land leasing for deer hunting. In more recent
years, many areas adjacent to Muleshoe Bend Park have been subdivided for rural residency with most
occupants either being either retirees or workers who commute to and from Austin each day.

Previous Archaeological Investigations
In the 1930s, when archeologists conducted a reconnaissance survey of the Lake Travis basin prior to the
construction of Mansfield Dam, one archeological site, 41TV250, was recorded in the Muleshoe Bend
park tract. Jackson (1938) described it as a small prehistoric campsite with shallow deposits. No
excavation work was recommended at that time. In 1979, Prewitt and Associates, Inc. recorded 16
additional cultural resource sites within the Muleshoe Bend

64

Figure 17. Section of the 2-ft. contour map of the Lake Travis basin and adjoining lands produced in
1936 showing the Muleshoe Bend area. House locations are shown as small hollow squares. In some
cases, the family names are shown with the house locations.
Recreation Area in advance of a proposed LCRA brush control project (Prewitt and Kotter 1979). These
consist of sites 41BT147 and 41BT148 in Burnet County and sites 41TV495 through 508
in Travis County. Site 41TV250 was also revisited during that investigation. In all, Prewitt and
Associates recorded 13 prehistoric sites, 3 historic sites, and 1 multi-component site that is primarily

65

historic, but includes a small amount of prehistoric material. Most of the prehistoric sites were described
as upland lithic scatters and lithic procurement areas. Only four of the prehistoric sites were described as
having campsite materials. The historic period sites all appeared to be former late 19th to early 20th
century house sites. Although the houses had been removed, surface features including cisterns,
foundation stones, rock walls, and root cellars were still evident. In summarizing the historic sites, Utley
(1996:64) later noted that of the four historic sites recorded by Prewitt and Kotter, 41TV499 and
41TV507 appeared to be the most stable and had been less damaged. Overall, Prewitt and Kotter (1979)
recommended avoidance of 13 of the prehistoric and historic sites during the proposed brush clearing
program.
Additional cultural resource survey work was conducted by the LCRA archaeological staff intermittently
from 1995 through 1998, and on a few occasions thereafter. Staff investigations in the 1995-1997 era
primarily focused on historic period house sites in conjunction with Utley’s (1996) historical research.
Other fieldwork that focused on prehistoric sites was conducted during droughts when areas normally
covered by the lake were exposed. All of these investigations were primarily reconnaissance level work,
and importantly, did not include any shovel testing or other types of subsurface probing. As a result of all
previous investigations, a total of 33 archaeological sites had been recorded prior to the current
investigation for the proposed CIP.

Methodology
Prior to the field survey, a cultural resource file search including a review of data on the THC’s
computerized Site Atlas database along with LCRA’s internal cultural resource files was conducted. The
file search showed that there were five recorded cultural resource sites within the four proposed
development areas. These consisted of the north end of site 41TV498 in Development Area 1, the eastern
parts of sites 41TV499 and 41TV505 within Development Area 2, 41TV508 within Development Area 3,
and the southern part of 41TV500 within Development Area 4. Additionally, it appeared that site
41TV507 was located either within or immediately adjacent to the southeast edge of Development Area 3.
The review of LCRA’s internal files also showed that LCRA had previously sponsored Utley’s (1996)
historical study of Muleshoe Bend that included archival research and oral history interviews. The results
of that study were reviewed prior to the field investigation.
The field survey for the CIP project consisted of 100% pedestrian survey along with shovel and auger
testing of all four proposed development areas. Additionally, areas immediately south of Development
Area 1 and east of Development Area 3 were surveyed because of concerns of secondary impacts to
previously recorded sites 41TV498 and 41TV507.
Appropriate USGS quadrangle maps and aerial photographs were used for ground orientation. During the
pedestrian survey, the archeologists walked 30-m transects across the project area. Bare and eroded areas
on the ground surface were examined for the presence of cultural materials. Overall, ground surface
visibility was found to be poor in most areas. Shovel tests were placed at locations where ground surface
visibility was poor in areas thought to have a potential for archeological materials. All shovel and auger
tests were excavated to a depth of 100 cm or to the top of the B horizon clay. Other shovel tests were
utilized to examine the subsurface deposits of previous recorded and newly discovered archaeological
sites and help define their boundaries. A total of 85 shovel tests and 35 auger tests were excavated
(Figure 18). All shovel and auger tests were excavated in 20 cm levels with soil matrix passed through ¼
wire mesh artifact recovery. The number of subsurface tests easily meets the Texas Historical
Commission’s (THC) requirements for subsurface examination of projects in the100 to 200 acre range.
Figure 19 shows the numbered locations of all subsurface tests within the four development areas. Data

66

generated from the shovel and auger tests were recorded on LCRA shovel test forms. Appendices 1 and 2
provide artifact tabulations and soil profile descriptions, respectively, for these subsurface probes.
All sites were recorded on the TexSite database and documented with sketch maps. Site were also
photographed and plotted on aerial maps.

Figure 18. Shovel testing in progress at site 41TV498.

Survey Results
As a result of the survey, four new cultural resource sites were discovered and six previously recorded
sites were revisited. Descriptions and assessments of these sites are provided by development area in the
following paragraphs.
Development Area 1
Site 41TV498
The far north edge of this cultural resource site is located within the southern edge of Development Area
1 (Figure 20). Topographically, the site is located on a terrace in the upland margins with a moderate
sized tributary inlet situated about 60 meters west of the west edge of the site. The site covers an area
measuring about 300 meters on a north-south line by 150 meters on an east-west line. The long axis of
the site is bisected by the main park entry road, and the site is currently covered with dense brush and
thick grasses.

67

Figure 19. Locations of all subsurface tests within and near the four development areas.
41TV498 was originally recorded during Prewitt and Associates, Inc.’s survey for proposed brush
clearing as a prehistoric lithic scatter/procurement site situated on the west side of the park entry road
(Prewitt and Kotter 1979). The site was assessed as not requiring avoidance since materials appeared to
be surficial and partially disrupted. In 1996 and 1997, LCRA staff expanded the site

68

Figure 20. Development Area 1 showing locations of sites 41TV498 and 41TV2291 and the locations of
all subsurface tests.
boundaries eastward across the park entry road where historic features and artifacts were found as were
additional prehistoric materials.
The historic component is a late nineteenth to mid twentieth century farmstead that is shown on the prereservoir 2 ft contour map as the Trammell house. Utley’s (1996) research indicates that the historic

69

component was one of seven tenant farm houses that were a part of the Turner Farm. While all of the
other farm house sites were demolished in the 1930s when LCRA became the owner of the land, the
Trammell house was left standing and it continued to be occupied by renters into the 1950s.
Historic features that are still evident at the site consist of a concentration of foundation stones at the
southwest end of the site where the house once stood, a collapsed outbuilding (Figure 21) located at the
southeast end of the site, and a cattle chute with a dilapidated cattle pen enclosure (Figure 22) which are
located at the northwest end of the site. Individual artifacts noted on

Figure 21. Remains of collapsed outbuilding at site 41TV498.

Figure 22. Remains of cattle chute and associated stock pen at site 41TV498.

70

ground surface at the site include window screens, bedsprings, a tractor seat, and fragments of glass, sheet
metal, and crockery. Two larger trash piles, that are located on the opposite side of the entry road from
the historic features, were also observed in 1996.
After the main features were relocated during the present survey and the surface was examined for
artifacts, shovel testing was untaken. Eleven positive shovel tests (STs 69, 70, and 74 through 84) were
recorded (Figure 23). These positive shovel tests indicate that the majority of the subsurface historic
artifacts cluster around the former house (see foundation stones on Figure 23).

Figure 23. Map of site 41TV498 showing locations of features and shovel tests.

71

and the collapsed outbuilding although the surface scatter of historic artifacts extends northward to the
area of the former cattle chute and stock pen. Historic artifact yield was densest in and around the areas
of the former house and collapsed outbuilding where STs 77, 79, and 80 were excavated (See Appendix
1). At ST 80 which had the highest artifact yield, several nails, a piece of copper wire, a whiteware sherd,
and several pieces of glass were recovered. These are most probably related to the former house and
associated domestic activities. These artifacts and the other recovered at the site confirm previous
estimations of an occupation that dated from the very late 19th through mid 20th centuries. While the
historic artifact yield in shovel testing is low, it should be noted that previous investigators had noted the
presence of two historic trash dumps further southwestward on the opposite side of the park entry road in
areas that were not examined for the current development project.
The shovel testing indicated that widely dispersed prehistoric artifacts, consisting of about 29 chert flakes,
one chert core, one mussel shell fragment are also present. These occur in nine shovel tests throughout all
of the twenty cm levels in the one-meter deep shovel tests. Thus, prehistoric artifact density is very low.
Only two burned rock fragments were found, and these are just as likely to be related to historic activities.
No prehistoric features or diagnostic artifacts were found, nor have any been reported by previous
surveyors.
Given the results of the current and previous archaeological investigations of the historic component, and
the relatively late date of this component, no further archaeological investigation of the portion of the
historic component within the CIP area is recommended. All pertinent oral history and archival data has
already been retrieved during Utley’s previous study. The prehistoric component appears to represent a
low density lithic scatter. Based this fact, along with the absence of prehistoric features or diagnostic
artifacts, no further investigation of the prehistoric component is recommended. Overall, the portion of
site 41TV498 that was investigated for the current CIP project is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in
the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) and not meriting formal designation as a State
Archeological Landmark (SAL).

Site 41TV2291
This prehistoric lithic scatter site is located within the east central end of Development Area 1 on gently
sloping terrain on the upland margins zones. Although no prehistoric materials are visible on ground
surface, small numbers of chert flakes were recovered at depths ranging from 20 to 80 cm below ground
surface in a cluster of four subsurface tests - STs 63, 113, 114, and 119 (Figure 24). No artifacts were
found in level 5 (80 to 100 cm) in any of the shovel tests, and most the positive and surrounding negative
yield shovel tests struck either basal clay or gravelly deposits at approximately 100 cm below ground
surface.
The site extends over an area measuring 140 m on a NW-SE line by 75 m on a NE-SW line. Total artifact
recovery from the four positive shovel tests consisted of 10 small chert tertiary flakes and chips. No
features or diagnostic artifacts were seen. The topsoils are the site range from loamy sands to fine sandy
loams. The fact that such soils are susceptible to bioturbation probably explains the 60 cm vertical range
of the artifacts. Site 41TV2291 is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not worthy of
formal designation as an SAL.

72

Figure 24. Map showing site 41TV2291 outlined in red.

Development Area 2
Site 41TV499
This historic period site is located in the northwest part of Development Area 2 on northeast part of an
upland terrace that overlooks the former Colorado River floodplain (Figure 25). The site

73

Figure 25. Development Area 2 showing the locations of sites 41TV499 and 41TV505 and locations of all
subsurface tests.
was first recorded during Prewitt and Associates, Inc.’s 1979 survey for LCRA’s proposed brush clearing
activities (Prewitt and Kotter 1979). Faint traces of a dirt road that extended on an east-west line from an
existing gravel-covered park road were noted along the southern end of the site. Overall, Prewitt and
Kotter (1979:3.4) estimated that the site covers an area of about 100 meters on an east-west line by 70
meters on a north-south line.

74

Features noted at the site during the original recording were described as including an apparent limestone
chimney foundation that was associated with two large, flat limestone boulders that were thought to
possibly be foundation piers for a house. The investigators stated that it appeared that the house had been
deliberately removed. Two segments of a low limestone wall were also seen, as were two subsurface
cisterns and a short section of aligned wooden fence posts. Prewitt and Kotter (1979:3.5) stated that
cultural debris seen at the site included pre-1914 era glass was indicative of a late 19th to early 20th
century occupation. They recommended avoidance of the site during the proposed brush clearing
activities.
Subsequent archival research by Utley (1996:69-70) indicated that this historic site was occupied by the
Boyd Heffington family in 1936, and that the family was among the tenants on the Turner Farm. Utley
located and interviewed Lyndon Heffington, a son who resided in Marble Falls. He stated that he was
seven years old when the family moved off the tenant house site, and did not remember much about the
farm, except for the in-ground cisterns associated with the house site. At the time of the interview, he was
the only surviving Heffington child who had lived at the site. Utley (1996:70) did learn from another
interviewee, Bea Ruth Tatum Collier that the Tatum family had also resided on the Heffington house site
for several years, and that the Robert Holliway family may have also resided in the house briefly.
Although the site has been become severely overgrown with invasive species, principally various catci
and bee brush, it was relocated during the current survey investigation (Figure 26). The cistern at the
western edge was found to have been capped with concrete in recent years for safety concerns since it lies
near one of the current park roads. The foundation stones and chimney foundation were relocated in the
central part of the site as was the limestone wall section shown on Kotter’s previous field sketch map to
the west of the house. However, the cistern previously mapped at the far northeast end of the site could
not be found, nor was the survey crew able to relocate the line of several limestone blocks previously
mapped by Kotter in 1979 on the far east edge of the site. It is probable that these features are now
covered by the dense vegetation present at that part of the site.
Six positive shovel and auger tests (STs 13, 33, 34, 35, 38, 39, 40) were excavated at the site. Artifact
yield was high in the first two levels of ST 35, which was excavated about 20 meters northwest of the
house and chimney foundation stones (see Appendix 1). Debris recovered from Level 1 of that shovel
test included seven square nails, one whiteware sherd, one brown slipped Albany type sherd, one wire
fragment, one metal bolt, three sheet metal fragments, one clear glass fragment, and one thick mussel
shell fragment. In Level 2 of ST 35 consisted of a horse shoe fragment, one metal door or drawer handle,
one metal strip fragment, and one shotgun shell cap.
Elsewhere, ST 39, which was placed about 10 meters south of the house and chimney foundation stones,
had one square nail, one blue plastic button, one clear glass fragment, one curved clear glass fragment,
and one patinated clear glass fragment in the first level. Level 2 of that shovel test was sterile. Five other
shovel tests yielded very small numbers of historic period artifacts. Based on the results of the shovel
testing, it appears that the majority of the historic artifact assemblage is located within 10 to 20 meters of
the former house.
Several prehistoric artifacts were also found during the subsurface testing. These consist of single chert
tertiary flakes found in Levels 1 and 6 in ST 13 and a chert secondary flake that was recovered in Level 1
of ST 38. No prehistoric diagnostics, burned rocks, or other artifact classes were noted. Since prehistoric
materials are extremely sparse, there appears to be no potential for any further useful investigation of the
prehistoric component at this site.

75

Figure 26. 41TV499 site map showing locations of features, subsurface tests, and proposed avoidance
area.
To conclude, the current investigations indicate that site 41TV499 covers an area measuring about 140
meters on a NE-SW line by 80 meters on a NW-SE line. Most of the historic period artifacts found at
41TV499 came from subsurface tests in the vicinity of the former house. Poor ground surface visibility
severely hampered inspection of the ground surface for additional artifacts, and is responsible, too, for the

76

inability to relocate two features at the northeast and eastern edges of the site. Although the majority of
the historic artifact assemblage appears to be typical of very late 19th to early 20th century sites, several
square nails were found in STs 35 and 39 in the area where the old house is thought to have stood. These
could be evidence of an earlier historic period structure had once stood at this location. Because of the
potential significance of the area around the old house, this part of the site is recommended for avoidance
(see Figure 26). The portion of the site around the old house is assessed as potentially NRHP and SAL
eligible. The remainder of the site is assessed as not NRHP and SAL eligible.
41TV505
This prehistoric and historic site is located at the northern end of Development Area 2 (see Figure 25). It
was originally recorded during the Prewitt and Associates, Inc. survey for an LCRA brush control project
in 1979 (Prewitt and Kotter 1979:3.8-3.9). The site was described as containing a possible limestone
chimney foundation, a light scatter of historic debris and a light scatter of prehistoric lithic debris located
on a small sand and gravel knoll approximately 30 meters south/ southwest of the lake in the northeastern
part of the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area. It was also noted that the site had already been disturbed by
previous recreational activities, namely camping and picnicking. When revisited in 1997 and 2005 the
site was described as further disturbed by recreational use.
During the current investigation, the cut limestone possible chimney foundation was relocated (Figure
27). This limestone pile is less than 2x2 meters and contained smaller, irregularly shaped rocks. Beyond
this pile of cut limestone, no prehistoric or historic artifacts were found in shovel tests or on the surface
other than recent camping and flood debris.

Development Area 3
41TV507
The historic site is located immediately east of the eastern edge of Development Area 3 (Figure 28). It
was originally recorded during the Prewitt and Associates, Inc. survey for an LCRA brush control project
in 1979 (Prewitt and Kotter 1979:3.10-3.11). The site was described as a former house site that is located
on low gravelly sandy rise in the northeastern part of the Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area. Historic
features discovered consisted of an approximately 10x10 meter limestone foundation outline that
appeared to represent the location of a former
house structure, a subsurface cistern with an above ground concrete collar, and a 2x3 meter subsurface
depression with corner wooden posts that was thought to represent a root cellar. Artifacts seen by the
surveyors included whiteware and stoneware sherds, glass fragments, and metal fragments. The artifact
assemblage suggested a late 19th to early 20th century occupation.
Prewitt and Associates, Inc. recommended avoidance of the site during brush clearing activities since the
site appeared to be fairly intact except for the removal of house structure.
Utley’s (1996:73) subsequent archival research and oral history interviews determined that the house had
been occupied by the John Tatum family and that it had been a part of the Turner Farm complex. Utley’s
interview with family daughter Bea Ruth Tatum Collier provided data on the former house. It apparently
was a four room structure of board-and-batten construction. These rooms consisted of two bedrooms, a
living room, and a dining room/kitchen. There were two

77

Figure 27. Map of site 41TV505 showing the locations of features and subsurface tests.
doors that faced onto a front porch. According to Mrs. Collier, the house was typical of the tenant houses
on the Turner Farm.
Mrs. Collier also indicated that the root cellar that had been noted by Prewitt and Kotter was a hand-dug
storm cellar that had heavy logs on its top and a doored entry (Figure 29). She remembered that there had
been a smokehouse, a chicken house, and a barn that also served as an automobile garage at the site. She
also recollected that the cistern was not always dependable. Thus, the family hauled water from the river
in barrels. To conserve water, the family bathed in the river, and watered their stock at the river.
According to local informants, the John Tatum house burned down around 1934.

78

Figure 28. Detailed map of Development Area 3 showing sites 41TV507, 41TV508, 41TV2287-2289
and locations of all subsurface tests.
During the time that Utley was conducted his research, the LCRA archaeological staff, revisited site
41TV507 to map the site and more precisely plot its location on a USGS map. In 2005, the LCRA
archaeological staff also checked the condition of the site. An additional feature, which consists of a
dispersed cluster of cut limestone foundation stones, was observed about 35 meters northwest of the
house foundation outline. These cut stones could mark the location of the barn,

79

Figure 29. View of storm cellar depression at site 41TV507 with faintly visible crew member standing in
the bottom of the depression.
garage, or smokehouse that had been described by Mrs. Collier. Because these stones lay on a terrace
edge overlooking a small tributary drainage, they may, instead, represent a trash dump.
During the present survey investigation, the site was relocated (Figure 30). The cistern, house foundation
outline, and storm cellar were easily identified, as was the new feature that had been discovered northwest
of the house area in 2005 . This site does not appear to have been as densely overgrown with invasive
species as other historic sites within the Muleshoe CIP areas. Thus, surface artifacts are more apparent on
ground surface especially immediately north of the house foundation outline. In that area various clear
and light green glass dish and glass fragments were seen as well as whiteware ceramic sherds.
Four shovel tests (STs 93, 94, 104, and 105) were also excavated to further gauge the potential for buried
archaeological remains. Topsoil deposits were found to range from 15 to 33 cm. Only two, STs 93 and
104, produced any artifacts, and the artifact yields of these were very minor. ST 93 had one metal fence
staple and a very small piece of sheet metal while at ST 104 the artifacts consisted of three clear glass
fragments. All of these artifacts were found in Level 1.
During the present field investigation, GPS readings indicated that the previous plotting of the site on
USGS maps was incorrect. As a result of the GPS readings, the site’s location was moved about 95
meters northeastward. The corrected location places the site just outside of the eastern boundary of
Development Area 3. At that point, field investigation of the site was halted.
However, the revisit to site 41TV507 did indicate that preservation and visibility of features are much
better than at other historic sites investigated during the survey. The good preservation of

80

Figure 30. 41TV507 site map showing locations of features and subsurface tests.

81

features may be due to the fact that LCRA did not have to use heavy machinery to demolish the house
since it burned down several years prior to LCRA’s purchase of the property. Despite the good
preservation of features, the shovel testing had a low yield. This site is among the group of tenant farmer
house sites that were a part of the Turner Farm that was researched by Utley (1996). The potential for
additional oral history data is thought to be very limited. Overall, the site has been documented and is
assessed as not eligible for NRHP or formal SAL listing.

41TV508
This historic site was first described in Prewitt and Kotter’s (1979:3.10) brush clearing report. It is
located in the western part of the Muleshoe CIP Development Area 3 on an upland terrace ridge (see
Figure 27). The site overlooks the now inundated former Colorado River floodplain terrace which lies to
the north of the site. The site was described as consisting of a few structural remains, a subsurface
cistern with an above ground collar (Figure 31), and an artifact concentration that appeared to date to the
early 20th century. The site was assessed as insignificant because of the absence of any substantial
structural remains.

Figure 31. View of above-ground collar of subsurface water cistern at site 41TV508.
Utley’s (1996) archival and oral history research confirmed that this site was another tenant farm house
site that was a part of the Turner Farm. Utley was not able to obtain much oral history data on this site,
but was able to learn that the site had been occupied by the Reeves and Vivian Tatum family for several
years in the 1930s.
In 1996, the LCRA archaeological staff revisited the site to map features and further assess the site’s SAL
status. The site location was replotted on a USGS map and a scaled map of the site was drawn depicting
its few features and scattered debris. Prehistoric materials representing an ephemeral prehistoric lithic
scatter were also noted. Based on the revisit, the LCRA staff assessed the site as not meriting formal
designation as an SAL.

82

During the current investigation, the site was relocated, but was found to be very densely overgrown with
tassajila, white brush, prickly pear, various grasses, Ashe juniper, and mesquite (see Figure 16). The
cistern was relocated at the southern of the site, and shallow depression was also observed about 20
meters east of the cistern (Figure 32). Elsewhere, a large limestone block was found on ground surface
in the north central part of the site. A cluster of late 19th to early 20th century artifacts was seen on
ground surface in the central part of the site about 10 to 30 meters north of the previously mentioned
shallow depression.

Figure 32. Map of site 41TV508 showing locations of features and subsurface tests.
Shovel Test 92 was then excavated on the eastern edge of this surface artifact cluster. This shovel test
yielded a high density of historic materials within a 12 cm thick cm surface deposit that had an ashy gray

83

color suggestive of previous burning. Artifacts from the shovel test consisted of 23 patinated clear bottle
glass fragments, 29 patinated clear flat glass fragments, 3 patinated brown bottle glass fragments, 2
patinated aqua bottle glass fragments, 5 milk glass fragments, 24 rusted metal fragments, 7 whiteware
sherds, 1 porcelin sherd, 4 light blue glazed flatware sherds, 1 blue and white glazed rim sherd, 1 orange
or terracotta colored sherd, and 3 tan stoneware sherds with interior black glaze. Beneath this gray, ashy
soil lens, the red clay B horizon was encountered at 12 cm below ground surface. Overall, the results of
this shovel test were suggestive of a trash dump at which materials had been purposely burned.
The other five shovel tests containing historic materials yielded much smaller amounts of historic
materials (See Appendix 1). Sparse numbers of prehistoric chert flakes, a core, and a battered cobble
were found in the five subsurface tests at the north end of the site within the approximately 30 to 35 cm
thick topsoil deposits. Several of these shovel tests also contained burned chert chunks but no burned
rock clusters were noted. Since historic artifacts were also present with the burned rocks, it is uncertain
whether these burned chunks are associated with the prehistoric component or are burned materials
associated with the historic period occupation.
In summary, site 41TV508 is a multi-component site containing a late 19th to early 20th century historic
sharecropper component associated with the Turner Farm and a sparse prehistoric lithic scatter. No
definite location for the house structure was found during the current fieldwork or during past
investigations. Observed features consist of a subsurface water cistern with an above ground collar and a
shallow depression that may represent a storm cellar. One artifact cluster was observed on ground surface
in the east central part of the site. A shovel test excavated at that location produced a high yield of
artifacts within an ashy deposit suggestive of former burning. The historic artifact assemblage appears to
date from the late 19th to early 20th centuries.
Prehistoric materials lack diversity and no features or diagnostic artifacts have ever been found at this
site. As with two previous assessments by Prewitt and Kotter in 1979 and LCRA archaeological staff in
1996, site 41TV508 continues to be rated as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not meriting
formal designation as an SAL.
Site 41TV2287
This prehistoric lithic scatter/procurement site is situated on a heavily eroded Pleistocene terrace ridge in
Development Area #3 (see Figure 28). The site appears to cover an area measuring 135 meters on a NWSE line by about 80 meters on a NE-SW line (Figure 33). The site is wooded with Ashe juniper, and
ground surface visibility was found to be poor due to dense grasses and brush. As a result, only a few
pieces of lithic debitage were evident on ground surface. Six shovel tests were subsequently excavated to
better determine the site boundaries and evaluate the subsurface potential of the site deposits. The four of
the shovel tests excavated on the main ridge produced a few pieces of lithic debitage with most of it
coming from the upper 40 cm of the very gravelly sandy loam deposits. These gravels mostly consist of
chert cobbles with a few quartzite cobbles also being present. The only tool fragment recovered was a
bifacially worked secondary flake that was recovered from Shovel Test #6. Moderate amounts of burned
chert cobbles and fragments were also evident throughout gravelly deposits that were shovel tested.
However, no burned rock clusters suggestive of features were seen, and it is probable that the burning is
the result of natural grass fires.
Since the gravels were laid many thousands of years before human occupation, it is probable that all
artifacts deeper than 10 cm below ground surface have worked their way downward into the gravelly
deposits by bioturbation. This potential for vertical mixing along with the lack of diagnostic artifacts and
features leads to an assessment that this site is not NRHP or SAL eligible.

84

Figure 33. 41TV2287 site map.

85

Site 41TV2288
Site 41TV2288 is a prehistoric lithic procurement/lithic scatter site that is situated in a narrow creek
valley at the base of the slope of an upland terrace in Development Area 3 (see Figure 28). Since chert
and quartzite cobbles occur in high density at the base of this slope, there is little vegetation to obscure the
ground surface (Figure 34). The gravel exposure measures about 40 meters on an N-S line by 8 meters on
an E-W line (Figure 35).

Figure 34. Archaeologist examining chert biface preform at base of upland slope at Site 41TV2288.
Several concentrations of cores and early stage bifaces were observed within the gravel deposit in the
central and northern parts of the site. The density of these materials is approximately 3 to 4 specimens
per 2 square meters. Due to the steepness of the slope, it is possible that some artifacts have moved
downslope through time. No shovel testing was attempted due to the density of the cobbles on ground
surface.
The site appears to have been a favored locale for procurement of chert cobbles and for initial reduction
of materials. Numerous exposures of chert and quartzite were noted on eroded sections of upland terrace
slopes throughout the course of the field survey. Although occasional evidences of tested cobbles and
cores were observed at some of those exposures, none of those has the density of materials like that at Site
41TV2288.
Although sites such as 41TV2288 are an integral part of the overall prehistoric settlement pattern at
Muleshoe Bend, the apparent lack of diagnostic artifacts and features hinders the potential for any useful
further investigations. Furthermore, there is a high probability that artifacts have been displaced by
downslope movement. Therefore, the site is assessed as not eligible for NR or SAL listing.

86

Figure 35. 41TV2288 site map.
Site 41TV2289
This prehistoric site is situated in Development Area 3 and extends over an area measuring 150 meters
east-west by 60 meters north-south (Figure 36). The site is located on an upland terrace that overlooks
the former Colorado River floodplain terrace. The site is a low density lithic scatter with cores and flakes
sitting on the surface of a Pleistocene terrace. Cultural materials are most common on the surface of the
terrace slopes on the far west end of the site. In that area, one piece of debitage appears to be present per
2 square meters of ground surface area. Further eastward across a gully, lithic debitage appears to be
sparser despite the excellent ground surface visibility on eroded surfaces.

87

Figure 36 . 41TV2289 site map.

Two shovel tests were excavated on the terrace top at the western end of the site where artifacts are more
plentiful on ground surface. Shovel Test #19 produced only one chert flake in gravelly sandy loam
topsoil that was only 8 cm thick. About 30 meters further southward where Shovel Test #20 was
excavated the red clay B horizon was encountered just below ground surface. No artifacts were found in
that shovel test.
The site is an ephemeral lithic procurement/lithic scatter with very little depth of deposits. No diagnostic
artifacts or features were observed. Given these facts, the site is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in
the NRHP and not meriting formal designation as an SAL.

88

Development Area 4
41TV500
The southern end of this prehistoric site covers much of the Development Area 4 area (Figure 37, 38).
The site was also among the sites recorded during the Prewitt and Associates, Inc. survey for an LCRA
brush control project (Prewitt and Kotter 1979:3.5). The site was briefly described as primarily a lithic
procurement/scatter site with battered chert cobbles, cores, and cortex flakes visible on the lower eastern
slopes of a gravelly sandy ridge. The surveyors also stated that materials were ephemeral. Avoidance of
the site during brush clearing activities was recommended.

Figure 37. Development Area 4 showing location of site 41TV500 and all subsurface tests.

89

Figure 38. Development Area 4. Typical view.
This site was revisited by LCRA archaeological staff in 1997. Staff noted that the southern part of the site
had been developed into a campground area since the time of the Prewitt and Associates, Inc. survey.
Very little prehistoric cultural material was seen during the revisit, although the site had become more
overgrown by grasses and Ashe juniper. The LCRA staff stated on their revisit site form that the site had
been fully documented, and that it did not appear to merit further avoidance or formal designation as an
SAL.
Since the southern part of 41TV500 lies within the north central part of Development Area 4, it was again
revisited during the current investigation. Very little prehistoric material was observed on ground surface,
and five shovel tests that were excavated were all negative. Based on the negative findings of the current
fieldwork, it does not appear that the portion of site 41TV500 within Development Area 4 is eligible for
NR or SAL listing.

Summary and Recommendations
During the period from mid November 2007 through January 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff
conducted an intensive cultural resource survey of the LCRA’s new Muleshoe Bend Recreation Area CIP.
Four proposed development areas totaling about 160 acres were field surveyed through a combination of
pedestrian survey and subsurface testing that consisted of both mechanical auger testing and shovel
testing.
As a result of the survey, four new cultural resource sites consisting of 41TV2287-2289 and 41TV2291
were discovered and assessed (Figure 39). Six previously recorded sites consisting of 41TV498-500,
41TV505, 41TV507, and 41TV508 were also revisited and assessed.
Prehistoric materials were found at 41TV498, 41TV500, 41TV505, 41TV508, 41TV2287, 41TV2288,
41TV2289, and 41TV2291. All of these sites can be classified as lithic scatters with lithic procurement
activities also evident at 41TV500, 41TV508, 41TV2287, 41TV228, and 41TV2289. In all cases, the

90

Figure 39. Section of the Pace Bend, Tx. 7.5’ USGS map showing all archaeological sites within the four
proposed Muleshoe Bend CIP areas.

prehistoric materials at these sites lack diversity, and except for two artifact concentrations at 41TV2288,
all of these sites have sparse materials. No diagnostic artifacts or evidences of intact features were found
at any of these prehistoric sites. Further, the archaeological materials at these sites either occur in sandy
loam deposits that show evidences of bioturbation or occur on upland surfaces in shallow gravelly sandy
loam deposits that are vertical mixed. Additionally, at site 41TV2288 some artifacts have probably rolled
downslope from their original locations. Given these facts, all eight prehistoric components examined

91

during the field survey are assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not meriting formal
designation as SALs.
Sites with historic remains consist of 41TV498, 41TV499, 41TV505, 41TV507, and 41TV508. The
recovery of eight hand-forged square nails from two shovel tests placed adjacent to the presumed former
house location at 41TV499, represent the earliest identified artifacts found at any of the historic sites.
Avoidance of an approximately 40x40 meter area centered on these finds is recommended (see Figure
38). This avoidance area is assessed as potentially eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and potentially
meriting formal designation as an SAL. If avoidance of this portion of 41TV499 is not feasible, then
additional investigations will be needed.
Historic remains at all other historic sites and at other portions of 41TV499 all appear to date from the
very late 19th century to as late as the mid 20th century. The dating of these components fits well with
the historic archival and oral history research conducted by Utley (1996) at Muleshoe Bend. His research
indicated that sites 41TV498, 41TV499, 41TV507, and 41TV508 were sharecropper house sites that were
a part of the Turner Farm. The tenant farming operation probably began in the 1880s or 1890s.
Production of cotton and corn on the lowland/floodplain terraces adjacent to the river channel was the
principal economic pursuit at the Turner Farm. Although the fifth historic period site, 41TV505, was not
researched by Utley, it is probable that it was associated with the tenant farming operation.
Aside from the potential earlier component at 41TV499 recommended for avoidance, the other historic
period archaeological remains at these five sites do not appear to have any potential in providing new
information about rural lifeways in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Utley’s historic archival and
oral history research has already provided a more detailed story about the history of the Turner Farm and
its tenants. Since Utley interviewed many individuals who were already quite elderly in 1996, it is
probable that very few of them or other individuals who were not interviewed are still living. Because of
the very limited potential for additional oral history research and lack of the archaeological potential to
provide new and interesting data, the historic components at 41TV498, 41TV505, 41TV507, 41TV508
are all assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not worthy of formal designation as SALs.
Similarly, the portion of site 41TV499 not recommended for avoidance is assessed as not NRHP or SAL
eligible.
Aside from the avoidance of a portion of site 41TV499, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff
recommends that the proposed Muleshoe Bend CIP project be allowed to proceed without any further
cultural resource investigations. As always, standard emergency discovery provisions are applicable to
this project. Should the construction personnel uncover any presently unknown buried archeological
features during construction work, then work should cease in the immediate area of the discovery and the
LCRA Archeology Services staff should be notified at 1-800-776-5272, ext. 6714 so that the find can be
assessed in consultation with the THC. In such a situation, work can continue in other areas where no
archeological materials are present.
