An Intensive Cultural Resource Survey of the Lower Colorado River Authority’s
Proposed T-113 Transmission Line Rebuild Project,
Austin and Colorado Counties, Texas

By
Daniel J. Prikryl
Principal Investigator

Texas Antiquities Committee Archeology Permit # 4479
Interim Report #1.08
Project # 6.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
February 2008

110

Abstract
In January and February 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource
survey of high probability areas along the proposed T-113 transmission line rebuild project. As a result of
the fieldwork, six new cultural resource sites, 41CD135-140, were recorded and assessed, and one
previously recorded site, 41CD132, was revisited and assessed.
Site 41CD136 is a historic period site that dates from 1855 to the late 20th century. The site was
originally established as the location of the William Harbert family plantation. The original plantation
house is said to have been situated just 10 to 25 meters north of the north edge of the transmission line
right-of-way. Despite the pre-Civil War date for the original occupation, the overwhelming majority of
artifacts recovered from shovel testing within the transmission line right-of-way appear to date to the 20th
century. An oral history interview with a family descendent, who is current landowner, indicates that
there were no structures or foundations present within the LCRA transmission line ROW that were visible
during the descendant’s lifetime. Thus, although the site has an interesting history, it does not appear that
LCRA’s transmission line rebuild project will negatively affect any significant cultural deposits. LCRA
assesses the portion of site 41CD136 within the transmission line ROW as not eligible for inclusion in the
National Register Historic Places and not meriting formal designation as a State Archeological Landmark.
The other five new archaeological sites, 41CD135 and 41CD137-140, recorded during the current
investigation are all prehistoric sites. Shovel testing at site 41CD135 produced a moderate density of
artifacts that primarily consisted of small chert tertiary flakes and chips. A few scattered burned chert
chunks were also recovered at 41CD135. No discrete subsurface components, features, or diagnostic
artifacts were found. Importantly, the artifacts occur in fine loamy sand deposits that show evidences of
bioturbation. For this reason, the portion of site 41CD135 within the transmission line ROW is rated as
not eligible for the National Register Historic Places and not worthy of formal designation as a State
Archeological Landmark.
At the other four new prehistoric sites, very low numbers of artifacts were found in similar sandy topsoil
deposits. Numerous backdirt piles from gopher burrows are present at these sites, and the integrity of
cultural deposits is questionable. No evidences of features were found at these sites, nor were any time
diagnostic artifacts. Based on the results of the fieldwork, the portions of sites 41CD137-140 within the
right-of-way are assessed as not National Register or State Archeological Landmark eligible.
The final site, 41CD132, is a previously recorded site that represents the old elevated railroad grade of the
Buffalo Bayou, Brazos, and Colorado Railroad that was constructed in 1860. At the terminus of this rail
line, the City of Alleyton was founded that same year just 100 to 700 meters further southward. Although
pedestrian survey and shovel testing did not reveal any artifacts or features adjacent to the section of the
railroad grade within the LCRA transmission line right-of-way, it is within the part of the old railroad
grade that has been previously formally designated as a State Archeological Landmark. Because of the
State Archeological Landmark designation, LCRA will avoid the railroad grade feature during
construction. This will include not constructing any of the new transmission structures within the site
area and minimizing any vehicular traffic that could damage the elevated railway grade feature.
Aside from the restrictions concerning the portion of site 41CD132 within the LCRA transmission line
right-of-way, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends that rebuilding of the T-113 transmission
line be allowed to proceed as currently planned.

111

Introduction
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to rebuild the section of the T-113
transmission line that extends from the Columbus CPL Substation to the Peters Substation in Colorado
and Austin counties, Texas (Figures 41 and 41). This section of the T-113 transmission line has a total
length of approximately 24.4 miles. The current transmission line consists of a combination of H-frame
wooden transmission poles and metal lattice towers. The wooden H frame poles which constitute the
majority of the existing transmission structures, will be replaced with single metal or concrete poles.
Some of the existing lattice tower structures, which are mostly located at stream crossings and at angled
turns in the transmission line right-of-way (ROW), may also be replaced with single poles. The erection
of the replacement poles for the wooden H frame structures will require the excavation of 4 to 6 ft
diameter holes to depths of 12 to 17 ft below ground surface. The new single pole structures for
replacement of the lattice towers will have a larger diameter that requires the excavation of 10 ft diameter
holes.
Where possible, LCRA is also proposing to increase the spacing between structures during the rebuild
activities so that many of the new structures will be placed at the locations in between the current
structures. As a result, ground-disturbing activities will occur in locations not previously impacted by the
placements of the original structures. The original T-113 transmission line route had not been previously
surveyed for cultural resource sites because its construction predates the enactment of the State
Antiquities Code. Based on these facts, a cultural resource survey appeared warranted.
In January and February 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted an intensive cultural
resource survey of high probability areas for cultural resource sites along the T-113 rebuild route. The
survey consisted of pedestrian survey and shovel testing, Personnel who conducted the fieldwork
consisted of LCRA staff members Daniel Prikryl (Principal Investigator) and Andrew Malof and
contracted archaeologists Cody Kiker, Patrick Hatten, and Glenn Goode. The investigation was
conducted under TAC Blanket Permits #4779, which was issued to the LCRA by the Texas Historical
Commission (THC) for Calendar Year 2008. All work was completed in accordance with methods for
fieldwork and reporting as proposed by the Texas Council of Archeologists (1992).

Environmental Background
The project area is located in Gulf Coastal Plain (Fenneman 1938:100-103) in Southeast Texas. At the
western end of the transmission line corridor, the project area crosses the Colorado River and its
associated floodplain. The transmission line also crosses the San Bernard and Little San Bernard rivers in
the eastern half of the project area.
Surface geology at the western end of the project area consists of Holocene era alluvium and Pleistoceneaged fluviatile deposits associated with the Colorado River channel (Barnes 1974). From the area north
of the Alleyton community across the remainder of the route the surface geology consists of the
Pleistocene era Willis Formation.
According to the generalized soil map for Colorado County, soils on the alluvial floodplain areas of the
Colorado River in the project consist of the Mohat loam, Gad loamy fine sand, Norwood silty clay loam,
Bergstrom silt loam, and Wilson clay loam. The transmission line also crosses a former sand and gravel
pit in one area of the river floodplain.

112

Figure 40. Sections of the Columbus, Alleyton, and Bernardo Texas 7,5’ USGS maps showing the T-113
transmission line corridor and survey segments 1-4.

113

Figure 41. Sections of the Cat Spring and Sealy Texas 7.5’ USGS maps showing the T-113 corridor and
survey segments 5 and 6.

114

Within the project area, topsoils present on the Pleistocene terrace flanking the Colorado River include
the Raabs clay loam, Burleson clay, Wilson clay loam, Gholson fine sandy loam, Dutek loamy fine sand,
and Trinity clay. Further eastward, soils along the transmission line corridor are
Mostly the sandy loams and loamy fine sand categories described in the previous paragraphs.
The far western edge of the project area is located within the Oak Savanna vegetation region with the
remainder of the project area being within the Oak-Hickory Forest vegetation region (Arbingast et al.
1976:13). Aside from bottomlands in some parts of the Colorado River floodplain and several creek
crossing areas such as Live Oak Creek, the overwhelming majority of the project area was cleared and
cultivated during the historic period. Much of this acreage is now pasture land for cattle.

Culture History
Prehistoric Background
The project area is located adjacent to the boundaries of the Southeast, South, and Central Texas
Archeological Regions (Patterson 1995; Hester 1995; Prewitt 1981). While Patterson places Austin
County within the Southeast Texas region, none of the authors of recent articles on these three
archeological regions claiming Colorado County as being within their respective region of interest.
Skelton (1977) described the transitional nature of Fayette/Colorado county area in the discussion of his
excavation work at the Fayette Power Project in nearby Fayette County. Skelton also noted that J.
Charles Kelley (1955) had previously advanced the notion of that this area was transitional zone in
prehistoric times. Specifically, Kelley discussed cultural relationships between coastal and inland
prehistoric groups and suggested that geographically diverse cultural groups may have co-existed within
this area.
Common prehistoric site types in the Austin and Colorado County area consist of open campsites, lithic
scatter/procurement sites, and cemeteries. Archeological investigations in neighboring areas have shown
occupation by Native American peoples for at least 12,000 years prior to the coming of Europeans. The
data show that throughout these millennia, prehistoric peoples were nomadic hunter-gatherers who moved
across the landscape exploiting seasonally available plant and animal resources. In terms of a
chronological sequence for these nomadic hunter-gatherer peoples, Patterson’s (1995:243) proposed
sequence for Southeast Texas, for example, is as follows: Early Paleoindian 10,000-8000 B.C., Late
Paleoindian 8000-5000 B.C., Early Archaic 5000-3000 B.C., Middle Archaic 3000-1500 B.C., Late
Archaic 1500-A.D. 100, Early Ceramic A.D. 100-600, Late Prehistoric A.D. 600-1500, Protohistoric A.D.
1500-1700, and Historic Indian A.D. 1700-1800.
Historic Background
The transmission line passes through the southern part of the City of Columbus and just north of City of
Alleyton, both in Colorado County. The transmission line also passes near the smaller crossroads
communities of Mentz and Bernardo in Colorado County and Peters in Austin County. Information on
these communities and important historical events is taken from The Handbook of Texas Online and is
summarized in the following paragraphs.
According to legend, the City of Columbus was originally the location of Indian village named
Montezuma. No specific data could be found on this village or its inhabitants. The first Anglo-American
settlers, Robert and Joseph Kuykendall and Daniel Gillehand, all members of the Stephen F. Austin’s Old
Three Hundred, moved to the area near Columbus in 1821. In 1823, Benjamin Beeson, another early
settler, established a ferry across the Colorado River at the fledgling community, which became known as

115

Beeson’s Crossing. As early as 1822, the settlers in the area gathered at this spot for elections of local
officials of the Mexican government’s Colorado River District.
At the time of the Texas Revolution, the settlement was known as Columbus and had about 25 families.
Although Houston’s army burned all of the buildings in Columbus during the Runaway Scrape, the town
was soon rebuilt after the Revolution and designated as the county seat of Colorado County in 1836. By
1847, it had 20 houses, 3 stores, 2 taverns, and a blacksmith shop. An early industry was a German cigar
factory that was established in 1840. Cotton production became the major economic pursuit in the 1850s
and continued to be important into the early 20th century.
By 1880, there were 1,666 farms in Colorado County. However, like many other areas in Texas, the
period after the Civil War was marred by violence. A fresh wave of killings even occurred as late as
1906. Cattle production was also important to the economy, especially in the 1870s and 1880s. In 1883,
the Columbus Meat and Ice Company was established in Columbus. Exploitation of large sand and
gravel deposits became a flourishing industry in the first decade of the twentieth century. By 1910,
Columbus was said to be surrounded by gravel pits. Sand and gravel mining continues to be important to
the local economy today.
After World War II, a large part of Columbus’ local economy began to focus on recreational activities. In
Columbus, this focus centered on historic buildings and “down-home atmosphere”. Numerous historic
buildings were restored and the Magnolia Homes Tour was begun in 1961. In the year 2000, Columbus
had a population of 3,916 and 326 businesses.
The City of Alleyton, which is situated about three miles east of Columbus, was first settled by five
brothers, Rawson, Abraham, John, Thomas, and William Alley, who arrived at various times in 1821 and
1822. It lies on the east bank of the Colorado River near the current intersection of Farm Road 102 and
Interstate Highway 10. In 1859, William Alley arranged for an extension of the Buffalo Bayou, Brazos,
and Colorado Railway to his property by donating land for the right-of-way. At the railroad terminus, he
also provided land for a new town, which became Alleyton. With the completion of the railroad to
Alleyton in 1860, the town became the terminus of the southernmost railroad in the state. During the
Civil War, cotton that brought by rail to Alleyton was then loaded on wagon trains and hauled to Mexico
to bypass the Union blockade of Texas ports. Wagon trains returning from Mexico brought military and
other supplies, which were shipped by rail from Alleyton to other parts of the Confederacy. After the
Civil War, the railroad was extended to Columbus, and Alleyton declined in importance at a railroad
terminus.
However, Alleyton remained a supply center for area farmers. In 1896, the town had a population of 320.
Cotton became the primary agricultural product in the early twentieth century in the Alleyton area.
Extensive sand and gravel mining, which occurred around Alleyton in the early twentieth century, led to
the destruction of valuable farm and ranch land. In 2000, Alleyton had a population of 165.
The communities of Bernardo and Mentz, which are all near the central part of the project area, were first
settled by German immigrants around 1845. Bernardo was as also known as Bernardo Prairie and was
also sometimes called Braden in its early years. The community was located on the main road from
Houston to inland settlements. During the Civil War it became a dumping station for cotton being hauled
to Mexico. Bernardo remained a small rural settlement through the remainder of the nineteenth century.
From 1898 to 1917 it had a post office, and from 1872 to 1911, there was a local Catholic school staffed
by the Sisters of Divine Providence. In 1911, the school merged with the Mentz Catholic school. In
2000, the community had a population of 155.

116

Mentz, which is located about 2.75 miles west-southwest of Bernardo, lies at the intersection of Mentz
and Frelsburg roads. The town’s name is derived from the fact that many of the early settlers were from
the Diocese of Mainz, Germany. In 1858, the settlers constructed a Catholic church and named it St Roch
after a chapel in their native country. From 1872 to 1916, the community had a Catholic school staffed
by the Sisters of Divine Providence. A post office sporadically operated at Mentz from 1853 until 1913,
when it was transferred to Alleyton. In 2000, St. Roch’s Church was still in operation and the community
had a population of 100.
A final rural community, Peters, is located in Austin County about 1.25 miles west of western edge of the
project area on State Highway 36. While Anglo-American settlement started around Peters in the 1820s,
the town was not founded until 1880 when it was established as a station for the Bellville-Sealy spur of
the Gulf, Colorado, and Santa Fe Railway. Peters had a post office from 1883 until the mid-1940s. Its
population peaked at 125 in 1925. In 2000, the population was 95.

Previous Investigations
There are relatively few previously recorded archeological sites within and around the project area. In the
west-central part of the project area, the transmission line does cross one previously recorded site,
41CD132, which represents the old railroad grade of the Buffalo Bayou, Brazos, and Colorado railroad
tracks just north of the town of Alleyton. Importantly, 41CD132, was been formally designated as a State
Archeological Landmark in 1998. A second related previously recorded site is 41CD133, the Alleyton
townsite. The north boundary of 41CD133 lays just 130 south of the point at which the transmission line
crosses the old railroad grade.
Other sites within 2 km of the transmission line in the western end of the project area consist of 41CD38
which is a prehistoric site and 41CD126 which consists of the historic concrete bridge supports for the
1898 to 1932 U.S. Highway 90 bridge over the Colorado River in Columbus (TARL:Colorado County
files).
In the central part of the project area, only one site 41AU50, has been previously recorded. The site is
located on an upland terrace about 800 meters east of the San Bernard River and about 1.9 km southeast
of the transmission line’s crossing of that river (TARL:Austin County files). Although the site is plotted
as covering a 500x300 m area, information about the size and contents of the site is sketchy. It appears
that recorder collected a large side scraper tool and two flakes. He also states that the landowner had
previously found a Bulverde point.
The closest large scale professional investigations consist of those for the proposed Columbus Bend
Reservoir and the Fayette Power Project. At Columbus Bend, which is located on the Colorado River
between La Grange and Columbus north of the western end of the project area, Nunley (1963) recorded
36 archeological sites within Colorado County. No follow up excavation work was conducted because the
proposed reservoir was cancelled. At the Fayette Power Project, a 2,400 acre survey area on the Cedar
Creek drainage yielded 10 historic and 19 prehistoric sites. Most of the historic sites were associated with
the historic Biegel Settlement, which was founded in the mid 19th century as a part of Austin’s Little
Colony. Seven prehistoric and three historic sites were recommended for excavation work. Skelton
(1977) subsequently excavated six of the prehistoric sites. Most of these prehistoric sites turned out to be
shallow lithic scatters interpreted as temporary upland work camps occupied intermittently over the last
8,000 years. At site 41FY74, however, the remains of a single butchered bison were found in association
with Late Prehistoric Toyah Phase artifacts and activity areas.

117

Methodology
Prior to the survey, the files search data was utilized to determine high probability areas for cultural
resource sites. Most high probability areas consisted of the portions of the transmission line corridor that
pass across the floodplains and first terraces adjacent to stream channels including larger streams such as
the Colorado, San Bernard, and Little San Bernard rivers, and smaller streams such as Live Oak,
Deadmans, Bernardo, and Sandy creeks. It was also noted that the Colorado River has a wide floodplain
with alluvial deposits where buried archaeological sites might be present.
Other high probability areas were established at likely locations for historic period sites such as areas
adjacent to the Alleyton and Bernardo communities. For the investigation of areas with high probabilities
for historic period sites, the early 20th century 15’ USGS Glidden, Eagle Lake, Bellville, and Sealy Tx.
quads were examined with the T-113 transmission line ROW being overlaid on these maps as an aid in
determining whether any historic period house sites might be situated within the transmission line
corridor. Unfortunately, the topographic survey teams who made maps such as the Glidden 1919 map did
not appear to put in any effort in mapping the locations of house structures like survey teams did in other
parts of the state. Similarly, the extent to which the survey teams plotted house sites on the other three
15’ USGS maps is questionable. In any case, no historic period sites were indicated on these maps as
being within the transmission line corridor. Importantly, too, the 1919 Bellville 15’USGS seemed to
indicate that the original townsite of Bernardo had been located 3 to 4 miles northwest of the project area
opposed to the current location near the transmission line on the Bernardo, Tx. 7.5’ USGS map.
Field survey methods used during the project consisted of a 100 percent intensive pedestrian survey of all
high probability areas as described above along with shovel testing. Pedestrian survey methods for high
probability areas consisted of walking the transmission line corridor in zig-zag transects while intensively
inspecting bare and eroded patches of ground surface. Additionally, the walls of cutbanks and gullies
were examined for evidences of buried cultural materials. During the pedestrian survey shovel tests,
which consisted of small 35 to 40 cm (13.5 to 15 inch) diameter holes excavated to maximum depths of
100 cm (39 inches) or to the top of the B horizon/bedrock, were judgmentally placed in areas of high
probability having poor ground surface visibility and also to help determine the boundaries of any
archeological sites. All shovel tests were carefully backfilled immediately after excavation to restore the
ground surface to its original appearance. Further, no artifacts were removed from the property of any of
the landowners. Any artifacts observed on the ground surface or found in the shovel tests were examined,
analyzed, and photographed in the field, as necessary, and then placed back in their original locations.
Appendix 1 provides artifact descriptions from the shovel tests by 20 cm level, and Appendix 2 lists the
soil descriptions from each shovel test.
Survey Results
Approximately 4.55 linear miles of the 24.4 mile long transmission line ROW were intensively surveyed
(Figures 42-47). Ground surface visibility was found to range from fair to poor. In many sandy topsoil
areas with poor ground surface visibility, there were numerous gopher burrow backdirt piles within the
ROW corridor. These backdirt piles were very helpful in determining if artifacts were present. In fact, at
four of the six newly discovered sites, buried cultural deposits were initially identified by the presence of
artifacts on gopher burrow backdirt piles.

118

Figure 42. Survey Segment 1 at west end of Project Area with City of Columbus on west side of
Colorado River channel.

119

Figure 43. Survey Segment 2.

120

Figure 44. Survey Segment 3.

121

Figure 45. Survey Segment 4.

122

Figure 46. Survey Segment 5.

123

Figure 47. Survey Segment 6.

124

Shovel testing on the Colorado River floodplain showed that in almost all cases, the depth of alluvial
deposits was less than one meter. Therefore, there does not appear to be a need for any additional
investigation of deeper deposits with heavy machinery. A total of 55 shovel tests were excavated within
the 4.55 mile length of the ROW that was surveyed. Since the width of the T-113 ROW varies from 60 to
100 ft in different areas, the 12.08 shovel tests dug per linear mile is about equal to the THC’s minimum
standard of 16 shovel tests per linear mile for a ROW having a 100 ft ROW. Also, the presence of
gopher burrow backdirt piles alleviated the need for some of the shovel testing.
As a result of the field survey, six new cultural resource sites, 41CD135-140, and one previously recorded
site, 41CD132, were recorded and assessed. Descriptions of these sites are provided in the following
paragraphs.
New Sites
41CD135
This Native American campsite is situated on the west bank of the Colorado River around the area of the
existing LCRA lattice tower Structure 23/7 (Figure 48). The site is located within the backyard of
suburban residence within the City of Columbus near the intersection of Harbert and Charter streets.
The east boundary of 41CD135 is a steep terrace slope that drops about 20 ft to the west bank of the river
channel. The west boundary of the site lies about 55 meters west of this terrace slope in the area between
ST #2 and #3. The known width of the site is restricted to the narrow 18.5 m (60 ft)wide LCRA ROW.
Although much of the site is covered with San Augustine grass and ornamental vegetation, four pieces of
chert debitage were found on ground surface of gopher backdirt piles, and in recently disturbed areas
along a chain link fence. Several other pieces of debitage were seen around a deck platform that is located
on the terrace edge overlooking the river. The landowner later told the crew that some of the disturbed
fill around the deck platform was fill excavated to install a subsurface swimming pool that is situated
immediately north of the transmission line ROW in the backyard of the residence. Thus, it is probable
that the site extends northward outside the ROW. The adjacent landowner to the south of the ROW also
told the crew that a spring issues from the terrace slope to the south of the ROW.
Three shovel tests were then excavated to help determine the vertical and horizontal extents of the cultural
deposits. Shovel Test #1, which was placed about 3.5 m west of the existing LCRA lattice structure,
yielded 22 artifacts. Level 3 of this shovel test had the highest artifact count (n=10). Level 2 followed
with 5 artifacts and Levels 1 and 4 each produced two artifacts. Almost all artifacts recovered from ST #1
were small chert tertiary flakes/chips. Two somewhat larger chert flakes were found in Level 2, however,
and three burned chert chunks, possibly hearthstone fragments, were found in Level 4.
ST #2 was excavated about 5 m east of the existing lattice tower structure. Nineteen artifacts, again
mostly small chert tertiary flakes/chips, were spread throughout the approximately 70 cm thick fine sandy
loam A horizon at ST #2. Other artifacts included a blade core or thick biface fragment from Level 1 and
four burned chert chunks (two each in Levels 2 and 4) that may be

125

Figure 48. 41CD135 site map.

126

hearthstones. The B horizon, which was encountered at 70 cm below ground surface (bgs), was mottled
yellow and red sandy clay.
Following the excavation of these two shovel tests, the surface of the area west of ST #1 was closely
examined, especially the backdirt piles of gopher holes. As no artifacts were seen, and the crew did not
want to damage the manicured yard of the home immediately to the north, a third shovel test, ST #3 was
placed further westward at the intersection of Harbert and Charter streets (see Figure 48). No artifacts
were recovered from this shovel test. The depth of the sandy loam topsoil above the B horizon was found
to be 70 cm.
The investigation of 41CD135 indicates that it extends for a length of about 50 m within the LCRA
transmission line ROW. Most artifacts are very small chert tertiary flakes suggestive of the final stage
production of chipped stone tools and the maintenance of such tools. Several burned chert chunks
probably represent hearthstones from cooking/heating features. The small size of the debitage and the
potential burned hearthstones suggest that 41CD135 is an aboriginal campsite.
The site materials are within loamy fine sand topsoil, and the backdirt of recent gopher burrows were
noted within the site area. In ST #1, the highest artifact yield came in level 3 and the bottom of the sandy
loam topsoil suggesting that artifacts may have moved downward in the loose sand, while in ST #2
artifacts seemed evenly distributed throughout all levels. No burned rock clusters suggestive of intact
hearth features were seen, nor were any diagnostic artifacts found. Some previous damage to the site
deposits from the original construction of the transmission line structure and two nearby houses is
probable also. Principally because of the bioturbation of deposits and the potential for damage from
previous construction, the portion of site 41CD135 within the transmission line ROW is assessed as not
eligible for inclusion in the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) and not meriting formal
designation as a State Archeological Landmark (SAL).
41CD136
This historic period site is located on the south side of Interstate Highway 10 (I. H. 10) about 1.8 miles
east-northeast of the I. H. 10 bridge over the Colorado River (Figure 49). The site is situated on the edge
of a Pleistocene terrace at an elevation that is about 5 to 10 feet higher than the river floodplain to the
south. The LCRA staff investigated this area because an old barn is plotted on the 1957 Columbus, Texas
7.5’ map at distance of about 30 meters from the centerline of the transmission line. Several other nearby
structures consisting of house present when the quad map was published in 1957 and two other newer
homes plotted when the map was revised in 1981 lay further northward at distances of 50 to 80 meters
north of the ROW.
The principal component at this site is the historic period farmstead component which is related to the
Harbert family. Although the overwhelming majority of artifacts found during surface inspection and
shovel testing within the transmission line ROW produced 20th century artifacts, the farm complex dates
back as early as 1855 when William Harbert constructed his residence at a location about 30 to 40 meters
north of the transmission line centerline where the old barn is plotted on the USGS map. Harbert had
become wealthy as a merchant and plantation owner in Madison County, Tennessee before moving with
his wife and eight children to Texas in 1854 or 1855. Arriving in Colorado County with his family and
more than 100 slaves that he owned, he purchased 3,000 acres in the J. Hadden league on the east bank of
the Colorado River between Columbus and Alleyton. He quickly became one of the wealthiest men in
Colorado County. In addition to producing cotton at his plantation, he also constructed a general store
and bank in the town of Columbus. He also became one of the biggest moneylenders to other citizens
(Colorado County Historical Commission 1986:82-83, 134-135).

127

Figure 49. 41CD136 site map.

128

During the Civil War, Harbert hauled his cotton to the Mexican border to bypass the Union blockade of
Texas ports. In 1865, he died in Clinton, Texas while returning from one of these trips (Jackson 2008).
According to James Wooten (Personal communication 2008), the current landowner and a descendent of
William Harbert, the plantation house was demolished in the 1960s and some of the wood from the house
was used to construct a barn on the same spot where the house had stood (Figure 50). This is the barn
shown on the 1957 Columbus, Texas 7.5’ USGS map at a distance of 10 to 25 meters north of the north
edge of the transmission line ROW. The plantation house’s cistern is still present about 5 meters
northeast of the northeast edge of this barn. The cistern has an above ground brick collar.

Figure 50. Looking east at Site 41CD136. Barn at left has been constructed on the spot where the preCivil War Harbert Plantation House formerly stood. LCRA transmission structure 22/5 and ROW
corridor to the right.
Mr. Wooten stated that a small building located about 20 meters west of the barn is a 1920s era farm
office and that the other three structures shown further northward as the 1981 era photorevised additions
to the Columbus, Texas USGS map consisted of a home that later burned down and two other houses that
were later demolished. Mr. Wooten recollected that there had been other structures further westward
present earlier during his lifetime that were said to have been some of the slave cabins associated with the
plantation house. These are no longer present. Other slave cabins were said to have been scattered
throughout the original plantation acreage. Mr. Wooten also stated that a two story brick smokehouse
built in 1857 by William Harbert was situated further northward on the opposite (north) side of I. H. 10.
That structure was destroyed by a tornado in the late 20th century. Importantly, Mr. Wooten stated that
there were no former structures or foundations of structures associated with the plantation house within
the LCRA transmission line ROW.
At site 41CD136, the LCRA field investigations consisted of pedestrian survey and shovel testing within
the ROW areas adjacent to the former plantation house location and to the northeast and southwest.

129

Pedestrian survey of area showed very little ground surface visibility, and the few bare areas present
revealed no artifacts. A total of seven shovel tests, STs 13 through 19, were then excavated. The artifact
recovery from these shovel tests is found in Appendix 1.
Artifacts were recovered STs #14-18 with the highest density (n=13) coming from ST #17, which was
excavated on the north ROW edge about 13 meters southeast of the southeast corner of the barn. ST #16,
which was located 13 meters south of the southwest edge of the barn had a total of 8 artifacts as did ST
#14, which was placed 45 meters southwest of the barn along a fenceline. ST #18, the last positive shovel
test, was located about 50 meters east-southeast of the barn and it had only 3 artifacts. While most of the
artifacts were recovered from Level 1, some were also found in Levels 2 and 3 in ST #17. ST #18 was
unusual in that all three artifacts found there came from Level 3.
Twenty-one of the 38 total artifacts from these four shovel tests are small brownish-orange brick
fragments that each average less than 1 cm in diameter. The dates of these bricks are uncertain. The only
two artifacts that definitely appear to be diagnostic of the 19th century are a dark green wine bottle
fragment from Level 1 of ST #15 and an olive green glass fragment with an etched line across its surface
that came from Level 1 of Level #16. Otherwise, the remainder of the artifacts consist of eight clear glass
fragments, two round headed nail fragments, one green glass fragment, one brown beer bottle glass
fragment, two plain whiteware sherds, and one rusted sheet metal fragment. Some of these are more
likely to relate to the activities associated with the small farm office structure that was constructed in the
1920s, while several such as the brown beer bottle glass and the green 7-Up soda bottle glass could date
to the late 20th century.
Overall, the artifact yield suggests that additional excavations for samples of 19th century artifacts would
not be fruitful. Further, Mr. Wooten had stated that, to his knowledge, there are no foundations or
features related to the plantation era house within the LCRA transmission line ROW. Based on these
facts, the portion of site 41CD136 within the transmission line ROW is assessed as not eligible for
inclusion in the NRHP and not worthy of formal designation as an SAL.
41CD137
This prehistoric site is located on a terrace slope on the west side of an unnamed tributary of the Colorado
River about 290 meters south-southwest of the point at which the transmission line crosses County Road
268 (Figure 51). The Colorado River, itself, is about 600 meters due south of the site at the closest.
Pedestrian survey in this area revealed four chert flakes/chips and one possible burned rock fragment on
ground surface in gopher backdirt piles. The excavation of four shovel tests, STs 22-25, in the vicinity of
these surface finds produced no additional cultural materials. Based on these findings, it appears that the
site represents an ephemeral prehistoric lithic scatter. South of the transmission line ROW, there is a
considerable topographic rise that lies closer to the Colorado River channel. That area looks to be a
probable location for a more substantial prehistoric site, and it may be possible that our staff’s finds at
41CD137 represent activities peripheral to that hypothesized site. In any event, the minor amount of
prehistoric materials found in the ROW at site 41CD137 does not appear to be NR or SAL eligible.

130

Figure 51. Map of sites 41CD137 and 41CD138.
41CD138
Site 41CD138 is located about 150 meters west of the point at which the transmission line crosses County
Road 268 (see Figure 51). It is also situated about 50 east of an unnamed tributary of the Colorado River.
The previously described site 41CD137 is located on the terrace on the opposite side of this tributary.
Although pedestrian survey of the terrace revealed no artifacts on ground surface or in gopher backdirt
piles, two shovel tests, STs 26 and 27, produced a total of three chert tertiary flakes in the upper 20 cm of

131

sandy topsoil deposits. A third shovel test, ST28, was negative. Since areas further southwest and
northeast of these three shovel tests were low lying areas that did not appear to be likely spots of
prehistoric occupation, no additional shovel tests were attempted.
The portion of site 41CD138 within the transmission line ROW is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in
the NRHP and not worthy of formal designation as an SAL due to low artifact yield and bioturbation of
the sandy topsoil deposits.
41CD139
This center of this prehistoric site is located about 155 meters southwest of the point at which the T-113
transmission line crosses Live Oak Creek (Figure 52). The site lies on the slope of a well-defined terrace
that is heavily wooded with oak, hackberry, and green briar except for the cleared, well-maintained ROW.
Pedestrian survey of this terrace led to the discovery of two chert flakes in eroded cattle trails and road
ruts. Three shovel tests, STs 40-42, were then excavated to gauge the subsurface deposits. ST #40 which
was excavated upslope from the surface finds was negative and indicated that the sandy loam topsoil was
only 23 thick at the terrace summit. ST #41 in the central part of the site yielded two burned tertiary
flakes and a burned chert chunk in the upper 20 cm of the 66 cm thick sandy topsoil deposits. ST #42,
which was excavated about 30 meters downslope from ST #41, produced one tertiary flake and one
burned chert spall in Level 1. Depth of sandy loam topsoil at ST #42 was 39 cm. Examination of gopher
backdirt piles and several eroded areas downslope from ST #42 produced no evidences of prehistoric
materials, so shovel testing was curtailed.
The sparse finds at site 41CD139 indicate that the portion of the site within the transmission line ROW is
a low density prehistoric lithic scatter. Soils have been disturbed from vehicular traffic associated with
the use of a deer blind in the central part of the site, and the presence of gopher backdirt piles suggests
bioturbation of the sandy topsoils. Artifacts lack diversity and no evidences of features or diagnostic
artifacts were seen during the investigations. The portion of the site within the transmission line ROW is
assessed as not eligible for the NRHP and not meriting formal designation as an SAL.
41CD140
This prehistoric site is located on the terrace on the opposite side of Live Oak Creek from Site 41CD139
(see Figure 52). The center of the site is located 170 meters northeast of the point at which the line
crosses Live Oak Creek. Following the discovery of several pieces of chert debitage during pedestrian
survey of the terrace, STs #43 and 44 were excavated in the area of the surface finds. ST #43, located in
the central part of the surface finds, yielded a total of five chert flakes/chips that were evenly spread
through the first three levels in fine sandy loam matrix. No additional artifacts were found in Levels 4
and 5 which was a gravelly sandy loam. ST #44, which was excavated about 12 meters upslope from the
surface finds, produced two chert flakes/chips and two pieces of burned chert shatter in Level 1. Two
chert flakes/chips were found in Level 2, and the B horizon clay was struck in Level 3 at 46 cm bgs.

132

Figure 52: Map of sites 41CD139 and 41CD140.

133

Previously Recorded Sites
41CD132
This previously recorded site is a section of the old Buffalo Bayou, Brazos, and Colorado Railroad grade
that is located within and north of the town of Alleyton, Texas. The extension of this railroad line to
Alleyton was completed in 1860 making it the terminus for the southernmost railroad in the state. During
the Civil War, Alleyton became the beginning point of the “cotton road.” Cotton was transported by the
railroad to Alleyton and then loaded on wagons and carried to Mexico in order to bypass the Union
blockade of Texas ports. Returning wagon trains brought
Various supplies were shipped on the railroad to other Confederate states. After the Civil War, the
railroad was extended from Alleyton to Columbus. As a result, Alleyton lost its importance as a railroad
terminus and its population declined.
The railroad grade was recorded as archaeological site 41CD132 in 1995 as was a broader site, 41CD133,
which encompasses the old Alleyton townsite. The recorders noted that within the town of Alleyton, a
street called Center Street now runs along the top of the railroad grade. The investigators found various
19th century artifacts in shovel tests along the flanks of the railroad grade including whiteware ceramic
sherds, bottle glass fragments, and cast iron metal objects. Site 41CD132 was formally designated as an
SAL in 1998.
The existing T-113 transmission line crosses site 41CD132 at a location about 100 meters north of the
north edge of the old Alleyton townsite (Figure 53). Although the site survey forms for 41CD132 and
41CD133 suggest that the majority of the archaeological materials associated with the railroad are located
southward within Alleyton, it appeared prudent to make a field visit to the railroad grade area within the
T-113 ROW.
The railroad grade ridge was readily apparent within the ROW as a humped ridge 60 cm high and 4
meters wide. No artifacts were found during pedestrian survey of the area, and it was noted that the area
immediately west of the railroad grade is a lowland area with standing water. Two shovel tests were
excavated to the east side of the railroad grade. ST #35 was placed about 25 meters east of the railroad
grade feature. No artifacts were found in the clay soil matrix. ST #36 was excavated on the opposite
(west) side of the county road that runs parallel with the feature. No artifacts were recovered from that
shovel test.
Since this site has been formally designated as an SAL, the LCRA will avoid impacting the site area by
ensuring that no new transmission poles are placed within the site.

Other Areas Surveyed with Negative Results
The area extending from the east bank of the Colorado River to the I. H. 10 ROW is a noteworthy area
that was surveyed with negative results at the west end of the project (see Figure 42). Most of the area
extending from the east bank of the river to Interstate Highway 10 is a lowlying flat terrace. Shovel Tests
#4 and 5, which were excavated in that area showed sterile brown loam topsoil down to one meter below
ground surface. There is, however, an abrupt 5 to 6 ft rise to a higher terrace surface at the east end of
this segment adjacent to the I. H. 10 ROW. Shovel Tests #6 and 7 were excavated down to the B horizon
clay with negative results in this terrace rise (see Figure 42).

134

Similar negative results came from STs #8-12 on the Colorado River floodplain on the south side of I. H.
10. Survey of the transmission line corridor northeast of site 41CD136 produced no additional historic
features or artifacts that might be related to the Harbert Plantation site. Mr. Wooten, our informant on the
plantation site, had also stated that there was a legend about a Confederate Regimental camp that had
been present somewhere between the plantation site and

Figure 53. 41CD132 site map. Dotted red line represents avoidance area during T-113 rebuild activities.

135

the town of Alleyton during the Civil War. The purpose of the Confederate camp was to guard the
railroad terminus in Alleyton. For this reason the entire transmission line route was surveyed
between the plantation site and terrace area where STs #32-34 (see Figures 43 and 44). Although
prehistoric sites 41CD137 and 41CD138 were found in this stretch, no historic artifacts that might have
been associated with the Confederate camp were found.
Other high probability areas where no archaeological sites were found include the San Bernard and Little
San Bernard river areas where STs 45-53 are shown on Figure 46. Survey of the Willow Creek area
where the modern-day Bernardo community is situated likewise yielded no sites. Last of all, survey of
the Little Bernard and Deadmans creek areas toward the northeast end of the project area produced
negative results (see Figure 47).

Summary and Recommendations
In January and February 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource
survey of high probability areas along the proposed T-113 transmission line rebuild project.
Approximately 4.55 linear miles of the 24.4 mile transmission line ROW were intensively surveyed. As a
result of the fieldwork, six new cultural resource sites, 41CD135-140, were recorded and assessed, and
one previously recorded site, 41CD132, was revisited and assessed.
One of the newly recorded sites, 41CD136, is a historic period site that dates from 1855 to the late 20th
century. The earliest component is of interest as it represents the location of the William Harbert family
plantation. Harbert was a wealthy merchant and plantation owner in Tennessee who moved to the
Columbus area in 1855 with his family and approximately 100 slaves. He acquired 3,000 acres on the
Colorado River and constructed his plantation house just 10 to 25 meters north of the north edge of the T113 transmission line ROW. Occupation of the house continued after the Civil War, and the house was
eventually demolished in the 1960s with a barn then constructed where the house had stood. Also, in
1920 a farm office building was constructed by Harbert’s descendents about 20 meters west of the
plantation house at a distance of about 25 to 40 meters north of the north edge of the transmission line
ROW.
Despite the pre-Civil War date for the original occupation, extremely few 19th century artifacts were
found in seven shovel tests dug within the transmission line ROW adjacent to the former plantation
house. An interview with a family descendent who is the current landowner indicates that there were no
structures or foundations present within the LCRA transmission line ROW that were visible during the
descendent’s lifetime. Thus, although the site has an interesting history, it does not appear that LCRA’s
transmission line rebuild project will negatively affect any significant cultural deposits. LCRA assesses
the portion of site 41CD136 within the transmission line ROW as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP
and not meriting formal designation as an SAL.
The other five new archaeological sites, 41CD135 and 41CD137-140, recorded during the current
investigation are all prehistoric sites. Shovel testing at site 41CD135 indicated a moderate density of
artifacts that primarily consisted of small chert tertiary flakes and chips. A few scattered burned chert
chunks were also recovered at 41CD135. These may represent hearthstones from former cooking/heating
features. The chert debitage may result from final stage production of bifacial tools or from resharpening
of existing tools. Such activities along with the possible hearthstones suggest that 41CD135 represents an
actual aboriginal campsite. However, the artifacts occur in fine loamy sand deposits that show evidences
of bioturbation. For this reason, the portion of site 41CD135 within the transmission line ROW is rated
as not eligible for the NRHP and not worthy of formal designation as an SAL.

136

At the other four new prehistoric sites, very low numbers of artifacts were found in similar sandy topsoil
deposits. Numerous backdirt piles from gopher burrows are present at these sites, and the integrity of
cultural deposits is questionable. No evidences of features were found at these sites nor were any time
diagnostic artifacts. Based on the results of the fieldwork, sites 41CD137-140 are assessed as not NR or
SAL eligible.
The final site, 41CD132, is a previously recorded site that represents the old elevated railroad grade of the
Buffalo Bayou, Brazos, and Colorado Railroad that was constructed in 1860. At the terminus of this rail
line, the City of Alleyton was founded that same year just 100 to 700 meters further southward. Although
pedestrian survey and shovel testing did not reveal any artifacts or features adjacent to the section of the
railroad grade within the LCRA transmission line ROW, the ROW is within the part of the old railroad
grade that has been previously formally designated as an SAL. Because of the SAL designation, LCRA
will minimize impacts to the railroad grade feature during construction. This will include not
constructing any of the new transmission structures within the area marked for avoidance on Figure 53,
and minimizing any vehicular traffic that could damage the elevated railway grade feature.
Aside from the restrictions concerning the portion of site 41CD132 within the LCRA transmission line
ROW, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends that rebuilding of the T-113 transmission line
be allowed to proceed as currently planned.
As always, standard emergency discovery provisions are applicable to this project. Should the
construction personnel uncover any presently unknown buried archeological features during construction
work, then work should cease in the immediate area of the discovery and the LCRA Archeology Services
staff should be notified at 1-800-776-5272, ext. 6714 so that the find can be assessed in consultation with
the THC. In such a situation, work can continue in other areas where no archeological materials are
present.

Results of Monitoring: Clearing of 42 Acres at LCRA’s Double Horn Preserve,
Burnet County, Texas and a
Reevaluation of Prehistoric Site 41BT41

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit No. 4779
Interim Report 4.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Community and Natural resource Services
March 2008

144

Management Summary
In April of 2007 LCRA Natural Resource Services requested coordination with LCRA Archaeology
Services in planning for an invasive species management project at LCRA’s Double Horn Preserve,
located in southern Burnet County, Texas (Figures 54, 55). Due to an unexpected reduction of force in
Archaeology Services staff, this project was not subjected to the normal internal review process. In
February of 2008, Archaeology Services was made aware that clearing needed to begin immediately
because of a March 1 deadline due to endangered species habitat being present in the area. After a review
of previous archaeological work and similar projects, it was determined that the project would be allowed
to proceed with avoidance measures for known sites in place, and with selective monitoring of clearing
activities. Avoidance areas were defined based on previous investigations, coupled with additional shovel
testing at previously recorded site 41BT41. Site 41BT272 was determined to be outside any proposed
impact zones. Monitoring was accomplished by way reviewing four machine-cleared transects prior to
allowing clearing to continue, and by subsequent spot-monitoring. As a result of these investigations it
was determined that site 41BT41 has very limited research potential. It is not felt to be eligible for formal
listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. No
new information was obtained on 41BT272. No new sites were recorded and no collections were made.

Introduction
LCRA’s Double Horn Preserve is an 800-acre tract of land located on either side of Double Horn Creek
where it drains into the Colorado River near the community of Spicewood, in southern Burnet County. A
total of 20 archaeological sites have been recorded within the property over the past several years. Some
of these are very extensive prehistoric campsites, and the remains of a small historic community are
present as well. The sites have been recorded for various brush and fence projects and ongoing inventory
and resource assessments. Notable among the projects as limited testing that occurred at 41BT37, where
a 2 x 2 m excavation unit was excavated within the high terrace across the creek from the present project
area (Prikryl et al. 2004). Middle Archaic through Late Prehistoric materials were recovered,
demonstrating a continuum of land use with a focus on hunted and gathered resources. Materials were
found to a depth of 2 m, and other testing and cutbank profiles indicate that cultural materials may be
present at depth exceeding 4 m. Other sites include an apparent pristine incipient burned rock midden,
hearths visible in cutbanks and in shovel tests, areas with bison bone, as well as extensive scatters of
artifacts and burned rocks. These are present along the creek and exposed along lower river terraces
when lake levels are low.
The present project is located within a previously plowed agricultural field, as demonstrated by dense
mesquite and, to a lesser extent, juniper regrowth within deep, brown loams. In order to recreate a native
grassland environment, the existing vegetation was bulldozed, then pushed and raked into piles for
eventual burning. Root plowing follows, which uses a 13 inch plow to aerate the soil and break the
subsurface roots into sections, thus killing them.
Prior to ground disturbance in the vicinity of 41BT41, previous investigations were reviewed and an
additional series of shovel tests excavated, for a total of 31 shovel tests defining the site’s boundaries
(Figure 56). Ten of these tests were positive for cultural materials, which were consistently small
amounts of chipping debris and occasional small burned rock and mussel shell fragments. The only
obvious tools were two biface fragments. Some groundstone (three or four manos, some sandstone
metate fragments) were visible on the ground surface, especially towards the west end of the site.
Examination of significant erosional gullies fronting Double Horn Creek over different occasions has
never resulted in locating any significant cultural materials at depths. In fact, these cuts are remarkably
devoid of artifacts.

145

Figure 54. Project area in Burnet County on Smithwick USGS quad.

146

Figure 55. Aerial detail of project area

147

Figure 56. Detail of shovel test at 41BT41, positive tests are red, negative are green.

148

It seems unlikely that this high terrace, so similar to that which holds 41BT37, directly across the creek,
would have received so little attention prehistorically. The working hypothesis is that a combination of
flood events over the past few hundred or more years has significantly altered the southern terrace,
washing away any accumulated cultural debris. There may be very deeply buried deposits, but they are
not visible at depths approachable by conventional means, and the deep erosional gullies do not lend
support to the concept.

Results of Monitoring

LCRA employs several methods of brush clearing and control. These are dependent upon resource
managers’ decisions as to the extent and nature of required clearing. LCRA Archaeology Services
normally does assessments before the fact, and therefore rarely is able to gauge the actual impacts from
such operations. It was therefore very informative to watch the bulldozer in action. It was found that the
initial clearing was actually relatively non-invasive; trees tended to snap at ground level rather than pull
their root systems with them. The tracked bulldozer certainly pushed soil around, but rarely was there an
impact below about 30 cm. In other words, impacts to the ground below the plow zone were minimal.
Four bulldozer transects were actively monitored on February 12 and 18, 2008. The bulldozer began
work on the south end of the field while Archaeology Services staff commenced shovel testing in the
vicinity of 41BT41 and conducted examinations along the edges of 41BT272. Once it was felt that the
boundaries of 41BT41 were defined, a bulldozer transect was created that established a buffer zone
around the site (Figure 57). Then, two additional bulldozer transects were cut. Each transect paralleled
the bank of Double Horn Creek, and each was approximately 10 m (30 ft) in width and 450 m( 1500 ft) in
length, for a total of about 4 acres, close to 10 percent of the total project. They were spaced at 75 to 150
m intervals.
The only non-modern cultural material that was seen while monitoring was a single piece of burned
limestone. A shovel test (ST 34) placed at this location was negative. Another shovel test (ST 33) was
placed near the center of the terrace where the field is at or near its highest point, and near another shovel
test excavated in 2005 that had recovered a single chert chip and a bone or tooth fragment from between
20 and 40 cm bgs. It was also negative. The single positive shovel test (ST 27) is therefore considered an
anomaly.
A final visit was made to the project area on March 27, 2008. Clearing was about 95 percent complete
(Figure 58), and some preliminary root plowing had taken place (Figure 59) within the deep soils (Figure
60). Two archaeologists walking wandering transects carefully inspected the ground surface for any
evidence of cultural materials and assessed the level of impacts from the clearing operation. Although
soils vary in areas from reddish brown to brown, they are all consistent sandy or silty loams, with
absolutely no rock content. The only cultural material seen was the handle of a salt-glazed mug or jug,
which was near a single link of an old chain and a single very small piece of white stoneware. Two very
small pieces of apparent burned limestone were also seen. No additional shovel tests were attempted.
The areas that had been root plowed were assessed for level of impact. It was seen to be very minor, and
did not exceed about 20 cm in depth.

149

Figure 57. Establishing buffer transect at 41BT41.

Figure 58. Completion of clearing.

150

Figure 59. Detail of root plowing.

Figure 60. Representative terrace cut along existing road to south of project area.

151

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA Conservation Services conducted clearing operations on 42 acres located in LCRA’s Double Horn
Preserve in Burnet County, Texas, under the supervision of LCRA Archaeology Services. Based on
previous types of projects, especially previous clearing on the north side of the creek (Hixson et al.
2007:156) and knowledge of the nature and extent of cultural deposits at 41BT37, coupled with extensive
shovel testing at known site 41BT41 within the project area, and the fact that the clearing was taking
place in an old agricultural field, it was felt this was the best method for allowing the project to proceed
while maximizing the potential for finding previously unrecorded archaeological sites.
Site 41BT41 was flagged for avoidance after additional testing was conducted to confirm its boundaries.
A total of 31 shovel tests was excavated within the site, with recovery sporadic and sparse at depths down
to 100 cm.
The monitoring determined that subsurface impacts were minimal, rarely exceeding 30 cm in depth, and
that follow up root plowing had no appreciable additional impact.
As a result of the monitoring and the consolidation of shovel test information it is now felt that 41BT41
does not contain significant research potential and is not eligible for formal listing as a State
Archeological Landmark or listing on the National Register of Historic Places. It is therefore
recommended that the portion of the project area which has been marked for avoidance be cleared for
mechanical brush removal. It is also requested that the Texas Historical Commission make a
determination of “No Effect” on the remainder of the clearing project.

An Intensive Cultural Resource Survey of a Rerouted Section of the Proposed LCRA Medina Lake
Transmission Line through Prehistoric Quarry 41ME193

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit No. 4779
Interim Report 5.08
Community and Natural Resource Services
Lower Colorado River Authority
April, 2008

156

Management Summary
LCRA is in the process of designing a new transmission line that will run from the existing Medina Lake
substation south and then east for a distance of about 9 miles to a point where it will tie in to a line being
built by CPS Energy of San Antonio. The line runs around the east side of Medina Lake and then east,
largely following SH 1283. It crosses the Medina/Bandera County line about one-third of the way from
the north end (Figure 61).
The line was surveyed for cultural resources by LCRA Archaeology Services staff during the fall of 2007,
and nine previously unrecorded archaeological sites were documented (Malof 2007). The sites (41BN104
and 41BN105, 41ME192-198) were both historic and prehistoric, and included trash scatters, stock
management areas, lithic scatters, a large quarry and two open camp sites. Two sites were recommended
for avoidance or additional work if avoidance was not possible. Site 41ME192 is an open camp in a
highly disturbed environment that appears to contain a small area of intact, subsurface burned rock. Site
41ME193 is a large lithic procurement area, a quarry where abundant Edwards formation chert was
extensively exploited for early stage tool production.
For logistical purposes, LCRA Transmission services determined that the portion of the line that runs
through 41ME193 required a substantial realignment, and the proposed line was redesigned 100 feet to
the east (Figure 62, 63). The reroute provided an opportunity to reinvestigate 41ME193 at a greater level
of detail and intensity than had been possible previously. As a result of these investigations it is now felt
that 41ME193 is fully documented, and that substantial additional information that would make the site
potentially eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark is lacking. It is therefore
recommended that the transmission line be allowed to proceed to construction without additional
archaeological investigations.
These recommendations are for 41ME193 only, and do not impact or affect other parts of the
transmission line already reviewed and agreed upon by THC, including 41ME192, where avoidance
measures are still recommended and are being planned for.
An extensive background section was prepared for the initial survey report, so the following report only
covers the specifics of 41ME1933. There was no collection of artifacts during this project, so there is no
need of curation.

Research Design and Methodology
The original survey recommended 41ME193 for avoidance or testing based on the premise that at the
time, insufficient data had been recovered to allow a firm recommendation for or against eligibility for
formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic
Places. Time constraints and other permitting needs required the report be submitted prior to what would
have been a preferred more intensive survey. The result was the recommendation for avoidance or
testing.
Because the realignment placed the potential impacts almost entirely within a previously unsurveyed area,
it was felt that a reinvestigation was appropriate. Because of the relatively short length of new alignment
(about 180 m within the site), it was possible to address more rigorously a series of questions that were
unanswered following the first survey.
Lithic procurement sites are rarely given in-depth attention. It is usually assumed that beyond locational
information they can provide little additional data on past technologies or settlement patterning. Site

157

Figure 61. Location of transmission line project in Bandera and Medina counties.

158

Figure 62. Location of 41ME193 on Medina USGS quad.

159

Figure 63. Site 41ME193 with locations of alignments, shovel tests, and Scatters.

160

41ME193 seemed to potentially be able to go beyond this basic assumption. One of the most striking
aspects of the site were very large flakes, up to 30 cm in diameter, which were struck from rounded
nodules of good to fair quality chert eroding from the surrounding limestone bedrock (Figure 64). There
also was observed buried cultural material in a single shovel test near the south end of the site that
reached about 30 cm before hitting bedrock.

Figure 64. Partially reduced cobble at 41ME193.
In order to determine formal State Archeological Landmark potential a series of questions were asked.
Are there intact buried deposits? Are time diagnostic artifacts present, and if so, can they be linked
differing technological aspects of the site? Is there spatial segregation of activity areas that could inform
on specific aspects of reduction? Is there evidence of activities other than lithic procurement?
On February 29, 2008, two LCRA staff archaeologists spent the day addressing these questions. This was
accomplished by a thorough examination of the ground surface, shovel testing in selected locations, and
additional intensive data recording at four locations identified in the field where there appeared to be an
increase in artifact density and/or patterning.

Results of Investigation
Six shovel tests were excavated along the realignment; two within each of Scatters (discussed below) 1
(STs 5 and 6) and 4 (STs 1 and 2) and two near a bottle and within a mounded area (STs 3 and 4) (Figure
65). Shovel Test 1 was excavated through 20 cm of heavy clay loam to limestone boulders. Recovery
consisted of three 5-cm hard hammer flakes and 17 hard hammer flakes and flake fragments that were
less than 5 cm in any one dimension. Shovel Test 2 had 15 cm of dark brown heavy clay loam overlain
by a narrow organic zone. A single small flake and three small chunks were recovered.

161

Figure 65. Shovel testing at 41ME193. Environment typical.
Because the early 1900s map showed a structure somewhere on the landform, a shovel test (ST 3) was
placed next to a liquor bottle that appeared to date to the early or middle part of the twentieth century.
This was excavated through 15 cm of limestone rubble and loamy detritus. One core fragment and three
large flakes were recovered, as well as one macro flake, which appeared to be the result of sequential
bipolar or hammer-and-anvil reduction, having the appearance of an orange slice, i.e., it appeared to
represent a round cobble that had been, at minimum, quartered. Shovel Test 4 was placed about 10 m east
of ST 3, within a mounded area suspected to be a bulldozer pushpile. This was quickly confirmed, as
there was no soil nor was there any recovery of artifacts.
Shovel Test 5 was near the edge of the road and within or immediately adjacent to Scatter 1 (Figure 66).
It was excavated through 15-20 cm of heavy, dark grayish brown clay loam. Recovery was much higher
here. There were four cores or core fragments, and 29 hard hammer flakes that were between 5 and 8 cm
along any one axis. Additionally, two apparent burned limestone rocks were recovered.
Shovel Test 6 was placed about 4 m south of ST 5. The profile was the same. Recovery consisted of a
burned macro flake (20 cm), a 15 cm core, three core fragments, eight 10-cm flakes, and 29 relatively
large flakes and flake fragments.

162

Figure 66. Recovery from Shovel Test 5.
This last shovel test was placed about 20 m north of the original test excavated during the original survey,
and referred to on the present site map as ST 7. Shovel Test 7 had a similar profile, one early stage
biface, two flakes and several chunks recovered.
These shovel tests serve to indicate that soils are shallow, the site has suffered severe impacts, and that
subsurface artifacts are representative of what is visible on the surface.
The pedestrian survey identified four areas where there appeared to be in increase in the observed density
of artifacts (Scatters 1-4). Three were clustered fairly close together at the south end of the site, and were
relatively small (32 to 60 square meters), while a fourth was located near the northern end, and was much
larger (about 400 square meters). In order to better quantify the numbers and types of artifacts present, a
series of observation units (OUs) were established in each Scatter. These were either .25 or 1 m units
(informally delineated) in which fairly detailed, but not necessarily consistent, artifact counts and
descriptions were recorded. Scatters 1 and 2 each had one meter OUs, six for Scatter 1 (55 m sq) and 4
for Scatter 2 (32 m sq). Scatter 3 (60 m sq) had five .25 m sq OUs, and Scatter 4 had four .25 m sq OUs.
Material density was variable in each scatter, and so the OUs were placed selectively where artifacts were
present, rather than where they were not. Observations therefore represent maximum counts per scatter,
and the combined figures represent likely maximum counts for the site as a whole. The smaller OUs are
considered to be more accurate than the larger ones, which were less intensively quantified. Combined,
however, they present some useful figures on site use (Table 15).
Within the 12.25 m sq total area (adjusted to 19 m sq) of OUs a total 1,825 artifacts were observed.
Artifact classes that were tabulated were cores or core fragments, macro flakes (greater than 20 cm in any
one dimension), flakes 10-20 cm in size, and flakes less than 10 cm in size. There were 31 cores (1.6
percent), nine macro flakes (0.4 percent), 28 medium flakes (1.5 percent), and 1759 flakes smaller than 10
cm (96 percent). The average artifact density across all OUs was 96 per m sq.
