totals

The site is estimated to cover approximately 14,500 m sq (3.6 acres). It therefore has a potential
maximum density of 1.39 million artifacts which would include roughly 23,000 cores, 6,800 macro
flakes, 21,000 medium sized flakes, and 1.34 million flakes smaller than 10 cm.
These numbers allow a gross estimate of the time span involved in creating the assemblage. Some recent
studies (e.g. Varien and Potter 1997, Sullivan 2008) have looked accumulation rates of artifacts using
Schiffer’s (1975, cited in Varien and Potter 1997) discard equation. The equation (TD=S*t/L, where TD
= total discard, S = “systemic number” - the total number of artifacts in use at any time, t = time and L =
the use life of the artifact) allows solving for time, once a series of rather broad assumptions are made.
This equation has apparently been used primarily for ceramic assemblages within relatively short
temporal periods. Despite this, the equation was applied to the lithic assemblage described above, with the
following assumptions.
The artifact class is the core or core fragment. The core use life (L) is one day (hours unspecified).
Assuming targeted, logistical use of the quarry by a group of 10 persons, the systemic number (S) is 10
(one core per person per day). The resulting equation is t = 23,200(1)/10, which results in 2,320 days
(6.35 years) to create the assemblage, if 10 people were working 365 days a year.
Further assuming retooling trips by local groups each lasting two days, the site could have produced the
assemblage in 1160 occasions. If the site was visited four times a year, it could have been created in 290

164

years. Unfortunately, besides a single apparent Late Archaic dart point fragment, it is impossible to
determine which years from the site itself.
Less than a mile from the quarry, however, is 41ME8, which produced a Plainview point (albeit
apparently from a burned rock midden) and Scorpion Cave (41ME107), which appears to represent a
possible discrete Late Prehistoric occupation from which 88 Edwards points were recovered.
The quarry was therefore likely used throughout prehistory, but quite possibly intermittently or
sporadically. It does appear that, at least within the two transects that were surveyed, that the raw nodular
chert was close to exhausted. The hundreds of thousands of pieces of waste debitage, however, would
still be a good resource for various tools, especially items like unifaces and arrow points.
Despite the numerous problems with this very simple analysis, (for example, it does not take into account
the very likely decay in core frequency as they are either completely reduced or removed from the site for
further reduction elsewhere), it creates a basic set of assumptions that potentially can aid in regional
settlement analyses.
One of the apparent very few serious attempts to understand and quantify a large quarry site was at
41BX901 (Potter et al. 1992), located about 30 miles due east of 41ME193. Although operating under
trying conditions (construction of a new school was under way), Potter et al. had the luxury of a large
quarry site grading into a large campsite, and were able to show statistically significant differences in the
types of assemblages between the two locations.
Importantly, for this study, Potter et al. also extrapolated assemblage density, but used weight rather than
counts as the currency. At 41BX901 they determined that there were 423,000 kilograms of lithic material
within an approximate 10 acre (40,470 m sq) area, or roughly 5 kg per square meter.
In order to allow a direct comparison between 41ME193 and 41BX901, Potter et al.’s adjustment factor
of 75 percent (based on observation and assumption of spotty distribution) was applied, and the total lithic
assemblage at 41ME193 was adjusted to 1.042 million pieces. Because weights were not recorded at
41ME193, sample pieces of chert were measured, and an average weight of 100 gm was assigned (these
were all relatively small pieces, no more than 10 cm in any one dimension). Assuming this can be
considered an average weight of all pieces at 41ME193, the total weight would be 104,250 kg for an
average weight of 7.1 kg per m sq. Given the uncertainties in and differing methods between the two
projects, the average weights per square meter between the two sites are tolerably comparable.
There did appear to be evidence of spatial variability. The differences are weak and to some extent the
basis of subjective observation, but are supported at least minimally by data gathered from the four
sample areas.
The most striking was a Scatter 4, where smaller sized flakes (less than 10 cm) made up 99.7 percent of
the observed assemblage (Figure 67). It also had the lowest percentage of cores (0.2 percent). Scatter 4
was located at the far northern end of the site, at about the last bench of limestone before a fairly steep
drop to the drainage below. This can be compared with Scatter 1, with only 88.9 percent of flakes smaller
than 10 cm, and the highest percentage of cores (4 percent), located not far from the southern end of the
site. Scatters 2 and 3 were more similar to Scatter 4 than they were to Scatter 1, both having a high
percentage of the smaller flakes and relatively few cores. Scatter 4 however, “felt” different from the rest.
It is interpreted as a location somewhat removed from the main part of the quarry, one where perhaps
secondary reduction took place.

165

Figure 67. Detail of lithics at Scatter 4.
Scatter 1, meanwhile, represented an area where primary acquisition was occurring more frequently, with
large cobbles being reduced to secondarily sized pieces, perhaps as part of a testing cycle. The large,
rounded cobbles would have required a substantial amount of force to initiate fracturing. It is highly
likely that this often occurred by way of simply throwing the cobbles onto the surrounding limestone
bedrock or against each other. Once split, hard hammer percussion could begin creating pieces suitable
for removal from the site for further reduction. The distinctive “orange slice” from ST 3 seems to
represent a more careful reduction technique, where possible bipolar reduction halved and then quartered
the large cobbles. Shovel Test 5 contained a core fragment that appeared to have been derived from a
similar piece, which had then been split crosswise for further reduction.
The quarry at 41ME193, unlike that at 41BX901, appears to represent a targeted, systematic logistical
operation, rather than a strategy embedded in other activities. There is little to suggest that any large
camps would have been located upon the same landform as the quarry. The nearest such camps would
most likely have been along the Medina River and its tributaries, and it is safe to assume that many are
submerged under the waters of Medina Lake. Such sites can only be addressed indirectly, and the data
gathered on 41ME193 can provide some little bits of information.
In recent years increasing attention has been paid to the role optimization plays in resource acquisition
(see Kelly 1995, for a starting point). Such questions ultimately address the fitness of the individual
(rather than the group) in the adaptive, selective process, and the physical elements of these processes
form in part, the archaeological record. The sometimes conflicting views of behavioral ecology and
selectionist archaeology (neo-Darwinism) overlap, at least at the seams, and literature from both camps is
referenced in the following discussion (Barton and Clark 1997; Torrence 1989a).
Some researchers have applied optimal foraging theory, usually reserved for acquisition of foodstuffs, to
the acquisition of raw material for stone tool production. Many of the same factors come into play,
especially when compared to plant resources (Hayden 1989). Lithic resources have a patchy distribution,
and have time and energy costs associated with search, procurement and processing. Time and effort are

166

essentially functions of distance, and the most efficient and therefore most likely strategy is to use
resources that are concentrated, of high quality and can be readily obtained on an as-needed basis
(Hayden 1989:9-10).
An important part of the equation also includes risk. Bamforth and Bleed (1997) go to great length to
describe what is meant by risk, but argue that, however it is defined, technology helps reduce it, and that
failures of one kind or another result in responses from technology as a mediating agent. Bousman, cited
in Bamforth and Bleed (1997:115) describes risk as “…the probability of economic loss…conceived in
terms of unpredictable environmental variations that influence getting enough food to supply a given
population” (Bousman 1993:64). Bamforth and Bleed appear to distill risk to the “probability of loss,”
with the understanding that the magnitude of the potential loss must factored in. Risk, therefore, is one
way to address basic questions within a “synthetic, conceptual frame work” (Bamforth and Bleed
1997:116).
In terms of lithic procurement, the risk is in not having the materials from which to make tools, or the
tools themselves, when such tools become critical to survival. The costs of acquisition, when balanced
against risk, begin to determine the following production and use strategies. In high cost situations, it is
expected that risk is minimized. In low cost situations, however, risk becomes less of a factor, and
selective pressures are less likely come into play, resulting in a more unpredictable and variable
assemblage (Bamforth and Bleed 1997:126). In lithic procurement activity, least-cost strategies are
expected (Torrence 1989c:64).
Two common currencies in determining costs are time and energy (Torrence 1989b:3). Energy is
commonly used, and makes sense when there is a direct energetic return on the investment (food). If the
return is indirect however, (energy essentially stored within a tool), then time might be a more important
factor: the less time spent gathering raw materials the more time that can spent putting tools to use. If
such is the case, then the distance from camp of the resource becomes increasingly important, especially
as levels of mobility drop, and embedded procurement is less likely to occur (Morrow and Jeffries
1989:29-30). Source materials that are distant become “expensive,” while those that are readily available
can be considered “inexpensive.” A relatively near, abundant source of high quality raw material would
be more heavily used than a less abundant and/or lower quality source, even if that second source was
nearer, as costs associated with more intensive extractive measures or risk associated with using lower
quality materials would increase (Hayden 1989:10).
The quarry at 41ME193 fits well within the above model. The artifact classes are highly variable, with no
apparent preponderance of technology other than generalized core reduction. Biface thinning flakes and
blades are both present, but not in any obvious quantities. And, unlike at some other quarry sites, failed
blanks or early stage bifaces are virtually nonexistent. (This may be the result of sampling error, or
perhaps collection by landowners over time, however this observation was consistent, and is felt to be
valid.)
The raw material, therefore, appears to have been inexpensive within this setting. This indicates
relatively nearby camps, a somewhat limited level of mobility, and long-term familiarity with the
landscape. It is useful to look at the site within its geologic context. The chert-bearing Edwards
Limestone formation begins just a bit west of here, and extends in a northeast trending arc for a distance
of about 85 miles which is some five to ten miles wide, and is cut by both the Medina and Guadalupe
rivers (Figure 68). This formation is also essentially the edge of the Edwards Plateau, and is therefore, if
not an ecotone, a transitional zone between the plateaus and high plains to the west and north and the
timberlands, prairies and low plains to the south and east. In short, it is potentially a highly favorable
environment for prehistoric foragers.

167

Figure 68. Edwards formation, undivided, and location of 41ME193.
Site 41ME193 is certainly not unique as a quarry, and undoubtedly is one of dozens, if not hundreds,
within this broad zone. As with 41BX901, the magnitude of the assemblage illustrates the importance of
this region for lithic resource acquisition (Potter et al. 1992:11). So, although the site itself can offer little
in the way of new information about discrete periods of time or help differentiate technological variation,
it does provide raw material from which to develop methods that link the artifacts to higher level or
general theory (Camilli 1989:17) and attempt to address larger questions. As Torrence states, “…the
study of lithics could play a much more fundamental part in the general debates about the nature of
human behavior than has traditionally been the case” (Torrence 1989b:6). The availability of raw
material may be the most important part in how stone tool technology is organized (Andrefsky 1994). It
is certainly one part of a mapped system in which people passed collected knowledge through time (Harry
Shafer, personal communication 2008), and helped with the success of succeeding generations.

Summary and Recommendations
An approximate 200 m-long segment of the proposed Medina Lake transmission line will be rerouted
through previously recorded lithic procurement site 41ME193, which had been recommended for
avoidance or additional testing to determine State Archeological Landmark status if avoidance was not
possible. Because the new alignment is almost completely within a previously unsurveyed portion of the
site, a revisit was considered necessary. This more intensive survey was able to address specific
questions left unanswered by the original survey, and the results are considered to be representative of the
site as a whole.

168

A total of seven shovel tests has been excavated within the site. Although artifacts were recovered from
all shovel tests, and in varying quantities, there is nothing in the recovery or profiles to indicate anything
other than a slow accumulation of soil covering the remains of generalized and mixed activities.
Both surface observations and shovel tests also indicate that at least portions of the site, and quite likely
the majority of it, has been severely impacted in recent times. Large limestone slabs were seen sitting on
end, and a mounded area was tested and confirmed to be a bulldozer pushpile. No evidence of a structure
seen plotted on a 1921 map was found.
Four areas were identified as relatively discrete scatters and were subjected to intensive field
observations. These observations were manipulated in the lab and demonstrated that there are potentially
almost 1.4 million artifacts present within the site, including some 23,000 cores and core fragments. The
“macro flake,” defined as being larger than 20 cm, and an item which had in part caused the initial
interest in the site, is predicted to be only 0.4 percent of the assemblage, and seems to represent a
necessary technological method for systematic reduction of large, rounded cobbles (Figure 69).
There was some evidence of differential spatial distribution of artifact classes (smaller flakes v. larger
flakes), with possible secondary reduction taking place nearer the northern portion of the site, and primary
reduction more common towards the south. This may also be a function of the distribution of raw
material by size. Regardless, there is no indication that distinctive or discrete activities, procurement or
otherwise, is archaeologically visible at the site.
No additional time diagnostic artifacts were discovered. The periods of use remain unsubstantiated, other
than an assumption of long-term use over thousands of years.

Figure 69. Unmodified chert cobble from 41ME193.
Site 41ME193 appears to compare well to 41BX901, one of few other quarries systematically sampled
and analyzed. Both are indicative of intensive utilization of locations where high quality raw lithic
material was readily available. At 41ME193 minimal procurement instances could have created the
assemblage in about 290 continuous years. More likely, it was used intermittently throughout prehistory
as human populations fluctuated in density through time. It appears that the site is presently largely
exhausted of easily obtainable unmodified cobbles; however, previously modified pieces are readily
available.

169

The creation of Medina Lake in the early 1900s undoubtedly inundated numerous prehistoric campsites.
These are unavailable for study within the foreseeable future. The “middle range” of data gathered from
41ME193 links, through more generalized theory, to technological and perhaps social aspects of these
sites. When encamped within the Medina Lake area, and quite possibly within much of the broad band of
Edwards Limestone forming this portion of the Balcones Escarpment, people were able to obtain raw
lithic material with relatively little effort. It appears that minimizing time spent at the quarry was more
important than controlling energy costs. The inexpensive nature of the resource allowed energy
investments to be spent more expeditiously on other pursuits.
These investigations demonstrated that such sites can produce information useful to a better
understanding of prehistory. Sites that are better preserved than 41ME193, assuming they are asked the
correct questions, can continue to inform on, and be informed by, both middle range applications such as
statistical approaches, and higher level generalized theory as touched upon here. Other applications, such
as information theory (e.g. Gero 1989) and landscape archaeology (e.g. Ebert 1992) can also be
addressed.
Site 41ME193, however, is considered to now be documented at a level far beyond that at which such
sites are usually accorded. Although it has produced some useful information, its scientific value is
severely limited by both recent artificial impacts and an absence of discernable activity areas and dateable
artifacts. The portion of the site in the vicinity of both surveyed corridors is therefore considered to be
ineligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of
Historic Places.
It is recommended that the project be allowed to proceed within either of the two surveyed corridors or a
reasonable distance from them without additional archaeological investigations. If, however, a drastic
realignment becomes necessary, especially any appreciable distance to the west or east, where the
potential for intact deposits or activity areas remain unknown, than investigations similar to this one are
recommended.

A Cultural Resource Survey and Assessment of the Lower Colorado River Authority’s Proposed Fence
and Vegetation Clearing at Buchanan Dam in Burnet County, Texas

By
Daniel J. Prikryl, Charles A. Hixson and
Andrew F. Malof

Daniel J. Prikryl
(Principal Investigator)

TAC Permit # 4779
Interim Report # 6.08
Project # 22.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Parks and Resource Conservation
April 2008

173

Management Summary
A 318-acre tract located a short distance below Buchan Dam was recently conveyed to the Lower
Colorado River Authority (LCRA) in order to create a security zone along the downstream (southeastern)
side of this facility and to ensure that real estate developers do not attempt to construct new residences in
flood prone areas downstream from the dam’s floodgates (Figures 70 and 71). As an initial step in
managing the new property, LCRA proposes to construct a barbed wire fence around the 3.25-mi
perimeter of the acreage. Preparation work for the installation of the fence will require clearing the
property lines of trees, brush, and other vegetation in some areas. The proposed vegetation clearing will
also create an approximately 8-ft wide corridor that will be utilized in the future for vehicular access and
will serve as a fire break. For most of the vegetation clearing, LCRA is proposing to utilize a sepii
machine that cuts vegetation including trees at ground level and then spreads the shredded remains on the
ground surface. However, a backhoe/front end loader or a small bulldozer will be used to clear vegetation
in some areas of rugged terrain where use of the sepii machine is not possible.
A cultural resource survey was conducted for the proposed fence construction and land clearing project
on April 8 and 9, 2008. The survey included revisit and assessment of three previously recorded cultural
resource sites. Additionally, high probability areas that will be affected by ground-disturbing activities
during the use of the backhoe/front end loader and small bulldozer were also surveyed. In all,
approximately 3 acres were field investigated, including overlapping survey from 2005 and 2007.
No artifacts were collected and no curation will be necessary.
Study Area
The project is located in western Burnet County immediately downstream from Buchanan Dam and north
of the Colorado River. Within the project area, the land rises steadily from the river towards the
northwest for a distance of 600 meters and then levels out on the crest of a prominent ridge that helps
impound Lake Buchanan. The slope presents a rough topography with frequent outcrops of Precambrianaged Valley Spring gneiss, one of the oldest rock formations in the state (Barnes 1981).
Soils in the project area are defined as the Keese-Rock outcrop association (Dittemore and Allison
1979:Map Plate 20). Relatively small areas with a soil cover can be found amongst the outcrops,
especially on the east side of a small drainage that bisects the southern third of the project area. The
source of this drainage is a seep that emerges from the base of a low cliff and is perhaps kept continuously
charged by the artificially high water table around the lake. Before construction of the lake, this drainage
probably ran only after rainfall. Areas on either side of the drainage are densely forested with ashe
juniper and live oak. The soils throughout the project area belong to the Keese series, which are
composed of thin sandy loams averaging 17 inches in thickness over decomposing gneiss. Other areas of
deep to very deep soils were seen on the northern boundary and in the south-central section of the
property.
Portions of the project area have been altered considerably from their natural state by water released over
the years through three immense gates in the dam on the Burnet County side. (The turn-of-the-century
USGS map shows only one creek in the eastern portion of the property - and no mapped structures). This
release has created wide channels perhaps following natural gullies scoured down to bedrock leading
from the gates and heading in a southwesterly direction to the river. Away from these scoured areas, the
soil and vegetation appear intact. A juniper-oak woodland covers a substantial area between the two
southern dam gates to the north of which are a number of meadows amid high rock outcrops. More
range-like conditions are found in intact areas below the northernmost gate, with a few large trees amid
expanses of dense brush and cactus.

174

Figure 70. Project area shown on USGS topographic map (Lake Buchanan and Kingsland quads).

175

Figure 71. Detail with site locations discussed in text.

176

Over 50 years of archaeological investigations in Central Texas have led to a general understanding of the
region’s chronology and prehistoric subsistence patterns (Collins 1995). The region’s chronology was
largely derived from excavations of deeply stratified terrace sites. From at least 11, 500 years ago up
until the Spanish period (circa AD 1700), the Edwards Plateau and much of the rest of Central Texas was
inhabited solely by hunter-gatherers. Even in the Late Prehistoric period (AD 700 – 1650) when native
groups in other parts of what is now Texas were making the transition to an agriculture-based economy,
the Central Texas groups continued to rely completely on wild resources. Much of the archaeological
record relates to subsistence activities, the presence of burned rocks from cooking facilities being a
notable example. At certain locations, burned fragments have accumulated to the point of forming low
topographic features that are known as burned rock middens, or locally as “Indian mounds.”
Texas has traditionally been divided into a number of different archaeological regions, each defined by a
set of shared cultural traits, such as projectile point forms. Many archaeologists today interpret huntergatherer cultures in terms of their adaptation to the natural environment, and one criticism of
archaeological regions is that they often encompass more than one environmental zone and so contain
more than one type of adaptation (Ellis et al. 1995). For example, the Central Texas Archeological Region
includes six natural regions, including the Llano Uplift where Buchanan Dam is located.
Beginning in the 1930s, a series of dams were built along the stretch of the Colorado River upstream from
Austin to control flooding and produce hydroelectric power for rural communities. Buchanan Dam was
the first of these to be completed. A consortium of private business interests initiated the project but the
Depression ended construction in 1932 when only 40 percent of dam had been built (Yarbrough n.d.).
Many of the workers continued to live along the river in the vicinity of the partially built dam in the hope
that the project would eventually resume. Construction on the dam did not begin again until four years
later when the state of Texas created the Lower Colorado River Authority, a non-profit agency which
could receive and administer the federal funds needed to complete the project. Buchanan Dam was
completed in less than a year and began impounding the flow of the Colorado River on May 20, 1937.

Research Design
Prior to the field survey, a cultural resource file search including a review of data on the THC’s digitized
Site Atlas database along with LCRA’s internal cultural resource files was conducted. The file search
showed that there were three previously recorded cultural resource sites along the property perimeter that
might be affected by the fencing and land clearing project. These include sites 41BT380 and 41BT387
which were recorded by LCRA Archaeology Services staff in 2005 for a project involving proposed
maintenance activities for LCRA’s T-108 transmission line (Hixson et al. 2006:335-337). The third site,
41BT424, was recently recorded by LCRA Archaeology Services during the survey of a once-proposed
52-acre land purchase below Buchanan Dam.
For the currently proposed fencing and land clearing project on the perimeter of the larger 318-acre tract,
pedestrian survey work and shovel testing concentrated on high probability areas adjacent to stream
channels. In particular, high probability areas where it is anticipated that heavy machinery will cause
ground-disturbing activities during vegetation clearing were intensively examined.
For the originally proposed 52-acre land purchase, a pedestrian survey was conducted in June, 2007 that
covered 100% of the project area with intact soil, amounting to approximately 30 acres. Areas scoured to
bedrock were not surveyed. Shovel testing was conducted mostly in the southern half of the 52-acre tract
area because it contains the most vegetation and therefore will be impacted more by the proposed clearing
activities. This area also has a higher potential for prehistoric sites because of water sources including the

177

drainage mentioned above as well as numerous potholes or tinajas in the gneiss outcrops. This particular
sale was canceled and so was not reported to THC.
In order to consolidate previous investigations within what is now LCRA land that may become a park,
all known sites within the 318-acre parcel are reported upon, at least briefly. Figure 72 shows the
locations of all known shovel tests.
All sites were recorded on the TexSite database and documented with sketch maps. A no collection
policy was maintained throughout the investigation.

Results
Three previously recorded sites, 41BT380, 41BT387, and 41BT424 were revisited and two new
archeological sites, 41BT433 and 434, were recorded during the survey. These sites are shown in Figure
71. In addition to the locations that have been given site designations, a half dozen or so isolated metal
artifacts probably belonging to the period of dam construction were noted. These include a large hammer,
truck parts including doors, and various metal pipes and scrap metal. Descriptions of sites 41BT381 and
382, along with 41BT419-423, recorded during the original survey of the 52-acre tract are included in this
report, as is a very revisit to 41BT57, although none of these sites will be impacted by the present project.
Project Area Sites
41BT380
Site 41BT380 is a prehistoric lithic scatter with materials exposed on ground surface on both sides of a
gully that was dry at the time of the current investigation (Figure 73). A larger drainage channel is
located about 90 meters west of the west edge of the site, but that drainage may have been formed in
recent times by releases of flood waters from one of the Buchanan Dam floodgates. Cultural materials
were found to extend for a length of 125 meters within the approximately 25 meter transmission line
ROW. Materials observed at the site consist of about 20 pieces of chert debitage and one chert biface
fragment. Almost all of the artifacts were seen on the west side of the gully. A shovel test excavated on
that side of the gully during the LCRA’s previous survey for proposed maintenance activities on the T108 transmission line yielded three pieces of chert debitage in 25 cm thick gravelly sandy loam topsoils.
During the current investigation, ST #6 was excavated to determine if buried artifacts might be present in
the proposed fence corridor in the eastern part of the site. This shovel test indicated that the depth of
gravelly sandy loam deposits was 40 cm. No artifacts were recovered from the shovel test fill.
No evidence of a historic period component that was described on the original site form was seen during
the current survey. It appears that this historic component is actually present at site 41BT381, which was
recorded further westward during the 2005 LCRA survey.

178

Figure 72. Locations of all known shovel tests in relation to property boundary. Triangles were
excavated during the present project, circles were excavated previously.

Figure 73. Sketch of 41BT380.

180

The prehistoric materials observed at 41BT380 are restricted to chert debitage and one tool fragment. No
burned rocks, features, or diagnostic artifacts were found. Because of these factors, the portion of site
41BT380 within the proposed fence construction corridor is assessed as not eligible for the NRHP and not
eligible for formal designation as an SAL.
41BT387
Site 41BT387 is a prehistoric lithic scatter that was also originally recorded during the LCRA’s cultural
resource assessment for maintenance activities on the LCRA T-108 transmission line in 2005 (Figure 74).
The site extends for a length of about 40 m within the transmission line ROW and it is located on the west
side of a small creek that now carries water released from a floodgate of Buchanan Dam. The site
appeared to be a low density prehistoric lithic scatter with thin topsoils. No diagnostic artifacts or burned
rocks were observed.
This site is located on the eastern edge of the project area on the southwest side of an unnamed tributary
of the Colorado River. Site 41BT387 is a low density prehistoric lithic scatter that consists of chert
debitage and a core. Overall, about 11 artifacts were observed on ground surface within the proposed
fence corridor and the LCRA T-108 transmission line ROW. These materials were found within an area
that measures about 100 meters on an east-west line by 40 meters on a north-south line. Shovel Tests #1
and 2 along with a previous shovel test excavated during an LCRA survey for proposed maintenance
work on the T-108 corridor were all negative. These shovel tests indicate that the depth of gravelly sandy
loam topsoil ranges from 22 to 27 cm in thickness.
No features, diagnostic artifacts, or burned rocks were observed during the current and previous survey
work. Based of the results of the survey, the portion of the site within the proposed fence construction
corridor is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not eligible for formal designation as an
SAL.
41BT424
This site was recorded by the LCRA Archaeology Services staff in 2007 during an intensive survey of a
smaller 52-acre tract below Buchanan Dam that LCRA originally proposed to purchase rather than the
larger 318-acre tract that LCRA has now secured (Figure 75). The site may represent a small prehistoric
campsite situated on a soil-covered bench midway up a slope and approximately 100 meters south of one
of the dam’s retaining walls. A steep rocky slope rising up towards the retaining wall forms the north site
boundary. On the south side the ground slopes down towards an artificial pond created where a dirt road
crosses the drainage. One flake, a biface fragment, and a burned rock were noted in an eroded area at the
southeast corner of the site. A mussel shell fragment was also noted near the center of the bench proper.
This was only cultural material seen on the surface.
In 2007, three shovel tests were dug in the soil on the bench. The first test was dug to a depth of 20 cm
through sandy loam, recovering five pieces of chert debitage. The second test was dug to a depth of 30
cm before encountering bedrock. Three flakes were recovered in the upper 20 cm of soil and two flakes
between 20 and 30 cm below the surface. The third test only reached a depth of 15 cm before
encountering bedrock and recovered one chert flake.
Although the soil cover on the bench is shallow, it appears to be undisturbed – its location on the slope
would prevent easy access by heavy machinery. The amount of material recovered from the shovel tests
suggests limited use of the area, probably by a small number of people over a relatively short period of
time. It is unlikely that the site contains more than one component. The site is tentatively identified as a
camping area but more testing is needed to verify this interpretation. Based on the small amount of
evidence collected so far, the site is potentially eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and for formal listing
as an SAL.

Figure 74. Sketch of 41BT387

Figure 75. Sketch of 41BT424.

183

The 2008 revisit consisted of identifying the site’s boundaries, and prominently flagging the site for
avoidance by equipment. The three previously excavated shovel tests were relocated, but no cultural
material was seen on the ground surface. LCRA Archaeology Services will communicate with
construction planners to determine what type of fencing will be placed within the site. There are no
concerns with driving of t-posts, but if postholes are required Archaeology Services may choose to
monitor construction. Vegetation will be hand-cleared.
41BT433
This site is located on the eastern edge of the project area on the northeast side of an unnamed tributary of
the Colorado River (Figure 76). The site is an ephemeral prehistoric lithic scatter that is situated on the
first terrace above the tributary. Cultural materials consist of chert debitage and one projectile point
fragment that appears to be diagnostic of the Late Archaic II Period. Overall, the chert debitage and the
single projectile point fragment seen on ground surface suggest that materials are sparse. Additionally,
the two positive shovel tests (ST #3 and 4) each yielded only one chert chip in sandy loam deposits that
ranged from 20 to 37 cm in thickness.
Based on the pedestrian survey and shovel testing, the site is estimated to extend for a distance of
approximately 20 meters on a northeast to southwest line along the proposed boundary fenceline.
Additional survey work is needed to determine the full horizontal extent of the site to the north and south
of the fence corridor. It should be noted that prehistoric site 41BT383 is recorded on the T-108
transmission line about 100 m further southeastward, and future survey work may show that these two
sites are actually one continuous lithic scatter.
The current survey investigation indicates that the portion of 41BT433 within the proposed fence
construction corridor is not eligible for SAL or NR listing. This assessment is based on the lack of
features and the low volume and low diversity of cultural materials.
41BT434
Site 41BT434 is a low density lithic scatter with a small amount of mussel shell and a possible burned
rock recovered from a shovel test (Figure 77). It is located in an area with deep to very deep sandy loams.
The vegetation is primarily tall grasses with prickly pear, bee brush and tassijillo and a few large oak
trees. The land form slopes moderately downward from north to south as the Colorado River is
approached. Mapped stream channels to the east and west are the result of tailraces of Buchanan Dam
having scoured soils down to bare granite bedrock. South of the site, the first terrace of the river appears
to have been dramatically altered, and the lagoons that are present are most likely the result of borrow pits
for dam construction.
The site was located through shovel testing following the observation of two clusters of chert debitage,
one with about five flakes in an eroded area at the south end of the site, and two seen on the ground
surface at about the site center. Five shovel tests were excavated along the proposed fenceline, only one
of which (ST 14) was positive. This test had a tertiary flake and a mussel shell fragment in the upper 20
cm, and two tertiary flakes, a mid-stage biface fragment, and one possible burned rock in the second 20
cm. The test was excavated through 60 cm of light brown to brown sandy loam before decomposing
granite was encountered. Elsewhere, soils ranged from 45 cm in depth at the southern end of the site to
over 1 m as higher elevations were reached towards the north.
Site 41BT434 is considered to be fully investigated within the project area, and to be ineligible for formal
state or federal listings. The contour break running east/west through the center of the site does appear to
be favorable for additional site development, especially towards the west where it becomes a lobe
overlooking the river to the south. It is also possible it is a continuation of, or links into, 41BT381, a

184

Figure 76. Sketch of 41BT433.

185

Figure 77. Sketch of 41BT434.

186

lithic scatter located about 200 m of the west along the transmission line. Any future intensive ground
disturbing projects should have this area evaluated before proceeding.

Other Sites
These are sites that are within the new LCRA holding that were previously recorded and so are part of
LCRA’s inventory. They will not be impacted by the present project, but are included here for future ease
of reference.
41BT57
This site was first recorded by Daymond Crawford in 1965. Only a key site card is available. Reported
were an unknown number of Nolan points and a possible Gower point, along with manos. The revisit
found a light but extensive flake scatter beginning at the north end of a cement curb along a dirt road.
Also seen were one mano and a middle stage biface. Soils appears intact for a distance of about 20 m east
of the road towards the creek and more substantially to the west of the road, where a fairly discrete trash
scatter suggests a building was once present, probably associated with the improved road and dating to
the period of dam construction. When the site can be more clearly defined and shovel tested, a new
TexSite form will be completed.
41BT381
This site was revisited briefly, and it was confirmed that this is the location with the light historic trash
scatter, essentially a few pieces of whiteware and some metal. Flakes were common and widespread, and
it is assumed that the unifaces on the site form were actually present. It appears that the site form for
41BT380 that is on file at TARL was incorrectly filled out and confused with 41BT381. Given the
uncertainty and lack of documentation, additional investigations are warranted here.
41BT382
Based on the sketch map of this site, it appears it was a very low density lithic scatter. It was not
discussed in the transmission line maintenance document, so it appears that no avoidance was considered
necessary within the transmission line ROW. However, since previous work was restricted to the ROW,
and LCRA now owns this property, a larger reinvestigation of this site is warranted, especially prior to
ground disturbing activities.
41BT419
Site 41BT419 represents the remains of a historic structure consisting of a stone chimney/fireplace and
concrete building piers with a nearby tin can trash heap (Figure 78). The location of this structure is
unusual in that it was constructed over a wash in a low spot between gneiss outcrops. Runoff from rains
has created a channel between the chimney and one of the piers. The chimney was built of undressed
local boulders cemented together, perhaps originally with lime mortar but with extensive repairs of
Portland cement. The piers were molded from concrete and appear more recent than the chimney, at least
in a technological sense. No household trash was noted in the vicinity except for the tin can trash heap
located 20 feet northwest of the chimney.
The previous land owner was told by “old timers” that the fireplace was part of a kitchen used to feed
dam workers in the 1930s. Although the fireplace/chimney appears older, the lack of household debris
and the tin can dump would support such an interpretation.

187

Figure 78. Sketch of 41BT419.

188

41BT420
This site represents a small surface lithic scatter bordered on the west side by the main drainage through
the project area and on the east side by a low granite ledge (Figure 79). The ground in between is covered
by sparse grasses and forbs with an open canopy of juniper trees.
The cultural material consists entirely of chert debitage. Approximately one dozen flakes and chips were
observed with a slight concentration near the southern limits of the site area where minor sheet erosion
has occurred. One possibly burned quartz rock was also noted on the surface.
41BT421
This site is very similar in size and composition to Site 2. It is located approximately 100 meters to the
north of 41BT420 on the east side of the same drainage. Approximately one dozen flakes were noted on
the surface but no burned rocks.
41BT422
This site is located along the dirt road that runs along the east side of the project area. Cultural material
was mainly seen in the road where visibility is good giving the impression that the site is long and narrow
along a northeast to southwest line. The blading of the dirt road may have carried some of the cultural
material along the road increasing its length somewhat.

41BT423
This consists of a prehistoric lithic scatter located towards the middle of the project area immediately
south of one the largest areas of bedrock exposed by water released from the dam (Figure 80). The
northeastern half of the site area is covered by numerous low granite outcrops stunted live oak trees, the
southwestern half has a thin soil cover and more open vegetation. A dirt road skirts the southeastern edge
of the site. Cultural material consists of chert debitage, several bifaces, and one triangular dart point
resembling the Early Triangular type.
Other Areas
Shovel Test 10 was placed near the river’s edge at the southwestern portion of the property. This test
found about 10 cm of organic topsoil over clay and gravel. Much of the fence will be routed through
standing or intermittently standing water. The ca. 1900 USGS map shows an island in this location, and
remnants of it may exist. Although scattered artifacts were seen on the river terrace south of 41BT434,
the land appeared very irregular, and the large ponds are very clearly artificial. So although there may be
portions of the river terrace that are relatively undisturbed, most is probably destroyed by presumed
borrow an quarry operations for dam construction.
The granite or gneiss outcrops west and north of 41BT423 had occasional flakes visible around their
edges. Shovel test 12 was placed in this area, and found 60 cm of spoil, likely pocketed. No artifacts
were recovered. More intensive survey actually found fewer flakes, so this previously surveyed area was
not recorded as a site or possible extension of 41BT423.
It is also in this general area that very large, impressive boulders overlooking significant viewsheds are
present on sloping bedrock, reminiscent of those seen at Enchanted Rock. Tongue-in-cheek references to
“places of power” need not be fully discounted, especially if a landscape-based interpretative design is
developed.

189

The two mapped drainages along FM 690 were investigated. Visibility was fair and there was no
evidence of cultural material. Elsewhere there appears to be a low probability for intact archaeological
sites along this existing fenceline.
Shovel Test 13 was placed in pocketed soils near where a single chip had been seen during pedestrian
survey. It found about 35 cm of sandy loam soils. Further examination found two additional flakes in the
general vicinity. These were not considered to be present in sufficient density to warrant site designation,
but future work should more closely examine this general area, which is bordered to the west by the one,
true creek.
This creek, presently unnamed on USGS maps, but referred to by Daymond Crawford as named in
reference to a nearby physiographic feature from which it originates could certainly use intensive survey.
Site 41BT57, 383, 387, as well as 41BT58, located outside LCRA property are all o or near this creek.
The drainage that originates from the easternmost floodgates or spillway was also investigated. Soils here
were in excess of one meter deep, as evidenced by the scoured drainage. No artifacts were seen, but
intensive ground disturbing activities should be investigated before proceeding in this area.
Summary and Recommendations
LCRA proposes to construct an approximate 3.25 mi perimeter fence around a newly-acquired 318-acre
tract located immediately below Buchanan Dam in western Burnet County, Texas. An 8-foot wide
corridor will be mechanically cleared along this boundary.
An intensive cultural resources survey was conducted by LCRA Archaeology Services that incorporated
two previous investigations with targeted survey and site revisits. Three previously recorded sites,
41BT380, 387 and 424, and two previously unrecorded sites, 41BT433 and 434, will be impacted by the
project. A total of 13 sites are mentioned in this report in order to consolidate data and more easily enable
any future investigations.
Three of the four sites recorded during transmission line investigations within the tract, 41BT380, 382
and 387, four of the six sites recorded during the original 52-acre survey, 41BT420-423, and the two new
sites 41BT433 and 41BT434, recorded during the present project, contain surface or superficially buried
deposits consisting of debitage and an occasional stone tool. These deposits are likely disturbed and
temporally mixed. The lack of evidence for activities other than stone tool making and repair also limits
the research value of these sites. Therefore, the investigated portions of these sites are not SAL-eligible
and do not have to be avoided during the project.
Site 41BT381 was previously recommended for avoidance. It was not intensively reinvestigated during
the present project, although the cursory examination suggest significant research yield would be low
within the transmission line corridor. Regardless, for the time being, those recommendations remain in
effect.
Site 41BT424, on the other hand, contains buried deposits relating to cooking activities in addition to the
stone tool manufacturing and maintenance. The small size of the site and low artifact density suggest that
it was occupied by a small number people over a brief period of time – such as during a period of group
fission when larger bands split up when food resources were scarce. A winter season camp seems likely,
especially since the site is well-sheltered from the elements. This site has potential for research involving
land use patterns, composition of fissioned groups, and activities conducted by small groups during
winter months. Therefore avoidance of the site is recommended during the clearing activities associated

190

with this project. If the area cannot be avoided, all vegetation including trees must be cut by hand in
order to avoid any ground disturbances. The site has been prominently flagged to facilitate avoidance.
Site 41BT419 is the only historic site recorded during the survey. Its location within a draw has resulted
in damage to the structure by rainwater runoff, including dislocation of its piers and removal of any
archeological deposits under and around the structure. A small tin can dump is located nearby but
extensive buried cultural deposits are not possible in the shallow soils. The site contains little additional
information other than what has already been recorded. Still, the chimney and the one in situ pier are part
of the history of the dam building and should be preserved. The site is not within the present project area,
but any future projects will be recommended for avoidance.
This property is topographically unique among LCRA landholdings. Possible future use as a park will
enable additional investigations. For the purposes of the present project, it is recommended that the
clearing and fence construction be allowed to proceed as described, with the exception of the flagged site
41BT424, where hand clearing and limiting of fence construction to t-posts is recommended. If this is not
possible, then additional testing is warranted and will be considered, along with potential monitoring of
any small postholes necessary for fence construction. More intensive impacts will have any further
investigations coordinated through the Texas Historical Commission.
