An Intensive Cultural Resource Survey of the Lower Colorado River Authority’s
Proposed Rim Rock to Jack Furman Transmission Line and Substation Project,
Kerr County, Texas

By
Daniel J. Prikryl and Charles A. Hixson

Daniel J. Prikryl
Principal Investigator

Texas Antiquities Committee Archeology Permit # 4779
Interim Report #12.08
Project # 25.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
August 2008

195

Abstract
During the period from May to July 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted an intensive
cultural resource survey for the LCRA’s proposed Rim Rock to Jack Furman Transmission Line and
Substation Project in Kerr County, Texas. As a result of the investigation, 10 new prehistoric sites
consisting of 41KR662-41KR671 were recorded and assessed. One previously recorded prehistoric site,
41KR649, was also revisited and assessed. These 11 sites can be classified as 3 burned rock midden sites
(41KR649, 41KR663, and 41KR666), 6 lithic scatters (41KR664, and 41KR667-41KR671), and 2 lithic
scatter/lithic procurement sites (41KR662 and 41KR665).
Cultural deposits associated with a burned rock midden feature at the south end of site 41KR649 were
assessed as potentially eligible for inclusion in the National Register of Historic Places and potentially
worthy of formal designation as a State Archeological Landmark. Avoidance of this portion of site
41KR649 is recommended during project construction.
The portions of the other 10 sites that are located within the project area are assessed as not eligible for
inclusion in the National Register of Historic Places and not meriting formal designation as State
Archeological Landmarks. The assessments for these 10 sites are based on various factors including
previous disturbances to cultural deposits, absence of depth of deposits, sparseness of cultural materials,
and the lack of discrete components, features, diagnostic artifacts, and dateable materials.

Aside from the avoidance measures at site 41KR649, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends
that construction of the Rim Rock to Jack Furman Transmission Line and Substation Project be allowed
to proceed without any further cultural resource investigations. As always, standard emergency discovery
provisions are applicable to this project.
No artifacts were collected and no curation will be necessary.

196

Introduction
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to construct the new Rim Rock to Jack
Furman Transmission Line and Substation Project in Kerr County, Texas. The project consists of the
construction of the new Jack Furman electrical substation along with about 6.6 linear miles of electrical
transmission line. The project area is situated west and southwest of Kerrville, Texas and will extend
from the LCRA’s existing Rim Rock Substation generally northward to connect to LCRA’s existing T368 transmission line (Figure 81). The transmission line will generally have an 80 foot wide corridor with
exceptions being a 140 foot wide corridor where the transmission structures are spaced at longer intervals
at the Guadalupe River crossing and on several steep slopes extending from upland ridges to canyon
bottomlands. The new substation will cover a rectangular area of approximately 4 acres near the north
end of the project.
During the period from May to July 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted an intensive
cultural resource survey for this project. The survey consisted of pedestrian survey and shovel testing of a
previously recorded archaeological site and high probability areas for unrecorded sites. Personnel who
conducted the fieldwork consisted of LCRA staff members Daniel Prikryl (Principal Investigator) and
contracted archaeologists Glenn Goode, Chuck Hixson, and Cody Kiker. The investigation was
conducted under TAC Blanket Permit #4779, which was issued to the LCRA by the Texas Historical
Commission (THC) for Calendar Year 2008. The survey investigation was also designed to meet the
requirements of Section 106 of the National Historic Preservation Act of 1966. All work was completed
in accordance with methods for fieldwork and reporting as proposed by the Texas Council of
Archeologists (1992).

Environmental Background
The project area is located on the Edwards Plateau within the southern edge of the Great Plains
physiographic province (Raisz 1957; Fenneman 1938). The transmission line corridor crosses the
Guadalupe River and several of its tributaries. Vegetation on the Guadalupe River floodplain consists of
live oak-mesquite-ashe juniper parkland while the uplands are live oak-ashe juniper woodland (McMahan
et al. 1984). It appears that extensive tree and brush clearing has occurred in upland areas in and around
the project area in recent years since many upland areas depicted as wooded on the current USGS map are
now predominantly grasslands (Figure 82).
In upland areas, the surface geology consists of the Glen Rose Limestone and the Fort Terrett member of
the Edwards Limestone (Barnes 1981). Importantly, the Fort Terrett member contains large amounts of
high quality chert that were an important resource to Native American peoples. Within the transmission
line corridor, low terrace deposits of either Holocene or Pleistocene age are present on the floodplains of
the Guadalupe River and its tributary, Bear Creek. These terrace deposits include some alluvium along
with large amounts of gravel.
Soils on upland parts of the project area mostly consist of the Eckrant-Kerrville Rock Outcrop
Association (Dittemore and Coburn 1984). These are mostly shallow clayey to loamy soils of 5 to 20 cm
thickness that contain limestone cobbles and gravels. These soils occur on landforms ranging from gently
undulating to those that are hilly and steep. A few upland areas at the south end of the project area have
topsoils of the Tarpley-Eckrant-Rough Creek Association. These are shallow to very shallow loamy and
clayey soils that contain limestone cobbles.
Nuvalde-Oakalla-Boerne soils are present on terraces and bottom lands adjacent to the Guadalupe River
and Bear Creek. These soils are loamy and clayey soils that occur on level to gently

197

Figure 81: Section of the Kerrville, Texas 7.5’ USGS map (1982) showing the transmission line route, the
substation location, and the areas surveyed.

198

sloping surfaces. These soils are relatively deep and can as thick as 4 meters in some areas. At the
Gatlin Site (41KR621), which is located about 2 miles downstream on the south side of the Guadalupe
River, prehistoric archaeological materials were found as deep as 1.2 meters below ground surface in a
Nuvalde silty clay deposit (Miller: Personal Communication 2008). Within the project area, an alluvial
terrace deposit is present on the north bank of the river but is absent on the south bank of the river. A 2.5
meter deep bulldozer cut for a low water river crossing on the north bank just upstream from the
transmission line corridor shows the terrace deposit has only a 30 to 50 cm thick topsoil with the deeper
part of the profile being a mostly a gravelly matrix (Figure 83).

Figure 82. View of cleared upland area at south end of project area.

Figure 83. View of bulldozer cut on the north bank of Guadalupe River showing terrace profile.

199

Culture History
The project area lies on the boundary between the Central Texas (Prewitt 1981) and South Texas (Hester
2004) archeological regions. Archaeological materials from both regions have typically been divided into
four main subdivisions, Paleo-Indian, Archaic, Late Prehistoric, and Historic. These subdivisions
combine to begin to describe the 12,000 or more years of human occupation. A longstanding, detailed
chronology for Central Texas that was proposed by Prewitt (1981, 1985), has recently been revised by
Johnson and Goode (1994) and Johnson (1995), who utilized new lines of evidence based on climatic and
geomorphological data to redefine period divisions. This can be contrasted with the research of Collins
(1995), who indicates climatic variables at times were somewhat different than those described by
Johnson and Goode. The ultimate goal of many modern archaeologists is to transcend the culture history
of an area and attempt to explain why change occurred in various cultures and regions. To this end
carefully revised chronologies are to be expected and encouraged.
Native American archaeological site types in Kerr County consist of camps, caches, isolated artifacts,
interments, cemeteries, kill/butcher locales, quarry/workshops, lithic scatters, and rock art sites (Collins
1995:363). The area is perhaps best known for the many burned rock midden sites that occur on the
Edwards Plateau. Numerous excavations of major campsites have been conducted along the larger
streams and rivers (cf. Prewitt 1982). Such excavations have demonstrated that Central Texas was
occupied for at least 12,000 years prior to the coming of Europeans. These also show that throughout
these millennia, Native American peoples were nomadic hunter-gatherers who moved across the
landscape exploiting seasonally available plant and animal resources.
Kerr County was first settled in 1846 by Joshua D. Brown and a group of shingle makers who settled at
the location of present day Kerrville (Odintz 1997). This soon became a community and was named
Brownsborough in 1848. In 1856 Brownsborough was renamed Kerrville. Indian raids were problematic
through 1878, and as a result, Camp Verde was established in 1855. The Civil War saw high levels of
violence between anti-secessionist German settlers and Confederate sympathizers. Ranching became
increasingly important in the area, and in 1880 the Y O Ranch was established by Charles A. Schreiner.
The San Antonio and Aransas Pass Railway reached Kerrville in 1887, which provided additional
economic benefits. By the 1920’s, production of mohair was a thriving industry. Tourism began in the
early part of the twentieth century as Kerr County acquired a reputation for a healthful climate.
Numerous camps, resorts, and health facilities were established, and remain an important part of the local
economy.

Previous Investigations
According the Texas Historic Sites Atlas, Kerr County presently has over 660 recorded archaeological
sites. Some sites in the Kerrville vicinity have been recorded by avocational archaeologists and many
other others were recorded any archaeological projects have been related to road or highway construction.
An example of more intensive work includes the Shep Site and the Wounded Eye Site (41KR109 and
41KR107) excavated during the improvements to State Highway 16 (Luke 1980). These were both
burned rock middens that contained primarily Middle Archaic projectile points with seemingly intrusive
materials of both earlier and later periods. Analysis was confined to the lithic assemblage, with the
principal conclusion suggesting that reduction activities were centered on biface manufacture and
expedient tool technology. These two sites may contain important data based on the preponderance of
Early Triangular points with lesser numbers of Travis and Nolan points suggesting a relatively short-lived
occupation during a the Middle Archaic period.

200

The Camp Pearl Wheat Site (Collins et al. 1990) was an Early/Middle Archaic camp with Martindale,
Uvalde, and Bell points. Of particular interest were archaeomagnetic and organic residue studies that
were conducted. This led to detailed conclusions regarding burned rock feature construction and use
sequences.
Many other previously recorded sites are burned rock middens that suggest abundance of substantial
Middle Archaic occupation areas during a time of relatively harsh and dry conditions (Collins 1995) when
persons may have retreated to the edge of the Plateau where water sources were more predictable, based
on the large numbers of springs that are present. Drier conditions may also have led to the beginning of
the burned rock midden phenomenon, when xeric adapted plants, in particular stool, began to expand their
range and become a more obvious food choice (Johnson 1995). Gaining a fuller understanding of the
Middle and Late Archaic Periods may be one of the most valuable research opportunities this part of the
Hill Country will provide.

Methodology
Prior to the survey, a files search was conducted to check for previously recorded archaeological sites
within and near the ROW corridor and determine high probability areas for unrecorded archaeological
sites. According to the THC’s Site Atlas there were no previously recorded archaeological sites within
the project area. The file search showed that there are numerous previously recorded archaeological sites
in the vicinity of the project area. The major prehistoric campsites and some of the burned rock midden
sites were located on the lower terraces of the Guadalupe River and larger creeks. Other burned rock
midden sites appeared to be located not only adjacent to smaller creeks but also on upland ridges near the
heads of tributary creeks, which seemed to imply that spring vents are present at heads of these creeks.
Prehistoric lithic scatters were also noted to be fairly common on the edges of upland ridges overlooking
stream channels. Based on the results of the files search, high probability areas for prehistoric sites were
then determined and these areas were plotted on the modern Kerrville, Texas 7.5’ USGS map for field
survey (see Figure 81).
An attempt was also made to determine high probability areas for historic period sites. An early
1:125,000 scale Kerrville, Texas USGS map was found on the University of Texas library system’s
website. This map was printed in 1885 and reprinted in 1920. Unfortunately, individual rural house sites
are not shown on this map. This map did show that no communities or former communities would be
crossed by the transmission line. No other USGS topographic maps were found for the era between the
reprinted 1920 map and the modern 7.5’ Kerrville, Texas USGS that was first published in 1964 and then
photorevised in 1982. Importantly, this 7.5’ map indicated that there are no standing structures within the
ROW corridor.
Field survey methods used during the project consisted of a 100 percent intensive pedestrian survey of all
high probability areas as described above along with shovel testing. Pedestrian survey methods for high
probability areas consisted of walking the transmission line corridor in zig-zag transects while intensively
inspecting bare and eroded patches of ground surface. Additionally, the walls of cutbanks and gullies
were examined for evidences of buried cultural materials. During the pedestrian survey shovel tests,
which consisted of small 35 to 40 cm (13.5 to 15 inch) diameter holes excavated to maximum depths of
100 cm (39 inches) or to the top of the bedrock/B horizon, were judgmentally placed in areas of high
probability having poor ground surface visibility and also to help determine the boundaries of any
archeological sites. All shovel tests were carefully backfilled immediately after excavation to restore the
ground surface to its original appearance. Further, no artifacts were removed from the property of any of
the landowners. Any artifacts observed on the ground surface or found in the shovel tests were examined,

201

analyzed, and photographed in the field, as necessary, and then placed back in their original locations.
Appendix 1 provides lists the soil descriptions from each shovel test.

Survey Results
Approximately 3.6 linear miles of the 6.6 mile long transmission line ROW were intensively surveyed
(see Figure 81). Ground surface visibility was generally fair due to the fact that shallow, stony topsoils
cover the ground surface in many areas. Also, in some upland areas, limestone bedrock is present on
ground surface. Additionally, because of extended drought conditions, vegetation was less dense than
under normal conditions (Figure 84). These factors reduced the need for shovel testing so that a total of
33 shovel tests were excavated rather than the 58 shovel tests that would typically meet the THC’s
minimum requirements.

Figure 84. Typical view of cobbly topsoil in canyon bottomland near tributary creek. Note lack of
vegetation due to drought conditions.
As a result of the field survey, 10 new cultural resource sites, 41KR662-671, and 1 previously recorded
site, 41KR649, were recorded and assessed. Descriptions of these sites are provided in the following
paragraphs according to their locations within Survey Areas 1-3.
Survey Area A
This survey area consists of the northern one-third of the proposed transmission line corridor and the
proposed new Jack Furman Substation. High probability areas targeted for survey in Area A include the
terraces on either side of the Guadalupe River and the head of a small tributary creek northeast of the
substation tract (Figure 85). The proposed substation area was also investigated because the chert-bearing
Fort Terrett limestone occurs in that area. One additional high probability area adjacent to a tributary
creek at the far north end of the transmission line route where it will tie-in with the LCRA’s existing T202

368 transmission was not checked because it had been previously surveyed with negative results during
the LCRA’s survey of the T-368 corridor (Hixson et al. 2004). The five archaeological sites recorded in
Area A are described below.

Figure 85. Section of the Kerrville, Texas 7.5’ USGS map showing Survey Area A and the locations of
archaeological sites.

203

41KR662
This prehistoric lithic scatter/lithic procurement site is located in the northern part of the project area. The
site lies south and east of Goat Creek Cut-Off Road and will be impacted both by the construction of the
transmission line and the new Jack Furman Substation (Figure 86). Because areas north and west of the
final substation location were originally considered as possible locations for the substation, the surveyors
were able to determine that the site covers an area measuring about 195 meters north-south by 115 meters
east-west. It is situated at the base of a moderately steep hill and about 420 to 580 meters southwest of an
intermittent creek. The western part of the site has been disturbed by former usage as a house trailer park.
The site has a diffuse surface scatter of artifacts consisting flint chipping debris, tool fragments, test chert
cobbles, and cores. Approximately 10 chert flakes, one core, one scraper, two biface performs, and a
finished biface fragment were observed. Several rounded chert cobbles with one to three flake removals
were also seen on ground surface as were some thinner, tabular chert cobbles of 2.5 to 5.0 cm thickness
that showed signs of initial flaking.

Figure 86. Map of site 41KR662.

204

The only subsurface artifact found at 41KR662 was a biface fragment which seen eroding out of ditch
that had been cut along a fenceline. The biface was found about 20 cm below ground surface on the
boundary between the grayish-brown gravelly clay loam topsoil and the underlying limestone bedrock.
Close inspection of the adjacent banks of the ditch, however, revealed no other artifacts. Additionally,
Shovel Test # 21 which was excavated in undisturbed soil near the location of the biface was negative.
Four other Shovel Tests (ST# 17-20) which were excavated within other parts of the site were also
negative.
Based on the low density of artifacts, the lack of features and diagnostic artifacts, the portion of site
41KR662 within the proposed electrical substation and associated transmission line ROW is assessed as
not eligible for inclusion in the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) and not meriting formal
designation as a State Archeological Landmark (SAL).
41KR663
This prehistoric burned rock midden site is located on the first terrace overlooking the north bank
Guadalupe River and, overall, the site appears to extend for a length of 95 meters on a north-south line
within the transmission line ROW (Figure 87). The site extends across the entire 24 meter (80 ft) width
of the transmission line ROW on an east-west line. The site is also known to extend at least 20 meters
further eastward and 40 meters further westward. A Late Archaic projectile point of the Marcos or
Castroville type was seen during a brief reconnaissance of that area west of the transmission ROW.
A broad surface scatter of burned rocks covering an area about 25 meters north-south line by 30 meters
east-west at the north end of the site is interpreted as representing the scattered remains of a former
burned rock midden feature (Figure 88). This portion of the site is a formerly cultivated field, and
plowing appears to have flattened the midden feature and spread the burned rocks and chert chipping
debris across the field. Although the burned rock does not approach the normal density of a burned rock
midden, the rocks are slightly denser in the central part of the scatter. Shovel Test #11 was excavated in
that central area to determine if any buried cultural remains are present. The shovel test produced 29
chert flakes and chips, 1 chert unificial tool fragment, and 12 burned rock fragments. However, almost all
of these artifacts came from the upper 10 cm of shovel test within the former plow zone. The overall
depth of gravelly clay loam topsoil in that area is 31 cm.
Shovel Test #16 was then excavated about 15 meters east of Shovel Test #11 in an area where the burned
rock scatter was less dense on ground surface. The artifact recovery consisted of only three chert flakes
and chips, with all of these found in the upper 10 cm of soil deposits. The shovel test was terminated
when a very dense gravel lens was encountered at 30 cm below ground surface.
Beyond the burned rock scatter, there are extremely few artifacts on ground surface. Shovel Tests #9 and
10 were excavated south of the burned rock scatter to determine if buried cultural deposits might be
present in areas closer to the river channel where deeper topsoils are present. Shovel Test #10 was placed
just 5 meters south of the south edge of the burned rock scatter. Recovery from Levels 1 and 2 consisted
of just one chert chip in each level. A solid gravel deposit was struck at 36 cm below ground surface
(bgs) and the shovel test was ended at 50 cm without reaching the bottom of the gravel deposit. Shovel
Test #9 was excavated about 55 meters south of the burned rock scatter at the location of proposed
Transmission Pole Structure #37 (see Figure 87). A few flakes and a tested cobble were found in Levels
1 and 3. Excavation of this shovel test ended at 1 meter below ground surface.

205

Figure 87. Map of site 41KR663.

206

Figure 88. View of northern part of site 41KR663 with shovel testing in progress within the burned rock
scatter.
Since much of the area between Shovel Tests #9 and 10 has been heavily disturbed by the construction of
a gravel road, no further shovel testing was attempted in the area south of the burned rock scatter. Shovel
Tests #12 and 13 were then dug north of the burned rock scatter. Immediately north of the burned rock
scatter, Shovel Test #12, located just 5 meters north of the scatter, was sterile while Shovel Test #13,
which was excavated about 30 meters north of the burned rock scatter, produced just one flake.
The survey results indicate that the burned rock midden feature at 41KR663 has been heavily damaged by
cultivation and land clearing. The vast majority of artifacts associated with this feature lay within the
plow zone. Beyond the horizontal limits of the midden scatter, artifact
density is low and there is little diversity of materials. With the exception of Shovel Test #9, dense
gravels were encountered at an average depth of 33 cm below ground surface. Shovel
testing provided no evidence of buried features and only one diagnostic, a Late Archaic dart point, was
found. Based on these factors, the portion of the site within the transmission line is assessed as not
eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not eligible for formal listing as an SAL.
41KR664
Site 41KR664 is a prehistoric lithic scatter that is located about 400 meters north of the Guadalupe River
on the outer edge of first terrace above the river channel (see Figure 85). Archaeological materials were
observed on the east side of a north-south running fenceline in an open field that is currently being
bulldozed to reshape the landscape for the upcoming construction of a large gas (Figure 89). This
fenceline marks the western edge of the transmission
line ROW, and most artifacts were observed in the backdirt of a one meter deep ditch that parallels the
fenceline (Figure 90). Other artifacts were observed in the vicinity of a separate 15

207

Figure 89. Map of site 41KR664.
ft high backdirt pile that lies about 15 meters east of the east edge of the transmission line ROW. This
backdirt pile appears to be the spoil material from a large pit that has been excavated further east of the
transmission line ROW.
The portion of 41KR664 within the transmission line ROW appears to be a low density prehistoric lithic
scatter. Approximately 12 pieces of chert chipping debris are spread across the disturbed backdirt of the
ditch for a length 90 meters. The only burned rocks found consisted of four specimens scattered over
10x10 meter area on the slopes of the ditch in the central part of the site. Additionally, an Ensor point
fragment was seen at the base of the larger backdirt pile east of the transmission line ROW.

208

Since the one meter deep ditch that extends on a north-south line down the length of the transmission line
corridor, inspection of the walls of the ditch were used in lieu of some of the shovel testing. The depth of
grayish-brown silty clay topsoil in the ditch was observed to range from 10 to 50 cm. A yellowish-brown
clay subsoil lay beneath this deposit.
Shovel Test #15 was excavated on the centerline of the ROW corridor east of the area where the burned
rocks were seen. This shovel test yielded no artifacts. Shovel Test #16 was placed at the proposed
location of Structure #38, the nearest proposed transmission line pole, to determine if the site extended
south of the materials exposed in the ditch. This shovel test was also negative.
Overall, the site appears to have widely scattered, diffuse remains that have little potential for further
investigation. The site is assessed as not eligible for the NRHP and no meriting formal designation as an
SAL.

Figure 90. View of existing ditch within transmission line corridor at site 41KR664.

209

41KR670
This prehistoric lithic scatter is located on a high upland ridge overlooking the south side of the
Guadalupe River channel basin (see Figure 85). While the main river channel is actually 200 meters
north of the site, an overflow slough associated with the river is just 110 meters north of the site.
Within the transmission line ROW, there is a very diffuse scatter of chert flakes and chips with most of
them being located near the proposed location of Transmission Structure #36 (Figure 91). Despite an
extensive search and good ground surface visibility in some areas, only five pieces of chert debitage were
found within the transmission line corridor.
Since the landowner accompanied the crew during the field investigation, he allowed the crew to briefly
survey adjacent areas outside of the transmission line ROW. Much denser cultural materials were found
at distances 10 to 15 meters west of the west edge of the transmission line ROW on the central part of a
topographic projection of the upland terrace. Among the materials found outside of the ROW were a
projectile point fragment possibly of the Angostura type and a biface fragment. Shovel Test #34 was
excavated within the transmission line corridor at a spot as close as possible to these finds outside the
corridor. That shovel test was negative. Another shovel test, #35, was excavated at the north end of the
site where Transmission Structure #36 will be placed. It, likewise, was negative.
Overall, the known part of the site covers an area measuring about 50 meters on a northeast-southwest
line by 22 meters on a northwest-southeast line. Although areas of interest may lie west of the
transmission line corridor, the portion of the site within the corridor does not have buried deposits,
features, or any density of materials to warrant any additional investigation. The portion of site 41KR670
that lies within the transmission line ROW is assessed as not worthy of formal designation as an SAL and
not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP.
41KR671
This prehistoric lithic scatter is located in a cleared field about 60 meters south of site 41KR670. The site
lies of the same upland ridge system as 41KR670, but further south of the Guadalupe River floodplain.
The diffuse scatter of artifacts observed on ground surface at this site consists of one chert projectile point
fragment of unknown type, a chert scraper, about 40 pieces of chert debitage, and 5 to 10 burned rock
fragments. These artifacts are spread over an area measuring about 180 meters north-south by 22 meters
east-west. Although the artifact scatter is not dense, higher numbers of artifacts were noted at both the
north and southwest ends of the site. Burned rock fragments are present only at the north end. An
additional artifact of interest is an isolated bullet of apparent 19th century age that was found on ground
surface in the southern part of the site.
Ground surface visibility at this site is good due to recent land clearing and the fact that the southern half
of the site has limestone bedrock exposed on ground surface in many areas. Because the only area with
any apparent soil depth was at the far north end of the site, Shovel Test #36 was excavated in that area
near several of the burned rocks seen at the site. The shovel test showed that the depth of soil deposits
above limestone bedrock was only 18 cm. No artifacts were found in the shovel test matrix.
The site appears to be a diffuse lithic scatter with the few burned rocks also suggesting the usage of
cooking or heating features. The shallow deposits have been damaged by land clearing and slope wash.
No intact features or dateable diagnostic artifacts were seen. The portion of site 41KR671 within the
transmission line ROW is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and no meriting formal
designation as an SAL.

Figure 91. Map of sites 41KR670 and 41KR671.

High probability areas that were surveyed in Area B, the central part of the project area include the
floodplain and low terraces where the transmission line will cross Bear Creek and two of its tributaries
(Figure 92). This includes one fairly long segment where the transmission line will be placed within the
narrow valley of a steep canyon in central and southern parts of Area B. In that area, the transmission line
ROW will extend more or less parallel to one of the tributaries.

41KR666
This burned rock midden site is located on the left (north) terrace of Bear Creek approximately a mile and
a half from its confluence with the Guadalupe River. The steep terrace slope, 70 meters north of the creek
channel and rising 20 feet, forms the southern boundary of the site. Cultural material becomes scarce
approximately 80 meters north of the terrace edge and ends 10 meters or more south of Bear Creek Road.

212

The area between the creek and the road is mostly covered by thick woods of ashe juniper and oak except
for a 10 meter-wide strip running perpendicular to the creek and road that has been cleared for use as a
water pipeline and electrical distribution line (Figure 93). The eastern half of the proposed transmission
line ROW mostly consists of this cleared utility distribution line corridor.
During initial survey work to locate the boundaries of the transmission line ROW, the west edge of a
small burned rock midden was observed. It was quickly determined that this feature is about 11 meters
east of the transmission line ROW corridor’s boundary (Figure 94). The midden appeared to be about 10
meters in diameter but size and plan uncertain because of rocks strewn out of a pit dug in the approximate
center by relic hunters. This feature should not be affected by the transmission line project. Cultural
material observed on the surface within the proposed ROW consists nearly entirely of chert debitage with
only one tool noted during the survey, a modified flake. Since much of the project area has been cleared
and is apparently frequented by relic hunters, many projectile points and other obvious tools may have
probably already been removed from the site.
Shovel Test #26 was excavated immediately adjacent to the proposed location of Transmission Structure
#30. The soil profile showed a 17 cm thick grayish-brown stony loam above limestone bedrock. Artifact
recovery consisted of three small tertiary flakes. Shovel Test #27 was excavated about 25 meters further
northward on the centerline of the transmission corridor in a heavily wooded area. It revealed a 21 cm
brown stony loam that lay on limestone bedrock. No artifacts were found in the screening of the fill.
The portion of the site within the proposed ROW contains mainly surface cultural material that is
disturbed and probably temporally mixed. The artifact scatter appears to be related to a burned rock
midden feature is located east of the transmission line corridor. The portion of the site within the
transmission line corridor is not considered NRHP or SAL eligible.

Figure 93. Shovel testing in progress at 41KR666 along edge of existing utility corridor.

41KR667
This prehistoric lithic scatter is situated within a steep-sided canyon in the upper reaches of a tributary of
Bear Creek (Figure 95). The site is located at the base of steep upland slope along the east side of a
deeply incised steam channel. The ground surface is partially covered with limestone cobbles and there is
little vegetation present in areas containing topsoil (see Figure 84).

214

Figure 95. Map of site 41KR667
The cultural material consists of a light surface scatter of chert debitage and one biface fragment with
these artifacts being spread over an area measuring 25 meters east-west by 20 meters north-south. No
burned rocks, bone, or other artifact categories are evident. In all, only about 10 artifacts were noted on
ground surface.
Shovel Test #32 was excavated on the east edge of the site where soil deposits appear to be thickest. It
failed to recover any artifacts and encountered solid limestone at 15 cm below ground surface. However,
given the topography, the site could continue further eastward outside of the transmission line ROW.

215

The creek channel along the west edge of the site has downcut about three meters into the canyon floor,
its east wall profile showing a thin soil cover over talus and bedrock. No artifacts were seen in the
cutbank.
This site contains a small amount of chipping debris and at least one tool that may represent a brief
episode of use as a knapping station at some unassigned period in the past, perhaps using material from a
nearby chert source. Since the portion of the site within the ROW corridor appears to have no potential
for useful further investigation, it is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not meriting
formal designation as an SAL.
41KR668
This prehistoric lithic scatter is located approximately 250 meters downstream from 41KR667 (see Figure
92). The site is situated on a small terrace in canyon bottomland on the east bank of a tributary channel to
Bear Creek (Figure 96). The terrace area has recently been cleared of cedar

Figure 96. Map of Site 41KR668.

216

trees and brush with several large piles of tree trunks and limbs present within and around the
transmission line corridor.
Several chert flakes and a thin biface fragment with a beveled edge were first seen on the western edge of
the transmission line ROW on an eroded terrace edge overlooking the creek channel. A close inspection
of the rising ground east of the creek channel found only a single flake. Shovel Test #33 was placed
about midway between this find and the location of proposed Transmission Structure #21. The shovel
test recovered no artifacts and struck limestone rubble at 15 cm below the surface.
Because of the thin soils shown in the shovel test and the churned nature of soils deposits in the cleared
field, no further shovel testing was deemed necessary. After further surface inspection, it was concluded

outside the transmission line ROW northwestward along the terrace paralleling the creek channel. No

features and diagnostic artifacts. The portion of the site within the transmission line ROW is assessed as
not eligible for NRHP or SAL designation.
41KR669
This prehistoric lithic scatter is situated on a terrace slope about 100 meters northeast of a tributary of
Bear Creek (Figure 97). The site consists of light scatter of chert cores, flakes, and tool fragments. These
materials cover a 40 meter length of the transmission line ROW. The artifacts were found across the 24
meter width of the ROW and the site should extend both northeast and southwest of the ROW. Most of
the artifacts were found on the western part of the site on the terrace edge overlooking the valley of the
tributary creek.
Ground surface visibility was variable within the site boundaries. Limestone bedrock and boulders were
visible in many cleared spots. Elsewhere, cleared spots on terrace slopes at the head of gully showed that
the overall depth of topsoil was in the range of 10 to 15 cm. A total of 10 chert flakes, 2 chert cores, and
1 projectile point fragment of the Ensor type were found.
The site has probably been damaged from previous land clearing and fence construction. The site appears
to have little depth of cultural deposits and no features were apparent. For these reasons, the site is
assessed as no meriting formal designation as an SAL and not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP.
Area C
This area covers the southeast end of the project area (Figure 98). A previously recorded burned rock
midden site, 41KR649, is located on the transmission line route in a high upland area. This area would
not have been predicted as a likely location for a site of this type. Given this fact, additional similar
topographic areas were added to the list of areas to be surveyed. Other high probability areas surveyed
consist of sections of several tributary creeks located in canyon bottomlands.

217

Figure 97. Map of site 41KR669.
41KR649
At the beginning of the transmission line project, LCRA learned from one of landowners, Mr. John
Redden, that an archaeological site was located on his property within the transmission line ROW. A
search of the Site Atlas indicated that site 41KR649 had been recorded in 2007 by Kay and Woody
Woodward and Bryant Saner on the Redden property. However, the plotting of the site on the maps at
TARL placed the site’s location far outside of the transmission line corridor on the neighboring property
rather than Mr. Redden’s property. The site was described as having two burned rock middens,
designated as BRM “A” and BRM “B”. The site area was described as covering an area of 78 meters on a
north-south line by 50 meters on an east-west line.

218

Figure 98. Survey Area C showing areas surveyed and locations of archaeological sites.

The LCRA archaeological staff then visited Mr. and Mrs. Redden at the start of the field survey. Mr.
Redden showed the field crew a copy of a brief report written by the Woodwards and Saner on site
41KR649. That report describes Mr. Redden’s surface collection from site 41KR649 as consisting of 11
Edwards arrowpoints, 2 Scallorn arrowpoints, 1 Edgewood dart point, and 1 Angostura-like dart point.
The field crew was also able to view these artifacts and verify their identifications at the Redden home.
Mr. Redden then guided the crew to the site, and the TARL sketch map was found to match to location of
two burned rock middens located in the vicinity of an angled turn in the transmission line ROW (Figure
99). It was determined that the site in the proposed transmission line ROW is indeed 41KR649, and that
the original plotting of the site at TARL is incorrect. It is possible that faulty GPS readings listed on the
original site form are the cause of the misplotting at TARL 600 meters further eastward.
Initial pedestrian survey by LCRA Archaeology Services staff at 41KR649 showed that ground surface
visibility was good in some areas due to previous brush clearing and because limestone bedrock was
exposed on ground surface in some areas. Other areas covered with thick grasses
had poor visibility. The walk-over indicated that although both burned rock midden features lay just
outside of the proposed transmission line ROW, a light scatter of chert chipping debris and
occasional burned rocks are present on ground surface within the transmission line ROW. Also, Mr.
Redden indicated that some of the arrowpoints in his collection had been found within the transmission
line ROW.

219

Figure 99. Map of site 41KR649 showing 10 meter (33 ft) wide avoidance area around burned rock
midden B. The site is located on the Redden property at the STR 18 angled turn in the transmission line.

220

Shovel Tests #1-4 and 6 were then excavated to better assess the potential for buried cultural deposits
within the ROW, especially in areas with poor ground surface visibility. All of these shovel tests showed
that only 15 to 22 cm of grayish-brown gravelly loam is present above limestone bedrock. Artifact yield
was very sparse with the highest numbers of artifacts coming from Shovel Test #2 where two chert flakes
and a burned rock fragment were recovered.
With the permission of the landowner the burned rock middens located outside the ROW corridor were
then examined more closely. Based a very slight rise in elevation and the presence of a surface scatter of
burned rocks, BRM"A" measures about 10 m north-south by 11 m east-west. The western edge of this
midden is situated just 2 meters outside of the transmission line corridor. It is located immediately north
of a dirt road in an area where brush was bulldozed, piled, and burned is past years. Parts of the midden
appear to have been gouged and pushed into piles by a bulldozer or front-end loader machine. Overall, it
appears that BRM “A” has been badly damaged by these activities. Shovel Test #8 was dug into the
approximate center of BRM "A". This shovel test showed a 9 cm thick surface accumulation of burned
wood and blackened dirt that apparently is a bulldozed pile of recently burned brush. Deeper, a 24 cm
thick deposit of burned rocks and chert debitage was found. These artifacts consisted of about 25 burned
rock fragments, 3 chert chips, and 1 chert chunk, and 3 pieces of burned chert shatter.
Shovel Test #7 was dug just seven meters southeast of Shovel Test #8 at a location just outside of the
boundary of BRM “A”. It produced 3 chert flakes but only 6 burned rock fragments.
BRM"B" is situated on a higher terrace surface about 50 to 60 meters south of BRM"A". The north edge
of BRM"B" lies just 2 meters south of the outer edge of the transmission line ROW. Although there are
several holes in this midden from either previous uprooting of trees or from pothunter activities, this
midden does not appear to have been damaged from bulldozing and other brush clearing activities.
BRM"B" is about 12 meters in diameter, and Shovel Test #5 which was placed in the northern part of the
burned rock accumulation showed a 15 cm thick soil above limestone bedrock. Much of the soil matrix
was a black-colored midden zone. Four chert flakes and two chert chips were recovered. Importantly,
Shovel Test #5 produced a much higher density of burned rocks than the shovel test in BRM “A”.
Overall, the shovel testing indicated that artifact density within the two midden features is much higher
than in areas outside of the midden features, even in locations just beyond the edges of the middens. A
similar trend was observed during the survey and shovel testing at site 41KR663 where shovel tests
outside of the burned rock midden proper, likewise, revealed hardly any artifacts. It appears, then, that
the immediate burned rock midden feature areas at site 41KR649 are the principal areas of concern. Both
burned rock middens are situated just outside of the transmission line ROW, and BRM “A” has been
damaged by previous tree and brush clearing, piling, and burning to the extent that the integrity of
deposits is low. BRM “B” appears to be more intact, has a higher burned rock density, and has a
definable, dark midden zone. Thus, BRM “B” is assessed as potentially NR and SAL eligible. Although
shovel testing in areas within the transmission line ROW adjacent to BRM “B” showed no evidence of
features and yielded few artifacts, additional caution seems prudent. A 10-meter wide buffer zone that
extends into the transmission line ROW is recommended around the northern half of the BRM “B”
feature to ensure that activities related to construction of the transmission line do not damage the feature.
Orange plastic fencing will be erected to mark the buffer zone boundaries. No ground-disturbing
activities or vehicular traffic should be allowed within the buffer zone. Any vegetation removal within the
buffer zone will need to be accomplished without damaging subsurface deposits. Aside from the
restrictions involving the buffer zone, it is recommended that construction be allowed to proceed within
other parts of 41KR649.

221

41KR665
Site 41KR665 is a prehistoric lithic scatter and lithic procurement area located on an upland ridge
between the canyon heads of two tributaries of Camp Meeting Creek (Figure 100). Cultural material
noted on the surface during the survey included approximately a dozen pieces of chert debitage, one Stage
2 biface, and one unifacial tool. These artifacts were spread across a 220 meter length of the transmission
line ROW without any particular area of concentration. Artifact density is extremely low, being
somewhere on the order of one artifact per 650 m2. The low

Figure 100. Map of site 41KR665

222

artifact density can be partly explained by the fact that limestone outcrops over a considerable area of the
site and artifacts were mainly observed in areas with soil cover. The soil is no more than a few
centimeters thick above the limestone bedrock, and according to the landowner, not long before the
survey was conducted, the entire site area was cleared of dense woody vegetation by bulldozer, leaving
dislodged boulders and burned brush piles. The lack of depth of soil and the previous disturbances made
shovel testing at this site unnecessary.
With the permission of the landowner, who was present with the survey crew, additional areas up to 40
meters beyond the existing width of the ROW corridor were surveyed to determine if any denser artifact
clusters or features were present. Additional occasional chert flakes were seen, but no artifact
concentrations or features were found. The site probably extends southwards to the canyon edge and
northwards for some undetermined distance.
The chert debitage and biface within the transmission ROW probably represent the leftovers of lithic
procurement activities and early stage biface reduction that took place where chert nodules were exposed
on the surface along the canyon edge. These chert deposits are not substantial and the cultural material
appears to reflect infrequent and light use as a lithic source. This site has little reach potential because of
the lack of discrete components or activity areas and is not considered eligible for NRHP or SAL
designation.

Summary and Recommendations
During the period from May through July 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted an
intensive cultural resource survey of the Rim Rock to Jack Furman transmission line project.
Approximately 3.6 linear miles of 6.6 miles of new transmission line corridor was surveyed as was a four
acre electrical substation tract located near the north end of the proposed transmission line corridor. As a
result of the investigation, 10 new prehistoric archaeological sites, 41KR662-41KR671were recorded and
assessed. One previously recorded prehistoric site, 41KR649, was also revisited and assessed. The sites
can be classified as three burned rock midden sites (41KR649, 41KR663, and 41KR666), six lithic
scatters (41KR664 and 41KR667-41KR671), and two lithic scatter/lithic procurement sites (41KR662
and 41KR665).
Although three sites are classified as burned rock middens, site 41KR663 is only one having a burned
rock midden feature within the actual project construction area. The midden feature at 41KR663 appears
to have been heavily damaged by previous cultivation and land clearing. The vast majority of artifacts
were found within the upper 10 cm of soil deposits in disturbed contexts. No other features were found
and the only diagnostic artifact noted is a Late Archaic dart point. Because of the extensive damage from
previous cultivation, the portion of the site that will be affected by the transmission construction is
assessed as not NRHP or SAL eligible.
At 41KR666, the actual burned rock midden feature was noted about 11 meters east of the outside edge of
the transmission line corridor. It will not be affected by proposed construction. Cultural materials within
the transmission corridor at 41KR666 are mainly surficial and probably temporally mixed. An
approximately 5 meter wide linear strip within the east edge of the transmission line corridor is a
previously disturbed existing utility line corridor. The portion of site 41KR666 within the transmission
line ROW is also assessed as not NRHP or SAL eligible.
A third burned rock midden site, 41KR649, has two burned rock midden features that lay just outside of
the transmission line corridor. These are shallow 15 to 25 cm thick middens, and the landowner’s artifact
collection suggests that they are of Late Prehistoric age. Cultural deposits within the actual transmission

223

line corridor at this site are sparse and deposits are shallow. The northern midden, which is called BRM
“A”, has been heavily damaged by brush clearing, piling, and burning to the extent that further
investigations would not be fruitful. However, potentially significant cultural deposits that may be NRHP
and SAL eligible are present at 41KR649’s southern midden, which has been designated as BRM “B”. A
10-meter wide buffer zone has been recommended around the northern perimeter of BRM “B” as a
precaution to ensure that the feature is not damaged by project activities including vegetation clearing and
vehicular traffic associated with the construction of the transmission line and a nearby transmission tower.
Aside from this buffer zone area, it is recommended that all other construction be allowed to proceed at
41KR649.
Six sites consisting of 41KR664, and 41KR667-41KR671 are prehistoric lithic scatters. Artifacts at these
sites lack diversity and are sparse in numbers. Other than single Ensor type dart points found at site
41KR664 and 41KR669, these sites lack diagnostic artifacts. No features were seen at any of these sites,
and soil deposits are consistently shallow. For all of these reasons, the portions of these sites within the
transmission line corridor are assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP and not meriting formal
designation as SALs.
The last two sites, 41KR662 and 41KR665 are lithic scatter/lithic procurement sites. In addition to chert
chipping debris and early stage tools, these sites include chert cores and tested chert cobbles obtained
from chert gravels present at the sites. However, artifact density at these sites is low and cultural deposits
are surficial. Both sites have been previously damaged by land clearing. The portions of both of these
sites within the project area are assessed as not NRHP or SAL eligible.
Aside from the need for the buffer zone around BRM “B” feature at site 41KR649, it is recommended all
other project construction be allowed to proceed, as currently planned. As always, standard emergency
discovery provisions are applicable to this project. Should the construction personnel uncover any
presently unknown buried archeological features during construction work, then work should cease in the
immediate area of the discovery and the LCRA Archeology Services staff should be notified at 1-800776-5272, ext. 6714 so that the find can be assessed in consultation with the THC. In such a situation,
work can continue in other areas where no archeological materials are present.
