Intensive Cultural Resources Survey of LCRA’s Proposed T-186, Plum to Winchester,
Transmission Line Rebuild Project, Fayette County, Texas.

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit # 4779
Interim Report No. 8.08
Parks and Resource Conservation
Lower Colorado River Authority, Austin
May, 2008

229

Introduction and Management Summary
LCRA proposes to rebuild the existing T-186 transmission line that runs for approximately 7.2 mi within
a 100-ft easement between the Plum and Winchester substations in western Fayette County, Texas
(Figure 101). A portion of the line that runs between the Plum substation and the new Piney Creek
switching station will be renamed T-535. The line crosses the Colorado River about 1.6 km north of the
Plum substation. Total project acreage is about 87.3 acres. The project will consist of removing existing
wooden H-frame and metal lattice structures and installing new metal or concrete single pole structures
that will be reconductored with higher capacity wire. In some cases the new structures will be placed at
or very near the exact locations of the present structures, but the majority will be in new locations.
The transmission line was surveyed by LCRA Archaeology Services staff in 2004, prior to regularly
scheduled ROW maintenance activities. This appears to have been the first cultural resources survey of
the line. Three previously unrecorded prehistoric archaeological sites, 41FY540-41FY542, were recorded
at that time. Site 41FY540 was a buried lithic scatter located on the second or third terrace south of the
Colorado River. Site 41FY541 was an open camp located on a high ridge overlooking the floodplain
terrace of the river to the south. Site 41FY542 was a lithic procurement site, really more of a locality,
located on ancient terraces towards the north end of the project area. Sites 41FY540 and 41FY541 were
both considered to be documented at a level that precluded assessments of eligibility for state or federal
registers or lists. Site 41F542 was considered to be fully documented and was considered ineligible for
formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic
Places.
One new archaeological site, 41FY551, was recorded during the present project, which was conducted by
LCRA Archaeology Services staff on May 12 and 13, 2008. This site is an ephemeral historic trash
scatter consisting of both clear and solarized glass, a few square nails, and a metal bar. The site is
considered to be fully documented and ineligible for formal state or federal listings.
LCRA conducts archaeological investigations for transmission line maintenance at a level commensurate
with expected impacts, which typically are low. Site eligibility recommendations are not typically
definitive, as site avoidance is usually easily accomplished.
For T-186, the initial survey created a set of expectations regarding the nature of soils along the
transmission line route. Shovel testing in the alluvial terraces and floodplains indicated that soils and/or
sediments were in excess of 1 m in depth in many places. After reviewing geologic maps and soil
surveys, it was decided to use mechanical augering to systematically sample soils at new structure
locations. A 30-cm diameter skid steer-mounted auger was able to quickly sample at depths of up to 1.7
m. When necessary, additional vertical control was achieved by placing hand-excavated shovel tests in
areas of interest.
A total of 20 auger tests was excavated. Additionally, six shovel tests were added to the 24 excavated
during the maintenance survey, for a combined number of 50 subsurface tests. This averages to about
seven tests per mile, well below suggested minimum survey standards. However, approximately 4.2 mi
of the line was considered to be of low probability for containing buried cultural materials, and the
emphasis on the floodplain is considered to be justified, where the subsurface testing rate averaged
approximately 17 tests per mile, which meets suggested minimum survey standards.
All four recorded archaeological sites, 41FY540-542, and 41FY551, are considered to be fully
documented within the portions of the project area that would directly impact them. No additional work
is considered necessary within the recorded portions of these sites.

230

Figure 101. Project area and site locations in Fayette County, Warda, West Point and Winchester USGS
quads.

231

The results of augering, however, indicated that there are portions of the northern terrace/floodplain that
may have the potential for archaeological deposits at depths greater than what can be seen by standard
testing methods. Monitoring of selected structure excavation is recommended.
No artifacts were collected during this project, and no curation will be necessary.

Environmental Background
The project area is located between the towns of Smithville and La Grange in Fayette County, and runs
northward from very near the community of Plum after which it crosses the Colorado River and continues
northward to the community of Winchester. It is within the broad Coastal Plain region of Texas, and
more specifically within a portion of the Post Oak, Mesquite and Grassland belt that is dominated by
sandy and clayey soils over sediments (Woodruff 1979). At the Plum substation the project is within late
Pleistocene fluviatile terrace deposits, but quickly drops into Holocene-aged alluvium within the
Colorado River valley. Proceeding northward it climbs out of the 2.4 mi-wide Holocene-aged valley,
once again crossing old terrace deposits before entering the much earlier Eocene-aged Yegua formation,
which is primarily sandstone, clay and lignite, with chert and quartz cobbles not uncommon (Barnes
1974). This last formation accounts for about the northern one-third of the project area. In addition to the
Colorado River, the project crosses or comes very near Criswell Creek at its junction with the river, an
abandoned channel (oxbow) of the river, Spring Branch, which feeds the oxbow, and Shiloh Branch,
which along with Millers Creek feed the Colorado River further downstream.
Additional research on the lower Colorado River portion of the Gulf Coastal plain (Blum and Valastro
1994) produced a data set for late Quaternary sedimentation rates and preliminary dates for a series of
recognized allostratigraphic units (Figure 102) consisting of a number of named allomembers or
formations, which in the study area consist of, from oldest to youngest: tertiary bedrock or older
Pleistocene; the Montopolis Terrace; Eagle Lake Alloformation (ELA); Columbus Bend 1 and 2 (CB 1
and 2); and Columbus Bend 3 (CB 3). A series of dates were obtained from buried soils and organic
materials, including from near West Point, where the CB 3 was as recent as 250 years old (and dated
generally elsewhere as no more than 600 years old), the CB 2 (undifferentiated from CB 1 on
distributional figures) at about 5,000 to 1,000 years old, the CB 1 developing during very the end of the
Pleistocene and into the middle part f the Holocene, and the ELA forming between about 18,000 and
10,000 years ago. The Montopolis terrace predated the study period, and along with the bedrock
members was included largely for reference purposes. Despite the problems associated with the dating
methods (Frederick 2002; Blum 2008, personal communication), the study of the formations creates at
minimum a relative sequence of deposition, and until a better set of time markers can be determined,
provides a baseline geomorphological chronology that aids in shaping research goals and objectives.

Cultural Background
The prehistory of Central Texas has recently been reviewed by several archeologists including Johnson
(1994) and Collins (1995). These papers build upon the previous work of Weir (1976) and Prewitt (1981,
1985) that established a detailed cultural chronology and cultural history for the region. Importantly,
Johnson’s paper includes new data on past climates in Central Texas while Collins discusses past and
present research theories and trends in prehistoric archeological research in Central Texas. The reader is
referred to these works for in-depth discussions of Central Texas prehistory.

232

Figure 102. Blum and Valastro’s (1994) alloforms overlaid on geologic atlas map.

233

Prehistoric site types in Central Texas consist of camps, caches, isolated artifacts, interments, cemeteries,
kill/butcher locales, quarry/workshops, lithic scatters, and rock art sites (Collins 1995:363). Central
Texas is perhaps best known for the many burned rock midden sites that occur on the Edwards Plateau.
Numerous excavations of major campsites have been conducted along the larger streams and rivers in
nearby areas. Such excavations have demonstrated that Central Texas was occupied for at least 11,500
years prior to the coming of Europeans. These also show that throughout these millennia, prehistoric
peoples were nomadic hunter-gatherers who moved across the landscape exploiting seasonally available
plant and animal resources.
The region now known as Fayette County was first crossed by the Spanish in the early eighteenth century
(Garrett 2001). Stephen F. Austin’s colonists arrived in 1822 and initiated settlement, particularly near an
important road crossing located near present day La Grange. In 1837 the county of Fayette was
established, named after the Marquis de Lafayette, with La Grange made the county seat. Early industry
was based on farms and plantations, which focused on production of corn, tobacco, wool and cotton.
Slavery became a mainstay of the economy, even as the county voted against secession. After the civil
war the plantation economy collapsed, and share cropping became common. German and Slavic
immigrants increased during the decades surrounding the 1870s, and former plantation owners sold large
plots which were subsequently subdivided to newcomers. Germans and Czechs became strong social
forces around the turn of the century. Cotton was the predominant cash crop through the Depression, but
by the end of World War II cattle became more important. Oil was discovered in the county in 1943,
which led to a new shift in industry. The LCRA’s coal-fired Fayette Power Project is now the largest
employer in the county.
The communities of Plum and West Point, south of the river, and Winchester, north of the river, were all
settled between 1831 and 1888 (Carroll 2002 a, b, c). Plum was originally known as Plum Grove, and
was the second oldest community in Fayette County, and the Plum Church now Hopewell, was the first
Baptist church west of the Colorado River (Carroll 2002a.). One of the original settlers of the West Point
area apparently established a fort in the vicinity, although the actual community was formed around the
junction of three railroads (Carroll 2002 b). Winchester was also at least partially dependent on railroads,
and served as a shipping point. These town have all seen population declines over the past several
decades, but as of 2000 maintained a relatively stable combined population of about 350 persons.
Previous Investigations

The line was previously surveyed by LCRA Archaeology Services Staff in 2004 prior to standard
maintenance and clearing activities. Three prehistoric sites, 41FY540 – 41FY452 were recorded at that
time (Figures 103, 104, 105). Sites 41FY540, a buried lithic scatter, and 41FY541, a high terrace open
camp, were both considered potentially eligible for formal listings. Site 41FY542, described as more a
locale than a site, was a widely distributed lithic procurement zone located in surficial, upland gravels.
The Texas Historic Sites Atlas indicates that a number of sites have been recorded in the general vicinity,
many of these in association with construction or expansion of SH 71. The vast majority of these sites are
described as open camps, and they are generally located along the edge of the Pleistocene ridge
overlooking the Colorado River valley to the north.
Less work has been conducted north of the river, but the general pattern of occupation sites outside the
Colorado River floodplain appears to hold. A series of open camps within the floodplain of Rabbs Creek
initially recorded by TxDOT personnel could not be relocated during later investigations prompted by a

234

Figure 103. Site 41FY540 with all tests from 2004 and 2008.

235

Figure 104. Site 41FY541 with all tests from 2004 and 2008.

236

Figure 105. Site 41FY542.

237

widening of FM 153. At least one site, 41FY53, produced an apparent Clovis point from a depth of about
1 m.
Other large survey projects in the general area include LCRA’s Fayette Power Project (FPP), the
Cummins Creek prospect, and work at Lake Bastrop and Camp Swift. Numerous historic and prehistoric
sites have been recorded as a result of these projects.

Research Design
The archaeological investigations were structured so as to relocate previously recorded archaeological
sites, identify any previously undiscovered sites, and to assess their research or other potential as
qualifying them for formal listing as State Archeological Landmarks or nomination to the National
Register of Historic Places. Data recovered during cultural resource investigations by LCRA
Archaeology Services in 2004 was combined with information from soils and geologic maps to determine
areas where there was a higher probability for buried prehistoric sites. Appropriate historic maps were
referenced in order to determine the potential for the presence of historic resources.
The previous subsurface investigations indicated that the majority of the approximate 2.4 mi wide
Colorado River floodplain had soils and/or sediments that were deeper than standard shovel testing
methods could penetrate. A skid steer mounted auger was therefore used to efficiently test at depths of up
to 170 cm across this floodplain (Figures 106, 107). Tests were placed at or very near planned new
structure locations. When necessary, hand excavated shovel tests were placed adjacent to auger tests in
order to improve vertical control. All matrix from auger and shovel tests was screened through ¼ in wire
mesh. In addition, geotechnical bores were monitored at each of three locations within the lower
floodplain terraces, which provided gross-level information on deposits at depth of up to 30 ft. Other
moderate probability land forms were examined with standard pedestrian survey and shovel testing.
Proposed new structures were located with coordinate information uploaded into a GPS unit. Aerial
imagery, modern USGS maps, and georeferenced historic USGS maps were used for ground orientation.
A no collection policy was maintained throughout the project.
Results of Investigations
2004 – 41FY540, 41FY541, 41F542
The 2004 survey was initiated near the Plum substation. The high probability area adjacent to the
Colorado River was the focus of the investigations in this area. A total of seven shovel tests was
excavated across a 600 m distance beginning at a small tributary creek and proceeding southwards. Six
shovel tests were placed at approximately 50 m intervals, and a seventh was placed approximately 200 m
further south from these. The tests were designed to sample the soils and determine if buried
archaeological deposits were present. There were no artifacts visible on the ground surface. These seven
tests found soils that were sandy loams and sandy clay loams grading to clay loams and loamy clays at
depths of 70 to 100 cm or more. Three of these tests, numbers 3, 5 and 6, found sparse to very sparse
lithic materials at depths of 40-80 cm, with the majority apparently present at approximately 60 cm.
Materials were found both above and below a contour break of approximately 10 ft that signals a shift
between the third and fourth terraces above the Colorado River. The site (41FY540) is interpreted as a
buried lithic scatter indicative of tool production or maintenance. Some of the material was burned,
suggesting that food processing or camping activities were conducted here as well. The location along
the river supports this contention. Although the materials were very sparse (a total of seven pieces from

238

Figure 106. Location of all subsurface tests south of the Colorado River. The two green symbols are
geotechnical bores.

239

Figure 107. Location of all floodplain and terrace subsurface tests north of the Colorado River. The
green symbol is a geotechnical bore.

240

three shovel tests), it is possible that additional buried deposits are present within or near the transmission
line corridor.
Four shovel tests, numbers 8 - 11, were excavated on the north side of the Colorado River. These tests
were excavated in very deep sands and sandy loams. Two tests were positive for cultural materials,
including relatively dense burned rock and lithic materials. These were immediately adjacent to a
transmission structure (STR 7/1). All material was less than 30 cm in depth, and some was visible across
the surface. One test found three large bones at a depth of 30 cm. No cultural material was associated at
this elevation, although a flake tool was recovered from above 20 cm. The cultural materials that were
present were suggestive of an intensively utilized open campsite. However, the materials were very
localized in both horizontal and vertical extents. It was determined the most likely explanation for these
materials was that they had been imported as fill material or road base either during transmission line
construction or by the landowner. This conclusion is supported by two nearby gas pipeline access points
where bare ground that had been churned by drilling activities was closely examined for cultural
materials. None was noted.
The next area considered to be of high probability for cultural materials was adjacent to an oxbow lake
located 2 km north of the Colorado River. Seven shovel tests, numbers 12 – 18, were excavated in this
area. The first (ST 13) was placed immediately below (south of) a ridge that runs E/W across the north
edge of the oxbow lake and continues westward to the present Colorado River. This ridge probably
represents an ancestral bank of the Colorado River. This shovel test was negative. The ridge is
approximately 30 ft higher than the valley floor. On top of this ridge accumulations of burned chert and
quartzite, chert debitage and stone tools were apparent across the land surface. Six shovel tests found
reddish-brown sandy loams at an average thickness of 50 cm over reddish brown and red sandy clays.
Cultural materials are moderate to dense and included burned rock, chert debitage, and at least one
projectile point base, which was most likely Late Archaic (4000-1200 years old) in age. Another
untypeable tool was found in the same test. On the surface was found an apparent Guadalupe tool or
adze. This is suggestive of Early Archaic (ca 6000-8000 years old) occupations. The shovel tests
indicated that cultural material extended from the edge of the remnant riverbank northwards for a distance
of at least 300 m, although material dropped in density towards the north. Another shovel test (ST 20)
was placed on the same elevation at the north end of the large open pasture. This shovel test also found a
small amount of cultural material. So although there is a gap of approximately 450 m between ST20 and
ST17, the final test on the south end of the pasture, the site is considered to extend across the entire
pasture. A shovel test placed just south of Shiloh Branch found dense gray clays and no cultural
materials. The site is therefore not considered to continue past the contour break at the north end of the
pasture.
This site (41FY541) is interpreted as an open campsite which was most likely along the bank of the active
Colorado River when inhabited. Burned rock and functional and time diagnostic tools are present. The
site was therefore felt to contain potential for additional information yield, and as such, should have
impacts minimized during maintenance activities.
An attempt was made to access the north bank of Spring Branch. An extremely rough pasture ended at an
artificially enhanced drainage that forms an apparent slough. The north bank of Spring Branch was
therefore not examined. Topographically, it appears the Spring Branch may also represent an ancestral
channel of the Colorado River. If this is the case, and an assumption (probably correct) is made that the
river has been migrating southward, then the possibility exists that the north bank of the creek may
contain cultural materials similar to, and most likely older than, those found at 41FY540.
The area on either side of Shiloh Branch was investigated. Within the transmission line corridor there
was excellent visibility within the deeply exposed cutbanks of the creek. Several other nearby exposures

241

were also examined. There was no evidence of cultural materials. Due to the high level of visibility no
shovel tests were excavated. There were no maintenance-related recommendations for this portion of the
transmission line.
Beginning at FM 153 approximately 1.8 mi of transmission line was alternately walked or subjected to
windshield survey. This upland area consisted of approximately 2000 linear m of dense gravels within
shallow soils and clays which gave way towards the north to relatively shallow sands over gravels and
clay. Within this area was seen occasional evidence of lithic procurement activity consisting of widely
scattered large flakes and tested cobbles. This is really more of a locale than a true archaeological site, as
there are no activity loci or any evidence of activities other than random and informal raw material
processing. This site was designated as 41FY542. There were no maintenance related issues for this
section of the transmission line.
The final 1300 m was considered to be of low probability for cultural resource sites. The setting was
generally similar to that seen within the surveyed uplands to the south. This final section was not
surveyed, and there were no recommendation regarding clearing activities.
2008
Geotechnical Monitoring
The first phase of the 2008 investigations consisted of monitoring geotechnical borings that were
scheduled for placement within the Holocene floodplain. These 10 cm diameter bores were excavated to
depths of 30-50 ft. The first ten feet are sampled at 2 ft intervals, and the remainder of the column is
sampled as the last two feet of every 5 ft interval (i.e. 13-15 ft, 18-20 ft, etc.). Monitoring results in gross
level soils information, which is helpful on the deep river terraces, but adds no relevant information on
shallow upland surfaces, where geotechnical bores were not monitored.
The first bore was immediately north of the Plum substation, very near the juncture of the late Pleistocene
fluviatile terrace deposits and the more recent river valley fill. It is just south of the mapped edge of
Blum’s Eagle Lake alloformation (ELA), although widely varying map scales might well be adjusted to
include this location. Soils are mapped as Smithville series, with a 16 in A horizon, a B horizon that
grades to clays and carbonates at 30-50 in, and a BCk horizon below 50 inches. The bore encountered 2
ft of yellowish brown sandy loam over varying clays to a depth of 6 ft, followed by varying degrees of
sand, gravels and clays, with the water table at about 28 ft.
The second bore was located at the last structure south of the river (Figure 108), within Blum’s Columbus
Bend 3 (CB 3) alloformation. Soils are mapped as Coarsewood silt loams, which have a B horizon
described to a depth of 48 in, and two successive C horizons to depths of 80 in. These soils are notable
for buried A horizons consisting of silt loams or silty clay loams in the upper 60 in. The bore produced 6
ft of yellowish brown silty or fine silty loams, and a brown clayey loam was encountered at about 5 ft.
This was interpreted as a possible buried soil, although various past disturbances (animals, roots etc) may
have created it as well. Below 6 ft the silts graded to clays and then mottled clays, with the water table
again encountered at about 28 ft.
The final bore that was monitored was located about midway across the northern river terrace within
Blum’s Columbus Bend 1 and 2 alloformation, in an area with mapped Gad series soils. These are
described as having formed in “Recent” sandy alluvium. They have a shallow, 11 in A horizon which
rests immediately upon a C1 and C2, at a final depth of 80 in. The bore encountered 2 ft of brown silty
loam followed by 4 ft of dark brown clay loam, then brown clay over light brown silty loam, then brown
silty and sandy clays before encountering wet sand, clayey sand and pebbles below 15 ft. The monitoring
ended at 25 ft, prior to encountering the water table.

242

Figure 108. Geotechnical investigation near Structure 45, just south of Colorado River.

Mechanical Augering
A total of 20 auger tests was excavated. Thirteen were north of the river, and seven south, all within the
“bedrock-confined” river valley (Blum and Valastro 1994), and all but the southernmost two within
Blum’s ELA, CBA 3 and CBA 1-2 (from south to north). Auger locations were determined by proposed
structure coordinate data uploaded into a GPS receiver, which was used to navigate to the correct
locations. Additional auger tests and/or shovel tests were excavated at locations where recovery was
ambiguous or additional information was felt to be necessary. Auger results are discussed in varying
detail below; a full list of auger data is included as an appendix.
Augering commenced (Auger Test [AT] 1) at the first structure north of the river, the location of shovel
tests (STs) 9-11 in 2004. This was also the location of the third geotechnical bore, and it was here that a
small amount of cultural materials was interpreted as being the result of introduced fill. The auger
recovered one possible flake fragment in the first 40 cm, and one tertiary flake in the second. The profile
was generally a yellowish brown sandy or fine sandy loam, although an 8 cm thick zone of grayish-brown
sandy loam was encountered between 30 and 38 cm. Below 90 cm a light tan sand was encountered. The
Gad series soils are consistent with this description, except for the buried soil lens. The combined
information suggests that the bottom of recent sediments has not been thoroughly explored here, and
despite the ambiguity of the cultural material, monitoring of construction activities is warranted.
AT 2 encountered large carbonate nodules at 80 cm, and this location is considered fully explored. AT 3,
however, did not demonstrably breach Holocene sediments, and is within the Coarsewood series soils that
may contain buried A horizons. Monitoring of construction activities is warranted. These first three
augers were within Blum’s recent CB 3 alloformation.

243

AT 4, located in Gad soils, encountered grayish-brown mottled clay below 80 cm. This was also the first
auger within Blum’s CB 1-2 alloformation (all the rest on the north side of the river were also in this
member). This area is considered fully explored.
AT 5 had very distinctive recovery, which prompted it to be bracketed by ATs 6 and 7. Over 1 m of
yellowish-brown fine sandy loam was followed abruptly by, and perhaps rested unconformably on, at
least 40 cm of yellowish-brown silty loam that was notable for very high quantities of land snails, and
even more remarkably, numerous pieces of charcoal, some exceeding 1-2 cm in diameter. AT 6, about 20
m to the south, had a similar profile, while AT 7, about 15 m north, had no snail or charcoal, and below
150 cm, a red, mottled and probably gleyed clay was encountered. AT 8, 265 m north at the next
structure location, likewise had some small amounts of charcoal below 90 cm. The recent age of the
mapped Gad soils do not appear to correlate well with the earlier CB 1-2 formation, and it is possible that
the more recent soils lap over the earlier formation, which would then be assumed to contain the snail and
charcoal. It may also be that a highly active floodplain laid drift deposits carrying organic material
including snail and charcoal, and later covered these with additional sediments.
While excavating ATs 5-7, we were approached by the landowner’s representative or ranch foreman.
When asked about flooding, he stated that the entirety of the terrace, other than a few high points, had
been underwater in both the 1991 and 1998 floods. He further stated that during the 1998 floods, a
channel some 15 ft deep opened across the field (probably where a low contour can be seen on the USGS
map, and that the scoured sediment accumulated around the base of an oil pump pad. They replaced the
soil within the erosional feature by bulldozer, and they have kept the fields planted in Coastal Bermuda,
rather than corn or other crops, since. A geodatabase of high water marks from 1998 shows an elevation
of 274.3 ft, taken just south of the angle structure, which illustrates that the majority of the terrace on the
north side of the river was indeed inundated at that time. South of the river, however, a long ridge
containing most of the transmission line route would have remained above water, with only the final 200
m or so being covered by water.
This consultant (informant), also stated that his father used to fish in the oxbow as a child, and indicated
that it had been the actual river at that time. He appeared to be in his 60s or 70s, and placed a date of
approximately 80 years ago on the story. Turn-of-the-century maps do not support this, and show an
apparently well entrenched river at essentially the present location. These same maps do indicate that the
oxbow flowed at least intermittently into the Colorado River. So, although it is remotely possible the
river had an extreme channel migration just prior to about 1900, it appears to be highly unlikely.
Although no artifacts were recovered in any of these four auger locations, construction monitoring at the
associated structures is warranted.
AT 9 had alternating zones of yellowish-brown fine sandy loam and grayish brown clay loams. Some
fairly substantial bone fragments were recovered at the contact between the upper 36 cm of yellowishbrown loam and grayish-brown loam. ST 25 was placed within a meter of this auger, and excavated to a
depth of 50 cm, through the grayish-brown zone. No more bone or any artifacts were recovered. Despite
this, there is no conclusive evidence the pre-Holocene deposits were encountered, and construction
monitoring is warranted.
AT 10 is at the last structure location on the lower floodplain. Its profile consisted of yellowish-brown
fine sandy loam to 70 cm over grayish-brown clayey loam. Construction monitoring should be
considered here as well.
ATs 11, 12, and 13 were placed on the broad pasture between the angle structure marking the southern
edge of 41FY541 and Spring Branch. Previous (2004) shovel tests found a light scatter of buried

244

chipping debris in this portion of the site although shovel testing indicated soils might be deep in places.
The auger tests all encountered red clays or heavily carbonated reddish silts between 55 and 80 cm.
There was some limited recovery of chert debitage, but nothing to suggest that any additional wok is
warranted in this area.
The remainder of the auger tests was placed south of the river. The first two, ATs 14 and 15, were within
previously identified site 41FY540, a light scatter of buried lithic debitage. Recovery was consistent with
previous findings, and consisted of a few flakes and flake fragments, some burned, as well as a small (3
cm) flake tool, or edge modified flake. The profile consisted of brown to yellowish-brown fine sandy
loam, and in AT 4, carbonate nodules below 80 cm. The combination of carbonate nodules and limited
recovery indicate research potential for the portion of the site within the ROW is low. No additional work
is considered necessary.
AT 16 was placed near the last structure south of the river, near the location of the second geotechnical
bore. Although there was no recovery of artifacts, it cannot be demonstrated that pre-Holocene deposits
were encountered, and hints of a buried soil horizon in the geotechnical bore warrant monitoring of
construction at this location.
The remaining four ATs, 17-20, were placed between 41FY540 and the Plum substation. All tests
encountered color, texture, or carbonate frequencies at depths below 65 cm or more, indicating preHolocene deposits. No artifacts were encountered.
Other Areas
An angle structure is currently located on the edge of the ridge overlooking the Colorado River valley
next to the oxbow channel. The structure consists of three wooden poles and numerous guy wires. It will
be replaced with a single cement or metal structure which will require a foundation excavation 8-10 ft in
diameter. This is the highest artifact density portion of 41FY541. Burned rock (quartzite and chert)
debitage, small amounts of mussel shell, bone of unknown origin, and at least three tools – a gouge, a dart
point base, and a thin biface/knife – have been seen or recovered here. A total of six shovel tests have
been excavated in this portion of the site, including one, ST 27, within or very near the footprint of the
proposed new foundation. These tests all show that soils are relatively shallow – no more than 50 cm on
average – before encountering reddish sandy clays (Figure 109). Although artifact recovery is moderately
high, it appears compressed and is almost certainly mixed, at least at the location of the structure. No
additional work is considered necessary at this location.
STs 22-24 were placed on the broad divide north of Spring Branch, on the broad divide which previously
had been unapproachable. These tests, (more accurately described as probes), all encountered sandy or
silty loams over reddish-brown loams or clays. No cultural material was seen in any of them, and closer
examination of the land form suggested it was of low probability for containing substantial prehistoric
materials.
The Shiloh Branch area was revisited. This is the location of 41FY551 (Figure 110). The site consists of
a sparse scatter of glass, nails, and a metal bar. All materials were present on the ground surface between
an old barbed fence and a new chainlink high game fence. The two fences run parallel on an east-west
line with an approximately 30 cm gap between the fences. There is good ground surface visibility in the
small gap between the two fences suggesting that some sort of herbicide may have been applied to the
ground during the construction of the newer fence. Ten of the 20 pieces of glass are solarized glass of
purple to pinkish color. One of four brown colored glass fragments is a jar base appears to be a snuff jar
of a type that was still being utilized as recently as the late 1960s. Three other glass fragments are clear
transparent glass. Three square metal nails were also observed. These may be nails from an older version

245

of the barbed wire fence or may have been trash dump material like the glass. The last observed artifact
is a corroded metal bar of about 12 inches length.
The area immediately north of the fences has very good ground surface visibility due to several cattle
trails, gullies, and eroded slopes that descend to the Shiloh Branch channel. Despite an intensive search
of these areas, no additional artifacts were found. The area south of the fences is overgrown with grasses
and weeds. A shovel test (ST 30) was placed in this area about 1.5 meters south of the fences. No
artifacts were recovered in the shovel test. Surface inspection further southward on a terrace knoll
showed reasonably good ground surface visibility, but no artifacts or evidences of a former house site
were seen.

Figure 109. Exposure at 41FY541, outside of ROW. Artifacts rest on or in the light zone about 30 cm
below ground surface. Oxbow is behind distribution pole in background.
The site is interpreted as a trash dump that may be a single event with a mixture of late 19th to early 20th
century artifacts. The presence of the square nails may be fortuitious - i. e. these are nails used for a
former boundary fence. The site could extend westward outside of the transmission line ROW. Overall,
the portion of the site within the transmission line ROW is assessed as not significant.
There appeared to be the possibility for historic sites to be located in the Winchester area, based on the
georeferenced Bastrop USGS quad from 1899. Various map discrepancies are apparent, however, and the
1948 USGS quad for the same area clearly shows the line does not cross the same set of structures from
the 1899 map (Figure 111).
The only other portion of the line suspected to possibly contain historic sites was at 41FY541. Between
at the angle structure and Spring Branch a minimum of three structures were present near the alignment.
The only artifacts present were the small amounts of bottle glass in shovel test associated with 41FY541,
and a single square nail seen in the dirt road near the angle structure.

Figure 110. Site 41FY551.

Figure 111. Project area on 1948 Bastrop, 1919 Giddings, 1919 La Grange, and 1922 Flatonia USGS
quads, clockwise from upper left.

A set of barns north of the line most likely represents structures mapped in that general location (Figure

No artifact concentrations, other than at 41FY551, or other indications of historic sites were found within
the transmission line ROW.

249

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA proposes to rebuild and enhance an existing transmission line between the Plum and Winchester
substations in western Fayette County, Texas. The line is approximately 7.2 mi long within an existing
100-ft wide ROW. The line was surveyed for archaeological resources in 2004, and then more
intensively in 2008 by LCRA Archaeology Services staff.
Four archaeological sites, prehistoric sites 41FY540-542, and historic site 41FY551, were recorded as a
result of these investigations. The sites consist of a buried lithic scatter (41FY540), an open camp
(41FY541), a lithic procurement locale or zone (41FY542), and an historic trash scatter (41FY551).
These sites are all considered to be fully investigated within the transmission line ROW, and are not
considered to be eligible for formal listing as State Archaeological Landmarks or nomination to the
National Register of Historic Places.
Extensive mechanical augering within the floodplain and lower terraces of the Colorado River valley
indicate that in places soils and relatively recent sediments exceed 1.5 m in depth. A depositional model
proposed by Blum and Valastro (1994) indicates that such deposits are of recent enough age to contain
cultural materials, conceivably within C horizons, although portions of the northern terrace (CB 3) where
construction is scheduled may actually be too young. Elsewhere, the CB 1 and 2 formations may be
mantled with younger deposits. The ELA formation, dated to before 10,000 B.P., makes up the majority
of the southern terraces, and reddish subsoils are consistent with Blum and Valastro’s descriptions
suggesting widespread removal of upland soils by late Holocene times. Additionally, up to three
structures within the CB 3 formation are within a soil series described as having the potential for buried A
horizons at depths of up to 5 ft. Of particular note were a set of augers on the north bank of the river that
found soils or sediments containing large numbers of land snails and fairly dense accumulations of wood
charcoal. This may be the result of extensive, recent reworking of terrace deposits, although the
possibility exists that recent sediments have covered, and perhaps sealed, older, mid-Holocene deposits.
The large oxbow located north of the river is described by Blum and Valastro as belong to the CB 3
formation (Blum and Valastro 1994: 1009, Fig. 6b). Associated dates from West Point are as recent as
250 years B.P., and a local consultant indicates the river changed course, moving out of the oxbow, as
recently as 80 years ago. Although it is highly likely he is mistaken, the possibility remains that the
movement occurred late in the 19th century, which does not completely negate the hypothesis put forward
in 2004 (Hixson et al. 2006) that site 41FY541 was once riverfront property. However, it is more likely
that the Late Archaic dart point is representative of that time, rather than the apparent Early Archaic
gouge.
A total of six proposed structures (numbers 38, 39, 40, 42, 44, and 45) were at locations where soils or
recent sediments exceeded the depth of mechanical testing. Because there was no indication that
archaeological sites are present at these locations, additional subsurface testing (such as backhoe
trenching) was not attempted. Additionally, such testing is out of scale with proposed impacts, and would
damage a similar volume of archaeological matrix as the actual construction. It is therefore recommended
that monitoring of construction take place in these locations (Figure 114). If archaeological materials are
encountered, they will be noted, but construction will not cease unless they are of an outstanding nature.
Instead, the results of monitoring will be reported to THC, and any potential additional investigations will
be discussed at that time.

262

A Cultural Resource Survey and Assessment of the Lower Colorado River Authority’s Proposed Natural
Gas Compressor Plant Project, Bastrop County, Texas

By Daniel J. Prikryl
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit # 4779
Interim Report No. 9.08
Parks and Resource Conservation
Lower Colorado River Authority
May 2008

263

Abstract
In May 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource survey of the LCRA’s
proposed Natural Gas Compressor Plant Project in Bastrop County, Texas. As a result of the fieldwork,
one new cultural resource site, 41BP838, was recorded and assessed. Site 41BP838 is a prehistoric
campsite that covers 70x100 meter area in the northcentral and northwest parts of the project area. Shovel
testing data indicates that the vast majority of the prehistoric materials occur within the upper 40 to 50 cm
of topsoil including the 15 to 20 cm thick former plow zone. Shovel testing and soil probing with metal
rods yielded no evidence of intact burned rock features in the fine sandy loam deposits that range from 10
to 90 cm in thickness. Additionally, no diagnostic artifacts, chipped stone tool fragments, bone, or wood
charcoal were found despite intensive work.
Overall, it appears the natural impacts of bioturbation as well as historic human activities including
previous land clearing and cultivation have heavily damaged the contextual integrity of the deposits.
Based on these facts, the site is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the National Register of Historic
Places and not meriting formal designation as a State Archeological Landmark. The LCRA Archaeology
Services staff recommends that the proposed construction of the natural gas compressor plant be allowed
to proceed without any further cultural resource investigations.

264

Introduction
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to construct a new natural gas compressor
plant in far eastern Bastrop County, Texas (Figure 115). The proposed four-acre sized project area is
situated at the intersection of two existing natural gas pipelines, and project will involve the transfer of
natural gas from one pipeline to the other. The principal construction element will be a small building
that will house two gas compressor units. The building, which measures 70x35 ft in plan view, will be
placed in the approximate center of the project area. Each of the two gas compressor units will be set on
an individual five-foot thick foundation slab within the building. Various piping and metering equipment
and an electrical transformer box will also be placed within and around the building. Several electrical
distribution poles will be erected on the north perimeter of the tract. A gravel-covered driveway will
extend northward from an adjacent county to loop around the building and then proceed eastward to exit
on the county road adjacent to the east boundary of the tract. Other portions of the project area will be
used as equipment laydown areas during construction.
On May 13 and 19, 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services conducted an intensive cultural resource
survey of the area to be affected by the proposed construction of the new gas compressor plant. LCRA
personnel involved in the fieldwork, analyses, and reporting were Daniel J. Prikryl (Principal
Investigator) and Andrew F. Malof along with contract archaeologists Cody Kiker and Glenn Goode. The
work was conducted under Texas Antiquities Permit #4779 in compliance with the State Antiquities Code
as amended and in accordance with methods for field work and reporting as proposed by the Texas
Council of Archeologists (1992).

Environmental Background
The project area is within the West Gulf section of the Coastal Plains physiographic province (Fenneman
1938:100-103) and within the Oak-Hickory Forest vegetation region of Texas (Arbingast et al. 1976:13).
Surface geology consists of Pleistocene fluviatile terrace deposits which in this vicinity contain lag chert
and quartzite gravels (Barnes 1974). Topsoils consist of the Axtell and Tabor fine sandy loams (Baker
1979:Map Plate 39). The soil profiles of these two soils typically consist of a 13 to 38 cm (5 to 15 inch)
thick surface layer of brown to pale brown fine sandy loam above a yellowish-red to yellowish-brown
sandy clay B horizon. Shovel testing at the project area, however, showed that the fine sandy loam
topsoil is 55 to 85 cm (25 to 33 inches) thick on the crest of a small knoll in the north-central part of the
project area.
The project area was formerly cleared and under cultivation with the principal crop being corn. The tract
is currently pastureland that has been cleared of brush in recent months (Figure 116). Prior to the advent
of historic land clearing and cultivation, the project area would have fallen within a region described by
McMahon et al. (1984) as containing a mix of post oak woods and forest, with some grassland mosaic.

Cultural Background
The project area is located on the eastern edge of the Central Texas archeological region (Ellis et al.
1995). The Central Texas archeological record has typically been divided into four main subdivisions.
These periods, in chronological order from most ancient to most recent are: the Paleoindian, the Archaic,
the Late Prehistoric, and the Historic periods. These periods are differentiated mostly by broadly defined
subsistence patterns. The prehistoric part of this chronology has been subdivided further into phases
based largely on changes in material culture,

265

Figure 115. Section of the 1982 Winchester, Texas 7.5’ USGS showing the Project Area outlined in red.
especially projectile point styles. Combined, this information begins to describe the 13,000 year history
of human occupation in Central Texas.
Prehistoric site types in Central Texas consist of camps, caches, isolated artifacts, interments, cemeteries,
kill/butcher locales, quarry/workshops, lithic scatters, and rock art sites (Collins 1995:363). Central
Texas is perhaps best known for the many burned rock midden sites that occur on the Edwards Plateau.
Numerous excavations of major campsites have been conducted along the larger streams and rivers (cf.

266

Peter et al. 1982; Prewitt 1982). Such excavations have demonstrated that Central Texas was occupied
for at least 13,000 years prior to the coming of Europeans. These also show that throughout these
millennia, prehistoric peoples were nomadic hunter-gatherers who moved across the landscape exploiting
seasonally available plant and animal resources.
A total of 15 archaeological sites have been previously recorded within a five-mile radius of the project
area (TARL: Bastrop, Fayette, and Lee County files). Seven of these sites have been recorded by
avocational archaeologists. These sites include 41FY53-55, and 41FY104 which were recorded by
Joseph Wilson, and 41LE32 and 41LE40 which were recorded by Jim Wolf with the assistance of Dr.
Dee Story of the Texas Archeological Research Laboratory. Although there is little available
information on these sites, most of them appear to be prehistoric campsites situated on small to mediumsized creeks similar to the tributary creek that lies 100 to 250 meters west of the current project area.
More detailed data is available on site 41BP78, which was recorded by avocational archaeologist Paul
Duke. This site which is located about 4.5 miles northwest of the project area yielded 7 Clear Fork tools
suggestive of Early to Middle Archaic age along with 27 projectile points of various types.
The other eight known archaeological sites in the vicinity of the project area were recorded by
professional archaeologists. Perhaps the most interesting of these is 41BP282, which was recorded by
Robinson (1987) during the Bastrop County Historical Commission’s Sesquicentennial Project. This site
is located about 5 miles west of the project area on a terrace overlooking the Colorado River floodplain.
In addition to large amounts of prehistoric campsite debris, approximately 50 prehistoric burials have
been recovered at 41BP282. Artifacts suggest that these burials date to the Middle and Late Archaic
periods.
The other noteworthy project conducted by professional archaeologists in the project vicinity is the LCRA
staff’s investigation of the LCRA T-186 transmission line southeast of the project area prior to
maintenance activities within the line corridor (Hixson et al. 2004). Three sites, 41FY540-542, were
recorded during that investigation. Sites 41FY540 and 41FY542 are prehistoric lithic scatters with
41FY540 being noteworthy for buried materials at depths of 40 to 80 cm below ground surface. Site
41FY541 is a large prehistoric campsite on the first terrace above the Colorado River floodplain with
artifacts from the Early and Late Archaic periods. Additional investigation of these three sites by the
LCRA staff is currently in progress for the proposed rebuilding of this transmission line.
The project area immediately south of a rural dispersed community called Center Union. Although there
is little readily available information about Center Union, it appears to have consisted of a school, two
churches, and several scattered residences in the late 19th to early 20th centuries (Smyrl 2002). In 1905,
it had a one-teacher school for about eighty black students. That school closed in the early 1960s, but still
serves as a community center building. Inspection of the 1948 Smithville, Texas 15’ USGS map does not
show any structures that might be related to Center Union community within the project area.
The project area is also located about four miles northwest of Winchester, Texas. During the historic
period, the first known settlers in the Winchester area were the John Ingram family who came to this area
in 1827 (Carroll 2002). The town of Winchester was platted in 1857 and became a stop on the Southern
Pacific Railroad. By 1900, the town had 18 businesses which served a wide area of prosperous farms. By
the 1950s, the number of businesses and the population of Winchester had greatly decreased due to
improved roads and the decline of cotton farming. Presently, much of the former farmland in and around
Center Union and Winchester is used as pastureland for cattle. Some additional revenues come from oil
production.
Overall the review of known archaeological sites in the project vicinity suggests that prehistoric sites are
frequently located on the sandy terraces adjacent to creek channels in the project vicinity. Such sites

267

range from lithic scatters to campsites. Some of these locations were also later the locations of 19th and
early 20th century historic house sites on rural farms. Much larger prehistoric campsites appear to occur
on the terraces overlooking the Colorado River floodplain, and at one known site, 41BP282, a prehistoric
cemetery is also present.

Research Design and Survey Methodology
The purpose of the cultural resource survey was to record and assess the State Archeological Landmark
(SAL) and National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) status of all cultural resource sites that may be
affected by the proposed project. The field survey methodology involved pedestrian survey, shovel
testing, and probing of topsoil deposits with steel rods. During the pedestrian survey, the project area was
walked by two archaeologists on 30 m wide zig-zag transects. Ground surface visibility was found to vary
from good to poor. Bare and eroded patches of ground surface were inspected as was disturbed soil along
the two existing pipeline corridors and in areas where trees and brush had been cleared in the recent past.
In areas with poor ground surface visibility shovel testing was employed in areas thought to have
possibilities for cultural materials and also to investigate the depth of subsurface deposits at the one
archaeological site that was discovered. The shovel tests were excavated in 20 cm increments with all
fill being screened through ¼ inch wire mesh. A total of 14 shovel tests were excavated. Appendix 1
provides a description of the soils from the shovel tests while Appendix 2 provides a tally of recovered
artifacts from these tests.
For additional assessment of the one archaeological site that was found, two metal soil probing rods were
utilized to probe the sandy topsoil deposits for concentrations of rocks that might represent the remains of
burned rock features. The two transects selected for the probing were in areas where previous shovel
testing had indicated the densest prehistoric deposits. A total of 135 such soil probes were made using the
probing rods. Three additional shovel tests were then excavated at spots where the probes indicated that
buried burned rock concentrations might be present.

Survey Results
An intensive pedestrian survey with shovel testing and soil probing with metal rods was completed. One
new archaeological site, 41BP838, was recorded and assessed during the investigation. Site 41BP838 is a
prehistoric campsite that covers the northcentral and northwest parts of the project area (Figures 116 and
117). The site also appears to extend northward outside of the project area although the area northward
was not investigated. The site is situated on a terrace with the center of the known part of the site being
about 200 meters east of an unnamed tributary of Pin Oak Creek. The site area was formerly under
cultivation and is presently mostly a cleared field with a few yaupon, oak, and cedar trees present,
especially on the south and east edges adjacent to a county road.
The densest cultural materials were found on a slight rise or knoll at the eastern end of the site where the
fine sandy loam topsoil averages 50 to 60 cm in thickness. In that area, some artifacts
consisting of chert chipping debris and burned rocks are visible on ground surface, especially

268

Figure 116. Sketch map of Project Area with 41BP838 site boundaries in red, soil probing transects in
dotted blue, and project area boundaries marked by circular black dots. * ST = shovel test.
on the knoll top. These surface materials appear to be randomly scattered with no visible concentrations
of materials. In areas south and west of the knoll, artifacts are much less numerous both on ground
surface and within the thinner fine sandy loam topsoil. Interestingly, no artifacts are visible in the
formerly disturbed soils of the two pipeline corridors located immediately south of the knoll. Sandy
topsoil is mostly absent in these corridors with clay subsoil present on ground surface.
A total of 14 shovel tests were excavated to sample the subsurface contents of soils in the project area
with no artifacts being found in Shovel Tests (ST) #4-7 (see Figure 116). A tally of artifacts recovered
from the other shovel tests is presented in the Appendix 2 table. The only artifact classes recovered in
shovel testing are chert chipping debris and burned rocks. Artifact density from the shovel testing was
highest in ST #1, 9, 10, 13, and 14. Shovel Tests #1 and 10 were placed on the crest of the knoll where
cultural deposits appear to be about 53 cm thick while ST #9, 13, and 14 are clustered at the southeast
edge of the knoll where the cultural deposits average 72 cm in thickness. Chert debitage and burned
rocks recovered during the shovel testing were sorted into four sizes by maximum artifact length with size
data provided by shovel test level on the Appendix 2 table. The size categories for both artifact classes
shown on that table are as follows: Small (S) 1-20 mm, Medium (M) 20-40 mm, Large (L)40-50 mm, and
Very Large (VL) 51 mm and up.
The burned rock fragments present at site 41BP838 are almost exclusively burned chert, with the source
of the material being the Pleistocene-era terrace deposits that are mapped on the geologic atlas sheet as
being present in this vicinity. Several pieces of burned quartzite were also noted, but none of the typical
burned limestone or sandstone rocks were present. The burned rock totals provided in the Appendix 2
table indicate moderately high numbers of small burned rock fragments in Size Category 1 (1-20 mm).
269

Very small burned chert fragments less than 10 mm in maximum length actually make up the majority of
these specimens. The small size of these specimens may be due to the fact that chert cobbles fracture
more rapidly under heat than limestone or sandstone. It should be pointed out, too, that some of the small
burned chert fragments may be chipping debris shatter that later was incidentally burned in hearth
features or was burned during historic land clearing operations. The numbers of larger burned rocks,
which are useful in identifying burned rock features, are smaller.
Chert debris resulting from the chipping of cobbles for the production of stone tools is lower in volume
and averages 8 pieces of debitage per level. Chert debitage totals are highest in Level 1 of Shovel Tests
#1 and 2. Although 14 shovel tests were dug, no chert tool fragments or edge modified flakes were
observed among the materials recovered.
During the first day of field investigation, no intact burned rock features were found on ground surface or
in shovel testing. Nor was any bone or charcoal evident. On a second day of fieldwork, two metal probe
rods were used to probe subsurface deposits in the central and southeast parts of the site to further
determine whether buried intact burned rock features might still be present. The soil probing transects are
marked by dotted blue lines on Figure 116. Approximately 135 probes were made. Three areas where
the probing seemed to indicate possible buried burned rock clusters were then shovel tested. These three
shovel tests are labeled as ST #10, 13, and 14 on Figure 116. As with previous shovel tests that had been
dug on the knoll top, individual burned rock fragments were found at various depths but no clusters that
might be features were encountered.

Figure 117. View of Site 41BP838 with shovel testing in progress.

270

The absence of any evidences of intact features is most likely due to bioturbation of the fine sandy topsoil
deposits. According to the landowner, the site area was also formerly under cultivation for corn
production, and clearing of yaupon and brush after the area was converted to pastureland has occurred in
recent years. The previous plowing and land clearing have undoubtedly also contributed to the
disturbance of the cultural deposits.

Summary and Recommendations
The LCRA is proposing to construct a new natural gas compressor plant on a four acre tract in far eastern
Bastrop County, Texas. The center of project area is located on a terrace about 200 meters east of an
unnamed tributary of Pin Oak Creek. Because a file search indicated that the area had a moderate to high
potential for unrecorded archaeological sites, an intensive cultural resource survey was conducted by the
LCRA Archaeology Services staff in May 2008.
As a result of the field survey, one new prehistoric archaeological site, 41BP838, was recorded and
assessed. Site 41BP838 is a prehistoric campsite that covers 70x100 meter area in the northcentral and
northwest parts of the project area. The site also appears to extend northward outside of the project area
an unknown distance. Shovel testing data indicates that the vast majority of the prehistoric materials
occur within the upper 40 to 50 cm of topsoil including the 15 to 20 cm thick former plow zone. Shovel
testing and soil probing with metal rods yielded no evidence of intact burned rock features in the fine
sandy loam deposits that range from 10 to 90 cm in thickness. Additionally, no diagnostic artifacts,
chipped stone tool fragments, bone, or wood charcoal were found despite intensive work.
Overall, it appears the natural impacts of bioturbation as well as historic human activities including
previous land clearing and cultivation have heavily damaged the contextual integrity of the deposits.
Based on these facts, the site is assessed as not eligible for inclusion in the National Register of Historic
Places and not meriting formal designation as a State Archeological Landmark. The LCRA Archaeology
Services staff recommends that the proposed construction of the natural gas compressor plant be allowed
to proceed without any further cultural resource investigations. However, should any presently unknown
cultural features such as rock-lined hearths or human burials be uncovered during construction, then work
should cease in the immediate area of the discovery, and the LCRA Archaeology Services staff and the
Archeology Division at the Texas Historical Commission should be contacted so that the finds can be
assessed.

Intensive Cultural Resources Survey of LCRA’s Proposed Buckner Boys Ranch Substation Project

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit # 4779
Interim Report No. 10.98
Parks and Resource Conservation
Lower Colorado River Authority
June 2008

277

Introduction and Management Summary
LCRA proposes to construct a new substation on approximately 3.2 acres of land near the community of
Kingsland in western Burnet County, Texas (Figure 118). The substation will be located at the
intersection of FM 2342 and CR 135.
An intensive cultural resources survey was conducted by LCRA Archaeology Services staff on June 2,
2008. The survey consisted of both random and controlled pedestrian transects along with shovel testing.
A total of eight shovel tests was excavated, meeting minimum survey standards (Figure 119).
No archaeological sites were recorded as a result of the investigations, and there was no collection of
artifacts and therefore nothing to curate.

Environmental Background
The project area is within the Llano Uplift region of Central Texas. Soils are of the Click series, which
are described as deep, excessively drained sandy loams formed in granite. It is about 250 m south of
Williams Creek (named Powdermill Creek on the 1909 USGS quad) and somewhat less than a kilometer
east of the Colorado River. Vegetation consists primarily of mesquite and juniper with some scattered
oak, as well as prickly pear, tassjillo and beebrush and other forbs and grasses.

Cultural Background
The project area is within the Central Texas archaeological region, well-described by Collins (1995) and
many others. Prehistorically, hunter-gatherers exploited a wide range of plant and animal resources and
maintained a semi- to highly nomadic lifeway.
The modern history of Burnet County begins in about the 1830s, but did not stabilize until perhaps the
1850s, especially after the establishment of Fort Croghan. According to the Handbook of Texas Online,
by the 1880s the nearby community of Kingsland had been established. Hoover’s Valley, which lies
between Kingsland and Burnet, was settled in the 1850s, and had an independent school system until the
1940s. The Buckner Children’s Baptist Home established Buckner Boys Ranch at about the same time.

Research Design

The survey was designed to identify any archaeological sites present within the project area by means of
files and records searches combined with standard on-the-ground survey methods. If any sites were
identified they would be assessed for potential eligibility for formal listings as State Archeological
landmarks and/or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. Field investigations combined
pedestrian survey with formal shovel testing. A no collection policy was maintained.

Results of Investigations

No archaeological sites were re corded as a result of the investigations. The 3.2 acre tract had varying
degrees of visibility – excellent where a series of dirt roads had been maintained, and poor to none where

278

Figure 118. Project area on Kingsland USGS quad.

279

Figure 119. Aerial imagery showing shovel test locations.

280

there was dense vegetation. The survey began with pedestrian survey which closely examined the banked
soil along the roadways as well as other exposures. One burned piece of burned chert was seen in a road,
as were two possible cores; however, a gravel quarry located just off the tract to the north could well have
produced the “cores,” and there was nothing else to associated the burned chert with, including from a
shovel test very near its location. A total of eight shovel tests was excavated. These tests all found brown
or light brown granitic sandy loams at depth of 15 to 40 cm over decaying granite. They were placed in
areas with poor visibility and/or in order to ensure adequate subsurface testing was accomplished. No
cultural material was found in any of the tests. There were at least two rusted pieces of sheet metal or tin
seen on the ground surface, but no other historic material was seen. The 1909 Burnet USGS quad map
showed no structures within or near the project area.

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted an intensive cultural resources survey of the 3.2 acre
proposed Buckner Boys Ranch substation location. No intact cultural resources were coated as a result of
the investigation. It is recommended that the project be allowed to proceed with standard emergency
discovery procedure in place.

Intensive Cultural resources Survey of LCRA’s Proposed Revision of the Gloster Bend Redevelopment
Project, Travis County, Texas

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit No. 4779
Interim Report 11.08
Parks and Resource Conservation
Lower Colorado River Authority
September, 2008

282

Management Summary
LCRA proposes to develop a portion of its Gloster Bend Recreational Area located in western Travis
County on the north shore of Lake Travis. Components will include both day use and overnight camping
facilities. In January of 2008, a report summarizing an intensive archaeological and historic survey of the
property was submitted to the Texas Historical Commission for review (Malof et. al 2008, Malof and
Prikryl 2008). The archaeological investigations focused on an approximate 170-acre portion of the park
conceptualized for immediate development, while the historic research, which included investigating deed
and other records, as well as an oral history interview with a long time resident of the area, was
comprehensive for the entire property. The THC concurred with all recommendations within the report.
Following consultation with a firm specializing in designing and developing parks, it was determined an
additional area totaling approximately 38 acres, located generally north of the previously surveyed area,
should be considered for development as well, primarily for RV pads and multi-use campgrounds (Figure
120). Impacts could include creating camp loops and pads, as well as providing appropriate utilities.
This additional acreage was surveyed by LCRA Archaeology Services staff during three days in August,
2008. Survey consisted of controlled pedestrian transects and both systematically and judiciously placed
shovel tests. A total of 19 shovel tests were excavated in the project area, which meets the minimum
survey standards as suggested by the Texas Historical Commission (Figure 121).
As a result of the investigations, one previously unrecorded prehistoric archaeological site, 41V2305 was
recorded. This site is a diffuse lithic scatter with apparent burned limestone. It is highly disturbed from
past clearing activities, and is not felt to contain information beyond that already obtained through its
recording. It is not considered to be eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or
nomination to the National Register of Historic Places.
Due to the extensive background section of the previous report, the short format report as suggested by
Council of Texas Archeologist guidelines is used here. No artifacts were collected for this project, so no
curation will be necessary.
It is recommended the project be allowed to proceed.
Study Area
The project area is within Glen Rose formation limestone and a transitional zone of between the lowland
floodplain of the Colorado River and the uplands to the north. It is on a relatively flat divide between 740
and 760 ft amsl. The point of land that comprises Gloster Bend is incised by a number of drainages,
indicating ground water was once more plentiful then at present. Soils consist of the Pedernales (Alfisol)
and Altoga (Inceptisol) series, both of which indicate relatively high moisture availability during soil
formation. At present there are thirty-one recorded archaeological sites at Gloster Bend, both historic and
prehistoric. Prehistoric sites include river-side camps, upland camps, lithic scatters, and lithic
procurement areas. Some of the upland campsites are distinctive in that there appears to have been fairly
intensive occupation at locations removed from immediate water sources.
Historically, the land was used for farming and ranching activities by members of the extended Singleton
family. Past clearing activities within the project area have resulted in a dense monoculture of juniper of
uniform size. The nearby Singleton Cemetery (previously Post Oak Cemetery) has numerous burials
dating to the 1870s, and some as early as the 1850s. A number of house sites have been recorded, most of
which correspond to locations seen on turn-of-the-century topographic maps, and on which additional
information was gathered from local long-time resident Mr. Vernon Hollingsworth.

283

Figure 120. Location of project area in Gloster Bend on Pace Bend USGS quad. All known
archeological sites are plotted – those in red are presently considered potentially eligible for SAL status,
those in green are considered ineligible.

284

Figure 121. Aerial imagery of project area with schematic transect centerlines and GPS’d locations of
shovel tests and site 41TV2035 outlined in red.

285

In general, the study area was considered to be of relatively low potential for intact prehistoric sites, and
there was no archival evidence of historic sites within the study area boundaries. However, as it was
unclear what, if any, investigations had been conducted previously, it was felt that it would be prudent to
conduct a cultural resource investigation prior to intensive development.

Research Design and Methods
The survey was designed simply to identify archaeological sites and, if present, to assess their potential as
formal State Archeological Landmarks. This was accomplished by teams of two to four archaeologists
walking generally parallel transects centered approximately 30 m apart. The transects wandered as a
result of dense vegetation, and as a means of surveying as thoroughly as possible. Shovel tests were
placed both systematically and judiciously. Survey standards adopted by the Texas Historical
Commission suggest a minimum of 19 shovel tests for a project of this size. Nineteen shovel tests were
excavated, mostly to depths of between 30 and 40 cm. This level of testing is felt to be adequate, as the
landform is not conducive to aggradation, and because soil profiles were suggestive of long-term
formation.

Results of Investigations
One previously unrecorded archaeological site, 41TV2305, was recorded as a result of the investigation.
No other indications of cultural resource sites were seen within the rest of the project area.
Site 41TV2305
Site 41TV2305 was recorded while conducting standardized transects (Figure 122). The site consists of a
diffuse scatter of flakes, cores, and apparent burned rock, both limestone and quartzite, located in sandy
clays. No lag gravel deposits are present. Flakes are primarily decorticate and thin. Site documentation
was hampered by dense to extremely dense juniper woods, although overall ground visibility was decent.
Two shovel tests were excavated within the site, both through hard clay soils, and neither with any
subsurface recovery. Topographically, the site is located across a low ridge located about 250 m
equidistant from two drainage systems to the east and west. The current channel of the Colorado River is
over a kilometer away in a generally southerly direction. The ground has been heavily impacted by past
clearing activities. The wooded area to the south was once a demonstration plot where two-way chaining
was conducted in 1987. This may explain why the site does not seem to extend in that direction. At
present it is unknown what clearing took place in the site area, although the juniper is of uniform size and
consistent in size with that seen to the south. The site may also be an extension of 41TV475, which is
located immediately to the north. That site was originally recorded as a hearth field, and apparent heaths
and burned rock scatters are still present. Other lithic (chert) artifacts seem uncommon, although the site
has not yet been intensively reinvestigated.
Gloster Bend is unusual, and possibly unique, given the apparent amount of fairly intensive upland
resource processing. Site 41TV477, 483, 1836, 2269 and 2267 all have similar indications. Site
41TV2305 appears to fall within this category as well. The cores that are present were brought from
elsewhere, presumably for expedient production of flakes at the processing or acquisition locale. Because
of the highly disturbed nature of the site, little else can be said, and its interpretive potential is considered
to be exhausted through its recording. The site is not considered to be eligible for formal listing as a State
Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places.

286

Figure 122. Site 41TV2031.

287

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA proposes to include an approximate 38-acre section of land to its proposed Gloster Bend Park
development project. The remainder of the primary development area was surveyed in 2007 and has been
reviewed by the Texas Historical Commission. This project is an addendum to that, and in no way
changes the results or recommendations form that project. Additionally, this project does not address any
potential development in other areas. Future modifications to development designs remain subject to
agreed-upon avoidance and/or additional testing measures, while development such as trails and
secondary entry stations will require separate assessment and review.
As a result of the survey of the 38-acre section, one new prehistoric archaeological site, 41TV2305, was
recorded. This is a diffuse scatter of chert chipping debris and burned and fire-cracked rock of possible
cultural origin. The site is felt to be fully recorded and ineligible for formal listing as a State
Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. LCRA therefore
recommends that development be allowed to take place within this defined area without further oversight,
with the understanding that standard emergency discovery procedure will be in place.
