An Intensive Cultural Resources Survey of the LCRA’s Proposed Rockwood Switchyard Addition
Project, McCulloch County, Texas

By
Charles A. Hixson

Daniel J. Prikryl
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit #4779
Interim Report No. 12.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Parks and Resource Conservation
September 2008

292

Management Summary
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to construct a new switchyard on its existing
Red Creek to Comanche Switch Transmission Line (T-424) in McCulloch County to serve as a
interconnect to the RES Rattlesnake Wind Farm (Figure 123). Construction will entail clearing and
grading an 800 by 700 ft (approximately 13 acre) area prior to the laying of an insulating gravel bed for
the circuit breakers and other switchyard equipment (Figure 124). The LCRA also plans to buy land
adjacent to the north and south sides of the proposed switchyard. The area to the north will be cleared for
the towers supporting the connections to the existing T-424 Transmission Line and will also serve as an
equipment laydown area for construction equipment and supplies. The area to the south of the proposed
switchyard will be set aside for future additions and connection projects. In all, the project area covers 30
acres.
LCRA Archeology Services surveyed the project area on September 11 and 12, 2008 during which one
new prehistoric archeological site, 41MK46, was recorded. The site contains evidence primarily relating
to lithic procurement activities. The deposits are shallow with a high likelihood of being temporally
mixed and disturbed by land clearing. Therefore, the site is not recommended for Stare Archeological
Landmark designation. Several other isolated artifacts were also found elsewhere in areas where chert
cobbles are exposed on the surface. To a limited degree, lithic procurement obviously took place over a
very wide area within the bounds of the proposed project.

Environmental Background
Geology
According to the Geologic Atlas of Texas, much of the project area is covered by recent or Pleistoceneage deposits made up of gravels, caliche, sand, and clay (Barnes 1976). The gravels range in size from
small chert and quartz pebbles to boulders of Cretaceous and Paleozoic limestone. These deposits were
laid down as alluvial fans from Cretaceous formations creating a great outwash plain between the Brady
Mountains and the Colorado River. These deposits incline to the north and are currently eroding along
the northern edge of the project area exposing Permian age sedimentary mudstones and limestones.
Soils
Two soils and one soil association have been mapped within the project area (Bynum and Coker 1974) as
shown on Figure 124. They are all considered upland soils whose parent material is the old alluvium that
covers much of the area. The Sagerton clay loam soil is found in the northwest corner of the project area
adjacent to a small dry tributary of the Colorado River. This is a deep soil derived from shaly clay
consisting of clay loam throughout its profile. None of the shovel tests dug during the current project
encountered this soil type and in actuality might not extend into the project area.
Mareta clay loam is found in the southeast corner of the project area where the ground is nearly level.
This is a shallow soil of dark clay loam over a thick bed of caliche. Two of the shovel tests (ST 14 and
15) encountered this soil although neither was dug deep enough to reach the caliche zone.
Most of the project area is covered by soils belonging to the Cho-Mereta association. These are shallow
soils consisting of loams over caliche. Shovel testing found considerable variation in soil thickness,
ranging from 16 cm to over 50 cm and usually encountering what in the field was thought to be limestone
bedrock. This material could be an extremely hard caliche although it was sometimes found to have chert
nodules embedded in it.

293

Figure 123. Section of the Doole, Texas 7.5’ USGS topographic map showing the project area in red.

294

Figure 124. Map showing area of proposed switchyard construction (red hatched) and area surveyed
(outlined in heavy red border) with soil types and soil associations.

295

Physiography and Landforms
The project area is located on the southern end of the North Central Plains physiographic region (Bureau
of Economic Geology 1977), a region also known as the Rolling Plains and Mesquite Plains (Lyndon B.
Johnson School of Public Affairs 1978). This region and the adjacent Edwards Plateau represent the
southernmost extension of the midgrass Great Plains grassland (Diamond 2008).
At a smaller scale, the project area rests at the north end of an extensive outwash plain originating along
the north edge of the Brady Mountains to the south and dissected by the Colorado River immediately to
the north. A shallow drainage dissects the northeastern end of the project area, eventually opening out
onto the Colorado River floodplain. This drainage has been dammed, creating a large stock pond
immediately west of the project area. A series of low, discontinuous terrace-like ridges or scarps run
northeast to southwest following the contours.
Vegetation
The project area lies in the southern portion of the natural subregion of Texas known as the Mesquite
Plains. The landscape has a plains like-appearance with large expanses of open grassland and few trees
other than mesquite brush. Much of the surrounding flat areas are under cultivation, while the project
area, itself, and continuing northwards to the floodplain of the Colorado River, is covered with mesquite
thickets (Figure 125) and small savanna-like clearings (Figure 126). Agarita is common shrub and
prickly Pair carpets small areas. Some recent brush clearing with heavy machinery has occurred with
brush piles present in a few parts of the project area.
Cultural Background
Prehistoric Background
The project area lies in the northwest corner of the Central Texas Archeological Region, as defined by
Prewitt (1981). The concept of an “archeological region” developed at a time (1950s and 1960s) when
most Texas archaeologists held the normative view that a culture was defined by a series of shared traits.
In part of Texas where hunter-gatherers were though to have lived, the traits that concerned archaeologists
most were projectile point forms followed by site types and cooking features. Later, in the 1960s and
1970s, many processual archaeologists argued that culture operated as a system that allowed a population
to adapt to its environment. Since Central Texas is an environmentally diverse region, many different
cultural systems must have operated in the past. The population in the Mesquite Plains would have had a
different adaptation than one that included the Balcones Canyonlands in their interactive sphere. With
little ethnographic data on the indigenous population of the region, research in recent decades has focused
on cultural ecology and the materialist aspects of culture, including lithic technology (e.g. Goode in Malof
et al. 2007), cooking technology (Black et al. 1997), and subsistence (Derring in Collins et al. 1997),
which can to see degree be understood using branches of the “hard” sciences.
Chronological frameworks for Central Texas have been proposed by researchers over the years, with the
one most prevalently in use being that of Johnson and Goode (1992). As with all such frameworks for the
region, projectile point forms are used as time markers to recognize various time periods and subperiods.

296

Figure 125. View of grasslands within the project area.

Figure 126. View of mesquite thicket area in southeast corner of project area.

297

Historic Background
The first written accounts concerning the area that would become McCulloch County were left by
explorer Jose Mares, who passed through in 1788 while seeking a route between San Antonio and Santa
Fe. In 1847, John O. Meusebach signed a treaty with the Comanche on the San Saba River at a site that
would later be called Camp San Saba (Smyrl 2008). McCulloch County was formed from the Bexar
District by the Texas Legislature in 1856 but Anglo settlement of the area only began in the late 1850s
after southern Comanche groups moved north to join other Comanche bands or to enter reservations.
Early population centers included Camp San Saba, Voca, and Lost Creek along or close to the San Saba
River and Millburn, which was located further northward near the Colorado River. More extensive
settlement occurred in the 1870s and about this the township of Brady was established on the creek of the
same name. The town was chosen as the county seat in 1876.
Previous Investigations

The nearest previous archeological investigation was conducted by PBS&J for the LCRA’s Morgan
Creek- Twin Buttes- Red Creek-Comanche Transmission Line. This transmission line borders the north
side of the project area. Although no cultural resource sites were found by PBS&J in the area adjacent to
the current project area, they did record 41MK43 at a location about 1.2 km to the east on a high terrace
on the south side of the Colorado River. This site was identified as a lithic procurement site as well as a
campsite, although the artifact inventory consisted of bifaces, cores, tested cobbles, and debitage. A
similar site, 41MK42, was recorded during this same survey on a knoll overlooking Bois d’ Arc Creek
about 2.5 miles to the west of the project area.
Nearby sites that have been excavated include 41MK8, 41MK9, 41MK10, and 41MK27 along FM 765
east of the current project area. These sites were excavated by TxDOT archaeologists in the late 1970s
due to upcoming impacts from road construction. Sites 41MK8 and 41MK9, known as the Corn Creek
sites, contain Archaic and Late Prehistoric burned rock middens that were reported by Black et al. (1997).
A large reservoir project – the O. H. Ivie Reservoir (previously called Stacy Reservoir) – on the Colorado
River as close as five miles to the project area included archaeological excavations in the 1980s. That
work was reported by Lintz et al. (1993).
Research Design and Methods
The purpose of the field investigation was to locate and record all cultural resource sites in the project
area and assess their eligibility for formal designation as State Archeological Landmarks (SAL). The
investigation consisted of a pedestrian survey with shovel testing. For the pedestrian survey, two
archaeologists walked transects at 30 meter intervals. Ground surface visibility was found to be variable.
Shovel tests were placed in the vicinity of surface artifact finds to search for buried artifacts and establish
site boundaries. Shovel tests were also placed in areas with poor ground surface visibility. A total of 16
shovel tests were dug during the survey (Figure 127). The survey had a “no collection” policy. Thus,
there are no artifacts to curate for this project.
Results of Investigations
One prehistoric site, 41MK46, was recorded during the survey. It is located in the western part of the
project area (see Figure 127). This site is a diffuse surface scatter of chert debitage – less than one flake
per 100 square meters – and also includes three Stage 1 bifaces, two, and one edge-modified flake.
These materials were spread over an area of approximately 1.5 acres. These artifacts were found spread
over a low, nearly level landform slope leading down to the drainage at the west end of the project area.

298

This area is open grassland with clumps of mesquite brush and small trees. The site was located when
Shovel Test #7 (ST#7) recovered a single flake in the upper 15 cm of topsoil. Four other shovel tests dug
on the cardinal directions at 15 meter distances from ST#7 failed to recover any additional subsurface
artifacts and all but one, ST#8, struck bedrock at depths between 10 and 22 cm. A close inspection of the
ground in this area revealed the other artifacts listed above. This surface scatter was found to extend to
within 40 m of two previous surface finds – a small core and a flake – that were initially considered to be
isolated artifacts. The site area was, therefore, extended eastward to include these two artifacts.
41MK46 lies within a larger area where chert cobbles are exposed by erosion of the old alluvium along
the flanks of drainages. This zone includes the northern and western parts of the project area, where in
addition to 41KM46 two other isolated Stage 1 bifaces were also noted during the pedestrian survey. It
appears that both early stage biface reduction as well as the production of flakes from multi-directional
cores were activities that occurred at this site in prehistoric times. In addition, some limited activity
involving the use of flake tools took place. No diagnostic artifacts or features were observed during the
survey.
Of the 16 shovel tests dug during the survey, only the already mentioned ST#7 recovered any
unquestionable artifacts. Shovel profiles for all of these shovel tests are presented in Appendix 1.
Summary and Recommendations
A cultural resource survey of the 30-acre Rockwood Electrical Switchyard Project led to the recording and
assessment of one new prehistoric site, 41MK46. The investigation of this site suggests that the site was
primarily used as a lithic procurement locale. The artifacts at the site are extremely widely scattered, and
although no diagnostic artifacts were found, it is probable that the site was utilized over multiple time
periods by prehistoric peoples. Additionally, the artifacts occur in thin soil deposits that have probably
been disturbed by land clearing episodes in the historic times. The site is considered to be fully recorded,
and it is doubtful that additional investigation would yield any useful information. Thus, site 41MK46 is
assessed as not eligible for formal designation as an SAL. It is recommended that the proposed switchyard
construction be allowed to proceed without any further cultural resource investigations.
As always, standard emergency discovery provisions are applicable to this project. Should the
construction personnel uncover any presently unknown buried archeological features during construction
work, then work should cease in the immediate area of the discovery and the LCRA Archeology Services
staff should be notified at 1-800-776-5272, ext. 6714 so that the find can be assessed in consultation with
the THC. In such a situation, work can continue in other areas where no archeological materials are
present.

An Intensive Cultural Resources Assessment of the New Location for LCRA’s Proposed Buckner Boy’s
Ranch, Burnet County, Texas

By Charles A. Hixson

Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit # 4779
Interim Report No. 13.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Community and Natural Resource Services
September, 2008

305

Management Summary
The LCRA is planning to build a new electrical substation in Burnet County between Backbone Ridge
and Lake LBJ. Since the proposed substation is located near the Buckner Boy’s Ranch, it will be given
that designation. In May of this year (2008), the LCRA submitted a report on an earlier site location for
this project (Malof 2008) and the THC concurred with our recommendation that the project proceed since
no archeological sites were found. Since then, the location for the new substation has been changed to a
site approximately 600 meters to the north on the opposite (north) side of Williams Creek (Figure 128).
The size of the area at five acres is approximately the same as that of the first location.
LCRA Archeology Services conducted an intensive survey of the project area on September 25, 2008,
finding no archeological sites within its bounds. Therefore it is recommended that the project proceed.

Environmental Background
Geology
Nearly all the project area is covered by Pleistocene-age sediments (Barnes 1981). Shovel testing during
the current field investigation found these sediments to be composed primarily of silts. PreCambrian
granite has been mapped along the northern margins of the project, however on-the-ground inspection
found only limestone outcropping on the slope adjoining the sediments.
Soils
Weswood silt loam covers approximately 95% of the project area having developed on the Pleistocene
sediments. These are deep soils consisting of zones of brown silty loam. Some Weswood soils have a
surface horizon of loam or sandy loam (probably encountered in Shovel Test 5). Within the Weswood
mapping unit are bands of Oakalla soils, dark grayish brown with lower horizons of clay loam (probably
encountered in Shovel Test 3).
Voca soils have been mapped along the northern edge of the project area. The soils form on granite or
gneiss, rocks not found to outcrop within the project area. Probably Voca soils can be found a short
distance to the north or west.
Physiography and Landforms
The substation project is located in the eastern part of the Central Texas Uplift (Llano Uplift, Central
Texas Mineral Region) at the interface between the rolling granite hills and the Paleozoic sedimentary
rocks that ring the region (Bureau of Economic Geology 1996). The substation will be built on a low, flat
strip of land adjacent to Williams Creek that blends seamlessly into an extensive flood plain of the
Colorado River. A low limestone escarpment borders this strip of land to the north.
Vegetation
The flat area is presently covered by dense thickets of whitebrush (Aloysia gratissima), small mesquite
trees, persimmon, a few ashe junipers, and a single large live oak (Figure 129). Movement by foot is only
possible through a network of trails have been blazed through the thicket to permit line-of-sight for deer
hunting. Because most of the sunlight is blocked by the time it reaches the surface, ground cover is
generally sparse. Immediately outside the project bounds to the north on the limestone slope grow fairly
large live oaks and junipers (Figure 130).

306

Figure 128. Project area shown in red on portion of USGS topographic map, Kingsland, Texas quad.

307

Figure 129. Whitebrush within project area.

Figure 130. Limestone slope along northern edge of project area.

308

Cultural Background
Prehistoric Background
The project area lies in the northwest corner of the central Texas archeological region, as defined by
Prewitt (1981). The concept of an “archeological region” developed at a time (1950s and 60s) when most
Texas archeologists held the normative view that a culture was defined by a series of shared traits. In
parts of Texas where hunter gatherers were thought to have lived, the traits that concerned archeologists
most were projectile point forms followed by site types and cooking features. Later in the 1960s and 70s,
many processual archeologists argued that culture functioned as a system that allowed a population to
adapt to its environment. Since central Texas is an environmentally diverse region, many different
cultural systems must have operated in the past. The groups residing in the Llano Uplift would have had
a different adaptation than one who included the Blackland Prairies in their interactive sphere. With little
ethnographic data on the indigenous population of the region, research in recent decades has focused on
cultural ecology and other materialist aspects of culture, including lithic technology (e.g. Goode in Prikryl
et al. 2007), cooking technology (Black et al. 1997), and subsistence (Dering in Collins et al. 1997).
Chronological frameworks for central Texas have been proposed by researchers over the years, the one
most prevalently in use is that of Johnson and Goode (1992). As with all such frameworks for the region,
projectile point forms are used as time markers to recognize various periods and subperiods.
Historic Background
Although parts of what is now Burnet County were included in colonizing contracts with the Mexican
government before 1836, actual settlement did not occur until the mid-1840s. Soon after Texas was
annexed by the United States, federal money financed a ranger station on Hamilton Creek to help defend
the frontier. This station and surrounding land was purchased one year later by Samuel E. Holland, who
built the area’s first permanent residence (Smyrl 1999). Fort Croghan was established in 1848, and by the
1850s such settlers as the Smithwicks, Vandeveers, and Kerrs, as well as Lyman Wight’s group of
Mormans moved into the region. In 1852 Burnet was officially designated a county. The area
contributed to the Confederate cause during the Civil War, although dissension between factions resulted
in several premature deaths, especially for Union sympathizers. Perhaps a carryover from these times, the
1860s and 1870s remained a violent and lawless period, exacerbated by an economic depression during
Reconstruction. Recovery came as ranching, agricultural, and mining operations increased.
In 1877 the land at the junction of the Llano with the Colorado River was purchased by Martin D. King
and J. M. Trussell (Greene 2001). Eventually the town of Kingsville began to grow at this location,
which soon had a cotton gin and trading post. When the railroad was extended to Llano in 1892,
Kingsville became a stop along the way. Railroad interests built a large hotel called the Antlers which
encouraged tourist to visit the spot by rail. By 1901 the town had been renamed Kingsland.
The area was hard hit by the Depression, and the Works Progress Administration put many people to
work, as did the dam building projects overseen by the Lower Colorado River Authority. The dams were
built along the Hill Country stretch of the Colorado River to control flooding and produce hydroelectric
power for rural communities. Buchanan Dam was the first of these to be completed. It had originally
been started by private companies but their collapse during the Depression ended construction in 1932
when only 40 percent of dam had been built (Yarbrough 1990?). Many of the workers continued to live
at the construction camps along the river in the vicinity of the dam in the hope that the project would

309

Figure 131. Aerial map showing shovel test location. Project area outlined in white.

310

eventually resume. Construction did not begin again until four years later when the state of Texas created
the Lower Colorado River Authority, a non-profit agency which could receive and administer the federal
funds needed to complete the project. Buchanan Dam was completed in less than a year and began
impounding the flow of the Colorado River on May 20, 1937. Wirtz Dam was begun in 1948 and
completed four years later, creating Lake Granite Shoals. The name was changed in 1965 to Lake
Lyndon B. Johnson.

Previous Investigations
The majority of the archeological investigations in the eastern half of the Llano Uplift region have taken
place along the Colorado River mostly because of salvage work conduced prior to the impounding of the
artificial lakes. Over 70 sites were recorded in the late 1930s along a 15-mile stretch of the river within
the uplift which was to be inundated by Lake Buchanan. Reconnaissance work was done in the early
1950s by the River Basins Survey of the area affected by the creation of Lake Granite Shoals (now Lake
LBJ), the reservoir less than a mile west of the project area. A large number of sites were located as a
result of the Granite Shoals survey however these were plotted on a large-scale map and their precise
locations are uncertain.
The Texas State Department of highways and Public Transportation excavated a part of 41BT6 in order to
mitigate its destruction by proposed road improvements (Young 1985). The excavations uncovered an
unusual sheet midden composed mostly of granite rocks arranged in a thin layer. The rock in this feature
appeared unburned. Also present were small sandstone hearths outside the sheet midden. Point styles
indicated that the site was used from the Middle Archaic through the Late Prehistoric. A similar
accumulation of granite rocks was uncovered by test excavations conducted by TPWD archeologists at
Inks State Park a few miles north of the current project area (Drake 1996).
Between 1988 and 1990, archeologists from the LCRA conducted three phases of excavation at the
Kingsland site (41BT215), a prehistoric site along the right bank of the Colorado far enough upstream
from Wirtz Dam to escape complete inundation by Lake LBJ (Nash et al. 1995). Prehistoric artifacts
were found in all units indicating the site encompasses the entire LCRA tract and presumably extends
outside on to private land. In the deeper soil close to the lake, cultural material was recovered down to
depths of 240 cm but artifact density was markedly higher in the upper 100 cm of soil than below.
Projectile frequencies indicate frequent use of the area from the Early Archaic (Split Stem and Uvalde)
through the early part of the Late Prehistoric (Scallorn arrow points). Some historic trash material was
also recovered although only seven artifacts were thought to date before 1900. Disturbances of the
deposits, apparently mostly by pedoturbation, prevented identification of stratigraphic components,
however the presence of small, more or less intact burned rock features at the same general elevation
suggests that components of a sort do exist but smaller cultural material has probably been displaced
vertically. The site is now part of the LCRA Nightengale Archaeological Center and regular tours for
school groups and the general public are conducted by LCRA staff and volunteers from the Llano Uplift
Archeological Society.
During a 2006 transmission line maintenance survey, LCRA archeologists recorded 41BT414 on the
south side of Williams Creeks across from the current project area. The survey found a few flakes and
core fragments on the terrace mostly within the transmission line ROW.
Research Design and Methods
The purpose of the field investigation was to locate and record all cultural resources site in the project
area and to assess their eligibility for SAL status. The investigation consisted of a pedestrian survey with

311

shovel testing. Shovel tests were placed in the vicinity of any isolated surface artifact, to establish the
boundaries of buried cultural deposits (usually following a positive shovel test). A total of 10 shovel tests
were dug during the survey (Figure 131, see Appendix for soil profiles).

Results of Investigations
No artifacts or cultural features were seen on the surface within the bounds of the project other than a few
pieces of modern trash. Two of the ten shovels tests (ST3 and ST7) placed at opposite corners of the
project area did recover one prehistoric artifact each. In ST3, located at the southwest corner of the
project, a small fragment of chert with regular microflaking along 15 mm of one edge was recovered from
a depth of 65 cm. The test continued to a depth of 80 cm at which point the soil became gravelly and hard
to dig. Tests were placed 15 m to the north and east of this unit with no additional artifact recovery.
In the northeast corner of the project a few meters below the limestone slope, a small flake fragment was
recovered from the upper 25cm of soil. Below 25cm, packed limestone rocks and pebbles were
encountered similar to what was visible on the slope and probably a subsurface extension of it. No other
cultural material was seen on the adjacent slope or ground beneath it.
Considering the distance between these two finds - 180 meters – and that no additional artifacts were
recovered from the eight tests placed in between, they do not seem to belong to single site extending
across the project area. These flakes may belong to unrecorded sites lying outside the project area or
they might represent isolated finds.
Summary and Recommendations
No archeological sites were recorded during the survey. It is therefore recommended that the project
proceed as planned.

Intensive Cultural Resource Survey of the Lower Colorado River Authority/Austin Energy Proposed
Transmission Line Connectors and Rebuilds Associated with the Lytton Springs Auto-Transformer
Project, Caldwell County, Texas

by
Daniel J. Prikryl
(Principal Investigator)
with a contribution by
Dan K. Utley

Texas Antiquities Committee Archeology Permit #4779
Interim Report #14.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Archeology Services
October 2008

316

Abstract

The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) conducted a cultural resource assessment for the proposed
construction of new transmission structures and movement of existing transmission structures within the
boundaries of Austin Energy’s (AE) existing Lytton Springs Substation for the Lytton Springs AutoTransformer Project in August and September 2008. The proposed work will involve work on a small
number of structures on LCRA’s existing T-382 and T-534 transmission lines and on the AE line that
extends from the substation to Slaughter Lane.
As a result of pedestrian survey and shovel testing, one new cultural resource site, 41CW121, was found.
Site 41CW121 is an historic farmstead site associated with two generations of the Booten family. The
site was occupied during the early 20th century, and family’s chief economic pursuit appears to have been
cotton farming. Although the house was dismantled in the late 1970s, several house foundation stones
and the underground portion of a brick-lined cistern are still present in the southwestern half of the site.
Artifacts observed on ground surface and recovered during shovel testing date to early 20th century and
are generally nondescript and lacking in diversity.
The site is considered to have been fully documented, and it is assessed as not meriting formal
designation as a State Archeological Landmark and not eligible for inclusion in the National Register of
Historic Places. It is recommended that construction be allowed to proceed without any further cultural
resource investigations.
Since no artifacts were collected during the investigation, no curation of artifacts will be needed.

317

Introduction
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) and Austin Energy (AE) are jointly working on the Lytton
Springs Auto-Transformer Project at the AE-owned Lytton Springs Substation in northern Caldwell
County. As a part of this project, the LCRA is proposing to erect six new transmission structures to
connect the LCRA’s existing T-382 and T-534 transmission lines at substation facilities. Four of these
new transmission structures will be constructed within previously undeveloped parts of AE’s Lytton
Springs Substation property that have not ever been surveyed for cultural resource sites. Erection of these
four structures will also require that two of AE’s existing structures be moved to new previously
unsurveyed areas within the substation property.
The intensive cultural resource survey of the previously unsurveyed areas where the four new LCRA
transmission structures will be constructed and the two AE structures will be relocated within the Lytton
Springs Substation was conducted by LCRA’s Archeology Services staff member Daniel J. Prikryl
(Principal Investigator) and Patrick Hatten in August and September 2008 (Figure 132). The
investigation was conducted under Texas Antiquities Committee Blanket Permit #4479, which was issued
to the LCRA by the Texas Historical Commission for Calendar Year 2008. The survey was also
undertaken in order to meet the requirements of Section 106 of the National Historic Preservation Act of
1966. Additionally, the work was done in accordance with methods for fieldwork and reporting as
proposed by the Texas Council of Archeologists (1992). The other two LCRA transmission structures
will be erected within the right-of-way (ROW) of the LCRA’s existing T-534 transmission line east of the
substation. Since that part of the T-534 line was previously surveyed for cultural resources with negative
results by Nightengale (1993), no additional investigation was undertaken of that area for the current
project.

Environmental Background
The project area is situated within the Blackland Prairie geographic province (Arbingast et al. 1976:13).
The surface geology consists of clays and marls of the Cretaceous-aged Navarro and Marlbrook Marl
(Barnes 1974). Soil mapped by within the project area is the Heiden clay (Lowther and Werchan 1978).
The Heiden clay typically has a 78 cm (24 inch) thick topsoil layer that consists of dark grayish-brown
calcareous clay. The next layer is dark grayish-brown calcareous clay with yellowish-brown mottles that
is also 78 cm (24 inch) thick on average. Deeper, a light brownish-gray to yellowish-brown shaly clay is
present.
According to Arbingast el al. (1976:13), the Blackland Prairie original vegetation commonly consists of
bunch and short grasses. During the late 19th and early 20th centuries much of the prairie was converted
to croplands (McMahan et al. 1984). By the 1970s, production of crops, principally cotton and corn, had
declined with the land becoming pastureland for cattle.

Culture History
The project area is located within the Central Texas archeological region (Ellis et al. 1995). The Central
Texas archeological record has typically been divided into four main subdivisions. These periods, in
chronological order from most ancient to most recent are: the Paleoindian, the

318

Figure 132. Section of the current Creedmoor, Texas 7.5 USGS map showing the cultural resource
survey area in red.

319

Archaic, the Late Prehistoric, and the Historic periods. These periods are differentiated mostly by broadly
defined subsistence patterns. The prehistoric part of this chronology has been subdivided further into
phases based largely on changes in material culture, especially projectile point styles. Combined, this
information begins to describe the 11,500 year history of human occupation in Central Texas.
A longstanding chronology of Prewitt (1981, 1985), has recently been revised by Johnson and Goode
(1994) and Johnson (1995), who utilized new lines of evidence based on climatic and geomorphological
data to redefine period divisions. This can be contrasted with Collins (1995), who indicates climatic
variables at times were somewhat different than those of Johnson and Goode. Though these chronologies
are invaluable to our knowledge base for Central Texas archeology, they must be used as a stepping-stone
to expand our understanding of the cultural history of an area by addressing the question of why changes
occurred in various regions and cultures when they did.
Prehistoric site types in Central Texas consist of camps, caches, isolated artifacts, interments, cemeteries,
kill/butcher locales, quarry/workshops, lithic scatters, and rock art sites (Collins 1995:363). Central
Texas is perhaps best known for the many burned rock midden sites that occur on the Edwards Plateau.
Numerous excavations of major campsites have been conducted along the larger streams and rivers (cf.
Prewitt 1982). Such excavations have demonstrated that Central Texas was occupied for at least 11,500
years prior to the coming of Europeans. These also show that throughout these millennia, prehistoric
peoples were nomadic hunter-gatherers who moved across the landscape exploiting seasonally available
plant and animal resources.
Although the project area is located close to the early Spanish Road known as El Camino Real (State
Highway 21), there are no accounts of activities associated with the road in the early historic period
(McGraw et al. 1991). Just prior to the Texas Revolution, a few individuals associated with DeWitt’s
colony may have settled as close to the project area as Plum Creek which is located about 8 to 10 miles
south of the project area in the vicinity of Lockhart (O’Bannon 1931:43).
The earliest established community in the immediate project vicinity is Lytton Springs, which is located
about 5 miles east of the project area. Settlement began in the 1850s with the community being known as
Albade (Smyrl 2008). A post office was established there in 1859, and the community came to be known
as Lytton Springs in the early 1860s. By the mid-1880s, the community had a steam cotton gin, a Baptist
church, a school, a general store, and about 125 residents. The 1890 census reported that Lytton Springs
had 300 residents. Although livestock ranching was the most important economic pursuit in the early
years, cotton farming became dominant during the period from 1900 to 1930. With the decline in cotton
production, ranching again became important. During the twentieth century, the population declined so
that by 1960, Lytton Springs had only 116 residents. However, in 2000 the population was recorded as
500.
Mendoza is a second community near the project area. It lies just 0.60 miles west of the project area on
U. S. Highway 183. It was named for an early settler and had a post office from 1892 to 1911 (Wagner
2008). The community’s population peaked in the first quarter of the twentieth century when the town
had 200 residents. The 1936 county highway map showed a school, a church, a cemetery, an industrial
site, several businesses, and numerous residences. In 2000, the population was 100.
Previous Investigations
Although there have not ever been any professional cultural resource surveys involving large blocks of
land in the vicinity of the project area, a number of cultural resource sites have been recorded within a 5
kilometer (km) radius. Most of these previously recorded sites were discovered during linear surveys for
roads, electrical transmission lines, and pipelines.

320

The earliest professional cultural resource survey in the project vicinity was the Fayette to Lytton Springs
Transmission Project (Kenmotsu and Freeman 1980). While many archaeological and historic sites were
recorded, the only one within the 5 km radius of the Lytton Springs Substation is 41CW22, which lies
about 3.7 km east of the current project area. This historic period site consisted of a deteriorated barn and
stable which were assessed as insignificant. Another cultural resource site, 41CW52, was recorded
during the State Highway 21 Improvement Project, approximately 5 km northeast of the project area.
This site is a prehistoric lithic scatter which was also assessed as insignificant (TARL: Caldwell County
File).
West of the project area, PBS&J has recently completed an intensive cultural resource survey for the
proposed S.H. 130 highway project. The portion of this new highway adjacent to the project area will be
constructed along and near the existing U.S. Highway 183. Three sites consisting of 41CW74, 41CW76,
and 41CW77 were recorded in the project vicinity (THC Site Atlas 2008). Site 41CW74 is a surficial
prehistoric lithic scatter while 41CW76 and 41CW77 are historic period house sites. All three of these
sites were assessed as insignificant.
Site 41CW108 is one of the more noteworthy previously recorded sites in the vicinity. It is the
Guadalupe-Mendoza cemetery, and it is situated about 1 km west of the project area. This cemetery has
primarily served the local Mexican-American community since the early 20th century. However, earlier
unmarked graves are said to be present as are a few late 19th century Anglo graves. Data on this
cemetery was recently utilized in a Masters Thesis project by Jonathan Jarvis (2007) of St. Edwards
University. It should also be noted that one of the earliest plotted archaeological sites in Caldwell
County, 41CW4, is just north of the cemetery. However, there is no information in the files at the Texas
Archeological Research Laboratory concerning this site.
A pertinent previous investigation is Nightengale’s (1993) survey of the Mendoza substation which is
situated about 1 km east of the current project area. This investigation also included the T-534
transmission line which was being constructed at that time from this substation to the Lytton Springs
Substation. No cultural resource sites were found during that investigation.
More recently the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource survey for the
proposed expansion of the Lytton Springs Substation (Prikryl 2008). Areas investigated are located
within the Lytton Springs Substation property in a 1.6 acre area to the north of the current project area.
No cultural resource sites were found.
Overall, the previously recorded sites in the project vicinity are primarily historic period sites that are
former rural house sites. These appear to date from the late 19th to early 20th centuries and are most
probably related to farming and ranching activities. The few prehistoric sites present are lithic scatters
that are located on small drainages and in upland settings where lag gravel deposits are present.

Survey Methodology
The file search conducted for the project suggested that there is a low probability that any prehistoric
archaeological sites to be present in the project area due to the absence of any water sources. In order to
determine the potential for any historic period archaeological sites to be present, historic maps consisting
of the 1910 Austin, Texas 30’ USGS map (field surveyed in 1895-1896), the 1955 Montopolis, Texas 15’
USGS map, and the 1968 Creedmoor, Texas 7.5’ USGS map were examined to determine if any
buildings had ever been present within or near the project area. While the 1910 map did not indicate any
structures in or near the project area, the 1955 Montopolis, Texas 15’ USGS map showed a farmhouse

321

that was located within or very near the southwest corner of the property area. That same structure is then
depicted as an abandoned house or barn on the 1968 Creedmoor, Texas 7.5’ USGS map. While a recent
aerial map indicates that the building is no longer present, there appeared to be some potential that
historic period archaeological remains such as building foundations and artifacts could be present within
or near the substation expansion area. For this reason, a cultural resource field survey appeared to be
warranted.
Field survey methods used during the project consisted of a 100 percent intensive pedestrian survey along
with shovel testing. During the pedestrian survey, the project area was walked on 30 m zig-zag transects.
Bare and eroded patches of ground surface were inspected as was disturbed soil in and around the existing
chainlink fence that surroundings the existing substation facility. Shovel tests, which consisted of small
35 to 40 cm (13.5 to 15 inch) diameter holes were judgmentally placed in areas of high probability having
poor ground surface visibility and also to help determine the boundaries of any archeological sites. Since
the main focus of the investigation was to search for historic period remains, it was assumed that such
remains would be present on ground surface or would be shallowly buried. Therefore, the shovel tests
were generally only dug to depths of 40 to 50 cm below ground surface or to the top of the B horizon
clay. The shovel tests were excavated in 20 cm increments with all fill being screened through ¼ inch
wire mesh. A total of 10 shovel tests were excavated within the approximately 2.4 acres that were
examined. This number well exceeds the Texas Historical Commission’s minimum requirements.
All shovel tests were carefully backfilled immediately after excavation to restore the ground surface to its
original appearance. Further, no artifacts were removed from the property of any of the landowners. Any
artifacts observed on the ground surface or found in the shovel tests were examined, analyzed, and
photographed in the field, as necessary, and then placed back in their original locations. Appendix 1
provides lists the soil descriptions from each shovel test, while Appendix 2 provides descriptions of the
artifacts recovered from the shovel tests.

Results of Investigations
One new archaeological site, 41CW121, was recorded and assessed during the survey (Figure 133). This
historic period site is situated at the spot where a house is shown on the 1955 Montopolis, Texas 15’
USGS map. It is also represented on the 1968 Creedmoor, Texas 7.5’ USGS map as an abandoned house
or barn. Although the structure is no longer present, an approximately 6 ft diameter underground cistern
(Figures 134 and 135) is visible as are two crude foundation stones. The upper part of the cistern
including the collar has been removed, and most of the cistern is filled in with dirt. The two foundation
stones lay about 60 ft apart in at spots 50 to 60 ft to the southwest of the cistern. A pile of wooden
boards, rusted tin sheets, and electrical cables was also noted at the site at a location about 50 ft to the
northeast of the cistern. The trash pile is quite compact as it covers an area of only 8x10 ft. None of the
materials in the trash pile appears to be very old, and the boards have round-headed nails. According to
the former landowner and current lessee, Mr. Archie Roberts (Personal communication 2008), the trash
pile is not related to the former house, but instead, is one of several piles of trash dumped illegally on the
property.

322

Figure 133. 41CW121 site map showing site boundary in dotted red line.

323

Figure 134. Looking west at archaeologist standing next to cistern feature at 41CW121.

Figure 135. Close-up view of interior wall of underground cistern feature.

Although much of the area containing the former house features is covered with thick grasses, some good
ground surface visibility was noted in areas 80 to 100 ft north of the cistern along the boundaries of the
chainlink fence that surrounds the existing substation facility. Surface finds in that area consist mainly of
clear glass fragments, but four pieces of purple (solarized) glass were noted there as were two whiteware
ceramic sherds.

324

At the time the site was discovered, two shovel tests (ST#1 and 2) had already been excavated at locations
about 35 meters northeast and north of the cistern, respectively. Neither of these shovel tests produced
any artifacts. The site was found when the surveyors approached the location of the existing AE Structure
#1 on the AE line that extends from the substation to Slaughter Lane. This structure, which lies just 15
meters east of the cistern, will need to be removed and relocated to spot 15 to20 meters southwestward.
Seven additional shovels were then excavated to define the boundaries of 41CW121 and determine if the
relocation of the AE Structure #1 would damage any potentially significant cultural resources.
The excavation of the shovel tests led to the conclusion that 41CW121 extends from the north fence
adjacent to County Road 177 north for a length of 60 meters to the interior southwest edge of the existing
substation facility (See Figure 133). The width of the site was measured at 48 meters on an east-west line
with the west boundary appearing to coincide with an existing dilapidated barbed wire fence.
Appendix 2 provides a listing and description of artifacts recovered from the shovel tests. Artifact yield
(n=44) was highest at ST # 3, which is located adjacent to the chainlink fence where most of the surface
artifacts had been noted. The second highest artifact yield (n=36) came from Shovel Test #8, which is the
approximate new location for the AE Structure #1. Other shovel tests produced much lower number of
artifacts. The artifact inventory from these shovel tests mostly consists of clear glass fragments, roundheaded wire nails, rectangular-headed cut nails, plain whiteware ceramic sherds, and small, deteriorated
pieces of sheet metal.
The next highest number of artifacts came from ST #5, which was dug adjacent to the western foundation
stone. Artifacts from that shovel test included several round-headed nails, two distinctive roofing nails,
several pieces of mortar, a snuff jar rim fragment, a metal knife handle, and a metal rivet along with
several clear glass fragments. Other shovel tests yielded lower numbers of artifacts, with ST #6 and ST #9
on the southeast edge of the site having the lowest numbers. The artifacts from all of the shovel tests came
from the upper 30 cm of soil deposits, with the majority being within 20 cm of ground surface.
Previous disturbance of the topsoils was noted in the soil profiles of Shovel Tests #3 and 7 at the north
end of the site adjacent to the existing substation chainlink fence. Elsewhere, the topsoils did not show
evidences of prior disturbance except at Shovel Test #8, where an introduced gravel layer was lay at
depths from 7 to 18 cm below ground surface. This gravel came may residue from a gravel road that led
from the nearby county road to the house.
The artifacts found at 41CW121 are predominantly early to mid 20th century materials, with a few
possible late 19th century artifacts being represented by the four purple (solarized) glass fragments seen on
the surface at the far north end of the site and several rectangular-headed cut nails found in shovel tests.
Additionally, a single olive green glass fragment that came from Shovel Test #3 may come from a 19th
century wine bottle.
The early to mid 20th century ages of the artifacts fits well with deed/title research, which was conducted
by Utley (see Appendix 3) for this project. Utley’s work shows that the project area lies within the league
of land patented to Sampson Connell in 1847. Connell apparently did not live on this land, but instead
resided in Milam County. In 1886 he sold land that includes the project area to Ewing H. Skaggs.
Skaggs did not reside on this land either. Utley’s research indicates that the first landowners to actually
live on this tract were John and Eliza Booten, who purchased a 192 acre tract from Skaggs in 1889.
Site 41CW121 represents the Booten family farmstead. The exact date of the construction of the house
at 41CW121 is uncertain, but it was probably constructed between 1889 and 1910. John Booten died in
1920, after which the family farmstead transferred to his son Lewis and wife Lea. The 1930 census lists

325

Lewis Booten as a farmer and cotton buyer. Lewis Booten died in 1951, and, subsequently, his wife Lea
and Arthur V. Smith (relationship unknown) divided the property into two equal sized parts in 1963.
In 1977 Clyde Hausmann acquired and further subdivided the property. Mr. Hausmann and his wife,
Betty, held the land for only a few months before selling it to Archie A. and Idell Roberts. The Roberts’
then sold the property to Austin Energy in 1979. In a telephone interview with Archie A. Roberts in
August 2008, Mr. Roberts stated that the house had been uninhabited for some time and was in poor
condition when he purchased the tract. He also said that he dismantled the house, and reused the wood
elsewhere.
Overall, the farm house appears to have been occupied in the early 20th century by the Booten family and
aligns with the era when cotton farming was the predominant economic pursuit. The only remaining
features are the damaged cistern and several foundation stones. The artifacts are consistent with an early
20th century occupation, but are generally nondescript and lacking in diversity. Site 41CW121 is assessed
as insignificant due to the fact that the house is no longer present and because the artifact assemblage does
not have any potential to provide any new and interesting data about the early 20th century lifeways of
cotton farming families who lived on the Blackland Prairie in the Mendoza area.
As previously stated, the existing AE Structure #1 on AE line that extends from the substation to
Slaughter Lane is located within site 41CW121. Current project plans call for moving this structure to a
new location about 15 to 20 meters southwestward. This new location will also be situated within the
boundaries of site 41CW121. The cultural resource survey of other parts of the project area did not reveal
any evidences of other sites. In particular, the areas where LCRA will need to erect four new structures
within the substation grounds as well as the location where AE will need to relocate its Structure #2 on
the line from the substation to Slaughter Lane were intensively inspected and shovel tested with negative
results.

Recommendations
The intensive cultural resource survey for the LCRA and AE transmission structure relocation and
connector project at the Lytton Springs Substation led to the discovery of one new historic period
archaeological site, 41CW121. This investigation of this site indicates that it represents an early 20th
century house site that was owned and constructed by the John Booten family in the very 19th or very
early 20th century. The Booten family was engaged in cotton farming and family member descendents
may have occupied the house into the 1960s. The house was dismantled in the 1970s, and the only
remaining features at the site are several foundation stones and a portion of an underground cistern.
Artifacts found at the site are predominantly nondescript and lack diversity. It is doubtful that further
investigation of the site and its contents could produce any new and interesting data on the Booten family
or the cotton-farming lifeways of the early 20th century. Therefore, the site is assessed as not eligible for
inclusion in the National Register of Historic Places and not meriting formal designation as a State
Archeological Landmark. It is recommended that all necessary work for removing and relocating the
existing AE Structure #1 within the boundaries of site 41CW121 be allowed to move forward without any
further cultural resource investigations.
Similarly, it is recommended that the work involved with all of the other components of the proposed
project be allowed to proceed without any further cultural resource investigations. However, should any
other presently unknown archaeological sites be uncovered during construction, then work shall cease in
the immediate area of the discovery and the Archeology Division of the Texas Historical Commission
should be contacted at (512) 463-6096 so that the find can be assessed and a determination can be moved
as to whether any avoidance/mitigation measures are needed. In such a situation, work can proceed in
other areas where no archeological materials are present.

Appendix
Deed/Title Research for 41CW121, the Booten Family Farmstead
By
Dan K. Utley

BOOTON FAMILY FARMSTEAD, Caldwell County
The Booton Family Farmstead site is located within a league of land patented to Sampson
Connell in December 1847. The patent listed the property as part of the Bastrop District located
on the south side of the Old San Antonio Road (Caldwell County Deed Records: D/561). The
same year Connell acquired the land, preparations began for a new county that would include
portions of Gonzales and Bastrop counties. Finalized the following year, Caldwell County began
with Lockhart as the county seat. Population initially centered on Lockhart and other small
communities to the south and east, but by the end of the century, settlers were moving to upland
areas farther north as land use in the county shifted from cattle to cotton production and the
advent of local railroad lines made farming more commercially viable. Among the settlements
that developed during that era was Mendoza, the commercial center of a dispersed farming
community. It included a post office, school and businesses, including a cotton gin (The New
Handbook of Texas, Vol. 1, pp. 896-898; The New Handbook of Texas, Vol. 4, p. 619).
Sampson Connell apparently did not live on his land in Caldwell County, as he is listed in
other documents as a resident of Milam County. He began selling off acreage as early as the
1850s, and one of the individuals who eventually acquired considerable holdings within the
Connell League was Ewing H. Skaggs, who owned sizable tracts in other abstracts as well. An
1886 conveyance to A.M. Skaggs that covered portions of several abstracts noted the portion in
the Connell League included 431 acres in prairie and 371 acres in farm “with seven farm houses,
cribs, lots and four tanks 60 acres enclosed pasture and two hundred and thirty to sixty acres in
timber in the Isaac Allen, Story and Sampson Connell Surveys.” The deed also mentioned the
presence of a gin house on a nearby survey. (Caldwell County Deed Records: 3/63) The
description of the cultural landscape, along with the size of the conveyance, indicates Skaggs
managed a sharecropping operation for cotton production, similar no doubt to others in existence
in the area at the time.
In 1889, E.H. Skaggs sold 192 and 3/5 acres to John M. Booton (Caldwell County Deed
Records: 8/64). Born in Virginia in 1840 and married to a Virginia woman named Eliza A.
(surname not noted) in 1873, Booton was a farmer. The year of their marriage, the couple lived
in Missouri, where they soon gave birth to their first child, son Louis Wood Booton. They moved
to Texas by 1879, however, when a second child, daughter Pearla, arrived. (U.S. Census, 1900)
John Booton died sometime prior to 1920, when his widow is shown living with Pearla, by then
Mrs. William Graham Baker. (U.S. Census 1910 and 1920) Following John Booton’s death, the
family farmstead transferred to son Lewis and his wife Lea (also shown as Lea), apparently
without deed. In the 1930 census, Lewis Booton listed his profession as farmer and cotton buyer.
(U.S. Census, 1930)

Cultural Resources Survey of LCRA’s Proposed T-216 Fayetteville to Bellville South Transmission Line
Rebuild Project, Fayette, Colorado and Austin Counties. Texas

By Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator
And Daniel J. Prikryl

TAC Permit 4779
Interim Report #14.08
Parks and Resource Conservation
Lower Colorado River Authority
October, 2008

332

Management Summary
LCRA proposes to upgrade its existing T-216 transmission line, which runs for approximately 28 miles
within a 80-100 ft-wide corridor from the Fayetteville substation in Fayette County eastward through the
Fayette Electric Coop’s Willow Springs substation, then through Colorado County and into Austin
County to the New Bremen substation, jointly owned with San Bernard Electric Coop, to a point referred
to as the Bellville Split, from where it turns northward to end at the Bellville South substation (Figure
136). The upgrade will involve replacing existing conductor and the majority of the mostly wooden Hframe support structures between Fayetteville and the Bellville Split. Between Bellville South and the
Bellville Split existing lattice structures will remain in place, although certain structural elements (e.g.
bolts, crossarms) will need to be replaced.
The portion of line between the Fayetteville Substation and the Bellville Split was partially surveyed in
2004 prior to regularly scheduled maintenance activities. These surveys are structured based on impacts
from maintenance, which often require little more than the shredding of vegetation within previously
cleared corridors, although special attention is paid to areas where stream crossing need to be enhanced or
created. This section was intensively surveyed for the present project.
The portion of line within the Mill Creek floodplain, between the Bellville Split and the Bellville South
substation was surveyed by Hicks and Company in 2003. Fifty-four shovel tests were excavated along
this approximately 3-mile long segment, much of which is a low-lying swamp or marsh. No
archeological sites were found. This section of line, which will not be having structures replaced, was not
resurveyed.
In total, approximately 25 miles was assessed, with an emphasis on high probability areas defined by a
combination of geology, soils, hydrology, topography and historic records. Discounting the previously
intensively surveyed section, approximately 10.3 mi was considered to be of high probability for
containing cultural resources. A total of 45 hand excavated shovel tests and 23 mechanically excavated
auger tests were completed, for about 40 percent of the recommended minimum. This number is felt to
be sufficient based on field observations that included excellent representative subsurface visibility based
on extensive rodent burrowing in many areas and the perceived and previously demonstrated absence of
cultural material within the broad, heavy clay floodplains of the major streams.
As a result of the investigation, nine previously undocumented prehistoric archaeological sites, 41FY552,
41FY554 and 555, and 41AU90-95, and one historic trash scatter, 41FY553, were recorded, and two
previously recorded sites, 41AU83 and 41FY546, were assessed (Figures 137, 138, 139). The new
prehistoric sites are all lithic scatters, some with burned or fire cracked rock, but none in sufficient
quantity to indicate an extensive camp site. Other than 41FY555, they are all within highly turbated
sandy soils, lack evidence of discrete components, have little in the way of diagnostic tools, and no
evidence of clearly associated organic preservation. These sites are considered fully recorded within the
transmission line corridor and are felt to be ineligible for formal listing as State Archeological Landmarks
(SALs) or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP). Historic trash scatter
41FY553 has sparse materials, some of which may predate 1880 but include later items such as wire
nails. These artifacts are unassociated with any known structures and appear to represent trash disposal
events. This site is not considered eligible for SAL or NRHP status. Previously recorded site 41AU83
was a lithic and historic debris scatter which was previously assessed as ineligible. Site 41FY546 is a pile
of farm implements associated with an old farmstead. The implements were recommended for avoidance.
41FY555 is a deeply buried lithic scatter with a small amount of fire cracked rock located on a levee or
terrace remnant of Cummins Creek. Despite the very small amount of material that was observed, the site
