is considered to potentially have additional research potential, and therefore is potentially eligible for
formal listing as an SAL or NRHP placement. It is therefore recommended that this very narrow
landform be excluded from impacts exceeding about 50 cm below the ground surface. If impacts cannot
be avoided, then additional investigations, most likely in the form of backhoe trenching should be
considered.
As for the rest of the project, the survey has produced additional information about prehistoric settlement
practices along what can be considered a random observational transect. This data clearly indicates that
certain prehistoric activities requiring the manufacture or maintenance of stone tools took place in well
drained sandy soils at relatively long distances from water sources, many of which are fairly small, but
probably spring-fed. Such activities were probably based on extraction of local food resources. Larger
campsites are certainly present in the area, so the prehistoric sites that were recorded for this project are
helpful in providing locational information, but can do little to more fully inform on specific aspects of
prehistoric life.

Environmental Background
The project area is situated West Gulf Coastal Plain physiographic province (Fenneman 1938:100-102).
Surface geology in most of the project area consists of the Miocene-aged Fleming Formation and the
Pleistocene Willis Formation (Barnes 1974). Elsewhere, Recent Alluvium is present on the lower terraces
of Cummins Creek, Mill Creek, and a narrow strip along Sandy Creek. Notably, the only Fluviatile
terrace deposits are crossed at the extreme north/eastern end of the project.
The largest stream channel traversed by the transmission line is Cummins Creek, a major tributary of the
Colorado River. The Cummins Creek crossing and those of several of its tributaries are all located in the
western part of the project area in Fayette County. Eastward, in Austin County, all stream crossings are
creeks that are parts of the Mill Creek drainage system. Mill Creek is a major tributary of the Brazos
River.
During field investigations it was noted that many of the small streams had clear flowing cool water and
hydrophytic vegetation. At least one actual spring was within or very near that transmission line corridor,
as were a number of small pools. This ground water, likely filtered through sandy soils, would have been
major draw for prehistoric people, and certainly factored into pulling them into more upland setting than
might otherwise have been expected.
Soils were analyzed by overlaying NRCS GIS-based SSURGO data on project maps. Over 30 individual
soils are crossed by the line. These soils were evaluated by proximity to water source, and by description.
There were clays (vertisols), loams (mollisols) and sands (alfisols). The most intriguing aspect of the soil
analysis was the presence of alfisols alongside streams in Tertiary sediments that are described as having
the potential for multiple buried A horizons. Informal discussion on the ListServ of the Texas
Archeological Society provided a general consensus that these buried horizons can develop in recent
(Holocene) times, thus creating the potential for buried cultural materials in otherwise unpromising
geologic settings.
The vast majority of the transmission corridor is within the Oak Savanna Vegetation Region with only the
extreme eastern edge falling within a different region, the Oak-Hickory Forest (Arbingast et al. 1976:13).
During the survey, the following observations were made about the vegetation of the project area (Figure
140). Beginning at the far west end of the project area and continuing to the west edge of the Cummins
Creek floodplain, the terrain is a Post Oak Woods, Forest, and Grassland Mosaic as described by
McMahan et al. (1984). Much of the Cummins Creek floodplain appears to have been cleared of its

338

original vegetation and placed in cultivation during the historic period. Currently, those former croplands
are pastureland. East of the Cummins Creek floodplain and continuing about 8 miles to just west of F.M.
109, the clayey topsoils in this area may originally have been mostly prairie grassland. Most of this area
is currently pastureland that was probably once under cultivation. Grasses in this area are a combination
of native and introduced species. Continuing eastward, the vegetation is again a Post Oak Woods, Forest
and Grassland Mosaic with sandy soils until the far eastern edge of the project area where prairie pockets
are absent. McMahan et al. (1984) describe this eastern edge of the project area as Post Oak
Woods/Forest.

Figure 140. View of typical eroded upland drainage.
Cultural Background
Prehistoric Background
The project area falls very near the eastern edge of what has traditionally been referred to as the Central
Texas Archaeological Region (Collins 1995, Prewitt 1981). More recent divisions, perhaps based in part
on Ellis et. al’s (1995) criticism of the Central Texas concept, would place the project squarely within a
Timbers and Prairies region (texasbeyondhistory.net) that roughly corresponds with what as been termed
the Post Oak Savannah of East Central Texas (Fields 1995). Fields, however, tosses the project area back
to Central Texas and the upper Texas coast, based on disjunction between the more northeastern groups
he focuses on, and on the relative lack of extensive projects west of the Brazos River, which precludes
making meaningful comparisons (Fields 1995:303).
As noted below in the Previous Investigations section, other researchers have attempted to place the area
centered around La Grange in a transitional zone that saw influence from the northeast and east, the
coastal plain, and Central Texas. Examples include ceramics at a Late Archaic site, Late Prehistoric
period Scallorn points associated with ceramics, and suggestions that persons using locally made pottery
had adopted technical aspects of lower Plains Toyah culture. The most notable difference between the
cultural chronology of Central Texas and the Post Oak savannah is lack of well-documented sites
predating the Late Archaic Period, although there seems to have been low population densities and an

339

emphasis on mobile foraging rather than logistical gathering during those times. Likewise, in the Post
Oak Savannah, Late Archaic period peoples appeared to be a bit more sedentary, and are using cultigens
such as squash, but in general remain highly mobile, albeit in a structured (seasonal) fashion and in
reduced territorial ranges. Late Prehistoric peoples, at least in the earlier part of the period, were more
settled in the (Navasota) river valley and used uplands for logistically structured procurement forays.
Meanwhile, Caddoan ceramics have become common, but it is unclear if they are accompanying Caddoan
speakers as they pass through or are a local adaptation.
Coastal influences within the project area are not uncommon. Some marine shells have been found at
various sites, as has asphaltum. The clear exception is at Allens Creek, where burial goods were
influenced by and derived from coastal sources. Site 41AU1 likewise had burials with marine shell
ornaments.
For the sake of simplicity and to create, in effect, a null hypothesis, it is most useful to associate the
project area with the Central Texas chronological tradition. Should exceptions or anomalies appear, they
can be compared with what is known and surmised about the adjacent regions, and interpretations can be
applied accordingly.
Central Texas prehistory has typically been divided into four main subdivisions, known variably as eras
or periods, which are subdivided further into phases or subperiods. These subdivisions combine to begin
to describe 11,500 years of history. A longstanding chronology of Prewitt (1981, 1985), has recently
been revised by Johnson and Goode (1994) and Johnson (1995), who utilized new lines of evidence based
on climatic and geomorphological data to redefine period divisions. This can be contrasted with Collins
(1995), who indicates climatic variables at times were somewhat different than those of Johnson and
Goode. The ultimate goal of many modern archaeologists is to transcend the culture history of an area
and attempt to explain why change occurred in various cultures and regions. To this end carefully revised
chronologies are to be expected and encouraged.
Historic Background
The project falls within Fayette County, a small portion of northern Colorado County, and eastern Austin
County. The area was being settled by Anglos as early as the 1820s, and is primarily associated with
Austin’s Colony. In 1828 the Cummins family had established a mill on Mill Creek (Jackson 2002), and
the Rabbs had built one about the same time in what is now Fayette County (Garrett 2002). San Felipe, in
Austin County, was an early economic center. Due in part to Indian conflicts, Fayette County was a bit
slower to develop, although towns such as La Grange, Fayetteville and Round Top had fully formed
school systems by the 1840s. The area was also deeply involved in the Texas Revolution, with Santa Ana
passing through Austin County and both counties providing soldiers.
German settlement Austin County began in earnest in 1838, when J. Freiderich Ernst established the town
of Industry. Germans had already arrived in Fayette County, where the Biegel Settlement was established
sometime after 1832. The Germans raised corn and cotton, and increasingly, tobacco, with cigar factories
at Industry, New Ulm, and Columbus.
German settlement was easily matched by that from the southern United States, and Czechs began
arriving in the 1850s, founding Dubina in 1856. Slaves came in from the south, and large areas were
cleared and planted, much in cotton. Austin County soon was one of the largest slaveholding counties in
the state, and also one of the wealthiest. The mix of slaveholding southerners and the somewhat poorer
and generally slaveless Germans and Bohemians resulted in friction during the Civil War, with Germans
voting decisively against secession and vigorously protesting requirements to serve in the Confederate

340

Army. Fayette County voted against secession, which was defeated narrowly, while nearby Austin
County voted overwhelmingly in favor of secession.
Reconstruction seemed to mark divergent paths for Austin and Fayette counties. Austin County had
received an influx of Black slaves during the Civil War, which permanently changed the racial makeup of
the county. Fayette County, meanwhile, was seeing increasing immigration of Germans, Czechs and
Wends, who bought large lands from now-destitute plantation owners and proceeded to subdivide these
properties. Czech immigration continued to increase during the latter half of the nineteenth century, and
several communities became established, allowing a distinct Texas Czech cultural identity to become
more fully realized. In eastern Austin County, violence against African Americans became routine, and
few crimes were prosecuted. The Freedman’s Bureau aided somewhat and African American churches
and schools became common.
Cotton and corn were still primary crops, tobacco having fallen from favor with the advent of the
railroads. Truck gardening became increasingly common, especially to the east where produce could
easily sold to Houston markets. Tenant farming remained common, and was comprised of both white and
black tenants. Between 1880 and about 1930 close to 47 percent of Austin County’s population served as
tenants. Increasingly, during the first part of the twentieth century, Mexican workers began arriving, and
in Austin County they were the primary immigrants.
Fluctuations in markets and agricultural conditions caused various industries to wax and wane. Cattle
raising became more important. Oil was discovered in Austin County in 1915, but not exploited until
1927. In Fayette County it was not discovered until 1943, when it became an important commodity.
Both counties saw a boom in oil production in the 1990s.
Many of the small towns that the transmission line project passes near, such as Fayetteville, Industry,
New Ulm, and Cat Spring are fairly well documented. Less information was available on New Bremen,
located in Austin County and only about one-quarter mile north of transmission line, it presently consists
of little more than a couple of house located at a crossroads dominated by an auto salvage yard. West of
there, however, is a sign commemorating the location of the New Bremen Colored School, which was
located about one-half mile distant from the New Bremen School. Adjacent to the Colored School is the
Travis Davis Cemetery, which is assumed to be an African American Cemetery, based on its location. At
a distance of about 2.5 miles by road is a small cluster of homes and a church. These houses appear to
date to the 1920s or 1930s, and have been added on to over time. At least some of the residents are
African American. The church is called The Church of God of Prophecy (Figure 141), presumably the
same Pentecostal church that originated in the Appalachian Mountains as a miracle-based congregation in
the 1880s (http://cogop.org/221595.ihtml). This small community was the only coherent aspect of New
Bremen that was seen, and appears to sit on a small sliver of land between large horse ranches. It is
assumed that these homes housed tenant farmers. The property may be the location of slave quarters, or
possibly a small Freedman’s community on donated land.
It was observed that there was a change in land use between Fayette and Austin counties, at least in the
vicinity of the transmission line. Properties in Fayette County tended to be smaller, and cattle production
was dominant. Austin County, meanwhile, had some very large landholdings that focused on horses. It
was evident that large amounts of capital had been invested in some of these. It is likely this is a result of
the greater fragmentation of properties in Fayette County, and to some extent a holdover of plantation
economies in Austin County, although it is also assumed that most of the horse ranches are new wealth
from the Houston area.

341

Figure 141. The Church of God of Prophecy, New Bremen.

Previous Investigations
According to the Historic Texas Sites Atlas, there are presently approximately 100 previously recorded
archaeological sites within a 5 mile radius of the project. Over 60 percent of these are in Fayette County,
which reflects the large amount of work done there, especially around Lake Fayette. The small portion of
Colorado County crossed by the project only had about seven sites within a five mile radius, while Austin
County had the remaining 25-30 percent.
A number of sites have been recorded during systematic projects, which have been both liner and areal in
nature. Information was available on a variety of transmission line projects. In 1979, a survey was
conducted of LCRA’s Bellville South to Waller transmission line. It began at the eastern end of the
present project and ran generally northeastward for about 15 miles. No sites were located, although
provisions were made for the possibility of sites being missed through the limited testing associated with
the project (Pliska 1979).
Also in 1979, the Fayette to Lytton Spring transmission line was surveyed. This began just west of the
present project area and ran for about 60 miles generally west and south west. Thirty-seven prehistoric
sites and 21 historic sites were recorded (Kenmotsu 1980). Prehistoric site types included lithic scatters,
lithic/burned rock scatters, and quarry stations. The scatters were primarily on open sandy knolls and
terrace edges, and only one was found on a creek floodplain. Only three quarry stations were recorded,
suggesting a relative scarcity of suitable gravel deposits. Twelve sites were recommended for “protective
maintenance,” three of these, 41BP176, 41FY165, and 41FY178 after having been tested.
In 1981 the Fayetteville to Glidden transmission line was surveyed. It ran for about 17 miles southward
from Fayetteville. A total of 10 prehistoric sites was recorded, and two were tested (Kenmotsu 1981).
These sites were all located on “sandy alluvial terraces along the margins of ephemeral upland
watercourses” (Kenmotsu 1981: 13) and were considered to represent short-term camps exploiting
seasonal or intermittent upland resources. Sites 41CD80 and 41CD83 were each tested for additional

342

research potential. Neither site was considered to have significant deposits within the ROW, and
remaining sites were considered too disturbed or ephemeral to warrant any additional work.
In 1982, the Fayette to Salem transmission line was surveyed. This ran northwestward from the west end
of the present project for a distance of 25 miles. Two prehistoric sites were recorded, but only limited
shovel probes were excavated. The two sites, 41AU553 and 41FY213, although not considered
significant at the National Register level, were thought to contribute to an “emerging identification of
pattering in prehistoric settlement which emphasized activities and site locations on stream terraces”
(Robinson 1982:9).
In 1989 additional lines between Fayetteville and Salem were surveyed. This project partially paralleled
the present project on the south for about one-third of the western portion before turning north to Salem, a
total distance of about 23 miles, of which about 12.5 miles was considered high probability and was
surveyed (Kotter 1989). A total of 14 sites was recorded, 13 of which were prehistoric. Four of these
sites, 41AU81, 41FY489, 41FY492 and 41WT60 were considered to be potentially eligible and were
recommended for avoidance. The majority of sites were lithic scatters, and it was noted that there was
pronounced absence of sites in clayey soils and in the broad floodplains of Cummins and Mill creeks. At
least one campsite exploited sandstone outcrops present at the site location. Land use on the minor
tributaries was attributed to either lithic procurement or occupations for a “short period of time for a
specific but unknown purpose” (Kotter 1989:47).
Also in 1989 the Winchester to Salem line was surveyed. This 36-mile line paralleled the present project
some distance to the north. About 23 miles was surveyed as high probability areas. A total of 14 sites
was recorded, 13 of which were prehistoric. Site types included lithic scatters, camp sites, and lithic
procurement sites. Both camps were within 50 m of a water source, one on Cummins Creek and on at
Rabbs Creek (Rogers 1990:34). There were only three lithic scatters, once more associated with
unknown extractive activities. Eight sites were lithic procurement areas, which indicates gravel deposits
were relatively common along this transect. Clay soils were once more blamed for what was considered a
relatively low site density.
In 2003 the Salem to Peters transmission line was surveyed. This approximately 40-mile long line ran
north from the eastern end of the present project, and included a double circuited portion of the present
project that runs from Bellville to appoint just south of Mill Creek. A total of 694 shovel tests failed to
find any archaeological sites (Jarvis and Miller 2003). It was felt that the majority of the project was in
non-aggrading pre-Holocene deposits, and that any sites should have been readily visible in upper soil
zones. The heavy clays of the broad Mill Creek floodplain were considered to be unsuitable for
preservation of intact cultural deposits.
Large areal surveys in the vicinity of the project area include those for cooling lakes (Lake Fayette,
Baylor Creek, Allens Creek), a lignite prospect (Cummins Creek) and a proposed reservoir on the
Colorado River. At Allens Creek, roughly 25 miles south of the eastern end of the project, a large
reservoir was surveyed beginning in 1972, resulting in the discovery of thirty-three prehistoric sites,
fifteen of which were recommended for testing (Hall 1981). The results of the testing led to additional
work at three sites, 41AU36, 41AU37 and 41AU38. At 41AU36 a major prehistoric cemetery was
subsequently discovered, and over 230 burials were recorded, many with burial goods. The other two
sites included substantial habitation debris, and at 41AU37, additional burials. Work at this area allowed
inferences on population trends through time to be developed, and also clearly demonstrated sophisticated
interactions between coastal and more central areas of Texas.
The Fayette Power Project resulted in survey of Lake Fayette and surrounding area, with a focus on the
2400-acre Cedar Creek Lake Fayette portion (Skelton 1977). Approximately 60 sites have been recorded

343

at various times since 1975. Six of these were excavated, including a single event bison kill.
Additionally, archival information was gathered on the Biegel Settlement, a largely German endeavor
started in 1832 (Carter and Ragsdale 1975). This report also documented the impact, through
displacement, the Fayette Power project would have on the local community and descendents of the early
settlers.
Cummins Creek, to the north, was at one time slated to become a lignite coal mine that was to provide
fuel for the Fayette power plant. The 13,000 acres associated with this project resulted in the recording of
56 prehistoric sites and 34 historic sites (Nightengale and Jackson 1983). The general area was
hypothesized as a transitional zone between coastal, eastern and central Texas cultural areas. It was also
suggested that resource availability in part determined site distribution and structure, with longer term
camps along Rabbs Creek and its spring-fed tributaries, and lithic procurement areas associated more
with Cummins Creek, which had more readily available raw lithic resources. These site distributions
would remain constant through time, if the resources themselves remained constant. Twenty prehistoric
sites were recommended for additional work to help address these questions. Various historic resources
were also recommended for National Register evaluation. An additional 77 prehistoric sites were
recorded 1983 during survey of an additional 23,000 acres. Forty-nine of these were recommended for
additional work (Nightengale et. al 1985). A management plant (Kotter et. al 1987) was developed to
further aid in addressing expanded research goals based on limited additional testing and mitigation;
however, the mine project was dropped by LCRA, and the additional work was no longer fundable.
Despite this, it was demonstrated that camp sites in the area trend towards creek side environments,
typically just below the crest of an interfluvial divide. Interesting initial findings, such as sandy paste
pottery at Late Archaic site 41FY326 and an apparent single component Scallorn site with higher quality
ceramics remain unaddressed.
In 1983 a proposed reservoir on the Colorado River at Columbus Bend, downstream from the project
area, was surveyed, resulting in the recording of 96 sites, most of which were prehistoric (Keller and
Campbell 1984), who developed a predictive model of site location. It was determined that the Colorado
floodplain was unlikely to contain near-surface sites, except on topographic ridges and relict features, and
that terraces and ridges near tributary drainages and other water resources at higher elevations were more
likely to contain prehistoric sites.
A pair of sites was excavated in the mid 1970s at the intersection of State Highway 71 and Cedar Creek
very near the western end of the present project. Site 41FY25, the Black Hopper Site, on the south side of
the creek, had mixed deposits and hearths with primarily Archaic-aged tools as well as a small number of
Scallorn points (Fullem 1977). It was suggested that during the Archaic period the site was at a juncture
of central, eastern and coastal peoples, based on characteristic traits of the stone tools, whereas the later
Scallorn occupation was now within a distinct cultural zone. The very small sample size of the Scallorn
assemblage (n=4) was recognized, but the hypothesis coincided with (or shaped) that of other researchers.
It clearly carried over into work at Cummins Creek.
Immediately across Cedar Creek from the Black Hopper Site was the Sandbur Site, 41FY135 (Kalter et.
al 2005). This site included Paleoindian points including an apparent Clovis preform, Folsom, Dalton,
Firstview and others, as well as “Angostura-like” points that have recently been a subject of interest
(TxArch archives) and numerous other Archaic-aged tools. However, it was only the late Prehistoric
Perdiz component that was felt to be intact. The Perdiz points were suggestive of “Classic Toyah,” while
the ceramics seemed to represent a more eastern tradition, with petrographic analysis showing production
on local clays and qualitative characteristics similar to other sites on the Backland Prairie and Coastal
Plains. If the stone toolkit and ceramic assemblage were manufactured by the same people, the argument
appears to be that it was a Toyah stone toolkit that had been adopted by a regionalized group, perhaps

344

including the Sanan, of eastern Texas, rather than plains Toyah people having infiltrated that far east
(Kalter et. al 2005 :219, 220).
Site 41AU1 was located about 1 km downstream from the T-216 crossing of Mill Creek, according to the
online Historic Texas Sites Atlas. Recorded in 1959, it consisted of at least 20, and possibly as many as
26 prehistoric burials associated with marine shell ornaments. Other reported artifacts included Pandale
points, Perdiz points, and ceramic sherds. The site had been heavily damaged and mostly destroyed, and
apparently Mill Creek had been channelized in that area. The site setting was very similar to that of the
upstream transmission line crossing – the junction of a small stream and the larger Mill Creek. As noted
above, survey and shovel tests by Hicks and Co. in 2003 found no sites on that portion of the transmission
line.
Site 41AU83 was recorded in 1995 prior to construction of the New Bremen substation. On the Historic
Texas Sites Atlas it is described as a very light prehistoric lithic scatter and a scatter of historic debris
associated with a house once located upslope. The site was felt to have very low research potential and
no further investigations were recommended. This site was not revisited during the present survey.
In 2004 LCRA Archaeology Services conducted reconnaissance survey for regularly scheduled
maintenance (tree trimming, shredding) along the transmission line (Hixson et. al 2006). Historic site
41FY546, a cluster of farm implements along the edge of the ROW, was recorded at that time. It was
near an old farm complex that could not be recorded due to its location outside the ROW.
Recommendations were for avoidance due to the unknown nature of the remainder of the site. This site
essentially overlaps site 41FY552, recorded during the present survey, but is left separate for management
purposes. Site 41FY546 was not formally revisited, although it was examined and no changes were
noted. Recommendations remain unchanged.
Summary of Previous Investigations
Previous work in the area allows a number of variables to be considered in prospecting unknown territory.
Lithic scatters seem to be quite common, and are often located near fairly small streams. Larger sites that
are clearly camps are much less frequent, while lithic procurement seems to be coincident with the
occurrence of gravels. Procurement gravels may be more common to the north of the project area. A
variety of resources seems to have pushed exploitation zones away from some of the larger streams, such
as Cummins Creek, which despite their size are intermittent and certainly lack the clear spring water seen
on a number of very small streams during the present project. Additionally, previous work is in fairly
close agreement about the preference for sites to be located in sandy soils, and a general lack of sites in
clay soils, regardless of setting. Sandy sites, meanwhile, are subject to disturbances, especially through
the burrowing of rodents, creating problems with site integrity.
Historic sites could date to the 1830s. Very early sites would likely be of German or southern heritage,
and slave activities could be represented. Later sites could exhibit Czech influence. Small farmsteads are
almost as likely to be tenant farms as not.

Research Design and Field Methods
The project was designed to identify sites along the previously unsurveyed portions of the transmission
line and assess the potential of any such sites for formal listing as State Archeological Landmarks or
National Register eligibility. In order to facilitate and structure the field work, a map review of
previously recorded sites conducted. Geological maps and soils data was examined, and physiographic
information was extrapolated from topographic maps. From this high probability areas (HPAs) were

345

determined, and a rating system devised to further refine the HPAs. The factors were based primarily on
geology, with Holocene floodplain terraces receiving a high rating; proximity to water sources, with
larger streams adjacent to well-defined terraces or interfluvial divides rated higher; and soils, which when
divided by clays, loams, and sand, were rated from low to high, respectively. Additionally, some soils
were described as having buried A horizons, for which additional points were added. Thus, stream-side
terraces with sandy soils containing buried A horizons would have a very high rating, while upland
divides with clay soils far from water sources would have a very low probability rating. These ratings
were tempered by field observations. As an example, 41FY554 was not within a proposed HPA, due
primarily to shallow mapped soils and distance from a major water source, but when approaching it from
the Cummins Creek HPA, it was obvious this landform needed to be tested, and indeed, a site was located
there. Other exceptions resulted from analysis of historic maps, which were georeferenced with modern
topographic maps and imagery, and the project then overlaid. Due to alignment issues associated with
differing scales, datums, and coordinate systems, structures mapped near the line were considered
candidates for being within the area of effect. In actuality, ground truthing demonstrated that plots from
early maps fell very closely to the modern interpolation, and no structures appear to be within the
transmission line corridor.
Field work was conducted by crews of two to five archaeologists walking meandering transects within the
transmission line ROW. Shovel tests were placed both systematically and judgmentally within proposed
and expanded HPAs. These tests were excavated in arbitrary 20 cm levels and screened through ¼
hardware cloth. The HPA calculations also indicated the single, unsurveyed broad Holocene floodplain at
Cummins Creek would be primarily heavy clay soils with potentially deep horizons. It was anticipated
that the preceding hot, dry summer would make hand testing of this landform impractical, if not
impossible. In this, and a couple of other select settings, a skidsteer mounted with a hydraulic 12 inch (30
cm) auger was employed. Vertical control is more difficult, so it was used mostly for a presence/absence
determination.
All test locations were recorded on GPS and transcribed later to GIS. Records include sketch maps, field
notes, and shovel test records. Ground orientation was by USGS topographic maps and aerial imagery.
All artifacts were analyzed in the field and returned to their findspots. Sites were later documented on the
standardized TexSite database and submitted to the Texas Archeological Research laboratory (TARL)
where permanent trinomials were assigned. This project is reported here as an interim report under the
terms of LCRA TAC Annual Permit No. 4779, and a final report will be included in the year-end report
of investigations submitted to the Texas Historical Commission.

Results of Investigations
Ten previously unrecorded archaeological sites were documented as a result of this project. Nine were
prehistoric, and one was a historic trash scatter. These ten sites, as well as two previously recorded sites,
are discussed briefly below. Sketch map figures for each are included at the end of this section.
41FY552 (Figure 142)
Site 41FY552 is an extensive but discontinuous lithic scatter containing chipping debris, burned chert and
quartzite, and at least one tool. It is located in far northeastern Fayette County across a broad interfluvial
lobe between Cliff Creek and another unnamed left bank tributary of Cummins Creek, which is about 700
m to the southeast. It is in Knolle loamy sand that formed on the tertiary Fleming formation, which is
primarily clay and sandstone.
The Willow Springs substation, owned by Fayette Electric Coop, sits on the high point of the divide, and
roughly marks the site center. The densest part of the site is located east of the substation. A shovel test

346

(ST 3) and three auger tests (ATs 21-23) were placed at about 50 m intervals. Only the shovel test had
substantial recovery, with about 120 pieces of chert debitage, mostly interior flakes with a very few pieces
of burned or shattered chert. About one-half of the material was within the upper 20 cm, the remainder in
the 40 cm below. A chert core and a small biface fragment were recovered from between 40 and 60 cm.
The soil profile consisted of about 40 cm of grayish-brown sandy loam that became mottled with orangish
clay below 40 cm, grading to sandy clay below 50 cm. The three auger tests had much less in the way of
recovery, (AT 21 was negative, and the other two had a total of 17 flakes and chips between them).
Profiles were similar, with clay encountered between 20 and 60 cm. Although the area around ST 3
seemed to have darker surface soil, possibly indicating a higher organic content, this entire half of the site
had been subjected to intensive agricultural terracing. Further to the east, the sands gave way to dark
clays along the Cliff Creek terrace, and erosional features in this area and the creek banks showed no
evidence of cultural materials.
To the west of the substation there was less evidence of ground disturbance (terracing), but five auger
tests (ATs 16 - 20) found only five chips, and three of the tests were negative. The sandy soils did not
exceed about 30 cm in depth before clays were encountered. As the western edge of the site was reached
soils disappeared completely, replaced by cobbly clay. A couple of tested cobles and one semi-formal
scaper were seen in this area.
Along the fenced west edge of the substation was a very light scatter of historic materials that consisted of
three or four white ceramics and some thick brown glass. This may be associated with a structure seen on
the 1919 Fayetteville quad, which appears to have been located to the north, out of the ROW. This
historic scatter is considered to be destroyed by substation construction, and so is not considered to be a
formal component to 41FY552. Also, corresponding with the western edge of 41FY552 is previously
recorded 41FY546, a collection of farm implements within the ROW associated with an obviously old
ranch or farmstead (outside the ROW), also present on the 1919 map. This site is kept separate for
management purposes.
Site 41FY552 is a lithic scatter and possible open campsite located on a sandy ridge above a permanent
water source. A small area of higher activity is present on the eastern portion of the site in the vicinity of
ST 3. However, extensive contouring of the landform has resulted in disturbances that would make any
further conclusions highly suspect. The portion of the site within the ROW is considered to be fully
documented and not eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the
National Register of Historic Places. No further work is recommended within the existing transmission
line corridor.

41FY553 (Figure 143)
Site 41FY553 is a light historic trash scatter on the left bank of Cliff Creek, just east of 41FY552. Cliff
Creek is aptly named, as the banks are steep and perhaps 30 ft above the creek channel. Deep erosional
gullies have formed along the bank. Within these gullies and for about 200m eastwards into cleared
pasture is a diffuse scatter of white stoneware, dark green glass, thick dark brown glass, a single piece of
purple glass, and nails, including apparent forged, cut and wire nails. No structures were mapped there in
1919, although there are some within 500 to 1,000 m, and some of these are still on the modern USGS
quad.
This appears to be an incidental trash dump, but there may have once been much more along the noweroded creek banks. The mixture of probable pre-1880s material with that dating much later in time may
represent various disposal events, or a single event associated with cleanup elsewhere. Regardless, there
is little to no interpretive potential at this site, and it is not considered eligible for formal listing as a State

347

Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. No further work is
recommended within the existing transmission line corridor.
41FY554 (Figure 144)
Site 41FY554 is a buried lithic and burned rock scatter located on a sandy lobe overlooking the Cummins
Creek floodplain to the east. It is within mapped Straber loamy fine sand on the early Pleistocene Willis
formation, which consists of gravel, sand, silt and clay. The site was defined by four shovel tests (STs 47) and an auger test (AT 2). The three positive tests had no more than 12 flakes each, but up to 90 pieces
of fire fractured chert and quartzite. These were almost exclusively very small, no more than one or two
cm in any one dimension. Soils consisted of about 30 cm of gravelly and sandy loams over sandy clay or
gravels. Within the ROW, the site is about 100 m in length.
The site is interpreted as a probable open campsite located in well-drained sandy soils overlooking the
heavy dark clays of the lower Cummins Creek floodplain. Within the ROW, the lack of soil depth, lack
of temporal or functional diagnostics, and the overall disturbed nature of the open pasture severely limit
the interpretive potential of the site. It is not considered eligible for formal listing as a State
Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. No further work is
recommended within the existing transmission line corridor.
41FY555 (Figure 145)
Site 41FY555 is a very discrete buried lithic scatter located on an apparent levee formation adjacent to an
overflow (and probable relict) channel of Cummins Creek. It is within Degola loam in recent alluvium.
Degola soils are described as potentially having three sequential A horizons to a depth of about 1 m, with
weathered B horizons extending to a depth of about 2 m.
The broad floodplain where the site is located becomes marshy east of the site, and immediately to the
west is a low swale which is bordered by another apparent (and older) levee. The two formations are
separated by about 140 m. Two auger tests (ATs 7, 8) were placed within the site. Both were sterile until
a depth of 60 cm, when small amounts of lithic debitage and burned shatter were recovered to a final
depth of 130 cm. Greatest recovery was between 80 and 100 cm (although there was only a total of five
chips or flakes and 14 pieces of burned shatter). Notably, the soil profile consists of brown to very dark
gray sandy clay, but at about 80 cm became a gray sandy clay loam, consistent with the NRCS description
of stratified gray to white sandy clay loam lower layers.
No tests were placed in the swale to the west, and an auger test on the next levee (or terrace edge) was
negative. The site is therefore interpreted as a discrete scatter of lithic debitage and burned rock, possibly
marking an open camp that was likely once on a relatively well drained bank of Cummins Creek.
Subsurface materials may be intact, and final depth of soil or cultural material remains unknown. It is
therefore recommended that this narrow (25 m wide) ridge be avoided by any construction activities that
would impact soils below a depth of 50 cm (about 2 ft). In other words, no structure should be placed
here. If that is not possible, then additional subsurface investigations (most likely by backhoe) are
warranted in order to determine if archaeological materials are present that could be eligible for formal
listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places.
41AU90 (Figure 146)
Site 41AU90 is a buried lithic scatter located in a discrete lobe that is part of a ridge system overlooking
Black Walnut Creek to the west. A smallish tributary parallels the site to the south. The site is in the
Tertiary clay and sandstone Fleming formation, and within mapped Catilla loamy fine sands. These
upland soils can be 40 to 70 in thick, and have a subsoil consisting of light yellowish brown to light gray
sandy clay loam.

348

The site was defined by five shovel tests (STs 13-15, 17, 18), four of which were positive. Shovel tests
13, 14, and 15 all had very light recovery, consisting of a total of 12 chip or flakes between them.
However, recovery started at 40 cm and continued to 100. Shovel Test 17 had a single chip in the upper
20 cm, and is not considered to be part of the site. The soil profile within the site was consistent with the
soils description, with yellowish brown sandy loam to light gray loamy sand encountered to the maximum
depth allowed by hand testing. The tests on either side of the site had mottled sandy loams grading to
sandy clays at depths no greater than 50 cm.
The site is interpreted as a lithic scatter and possible open camp that follows an apparent trend of favoring
well-drained sandy ridges overlooking streams rather than lower and generally more clayey stream banks
for active use areas. The relatively small amount of material shows no ready vertical patterning, and the
soils have been highly turbated by animal activities. Additionally, the lack of tools or evidence of
features makes any worthwhile additional investigations at this site highly improbable. 41AU91 is not
considered eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National
Register of Historic Places. No further work is recommended within the existing transmission line
corridor.
41AU91 (Figure 147)
Site 41AU91 is a buried lithic scatter located directly west of 41AU90, on the other side of Black Walnut
Creek. It is within the same Catilla fine sandy loam. The site is located on the early Pleistocene Willis
formation, formed of gravel, sand, silt and clay.
The site was defined by seven shovel tests (STs 16 19-21, 36, 37) with the last, ST37, falling outside the
site’s western boundary. The eastern boundary was determined through observation of thinning soils and
erosional exposures towards Black Walnut Creek. Recovery was relatively moderate, with a total of
about 50 flakes and chips and a very few pieces of burned material recovered from the six positive tests
(although only two were recovered from the westernmost, ST36, and ST16, on the center of the lobe in
the eastern portion of the site had about 12 flakes per 20 cm level). Recovery was fairly consistent
throughout the soil column, which ranged from about 60 cm of fine sandy loam over mottled sandy clay
to 100 or 120 cm of fine sandy loam (no subsoil encountered).
The site, like 41AU90, is interpreted as a lithic scatter and possible open camp that follows an apparent
trend of favoring well-drained sandy ridges overlooking streams rather than lower and generally more
clayey stream banks for active use areas. The relatively small amount of material shows no ready vertical
patterning, and the soils have been highly turbated by animal activities. Additionally, the lack of tools or
evidence of features makes any worthwhile additional investigations at this site highly improbable. Site
41AU91 is not considered eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to
the National Register of Historic Places. No further work is recommended within the existing
transmission line corridor.
41AU92 (Figure 149)
41AU92 is a buried lithic scatter located on a broad sloping terrace between two right bank tributaries of
Sandy Creek, which in this area has formed a narrow Holocene floodplain. The site, however, is in the
early Pleistocene sand, silt gravel and clay Willis formation overlooking the younger floodplain. Soils are
of the Tremona loamy fine sand series, a deep upland soil that can be very gravelly and grade to gray
sandy clays and white sandy clay loam below 20-40 in.
The site was defined by four shovel tests, STs 23-26, with the last outside the site to the west. Further
west, a drainage with excellent visibility in erosional features had no evidence of cultural materials, and
previous testing to the east (ST9), in the vicinity of Sandy Creek found no evidence of cultural material.
Only STs 23 and 24 were positive for cultural material. Shovel Test 23, placed near an isolated surface

349

artifact (a small core) had two chips in Level 1 then no recovery until Levels 4 and 5, where a total of
nine small chips and a small biface edge were covered. Shovel Test 24 had a single chip in Level 1. Soils
were a mottled yellowish-brown fine sandy loam that encountered consolidated orangish clay or heavy
clay mottling at 90, 30, 65, and 70 cm, respectively.
Site 41AU92 is felt to have very limited research potential within the ROW, and is considered fully
investigated. It is not considered eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or
nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. No further work is recommended within the
existing transmission line corridor.
41AU93 (Figure 150)
Site 41AU93 is a buried lithic scatter on the edge of a dissected ridge over looking Sandy Creek to the
west. It is within Straber loamy fine sands that formed in the Willis formation. The site was defined by
three shovel tests (STs 40-41) with ST42 defining the eastern boundary and the sloping landform defining
the western boundary in that direction.
The site appears to be centered on the area where ST 41 was excavated, which is a low rise near the edge
of the lobed ridge. Two chips were recovered from Level 1 and a chunk and a chip from Level 3. Shovel
Test 40 had two flake fragments and a fragment of a crude uniface. Soils were deepest at ST 41, with
light yellowish brown fine sandy loam grading to dark brown fine sandy loam at about 50 cm, and fairly
dense manganese concretions appearing at depth. The other two tests had no more than about 30 cm of
sandy soil over sandy clays.
The site is a discrete occurrence of buried cultural material, however there is little chance of there being
additional research potential within the ROW. 41AU93 is considered fully investigated. It is not
considered eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National
Register of Historic Places. No further work is recommended within the existing transmission line
corridor.
41AU94 (Figure 150)
Site 41AU94 is another very isolated lithic scatter located immediately west of 41AU93, on the opposite
side of Sandy Creek. Soils are mapped as Bosque clay loam, although the shovel tests suggest it is more
likely the Newulm loamy fine sand which is found immediately to the west. The site is on the mapped
boundary of the Willis and Fleming formation. The site was defined by two shovel tests (STs 43 –
positive; and 44 – negative) and exposures of Sandy Creek to the east.
Recovery was limited to a single chip in Level 1 and a single chip in Level 2. The profile in both tests
was similar, with about 20 cm of light brown sandy loam transitioning through mottled yellowish-brown
sandy loam that became yellowish brown sandy loam below 40 cm and continued to final depths of 100
cm. Absolute soil depth remains unknown.
This minor lithic scatter is considered fully documented within the ROW. It is not considered eligible for
formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic
Places. No further work is recommended within the existing transmission line corridor.
41AU95 (Figure 150)
Site 41AU95 consists of a single secondary flake found in ST 32 and two chips and a small core fragment
found in ST 30. It is within Straber loamy fine sands in the Fleming formation. An additional test placed
to the east (ST31) and examination of the sloping landform and excavation of ST33 to the west failed to
find additional material. The site is located on an extended interfluvial lobe between two short left bank
tributaries of Dry Creek.

350

Shovel Test 32 found the secondary flake at a depth of about 100 cm, resting on sandy clay at the bottom
of light yellowish brown and light brown fine sandy loams. Shovel Test 30 had a chip in Level 1 and
another chip and the core fragment in Level 4. Some weathered bone fragments were recovered from
Levels 1 and 2, but these cannot be assigned to any time period. The soil profile in the three tests on the
lobe consisted of loamy fine sand sands to depths of 70, 50, and 100 cm, respectively. Shovel Test 33,
placed near the westernmost drainage, had only 10 cm of light brown fine sandy loam over mottled
orange and sandy clay.
41AU95 is considered fully documented within the ROW. It is not considered eligible for formal listing
as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. No further
work is recommended within the existing transmission line corridor.
Previously Recorded Sites
Site 41AU83 was recorded in 1995 prior to construction of the New Bremen substation. On the Historic
Texas Sites Atlas it is described as a very light prehistoric lithic scatter and a scatter of historic debris
associated with a house once located upslope. The site was felt to have very low research potential and
no further investigations were recommended. This site was not revisited during the present survey.
In 2004 LCRA Archaeology Services conducted reconnaissance survey for regularly scheduled
maintenance (tree trimming, shredding) along the transmission line (Hixson et. al 2006). Historic site
41FY546, a cluster of farm implements along the edge of the ROW, was recorded at that time. It was
near an old farm complex that could not be recorded due to its location outside the ROW.
Recommendations were for avoidance due to the unknown nature of the remainder of the site. This site
essentially overlaps site 41FY552, recorded during the present survey, but is left separate for management
purposes. Site 41FY546 was not formally revisited, although it was examined and no changes were
noted. Recommendations remain unchanged.
Other Areas
Cummins Creek Floodplain
The Cummins Creek floodplain was explored systematically through mechanical augering, although
visual analysis of the general landform indicated that the low lying, heavy clay soils were not conducive
to preservation of cultural resource sites, or prehistoric settlement, for that matter. As noted in the
previous investigations section, other researchers in the area have made similar comments.
Cummins/Mill Creek Divide
This was the most uplandish portion of the project, and was spot checked accordingly. Despite a small
spring seen at Willow Branch (of Poplar Creek) there was no evidence of archaeological materials within
the highly visible soils. The east side of Poplar Creek was low and boggy, and was considered to be of
low probability. These were the last streams on the Cummins Creek side of the divide. The first stream
associated with the Mill Creek drainage was the headwaters of Pastoren Creek. This section was not
surveyed.
Elsewhere, especially in Austin County, extremely fine loamy sands had extensive rodent burrowing
which provided excellent proxy subsurface visibility. At least two sites were located through artifacts
within backdirt piles. However, sites were also located in areas where such backdirt piles had no artifacts,
but these were very minor lithic scatters. Such burrowing remains a good way to locate sites with larger
artifact assemblages.

351

Discussion
A number of previous studies indicated that lithic scatter not associated with raw material procurement
activities are commonly at some distance from water. These distances were not quantified, however. For
the newly recorded prehistoric sites, the distance from water, both linear and elevational, was measured,
using either the site centroid or activity nucleus as the spot from which distances were measured. The
appropriate USGS quad maps were used for elevations, although GIS-based stream data from TexDOT
superseded the USGS map as the nearest water source. The distance and change in elevation allowed an
index to be established, which is equivalent to the percentage of the slope (Table 28).
The average distance from water is 132 m, the average change is elevation is 9 m, and the average slope is
7.2 percent. Although no clear patterns emerge form this comparison, the outlier, 41FY555, does stand
out both for distance from and elevation above water. However, this site probably once fronted Cummins
Creek, and so may be non-representative. Otherwise it is still remarkable that no site was closer than 60
m from a water source. Post depositional factors such as floods may account for this, but also past
environmental conditions, such was vegetation and perhaps mosquitoes may also play a role.

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA propose to rebuild the existing Fayetteville to Bellville South (T-216) transmission line located in
Fayette, Colorado and Austin counties. The 28-mile long line is within an average 85 ft-wide ROW,
resulting in a total of approximately 288 acres. The extreme eastern three miles, between what is referred
to as the Bellville Split and the Bellville South substation was previously surveyed by Hicks and
Company for a similar project. No archeological sites were recorded as a result of that investigation, and
the project was allowed to proceed. This section was not resurveyed.

The remaining 25 miles was assessed by LCRA Archaeology Services staff, with a focus on high
probability areas that were determined by a combination of factors including underlying geology, soils,
terrain, and historic maps tempered by field observations. Just over 10 miles was intensively surveyed.
A total of 68 subsurface tests was excavated, about 40 percent of the recommended minimum for that
length. This number is felt to be sufficient based on large areas of excellent visibility, examination of
exposures such as erosional cuts and especially extensive burrowing by rodents in sandy soils, as well as

352

the demonstrated general lack of sites in broad clay-filled floodplains similar to that encountered at
Cummins Creek.
Ten previously unrecorded archaeological sites were documented as a result of the investigation. Two
previously recorded sites were not formally revisited (Table 29).
Sites 41FY552, 554, 555 and 41AU90-95 are all prehistoric lithic scatters with varying amounts of firecracked rock. These are low density scatters, with the exceptions of 41FY552 and 41FY554, which both
contain moderates amounts of materials. All the newly recorded prehistoric sites except for 41FY555 are
in sandy soils and have been highly disturbed and/or contain so little in the way of visible cultural
material as to make additional investigations of little value. These sites are considered fully recorded
within the ROW and ineligible for formal listing as State Archeological Landmarks or nomination to the
National Register of Historic Places.
Site 41FY555 appears to be an exception to the sandy site premise. It is located on a low terrace remnant
or levee formation on the south side of Cummins Creek. Cultural materials were recovered from depths
below 60 cm, with the majority of material between 80 and 100 cm. This site may have structural
integrity, and therefore possibly has additional research value. Until such potential can be discounted, it
should be considered potentially eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark and/or
nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. This site is recommended for avoidance by deep
soil disturbance (i.e., structure placement). If the site cannot be avoided than additional investigations are
warranted.
Site 41FY553 is an historic trash scatter located in a pasture adjacent to a steep-walled creek. Although
some materials may predate the 1880s, others are clearly from the twentieth century. This ephemeral
dump is not considered eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark and/or nomination
to the National Register of Historic Places.

Previously recorded site 41FY546 is a cluster of farm implements associated with a nearby farmstead that
almost certainly predates 1900. The implements themselves are not considered particularly significant,
but their association with the otherwise unknown farmstead is sufficient to warrant their avoidance by
construction impacts. Their location in a group of low oak trees on the extreme edge of the ROW should
not hinder transmission line construction.

353

Previously recorded site 41AU83 was a multicomponent site consisting of a light lithic scatter and
historic trash. This site, which was determined ineligible, was recorded previous to construction of the
New Bremen substation, and is now considered destroyed within the ROW.
This survey was useful in that produced additional, largely confirmative information about prehistoric
settlement in this part of the Colorado and Brazos river valleys. Additional work in the area should
consider the potential for more substantive sites to be located in upland zones that are relatively distant
from major water sources. Such sites, if functional or time diagnostic implements are found, could help
continue to address major research questions that have been previously posed.

Cultural Resource Investigations of LCRA’s Proposed Raymond F. Barker to Verde Creek (T-507)
Transmission Line Upgrade Project, Kerr County, Texas

By
Andrew F. Malof and George B. Thomas
Andrew F. Malof
Principal Investigator

TAC Permit No. 4779
Interim Report No. 15.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Parks and Resource Conservation
November, 2008

372

Management Summary:
A planned upgrade of a 1.91 mile section of LCRA electrical transmission line between the Verde Creek
and Raymond F. Barker substations, Kerr County, Texas (Figure 151), will involve replacing existing
lattice towers with single cement or metal pole structures. These are placed within holes (direct embed)
or upon cement foundations poured into holes between 4 and 8 ft in diameter and 15 to 25 ft deep. New
structures will be typically placed within 50 ft of existing structures. The modifications will not change
the current 100-ft right-of-way width or alignment. Total project area is approximately 23 acres.
The project area has been previously surveyed. First in 1981 during initial construction (Moncure 1981)
and then in 2003 prior to another upgrade planned at that time (Malof 2003, Malof et. al 2005). Three
sites, 41KR200, 41KR602 and 41KR603 are located within the present project area. Site 41KR200 was
recommended for partial avoidance when recorded in 1981. Sites 41KR602 and 41KR603 were
considered ineligible when recorded in 2003. The present project therefore focused on 41KR200, and on
the broad floodplain south of the Guadalupe River which was not considered to be fully investigated.
Field work was conducted on November 6, 2008, by a crew consisting of LCRA archaeologist Andrew
Malof and subcontractors George Thomas, Christine Gauger, and Patrick Hatten. The crew dug six
shovel tests in all. The tests on site 41 KR 200 were both 50 cm in diameter and controlled by 10 cm.
levels. Elsewhere the tests were approximately 30 cm. in diameter and controlled by 20 cm. levels. Soil
color and texture were recorded for each shovel test profile. Artifacts were recorded by level and
morphology and returned to the shovel tests during backfilling. As no artifacts were collected no curation
will be necessary. Due to the nature and extent of previous investigations and reports the short format
report as suggested by the Council of Texas Archeologists guidelines is used here.

Study Area
The project area is in eastern Kerr County. The Raymond F. Barker and Verde Creek substations are both
located in Glen Rose Formation limestone north and south, respectively, of the Guadalupe River. The
river at this location has formed a Holocene-aged fluviatile terrace some 1.3 km wide. North of the river
this terrace is in active cultivation. There are some very large sand and gravel operations present a couple
of miles to the west, and a smaller pit is also present immediately west of the transmission line. To the
south of the river the terrace is enclosed by a high game fence. Exotic game is kept on large ranch here.
The pasture was almost certainly once under cultivation as well. Online SSURGO data shows
terrace/floodplain soils as Barbarosa and Oakalla silty clay loam as well as a narrow strip of Boerne fine
sandy loam along the southern river bank.

Research Design
The project required a combination of a review of previous projects and field investigations. The area for
field work was at 41KR200, which because it can no longer be avoided, required additional evaluation,
and across a broad (about 400 m under the transmission line) floodplain terrace south of the Guadalupe
River which does not appear to ever have been shovel tested, although it had been walked over in 2003.
Combined, these areas contain three transmission lattice support structures numbered 104, 105 and 106.
Work was designed to make a definitive decision of the integrity and research value of site 41KR200 and
to identify and evaluate any other previously unrecorded sites on the southern floodplain that might be
affected by the transmission line upgrade project. All other areas were considered to be fully investigated
by previous surveys, and no additional work or revisits to sites 41KR602 and 41KR603 were conducted.

373

Figure 151. Project area on Center Point USGS quad.

374

Results
The 1.91 mile section to be upgraded crosses the Guadalupe River approximately one mile upstream
(west) of the town of Center Point. At the crossing location the transmission line bisects previouslyrecorded prehistoric site 41 KR 200 above the north bank on the second terrace (Moncure 1981: 19-20).
The site is impacted by tower structure 106, low-density vehicular traffic, a recently-formed gravel
vehicle trail leading to two warehouse structures, and a borrow pit immediately below.
Shovel testing at and near structure 105, on the south bank terrace of the Guadalupe River, led to the
identification of a new site (41KR674) on the terrace edge and on both sides of the road leading eastward
to Center Point.
Additional shovel testing was conducted at structure 104, located at the southern edge of the broad
southern terrace. No artifacts were recovered.
41KR674
Site 41KR674 is located on the first stable terrace south of the Guadalupe River (Figures 152, 153). It is
covered in dense coastal Bermuda grass, and drops abruptly into the active floodplain of the river just
north of STR 105. Three shovel tests were excavated, the first (ST 3) just south of the structure, the
second (ST 4) at the lattice structure center, and the third (ST 5) in open pasture on the south side of the
road.

Figure 152. Site 41KR674, looking generally north. Site extends from large oak tree to lattice structure at
terrace edge.
Shovel Test 3 was located at STR 105, south of the Guadalupe River between the structure and the road
leading into the town of Center Point. Here the test passed through 18 cm. of road gravels before
reaching undisturbed, light-brown silty loam, with a deposit of water-rolled gravels at 80 cm. An edgemodified uniface fragment, a chert flake and chert fragments were located between 20 and 40 cm. in
depth. There was no other recovery.
Shovel Test 4 was placed at the center of Structure 105, a short distance north of the road and away from
the modern road gravel deposit. Here several chert chunks and a mussel shell fragment were mingled

375

Figure 153. Site 41KR674.

376

with brown glass sherds in the upper 20 cm. One chunky flake was in the second 20 cm, and a wire nail
was located between 40 and 60 cm. in depth, just above the river gravel layer at 60 cm. Shovel Test 5
was located across the road and south of Shovel Test 4, across a game fence and gate. It yielded two
chert flake fragments between 20 and 40 cm in depth in brown, silty loam overlying river gravels at 70
cm. There was no other recovery.
Elsewhere, a single chert core was seen in disturbed soil along the fence boundary on the south side of the
road. No other artifacts were noted.
41KR674 is a light lithic scatter in disturbed context. It is considered to be to be fully documented and
ineligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of
Historic places.
41KR200
Special attention was paid to 41KR200, which was recommended for partial avoidance prior to
transmission line construction (Figures 154, 155). Shovel tests were 50 cm in diameter and excavated in
10 cm levels in order to develop a greater level of control than typical 30-40 cm diameter testes excavated
in 20 cm levels. The first test (ST 1) was placed adjacent to the end of the presumed location of the
previous backhoe trench (Moncure 1981: Figure 6). Recovery was moderate, with the greatest recovery
between 30 and 40 cm, where 32 flake fragments were recovered along with two relatively large pieces of
burned limestone. The only tool that was recovered was a burned edge modified flake from Level 1. The
profile was 16 cm of dark yellowish brown heavy clay loam over 7 cm of dark brown heavy clay loam
followed by yellowish brown sandy clay then dense limestone cobbles in sandy clay (Figure 156). The
test was excavated to a final depth of 50 cm. The soils and artifact profile is consistent with the profile
from Moncure’s trench as well as the mapped Barbarosa series soils.
Shovel Test 2 was placed on the edge of the ROW where it was perceived the site may have been more
intact or dense. Level 4 once more had the greatest recovery, with 29 flake fragments, a core fragment,
an edge modified flake, two large hard hammer flakes, and a small amount of burned rock. An apparent
biface fragment was recovered from Level 3, and there were 25 small flake fragments in Level 1. Soils
were once again consistent with the mapped Barbarosa series.
While working at the structure the crew was approached by the new landowner. He was well aware of the
presence of the site, and had been informed that an “Indian mound” had been bulldozed and spread across
the surrounding pasture by the previous owner, accounting for the widespread occurrence of
archaeological materials. He also pointed westward and mentioned a spring where apparently much more
material has been located.
Based on recovery from the shovel tests, the conversation with the landowner and Moncure’s somewhat
ambiguous recommendation that the backhoe trench had “no clear stratigraphic integrity…and [t]he vast
majority of the site [was] located outside the corridor” (Moncure 1981:36) site 41KR200 is considered to
be largely destroyed, at least within the ROW. The portion within the ROW is therefore now considered
to be fully documented and ineligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination
to the National Register of Historic Places.

377

Figure 154. Site 41KR200, south end detail. Site extends northward from road and covers entire field.

378

Figure 155. Excavating ST 1 at STR 106, 41KR200. Private shed is outside ROW. Looking generally
south towards the Guadalupe River and 41KR647.

Figure 156. Limestone cobbles in bottom of ST 1.
Structure 104
Structure 104 s located at the southern edge of a large pasture that forms the same terrace as that which
contains 41KR674. It is located at the base of limestone-based hills that extend for the remainder of the
project in that direction. A single shovel test was excavated within the mapped Oakalla series soil. It had
40 cm of dark brown loam, over another 40 cm of brownish clay or heavy clay loam. The test was
terminated at a depth of 80 cm. No artifacts were recovered. No more work is recommended at this
structure location
Summary and Recommendations
LCRA proposes to rebuild a 1.91 mi section of transmission line between the existing Raymond F. Barker
and Verde Creek substations in eastern Kerr County, Texas. The project will require replacement of

379

existing lattice towers with new single pole structures, typically within 50 ft of the old one. The line has
been surveyed and assessed at various times, most notably during the original construction phase
(Moncure 1981) and then in 2003 as part of a larger rebuild project (Malof 2003, Malof et. al 2005). All
previous work within the presently proposed project area had been cleared, including at sites 41KR602
and 41KR603. Site 41KR200 has been recommended for avoidance during maintenance and upgrade
projects.
Site 41KR200 was reinvestigated with the knowledge that a new transmission line support structure
would be necessary at the location of existing STR 106. Two shovel tests were excavated and compared
with the results of a previous backhoe trench. Although archaeological material is buried to depths of 4050 cm within the ROW, there is little to suggest that additional investigations would yield additional
information. Further, the present landowner states that the majority of the site was bulldozed and
subsequently plowed across the surrounding large agricultural field. Within the ROW, site 41KR200 is
now considered to be lacking in the integrity that would make it eligible for formal listing as a State
Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places.
Site 41KR674 was recorded at the location of STR 105, on the south bank of the Guadalupe River. It is a
light scatter of buried lithic material with a single chert core on the surface. This site is considered fully
recorded and ineligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the
National Register of Historic Places.
Structure 104 is located at the extreme edge of a large pasture south of the Guadalupe River. A single
shovel test found dense clay loams and no artifacts.
It is recommended that construction be allowed to proceed with the understanding that new support
structures will be placed within 50 ft of existing structures 104 and 105. Elsewhere, it is recommended
there be no constraints on the placement of such structures. If structures are required outside the proposed
50-ft radius then additional investigations should be considered at those locations.

Part 2. Negative Surveys on Projects Less Than 2 Acres in Size, Transmission Line Maintenance
Projects, and Projects Cleared through File Searches
This section contains the texts of the results of 21 cultural resource assessments for minor LCRA
construction/development projects. Included are the summaries of reports of 15 small cultural resource
field surveys that yielded negative results, and were each less than 2 acres in area, or were previously
agreed-upon monitoring. The AD at the THC has not previously reviewed the results of these 15
investigations. The results of six file searches that indicated that proposed development areas had been
previously surveyed with negative results are also found in this section.
Additionally, the survey reports on 21 LCRA transmission line maintenance and clearing projects are
presented in this section. Five of these required field surveys and 16 were cleared through file searches.
The LCRA Archeology Services staff has continued conducting cultural resource assessments on the
maintenance and clearing projects as in previous years in accordance with the Memorandum of
Understanding (MOU) in effect since 2005. Information on archeological sites discovered during these
surveys has not previously been reviewed by the AD, so detailed locational data is provided in this
Annual Report, but redacted in versions available to the public.

Project No.

1.08

County:

Project Description:

Camp Balcones Trail and Road Maintenance

Figure References:

3, 157

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

January 2008

Camp Balcones Springs has requested they be allowed to conduct road maintenance on existing roads and
create a hiking trail for campers staying at their adjacent private facility, as well as trim branches along an
existing road (not a concern for Archaeology Services) (Figure 1). Road maintenance will consist of
grading and/or placement or replacement of culverts in areas where erosion is threatening road stability or
creating safety concerns. The trail will be created by trimming branches and selectively removing
vegetation by hand in order to create a “path of least resistance” that hikers can follow. Because of the
proximity to previously recorded archaeological sites and the hurried and undefined nature of the 1979
brush control survey, LCRA Archaeology Services conducted a cultural resource assessment of the
project. The assessment was a combination of similar work conducted in 2007, as well as survey of
previously un-assessed portions of the project, including near 100 percent survey of the hiking trail. The
project was evaluated for both direct (construction-related) and indirect (potential for unauthorized
artifact collecting) impacts.
There are seven areas proposed for road maintenance, numbered 1-3 and 5-7, (4 was removed from
consideration in 2007). Numbers 1, 3 and 5 were evaluated in 2007, and recommendations remain the
same, as follows: “Any maintenance activities done to the road will not impact cultural resource sites as
long as all ground disturbing activities are restricted to the roadbed. At Locations #1 and #5, the ground
immediately outside the existing roadbed can be disturbed as long as these disturbances occur within one
meter of the roads edges. At Location #3, all ground disturbances must take place within the roadbed
only and the walls of the existing road cut must not be disturbed. If Camp Balcones wishes to widen the
road at Location #3 by cutting back the existing road cut, then Archeology Services will first need to send
a report to the Texas Historical Commission (THC) for a 30-day review. The cultural deposits at
Location #3 do not appear to be significant and the THC would probably concur with our
recommendation that Camp Balcones be allowed to widen the road, but by no more than two feet on
either side.”
The remaining three areas were evaluated on January 4, 2008, by LCRA Archaeology Services staff.
Area 7 is near historic site 41BT155. Examination of deposits showed that culvert replacement would not
impact any cultural materials which had not previously been disturbed. Areas 2 and 6 were likewise
previously disturbed areas with no evidence of cultural deposits. Therefore, road maintenance may
proceed in these areas provided care is taken to minimize impacts to surrounding, possibly undisturbed
soils.
The proposed trail was surveyed on the same day. It is primarily along the rim and edge of an upland
ridge overlooking Hickory Creek to the west. No cultural material was observed, and due to the low
impact nature of the trail construction no additional archaeological work is needed. If, however, it is
determined that any type of equipment will be required to mechanically clear brush or create a trail tread,
then any such areas will need to be separately reassessed.

382

In summary, road maintenance and trail construction activities may proceed without restriction at all areas
except area # 3, where ground disturbance should occur should only take place within the existing road
bed. Otherwise, as long as best management practices are followed, no additional cultural resource
consultation is required at this time.
Note: A hand-dug well located in the floodplain was shown by camp staff, and has been reported to Ryan
Kainer of LCRA. It is considered to be part of site 41BT155
