Project Description:

T-253 Overhaul

Figure References:

4, 159

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

January 2008

LCRA proposes to overhaul transmission line T-253 between the Fayette Power Project and the
Fayetteville substation. Activities will include replacement of specific structures at their existing
locations. An archaeological survey was conducted by LCRA Archaeology Services staff on March 7,
2006. The survey consisted of pedestrian investigation of select areas considered to be of high or
moderate potential for containing archaeological sites. These consisted of creek crossings and high creek
terraces.
Two previously recorded archaeological sites, 41FY483 and 41FY484 are located on either side of Camp
Creek near the Fayetteville substation. These were recorded as light, surficial lithic scatters. These two
sites were not revisited. Site 41FY68 was recorded in 1975, and was described as a buried midden with
burned rock and lithic material. It was plotted on the right bank of Cedar Creek approximately 40 m
north of the present transmission line corridor. Within the corridor, two shovel tests were excavated on
either side of STR 19/2 for the 2006 survey.
Shovel Test 1 was located 20 m west of the structure and encountered two flakes and three small burned
chunks in the upper 20 cm of gray clayey sand with increasing clay with depth. Dense clay was found
below 20 cm to a depth of 30 cm, where the test was terminated. Shovel Test 2 was placed 15 m east of
the structure and excavated through 10 cm of sandy light gray clay into dense gray clay below 10 cm. No
artifacts were recovered.
Site 41FY68 is therefore seen to extend southwards into the transmission line corridor between STR 19/2
and Cedar Creek. Care should be exercised in this area. If clearing is necessary it should be
accomplished without disturbing the ground surface, i.e., without mechanical removal. If this is not
possible, additional investigations may be necessary. This area should be considered an “environmentally
sensitive area.” If structure 19/2 requires replacement, it should be approached from the east, and ground
disturbing activities should not occur beyond a distance of 30 ft west of the structure.
Two house or barn structures plotted on USGS quads of the area were not relocated. These were mapped
west of Burnside Road and immediately south of Lake Fayette. A fairly large depression may mark
where a house stood. Further to the north “hog wire” with dimensional lumber (1 x 4) and wire nails
were present. One single tire was also seen nearby. As there was no indication of age greater than 50
years and because any structures had been completely removed, no shovel tests were attempted, as there
is not felt to be sufficient age or integrity left to warrant designation as a site.
Other areas that were investigated showed no evidence of cultural materials.
In summary, three previously recorded archaeological sites, 41FY68, 41FY483 and 41FY484 are within
the T-253 ROW. Only one of these, 41FY68, requires impact minimization. It is recommended that no
ground-disturbing activities take place between Cedar Creek and 30 feet west of STR 19/2. If this is not
possible Archaeology Services should be notified so that any potential additional investigations can be

386

coordinated. Work may proceed in all other areas. Future, more intensive projects (e.g. a rebuild), may
require additional cultural resource investigations.

Figure 159. Project 3.08, T-253 Overhaul, on Fayetteville La Grange East USGS quads.

Project Description:

Starcke Dam Septic System

Figure References:

3, 160

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

February 2008

Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) Archaeology Services staff has completed a cultural resources
assessment for the proposed septic system replacement within an existing septic drain field at its Max
Starcke Dam facility. The existing drain field is approximately 0.04 acres in area and is located on the
bluff above and to the south of the dam (Figure 1). Due to its upland location this area likely had very
little soil to begin with and most of that soil, along with a sizeable amount of limestone were scraped
away to make room for the adjacent bluff-top substation. The septic drain filed is covered by fill
introduced when the existing septic system was installed years ago.
Field work was conducted by contracted archaeologist Cody M. Kiker on February 12, 2008. Though it
was obvious that the soil consisted of introduced fill, three shovel tests were excavated to test for intact
soils below the fill. No intact soils were encountered below the fill in any of the three shovel tests. The
fill is composed of tan to light brown sand with high concentrations of polished gravels. The fill ranges
from 20-30 cm deep in western part of the project area to 50-60 cm deep in the eastern part. Below this
fill is limestone bedrock.
No artifacts were recovered and due to the fact that the soils in the project area consist of introduced fill
and have zero potential to contain intact cultural deposits the project may proceed as planned.

Project Description:

T-138 Little Lucy Creek Crossings

Figure References:

3, 162

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

February 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to construct low
water crossings at two locations on Little Lucy Creek in Lampasas County, Texas in order to facilitate
work on the LCRA T-138 maintenance project (Figure 1). The LCRA Archaeology Services staff had
previously conducted a cultural resource assessment for the T-138 maintenance project. Although staff
did not have time to check the Little Lucy Creek area, staff stated that the Little Lucy Creek area had a
high probability for cultural resource sites and that the maintenance crews should exercise caution in this
area.
Since LCRA’s proposed work for the two stream crossings will include ground-disturbing activities in
areas adjacent to the stream channel, the Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource survey
of the two areas on February 21, 2008. Fieldwork consisted of pedestrian survey and shovel testing on
the terrace slopes adjacent to the stream crossings. Ground surface visibility was found to be fair at both
crossings. No evidences of cultural materials were seen on ground surface and cutbank exposures. At the
proposed crossing of Little Lucy Creek between existing transmission structures 56/5 and 56/6 two shovel
tests were also excavated. The soil profiles showed a 17 cm thick dark grayish brown clay loam topsoil
underlain by a gray clay loam. At the other proposed work area further north adjacent to Little Lucy
Creek outside of the transmission line right-of-way in the vicinity of structures 56/8 and 56/9 a shallower
clay loam deposit was found to a depth of 40 cm above a pale yellow clay subsoil.
Based on the results of the field investigation, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends that the
proposed work to construct the stream crossings on Little Lucy Creek be allowed to proceed. As with all
transmission maintenance projects, our staff will report the results of the investigation at the end of the
year in our Annual Report to the Texas Historical Commission for Calendar Year 2008.

Project Description:

Eagle Lake Ditch Erosion Control

Figure References:

5, 163

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

LCRA proposes to stabilize approximately 500 feet of an existing canal wall by placing large pieces of
concrete along an eroding bank. The concrete will be placed with a trackhoe or similar piece of
equipment that will travel down the canal bed, retrieving concrete that will be brought in along an existing
county road. The equipment will access the canal by way of a small existing drainage located on private
property.
Because of the potential for ground disturbance and the need for a short review period a field
investigation was conducted by LCRA Archaeology Services staff on February 26, 2008. The
investigation consisted of a visual examination of the project area. The only portion of the project that
might impact previously undisturbed soils was the point of canal access. The walls of this drainage were
closely examined for evidence of cultural material. None was seen. Because of the excellent visibility
within this drainage no shovel tests were necessary.
As no archaeological sites are present and because the project is less than two acres in size there is no
need for coordination with the Texas Historical Commission (THC) at this time. The results of this
investigation will be reported in the year-end report to THC.
The project may proceed without additional coordination with Archaeology Services. However, if
unexpected archaeological materials are discovered during the course of the project please cease work in
the immediate area of discovery and contact Archaeology Services at ext. 6714 so that any possible
additional investigations can be coordinated.

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to overhaul
activities on its T-461 transmission line (Figure 1). Portions of this transmission line had been previously
surveyed for cultural resource sites by Espey, Huston & Associates in 1988 as a part of the larger
Lampasas to Goldthwaithe Transmission Line Rebuild Project. On the T-461 portion of the EH&A
survey, sixteen new cultural resource sites (41SS53-58, 41SS63, 41SS64, 41MI147, and 41MI151-156)
were recorded were recorded and one previously recorded site (41SS28) was revisited.
In preparation for maintenance work on the T-461 in 2006, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff
revisited all four sites on the T-461 line that EH&A had rated as potentially significant. These sites are
41SS28, 41SS63, 41SS64, and 41MI147. While in the field the LCRA staff also recorded a new site,
41SS163. Staff noted that materials on and near ground surface at all of these sites had been previously
disturbed by plowing. Staff also stated that while there was a potential for buried cultural materials below
the plow zone at sites 41SS28, 41SS63, 41SS64, and 41MI147, it was doubtful that maintenance activities
would have any effect on those deposits.
Existing transmission structures within or near these four archaeological sites are as follows:
Site 41SS28: Structures 1/8, 1/9, and 1/10
Site 41SS63: Structure 2/1 is nearby to the south of the site
Site 41SS64: Structure 2/3
41MI147: Structures 10/3 and 10/4
Although the engineering plans for the current overhaul work do not include the replacement of any
structures, other types of activities such as the replacement of cross arms, the addition of bird guards, and
the bonding of shield wires to towers will require heavy vehicles to pass through the transmission line
right-of-way. LCRA should avoid sending any heavy vehicles through the areas where the Structures
listed in the previous paragraph are located after heavy rainfall events to avoid creating any tire ruts that
go deeper than the 6 to 8 inch deep plow zones. Elsewhere, work can proceed without any restrictions.

Project No.

17.08

County:

Gillespie, Llano

Project Description:

T-130,191/192 Transmission Line Maintenance. Phase 2

Figure References:

4, 165

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

April 2008

Introduction
In September 2007, the Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) Archaeology Services staff completed a
cultural resource assessment for maintenance work on the portions of the T-130 and T-191/192
transmission lines that extend from the Gillespie Substation to the Eckert Substation. The LCRA
Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct maintenance and clearing activities on the remaining
parts of the T-130 and T-191/192 transmission lines. This second phase of work includes all portions of
these transmission lines extending north and east of the Eckert Substation. The LCRA Archaeology
Services staff has now completed fieldwork on a majority of the high probability areas in the Phase 2
areas. Nine new cultural resource sites were recorded and assessed during the second phase
investigations. Data on the new cultural resource sites are provided in the following paragraphs.
Recommendations are then provided both on these sites and on other high probability areas that could not
be accessed during the field investigation.
Cultural Resource Site Descriptions
41GL393
41GL393 is located on the right (south) bank of Legion Creek, prior to its emergence as a large stream.
In this area the T-130 and T-191/192 lines run parallel to each other within an approximately 200 ft wide
ROW. The area within the ROW is primarily grasses, and appears to have been bulldozed. The T-130
transmission STR 20/1 is located about 30 meters southwest of the south edge of the site.
The site was first seen when a number of flakes were seen within a gravel road leading to the ROW, about
150 m south of the creek. Further inspection found a few flakes and some apparent burned rock, along
with a small gouge Figure 166) in an area with visibility along a fenceline some 60 m north of the road.
Tall grasses made visibility poor. Two shovel tests (STs 4 and 5) were placed near the creek channel, in
areas originally thought to be minimally disturbed. Both found large amounts of recent charcoal in the
upper 20 cm of soil, and both were excavated through fine loams to depths 80 and 50 cm, respectively,
with no artifact recovery in either. Two shovel tests (ST 6 and 7) were placed on either side of the flake
scatter seen within the road. This scatter contained at least one hundred interior flakes of various types
and colors, and was densest within about a 30 m length of road. St 6, just south of the road, had one flake
in Level 1 and two very small chips in Level 2. The test was terminated at 30 cm in reddish brown sandy
loams with high calcium carbonate content and increasing clay. Shovel test 7 had a similar profile, but no
artifacts were recovered. These two tests suggest that the flakes within the road were imported along with
crushed granite road base, as it seems highly unlikely a single road cut would have exposed this amount
of debitage in such an isolated area. The gouge, flakes and burned rock along the fence are likely
representative of an actual site, but it appears that any such site within the ROW as been essentially
destroyed by private land clearing activities. The general paucity of surficial materials indicates that the
site was ephemeral to begin with, and unless there are deeply buried deposits, the site lacks the integrity
for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark (SAL) or nomination to the National Register of

398

Figure 165. Project 17.08. T-130, 191/192 Maintenance, Phase 2, in Gillespie and Llano counties.

399

Historic Places (NRHP). Any proposed maintenance activities will not adversely impact the site, and
there is no need for any type of additional work or oversight at this time.

Figure 166. Gouge found at 41GL393.
41GL394 is located immediately across Legion Creek from 41GL393 within the combined T-130 and T191/192 corridor. The T-191/192 STR #117 is located about 15 meters northeast of the north edge of this
site. One biface fragment, an apparent dart point preform (Figure 167), four flakes, and a likely burned
rock were seen within a 30 m diameter area. Two shovel tests (STs 1 and 2) were placed within this area.
Both had a profile of about 15 cm of dark sandy loam over decomposing granite. ST 1 had a single
possible burned piece of sandstone, and ST2 had one small chert chip and two possible burned rocks.
One additional shovel test (ST 3) was placed about 30 north of this area, above an exposed limestone
ledge where it appeared there was potential for soil development. Its profile was similar and no artifacts
were recovered. Disturbance was les severe here then at 41GL393, although a large tock tank had been
constructed underneath the line. Careful examination of the soil around the stock tank failed to find any
significant amounts of artifacts. This site is therefore interpreted as a minor lithic scatter with little
interpretive potential, and ineligible for formal listings. There are no concerns with maintenance
activities at this location.

Figure 167. Dart point preform from 41GL394.

41LL475
The site is located within the LCRA T-130 transmission line right-of-way on a small 6- foot high terrace
projection on the northeast side of a tributary of Byrnes Creek. The site lies 40 to 80 meters southwest of
the T-130 STR 3/2, and STR 3/3 is located on the opposite side of the tributary from the site. The
transmission line corridor has been cleared of trees so that the current vegetation is short grasses, cacti,
and brush. Outside of the transmission line corridor there is a narrow band of live oak trees bordering the
creek channel. Topsoil is a gravelly sandy loam.
The portion of the site within the LCRA T-130 right-of-way appears to be a prehistoric lithic scatter. Of
the approximately 20 artifacts seen at the site, most of the artifacts are small secondary and tertiary flakes
and chips. No definite prehistoric burned rock fragments or other artifact classes were seen. Nor were

400

any features or diagnostic artifacts found within the right-of-way. The soils are rocky and the site
probably has little depth of deposits. The site probably extends eastward outside of the right-of-way
toward a smaller side tributary that has its confluence with the larger tributary about 40 meters southeast
of the transmission line right-of-way. The portion of the site within the transmission line corridor is
assessed as not eligible for the NR or SAL listing.
41GL395
41GL395 is located just north of the T-130 and T-191/192 split on the left (west) bank a broad bend of
Legion Creek at point where the T-130 transmission line runs over the creek bank for a short while. The
ROW is impassable at this point (north of STR 19/3), and the site has to be approached from the north.
The entirety of the known portion of the site is no more than 10 m in diameter. The site is located within
a steep, 2 m cutbank of Legion Creek, at a point where a small gully joins the creek. The cutbank profile
consists of approximately 70 cm fairly coarse granitic loams over very fine grayish brown silts. The
badly decomposed mandible of a large grazing animal, presumed to be bison, is eroding out of the
granitic portion of the cutbank (Figure 168). Scattered about the lower portion of the bank are several
flakes and two edge modified flakes, one of which was made into an informal biface. The medial section
of an untypeable dart point was seen upon the upper portion of the bank, and a single vertebra was within
the cut some 8 m south of the mandible. Some other few bones were also located on the upper portion of
the terrace, and at least some are almost certainly recent. A single shovel test (ST 9) was placed on the
terrace top near the mandible. It was excavated through 90 cm of reddish brown sandy/gravelly loam.
No artifacts were recovered. This site is interpreted as a single-event animal processing location,
although it is possible that the association of the buried mandible and stone artifacts is coincidental. The
site is probably mostly destroyed through natural erosion, although it is possible additional bones and
artifacts remain buried. For this reason the site is considered potentially eligible for formal listing as an
SAL and nomination to the NRHP. It is not expected that there will be any maintenance-related activities
in this area. If any such become scheduled, the site should be avoided, and if it cannot be avoided, then
additional investigations are warranted.

Figure 168. Location of mandible in creek bank at 41GL395, pencil for scale. Note profile.
41LL476
41LL476 is located on the T-130 line on a relatively high bluff overlooking a left bank tributary of Hondo
Creek. The T-130 STR 12/1 lies within the northern part of the site. The site consists of flakes and flake
fragments as well as at least two biface fragments and apparent scattered burned rock. The site extends
for a distance of about 100 m north from the edge of the creek. There is some soil development in places,
and a single shovel test (ST 11) was placed at the southern end of the site. It encountered brown sandy

401

loam that graded to pale yellowish brown silty loam over decomposing limestone at about 50 cm. Level 1
had two small decorticate flakes, a piece of chert shatter, and an apparent flake of pure quartz, as well as
seven burned rocks. Level 2 had two flakes and four burned rocks. The site probably lacks the integrity
that would make it significant in the regulatory sense, but would need additional testing before such a
statement could be formalized. Maintenance activities, including potentially improving the existing
stream crossing, are not considered to produce negative impacts. If more intensive impacts (e.g., a
rebuild) are necessary, then additional investigations are warranted prior to ground disturbing activities.
(Note: a lens of possible burned rock was visible in the cutbank opposite 41LL476. No definite cultural
material was visible, however. This area should be looked at more closely if future work included
intensive ground disturbance.)
41LL477
41LL477 is located on the T-130 ROW on two left (east) bank tributaries of Hondo Creek. The site
extends for a total length of 590 meters making it the largest site documented during the survey. The site
area encompasses areas on both sides of the southern tributary and continues northward to the southeast
bank of the northern tributary. T-130’s STRs 11/1 and 10/9 through 10/7 lie within the site area.
The two tributary creeks issue out of the edge of the Riley Mountains, and based on vegetation within
them, appear to be spring fed. The site consists of a scatter of lithic artifacts, primarily flakes, with a
possible disrupted hearth on the south side of the southern tributary. On the north side of this tributary
two arrow points were found, one a Scallorn and the other with a broken base but which is presumed to
also be a Scallorn point (Figure 169). Approximately 120 meters further northward, the distal part of a
dart point was found.

Figure 169. Arrow point fragments seen at 41LL477.
Two shovel tests (STs 12 and 13) were excavated on either side of the southern tributary. Shovel test 12
was placed near the apparent hearth on the south side of the creek. It was found about 30 cm of grayish
brown silty loam over limestone cobbles. One chert chip and one burned chunk were in the upper 20 cm,
and one flake was found in the lower 10 cm. Several angular pieces of limestone and granite were also
recovered, and may be fire-cracked. Shovel test 13 was placed on the north bank of the creek in an area
with apparent soil deposition. It was excavated through 40 cm brown silty loam with high calcium
carbonate content. Final soil depth is unknown. The upper 20 cm had nine small flakes and flake
fragments, and importantly, a mussel shell fragment. The second level two fairly large flakes and six
smaller fragments.
This site is considered to be potentially eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark
based on the potential for horizontal and/or vertical separation of components, presence of features, and
preservation of organic material (the mussel shell, the most likely source being either the Colorado or
Llano Rivers). Maintenance activities, however, are not expected to impact previously undisturbed
components of the site. The creek is impassable by vehicle though. Should it be determined that a stream

402

crossing is necessary, additional investigations are warranted, as well as if any future projects impact the
site to greater extent than standard maintenance procedures.
41LL478
41LL478 is located near the north end of the T-130 line with the north end of the site being about 20
meters south of STR 2/1. The site lies on the north bank of a short, unnamed right bank tributary of the
Llano River. The property here is actively managed by a large exotic game ranch, and much of the ROW
has been cleared and root plowed (Figure 170). At the site location the stream crossing has been heavily
modified, and the root plowing extends well upslope towards STR 2/1. Within the plowed field was
found a surprising number of artifacts, given the lack of known readily available chert resources in the
immediate vicinity. It is possible that the Llano River, a little over a mile away to the northwest, provided
sufficient raw materials to explain the relatively causal discard seen at the site. Present were a core with
crushed edges (a crusher), 30 to 40 flakes both corticate and decorticate, a large edge modified blade, a
late stage biface/possible knife, and a broken dart point probably of Late Archaic age (Figure 171). These
were all in an area approximately 40 m in diameter, and clearly decreased in frequency towards the north.
No shovel tests were excavated due to the excellent visibility and the obvious lack of any maintenance
needs. The site is probably destroyed within the ROW, although there is a slight possibility that there are
deeper, undisturbed deposits. The site is significant in that it aids in interpreting the local settlement
patterning, but it is doubtful it is eligible for formal state or national listings. No special restrictions are
needed for the upcoming maintenance activities. However, should a major ground disturbing project
falling under the formal review process become necessary, limited testing to confirm the nature of the
surface deposits is warranted.

Figure 170. View of 41LL478.

Figure 171. Dart point at 41LL478.

403

41LL479
This prehistoric site extends for a length of 200 meters on the T-130 corridor near the base of the Riley
Mountains on the north and east sides of Bailey Branch. The existing T-130 STR 9/2 is situated within
the southern part of the site and STR 9/1 lies just 13 meters north of the north edge of the site.
Moderately dense prehistoric materials consisting mostly of chert debitage and tool fragments are
exposed on eroded portions of ROW corridor, particularly on the eastern half of the corridor in the central
part of the site. Diagnostic tools that were seen at the site consist of an Ensor point fragment, a Darl point
stem, and an arrow point preform possibly of the Alba type. Several broken biface fragments were also
seen. Some sandstone and limestone rocks present on ground surface have characteristics of burned
rocks, but it is not certain as to whether these are prehistoric hearthstones or result from tree and brush
burning during land clearing activities.
Although no shovel testing was conducted, there appears to be good potential for buried deposits on the
western edges of the transmission corridor in the central and northern parts of the site. Even better
deposits may be present further westward outside the corridor towards the Bailey Branch channel. At the
south end of the site, soils are shallow and rocky at the T-130 crossing of Bailey Branch. Therefore, no
special precautions are needed should this area need to be bladed for an improved low water crossing over
the creek channel. While other standard maintenance level activities should not have any adverse impacts
on the site, additional investigations would be needed in the central and northern parts of the site should
LCRA propose more intensive ground-disturbing activities such as rebuilding the line or widening the
ROW.

41LL480
This prehistoric site is situated on the T-130 corridor on the southwest side of Bailey Branch opposite
41LL479. The site is a prehistoric lithic scatter that consists of chert debitage. A very small number of
burned rocks were also seen, but these may have been burned during recent land clearing operations. No
features or diagnostic artifacts were seen, and artifact density is lower than that at nearby 41LL479.
Overall, the surface exposure of artifacts extends for a length of about 70 meters within the ROW. The
topsoil consists of a sandy deposit of unknown depth. The north end of the site lies 60 meters south of
Bailey Branch with the area between the site and creek being mostly exposed bedrock. No special
conditions are needed for maintenance work within the boundaries of 41LL480.
Recommendations for Maintenance Activities
Special conditions are recommended at 2 of the 9 cultural resource sites that were recorded. At 41GL395,
the maintenance crews need to avoid impacting an area that is a 30 foot diameter area centered about 300
feet north of T-130 STR 19/2. It consists of a gully and cutbank where animal bones are exposed. At
41LL477, should it be determined that ground-disturbing activities are needed to create a low water
crossing on the creek channel that lies about 345 feet north of STR 11/1 on the T-130 line, then additional
cultural resource investigations will be needed before the crossing can be constructed.
Additionally, there are number of high probability areas that could not be investigated due to access and
scheduling problems on the T-191/192 line. These consist of the terrace areas on either side of Coal
Creek, the first two unnamed tributary creeks west of Coal Creek, the first two unnamed tributary creeks
east of Coal Creek, the terraces on both sides of Comanche Creek, the first tributary creek west of
Comanche Creek, the first tributary creek east of Comanche Creek, the terrace of the east side of White
Creek, the first tributary east of White Creek, the terrace of the west side of Walnut Creek. Should the
maintenance work require any low water crossings at any of these areas, then the LCRA Archaeology

404

Services staff should be contacted in advance so that the archaeologists can conduct a field visit to ensure
that no significant or potentially significant cultural resource sites are present in areas that will require
ground-disturbing activities for construction of the low water crossings.

Additional Concerns for Future Projects Such as Rebuilds or Widening the ROW Corridors
There appear to be very deep soils on either side of Sandy Creek. The T-130 line crosses very near
previously recorded 41GL3951LL397, on the south side of Sandy Creek. Two shovel tests in this area
found no artifacts, and there were no visible signs of the site. Any future projects with intensive ground
disturbance would likely require more intensive investigations on either side of this creek.
A few flakes were seen in the vicinity of STR 19/3 on the T-130 line. These were in areas of excellent
visibility and relatively high disturbance. The artifacts were too widely scattered to warrant a site
designation.
A mapped spring near STRs 17/8 and 17/9 on the T-130 was found to feed two large stock tanks. Much
of this area had been bulldozed, and no artifacts were seen. One low spot near the west edge of the ROW,
just north of the stock tank appeared to have intact soils, but it was not tested as no impacts are expected.
This location should be revisited if future work involves intensive ground disturbance.
On the T-130 line, a number of left bank tributaries of Legion Creek were examined, and no sites were
discovered, although seemingly randomly occurring isolated finds of flakes, an early stage biface and the
medial section of a biface were plotted. Future major projects might require at least spot checking of
some of these crossings.
Overall, this project recorded a fairly large number of sites in an area containing few to no chert
resources, but one that probably had a predictable water supply. It appears that some of the tributary
creeks that descend from the slopes of the Riley Mountains are spring-fed, and this fact may account for
greater density of sites than is suggested by the present statewide database. The artifact assemblage at
most of these sites was limited, both in numbers of artifacts and diversity of tool types with 41LL479
being an exception. The actual lithic material used to produce chipped stone tools, however, is quite
diverse, with a wide variety of colors including white from an unknown source, pinks and browns from
lag cobble sources, and black tabular chert similar to that seen in the Lampasas area (e.g. quarry
41GL3951LM44), as well as the quartz flake possibly coming from the Lake Buchanan area (or perhaps
the feature called Quartz Knob further east along Sandy Creek). The small sizes and low counts of the
flakes at the majority of the sites indicate that chert was being used conservatively, as would be expected
when it is difficult to replenish supplies. It also suggests that these sites were short term camps or
resource acquisition locales, with the frequency of the sites indicating fairly extensive use of the wider
landscape at a minimally intensive level.

On March 26, 2008, LCRA Archaeology Services staff monitored construction of a 15 x 15 ft pad

that was compared against files and records at LCRA and online with the Texas Historical Commission, it
appears that 41BT160 does not extend into the present project area. Because of negative findings of both
present and past work there is no need for additional archaeological investigation for this project as
described and proposed. If there significant changes to the scope of this project or future projects require
significant ground disturbance, additional investigations are warranted.
A visit was also made to the park host/entry station site. This visit confirmed earlier assessments that no
further archaeological work was need in this area.

Project Description:

T-138 Salt Creek Crossing

Figure References:

3, 176

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

April 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to construct a
low water crossing on Salt Creek in Lampasas County, Texas in order to facilitate work on the LCRA T138 Maintenance project (Figure 1). LCRA Archaeology Services staff had previously conducted a
cultural resource assessment for the T-138 Maintenance project and although the Salt Creek area was not
checked, staff stated that the Salt Creek area had a high probability for cultural resource sites and that the
area would need to be surveyed if any ground disturbance was to be done.
LCRA Transmission Serviced proposes to place gravel fill within and adjacent to the creek channel in
order to facilitate crossing. Because this work could potentially include ground-disturbing activities,
Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource survey of the area on April 8, 2008. Fieldwork
consisted of pedestrian survey and shovel testing of terraces and slopes on both sides of Salt Creek
(between STRs 70/5 and 70/6). Ground surface visibility was good on the terraces and very good closer
to the creek. Cutbank exposures were also utilized determine subsurface conditions. Gravels were
moderate in the area and very dense closer to the creek. Fossilized shells were observed in cutbanks and
surface gravels. Shovel test 1 (ST) was excavated to depth of 45cm on the south/east side of Salt Creek.
The soil consisted of yellow-brown gravelly sandy clay loam with mottling increasing with depth. Shovel
test 2 was excavated to a depth of 80cm on the north/west side of Salt Creek. This test had 65cm of
brown loamy clay with some gravels above 15cm of brown sandy clay loam with increasing numbers of
gravels. Calcium carbonate (CaCO3) nodules were found in the lower depths of both shovel tests and
various cutbanks. No cultural material was recovered from either shovel test. A purple glass bottle neck
was observed in the gravels adjacent to the creek, but no other cultural material, either historic or
prehistoric, was observed; this artifact is subsequently considered an isolated find.
Based on the results of the field investigation, LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends that the
proposed work to construct the crossing on Salt Creek be allowed to proceed. As with all transmission
maintenance projects, out staff will report the results of the investigation at the end of the year in our
Annual Report to the Texas Historical Commission for Calendar Year 2008.

Project Description:

Lake Buchanan Pipeline to Storage Tank

Figure References:

3, 178

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

June 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Water Services plan to install an elevated water storage
tank on the north slope of Long Mountain in Llano County, Texas. A new buried pipeline will connect
the tank to an existing water pipeline that runs along Hwy 29. This new pipeline will run from the
proposed tank site northwards to the north edge of the existing transmission line ROW, a distance of less
than 100 feet, then eastwards along the north edge of this ROW for a distance of 300 feet, and finally turn
north for a distance of 300 feet to the south edge of Hwy 29. At this point it may tunnel under the
highway to connect with the existing pipeline or, as an alternative route, run to the west along the south
edge of the highway for 700 feet to tie into a section of the existing pipeline on the south side of the
highway. The proposed 30-foot wide easement for this new pipeline runs for 650 feet from the proposed
tank site to the highway, covering 0.45 acre. A permanent service road composed of compacted dirt or
crushed gravel will also be constructed within this easement.
The tank site and nearby portions of the transmission line ROW were previously investigated by LCRA’s
Archaeology Services staff in 2004. However the proposed easement between the transmission ROW and
highway was not. Since the ground within the easement will be disturbed by clearing and pipeline
trenching, impacting any archeological deposits that might be present, a cultural resource survey was
conducted by LCRA’s Archaeology Services staff on June 2, 2008.
The survey consisted of walking the entire route of the proposed pipeline between the transmission line
ROW to Hwy 29, a distance of 300 feet. In addition, the 700-foot alternative route along the south side of
highway was also walked. During the survey, the easement between the highway and transmission line
ROW was found to contain thin rocky soils or rock outcrops on moderately sloping ground. Only a small
area 100 feet south of the highway appeared to have soil depth sufficient to harbor cultural deposits. A
shovel test placed in this area encountered soft sandstone bedrock 15 cm beneath the surface with the soil
above being extremely rocky and containing no artifacts. The 700-foot alternative route was found to run
through land that had been graded and stripped of all natural soil cover by highway construction.
Since the project area is less than two acres in size and no cultural resource sites were found, a report does
not have to be sent to the Texas Historical Commission at this time for review and comment. Instead, the
proposed construction may proceed at this point, and our staff will report the results of the investigation
later in our Annual Report to the Texas Historical Commission for Calendar Year 2008.

Project Description:

Grason Volente Waterline

Figure References:

3, 179

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

June 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is considering granting an easement to Grason Volente
Investments, Ltd. for a potable waterline through a portion of the LCRA-owned Wheless tract in Travis
County, Texas. The proposed easement comprises a 50-foot strip of wooded land bordering the western
edge of Lime Creek Road starting from the southeast property line of the Cedar Park Water Treatment
Plant and extending southeastward to end at the southwest property line of the Preserve. The proposed
easement will run for a distance of 1640 feet (500 meters) and cover an area of 1.9 acre.
Should the project proceed, the wooded easement area will have to be cleared using heavy machinery and
a 1640-foot long trench excavated for the waterline. Therefore, considerable ground disturbances are
anticipated, impacting any buried archeological deposits or features that might be present. Since this
portion of the Wheless tract has not received any prior archeological investigation, LCRA’s Archeology
Services conducted a cultural resource survey of the proposed easement area on May 28, 2008.
The survey consisted of walking in a zigzag fashion two transects separated by 5 to 10 meters lengthways
through the proposed easement area. Surface visibility was good with little ground vegetation and,
significantly, topsoils were thin to nonexistent. No cultural material was seen on the surface other than
modern trash. Two shovel tests were dug in areas that appeared to have sufficient soil depth. One test
encountered bedrock at 36 cm below the surface and the other at 16 cm. Neither shovel test recovered
artifacts and the shovel test walls showed a truncated soil profile probably resulting from past land
clearings.
Based on the negative results of the fieldwork, no additional cultural resource investigations are needed.
The LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends that the proposed easement be granted. As with all
projects of less than two acres without archeological sites, our staff will report the results of this
investigation in our Annual Report to the Texas Historical Commission for Calendar Year 2008.

Project Description:

T-123 T-122 T-243 T-225 Maintenance

Figure References:

4, 181

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

LCRA proposes to conduct standard maintenance on four transmission lines located in the Kendall,
Comfort and Kerrville areas. These lines variously intersect and are double circuited with each other at
various locations. The lines have all be previously surveyed for cultural resources except for T-243 and
the corresponding double-circuited portion of T-123, which makes up approximately 1 mile of line at the
eastern end of the system.
There are a number of previously recorded archaeological sites on the remaining portion of T-123 as well
as on T-122 and T-225. Only four of these, however, require any avoidance. These are sites 41KR200,
41KR593, 41KR595, and 41KR598. Recommendations are discussed below in relation to associated
transmission line structures when available, and other landmarks when no structure information is
available.
Site 41KR200 is an open prehistoric campsite located on T-225 just north of River Road which parallels
the north bank of the Guadalupe River. It is centered around the first lattice structure north of the river
and extends across the plowed fields north where it ends in the vicinity of the first structure north of SH
27. Due to monitoring following a recent rebuild, it is not expected that much clearing will be necessary.
Recommendations are for the access from River Road to the lattice structure to be maintained along the
existing dirt track, and the clearing of vegetation should be accomplished by non-ground disturbing
methods.
Site 41KR593 is located on lines T-122 and T-123 between STRs 24 and 25. This site is recorded as a
lithic scatter consistent with procurement activities. Just outside the transmission line ROW, however, is
a circular mound of large limestone rocks. Because it was outside the ROW it was not investigated
beyond noting its presence. However, it is within an area of potential effect, and could be damaged by
equipment traveling or maneuvering outside the ROW. Therefore, between STRs 24 and 25 vehicular
movement should be restricted to the ROW.
Site 41KR595 is located on lines T-122 and T-123 between Cypress Creek Road and the base of the slope
that is 500 ft west of STR 8. This is a large open camp located within open pasture and at the base of the
slope. Previous monitoring and later investigation by the Llano Uplift Archeological Society (LUAS)
indicated a high potential for intact buried deposits. Existing roads and access points should be used, and
clearing should be accomplished by non-ground disturbing methods, from between Cypress Creek Road
(approximately the location of STR 8) to a point 500 ft west of STR 8.
Site 41KR598 is located on lines T-123 and T-225 at a point 31,500 line ft east of the Kerrville Stadium
substation and 12,000 ft west of the “T” formed by the intersection of lines T-122, T-123 and T-225. It is
within a deep canyon containing a spring-fed stream. The site consists of a water management system
associated with the Faltin family, and apparently is still in use. Because of its location within the steepwalled canyon, no impacts from maintenance are expected. Post-rebuild monitoring showed that clearing
stopped well short of both canyon walls. Such avoidance should be maintained, and no attempts to cross
the canyon floor in vehicles should be made.

424

Figure 181. Project 34.08, T-123 T-122 T-243 T-225 Maintenance in Kendall and Kerr counties.

425

It does not appear that the final, eastern mile of T-123 that corresponds with T-243 between the Comfort
and Kendall substations has ever been surveyed for cultural resources. This is an area of fairly low
probability for cultural sites, and aerial imagery suggests that the highest probability portion of this
section is within a golf course. It is recommended that best management practices, primarily minimizing
unnecessary disturbances, be followed while conducting maintenance along T-243 and the corresponding
section of T-123.
To summarize:
T-225: Use existing access points and roads, avoid ground disturbance in vicinity of first lattice structure
north of the Guadalupe River
T-122 and T-123: Stay within the ROW between STRs 24 and 25; use existing access points and road,
avoid ground disturbance from STR 8 and Cypress Creek Road west for a distance of 500 ft.
T-123 and T-225: Do not cross steep canyon located 31,500 ft line east of Kerrville Stadium substation
and 12,000 ft west of major “T” transmission line intersection.
T-225 and T-123: Avoid unnecessary ground disturbance between the Kendall and Kerrville substations.
Please note: These recommendations are for maintenance activities only. Potential future projects such
as overhauls and rebuilds should be evaluated based on associated levels of possible impacts.

Project Description:

McKinney Rough Fire Guards

Figure References:

5, 182

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

On June 25, 2007, LCRA Archaeology Services staff visited McKinney Roughs (Figure 182) to evaluate
impacts associated with recent firefighting activities and subsequent firebreak construction to
archaeological sites that are formally listed as State Archeological Landmarks (SALs) or those considered
potentially eligible for listing as SALs. There are presently five SALs on McKinney Roughs property –
41BP88, 41BP444, 41BP449, 41BP455 and 41BP463 (Figure 183). Only one of these, 41BP463, was
directly impacted by the fire and firefighting activities. None of these sites will be impacted by firebreak
construction as presently proposed; however, site 41BP681, which is considered potentially eligible for
SAL status, will be subjected to impacts through clearing along the property boundary.
An inspection of 41BP681, located at the most southeasterly corner of the property, found that shredding
of vegetation had already occurred, as per verbal and written communications between Archaeology
Services, Conservation Services and McKinney Roughs staff, and the Texas Historical Commission
(THC). Ground disturbance was minimal, but I marked the site boundaries with flagging tape and visited
with the clearing crew informing them of the need to minimize, as much as possible, impacts to that
location. The remainder of the property line scheduled for clearing has been surveyed at various times in
the past. There is no need for additional coordination with Archaeology Services for the remainder of the
clearing as proposed. This recommendation covers fence removal and replacement as well, with the
understanding that the fence will be replaced in kind (i.e. predominantly t-posts). Should the project
scope change and bulldozing or more substantial fence construction be required, then additional
coordination with Archaeology Services will be needed. These recommendations do not cover additional
firebreak construction in the interior of the park, except along existing trails. It was also discovered that
the existing portion of the fence along the N/S axis was about 40 ft too far onto neighboring property.
The new fence will be just inside the actual property line, meaning that L CRA loses a good portion of the
site.
A site visit to 41BP463, located along Bobcat Ridge Trail within the main fire area, found that
firefighting activities had not impacted it, other than for two incidental trails created by light vehicles.
This site should be avoided during firebreak construction if at all possible. If any substantial ground
disturbance becomes necessary here then a consultation process with THC will be necessary.
Site 41BP444, within the loop formed by the northern intersection of Bobcat Ridge Trail and Riverside
Trail, was also visited. This site had not been impacted by either the fire or firefighting activities. Site
41BP88, at the former wetland area, was not examined, as it was not within or near the fire area. Both of
these sites, as well as SALs 41BP449 and 41BP455 (see Figure 1) should also be avoided if at all
possible. If avoidance is not possible, then Archaeology Services should be consulted prior to any
ground-disturbing activities, except for standard maintenance of existing trails. New ground disturbance
may require field investigations and a 30-day review and clearance process through the THC. It is
recommended that fire guards be created by a seppi or similar equipment. Bulldozing is not
recommended and may require additional investigations and possible delays. Other firebreak
construction will need to be evaluated separately.

Keith Treadwell also showed an artifact scatter he discovered near the intersection of Ridge and Valley
View trails. This scatter consists of apparent multiple trash disposal events within the past 30 or 40 years.
As such, it is not considered an archaeological site, and no additional measures are necessary at this
location.

Project Description:

T-134 Overhaul

Figure References:`

5, 184

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct
overhaul work on the T-134 transmission line which is also called the Flatonia to Yoakum Transmission
Line. This transmission extends for a total length of 28.86 miles, and it is situated in Fayette and Lavaca
counties, Texas.
A file search showed that this transmission line was not surveyed for cultural resource sites prior to its
original construction because that construction predated the enactment of the State Antiquities Code.
However, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource field investigation on this
line prior to proposed maintenance work in August 2006. As a result of that investigation, four cultural
resource sites, 41LC22-25, were recorded and assessed.
Sites 41LC22 and 41LC24 were assessed as insignificant and, thus, no special conditions were needed for
the proposed maintenance work. Site 41LC23 was rated as a potentially significant site, and special
conditions were placed on the proposed maintenance activities in and around this site. The restrictions for
the 41LC23 site area consisted of avoiding damage to subsurface deposits, hand clearing of vegetation,
and restricting vehicular traffic to usage of any existing dirt road. The fourth site, 41LC25, is the historic
St. Paul AME Church building, which appeared to be partially situated within the normal transmission
line corridor. Information in the Special Conditions book for the maintenance project indicated that the
LCRA does not have a formal 100 foot wide easement on the land tract in which the church is located.
Assessment of the significance of the church building was not necessary because it did not appear that
any maintenance work would be needed in the vicinity of the church or its well-maintained grounds.
Based on the review of the cultural resource data, some restrictions are needed for overhaul work in and
around site 41LC23. Existing Structure #19/5 is situated about 50 feet south of the south boundary of
site 41LC23. Should overhaul work be needed on this structure, it is recommended that any new pole(s)
be placed in the holes of the existing H-frame structure. Should the location of this structure need to
slightly moved, then it is recommended that it be moved southward rather than northward. Care should
also be taken to utilize the existing dirt road present within the transmission line corridor, as much as
possible, and avoidance of the portion of the dirt road that lies 50 to 130 feet northeast of the existing
Structure #19/5 during wet weather conditions is recommended.
Review of data on site 41LC25 indicates that existing Structure #8/10 is located just 42 feet southeast of
the southeast corner of the church building. Any needed overhaul work on this structure may proceed
without any special conditions.
Aside from the restrictions recommended in the vicinity of site 41LC23, LCRA Archaeology Services
staff recommends that all other proposed overhaul work proceed as currently planned. However, should
the maintenance personnel uncover any presently unknown buried archaeological materials during work,
then work should cease in the immediate area of the discovery. The LCRA Archaeology Services staff
should be contacted at 1-800-776-5272 ext. 6714 so that the find can be assessed in consultation with the

431

Texas Historical Commission, and a determination can be made as to whether any avoidance or
mitigation measures are needed.

Project Description:

T-218 Overhaul

Figure References:

4, 185

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct
overhaul work on the T-218 transmission line which is also called the Wirtz to Burnet Transmission Line.
This transmission extends for a total length of 16.14 miles, and it is situated in Burnet County, Texas.
A file search showed that this transmission line was not surveyed for cultural resource sites prior to its
original construction because that construction predated the enactment of the State Antiquities Code.
However, in recent years the LCRA Archaeology Services staff has conducted cultural resource field
investigations on this line prior to proposed maintenance work. These investigations consist of field
surveys of the T-108 and T-187 lines which are double circuited with the north and south ends of T-218,
respectively, and the remaining center section of T-218 which was field surveyed in 2007. As a result of
those investigations, two cultural resource sites, 41BT425 and 41BT426, were recorded and assessed.
Site 41BT425 is a low-density prehistoric lithic scatter that was assessed as insignificant due to erosion,
gullying, and the more recent impacts from vehicular traffic. Site 41BT426 is a small rockshelter located
in the walls of steep canyon. Potentially significant buried prehistoric deposits were found within the
rockshelter overhang at 41BT426. Traces of historic carvings and graffiti were also found on the walls of
the limestone ledge. Although 41BT426 is a potentially significant site, it will not be affected by any of
the proposed overhaul work due to its location on a steep canyon slope far distant from any of the existing
transmission line structures and access roads.
Based on a review of data from previous cultural resource field investigations, it does not appear that the
proposed overhaul activities have any potential to impact any important cultural resource sites. LCRA
Archaeology Services staff recommends that the proposed T-218 overhaul work be allowed proceed
without any special conditions. However, should the maintenance personnel uncover any presently
unknown buried archaeological materials during work, then work should cease in the immediate area of
the discovery. The LCRA Archaeology Services staff should be contacted at 1-800-776-5272 ext. 6714
so that the find can be assessed in consultation with the Texas Historical Commission, and a
determination can be made as to whether any avoidance or mitigation measures are needed.

435

Project No.

38.08

County:

Bastrop Caldwell

Project Description:

T-153 Maintenance

Figure References:

5, 186

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

LCRA proposes to conduct standard maintenance on transmission line T-153 between the Sim Gideon
and Lockhart substations. Because most of the line had not been previously surveyed for cultural
resources, a cultural resources survey was conducted by LCRA Archaeology Services staff on June 24-26,
2008. The survey focused on higher probability areas, primarily stream crossings. As it did not appear
that there would be a need for extensive ground disturbance, the survey was conducted largely at a
reconnaissance level. Very few shovel tests were excavated, and of the six newly discovered
archeological sites discovered, most are not documented at a level sufficient to determine State
Archeological Landmark or National Registry status at this time. Future, more intensive projects, such as
a transmission line rebuild, would require additional investigations. The six new archaeological sites are
described below, as are select areas that were not investigated due to lack of clearing needs. The
recommendations in regards to cultural resources are then summarized. Additional information is on file
with Archaeology Services.
41CW117
41CW117 is a prehistoric lithic scatter located for a distance of about 100 m on the lobed edge of a high
ridge overlooking the floodplain valley of Plum Creek to the east. It is within an actively cleared and
managed ranch. Vegetation consists of short grasses and scattered trees. Observed artifacts consisted of
widely scattered pieces of lithic debitage with occasional tested cobbles. The debitage was almost
entirely cortical with characteristics typical of hard hammer, early stage reduction. This is consistent with
the nature of the ridge, which is covered by thin soils overlying a gravel deposit (Queeny gravelly loam).
A single tool was noted near the eastern portion of the site. This was similar to form to a Guadalupe tool,
although there was not a formal bit end.
Although no shovel tests were excavated, the type of site combined with the shallow nature of the soils
indicate the site is fully investigated and that no significant amounts of new information would be present.
The site is not considered eligible for formal listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to
the National Register of Historic Places.
41CW118
41CW118 is located on either side and within the channel of a branch of Pecan Branch, a tributary to
Plum Creek which is located about 550 m to the east. Topographically, the series of drainages that
include Pecan Branch appear to represent meanders of Plum Creek. The site is within an actively
managed ranch. The area along the creek is wooded, but there is pasture on either side. The creek
channel is shallow, no more than three feet deep, and broad, perhaps 20 feet wide, with sloping banks.
Cultural materials consist of burned limestone, chert and quartzite, several flakes, a core, and two early
stage bifaces. These are most common within the west bank of the creek, and in the northern half of the
ROW, and extend north out of the ROW. Some less concentrated material was visible along the pasture
fence east of the drainage, and also south of the ROW. There were also several pieces of weathered,
patinated bone, including long bone fragments, a portion of a jaw, and sections of vertebrae, all from a
large mammal, most likely a cow or bison. The patination was similar to that seen in bone from buried
archaeological contexts, but burial in flood silts may also have caused the appearance. These bones were

436

Figure 186. Project 38.08, T-153 Maintenance in Bastrop and Caldwell counties.

437

largely within the creek and within its eastern bank, mostly centered in the ROW. There is some spatial
association of bone, burned rock and debitage. On top of the eastern creek bank there was evidence of
recent rush burning. This was easily distinguishable from the artifacts and burned rock within the creek,
but make assessment of the upper creek bank much more problematic. Because there are no impacts
expected from the maintenance project, no shovel tests were excavated. Possible future projects that
involve subsurface disturbances (e.g. a rebuild, construction of creek crossing) should involve additional
investigations to determine what, if any, additional aspects of the site are present. Eligibility for SAL or
NRHP status remains unknown.
41CW119
41CW119 is located on the end of a low interfluvial lobe between Plum Creek, located about 400 m to the
west, and a minor tributary located about 100 m to the east. The site consists of a widely dispersed scatter
of burned rock (quartzite and chert) and chert debitage, including a few interior flakes and 3-4 larger hard
hammer flakes. The existing dirt ranch road had been recently bladed which had exposed a few discrete
areas of blackened soil. These almost certainly represent recent burning episodes, but there is a remote
chance they could be the remains of earlier cultural activities. The burned rock may also be, at least
partially, the result of recent brush burning, but the flakes and burned rock are typically spatially
associated, and do exhibit clustering. Because no subsurface impacts are expected here, no shovel tests
were excavated. The visible materials with in the dirt road are certainly disturbed, but it is unknown to
what extent the site may extend within the rest of the ROW. Possible future projects that involve
subsurface disturbances (e.g. a rebuild, construction of creek crossing) should involve additional
investigations to determine what, if any, additional aspects of the site are present. Eligibility for SAL or
NRHP status remains unknown.
41CW120
41CW120 is located on the sloping western bank of Dry Creek. It is open pasture in apparent deep, sandy
soils. The site consists of two burned rocks and a flake seen in an exposure about 60 m from the creek
channel. Cow trails within the creek bank were examined and no other cultural material was noted.
Because no impacts of any kind are expected here, no shovel tests were excavated. The site is being
recorded for management purposes. Possible future projects that involve subsurface disturbances (e.g. a
rebuild, construction of creek crossing) should involve additional investigations to determine what, if any,
additional aspects of the site are present. Eligibility for SAL or NRHP status remains unknown.
41BP840
41P840 is located at the edge of a cleared and sometimes plowed field on top of an interfluvial lobe
overlooking Elm Creek located 300 m to the west, at an elevation of 40 ft below the site. The site is a
lithic scatter consisting of the very small (5 mm) patinated edge of a biface and a fractured cobble of
purple quartzite. No other cultural material was seen on the slopes or banks on either side of Elm Creek,
although visibility is often limited by fairly dense young mesquite growth. . Due to the low impact
nature of the project, no shovel tests were excavated. This small scatter is being recorded as an
archaeological site for management purposes. Possible future projects that involve subsurface
disturbances (e.g. a rebuild, construction of creek crossing) should involve additional investigations to
determine what, if any, additional aspects of the site are present. Eligibility for SAL or NRHP status
remains unknown.
41P841
Historic Site Description
This historic site extends from approximately 30 meters northeast of the point at which the transmission
line crosses State Highway 304 to about 110 meters southwest of STR 9/3 for a total length of about 100
meters. Most of the materials observed at the site are located in the eastern half of the transmission line

438

ROW, and it is probable that the site extends further eastward outside of the ROW. Two historic features
– an underground cistern with above ground collar and concrete water trough – are present within the
transmission line corridor along with an artifact scatter.
The collar of the cistern is readily visible in the south-central part of the site as it rises 89 cm above
ground surface directly under the transmission centerline. The cistern appears to be constructed of
cement and the collar has an additional exterior coating of reddish-brown bricks. Inspection of several
bricks that have fallen off the collar walls showed that they have no maker’s marks and that they are
probably of local late 19th century manufacture. The interior opening of the collar has a 55 cm diameter
while the overall outer diameter of the collar is 101 cm. The subsurface interior of the cistern is filled up
to a level of about 3 meters below ground surface with trash debris that seems to consist of items less than
50 years in age.
The concrete trough is situated at the extreme southwestern edge of the site. It measures about 2 meters
in length and 85 cm in width. It is less well-preserved than the cistern, and is probably not as old as the
cistern. The trough probably functioned as a feeding or watering trough for farm animals.
Although no artifacts were noted around the trough, several yellow and reddish-brown bricks were
observed in the ROW corridor east and southeast of the cistern. Two sandstone boulders that may be
foundation stones for a structure were noted at distances 1.5 meters east and 17 meters southeast of the
cistern collar.
Most artifacts, however, were found about 20 to 40 meters northeast of the cistern within and north of the
bed of a dirt road. Artifacts observed in that area include approximately 50 plain whiteware ceramic
sherds, 1 porcelain fragment, 6 pieces of aquamarine colored glass, 4 purple (solarized) glass fragments, 1
clear glass plate rim fragment, 8 rectangular-headed nails, 1 metal wood stove leg fragment, and 1 metal
door or cabinet handle. Also, one whiteware sherd fragment with a portion of a maker’s mark was seen.
The maker’s mark consisted of the letters R JAU in a line above a second line having the numbers and
letters 5 4 N.
About 13 to 20 meters east of the east edge of this artifact scatter, a cluster of small and mostly
rectangular shaped sandstone rocks could be seen on ground surface. Since this area is outside of the
transmission line corridor, it was not intensive inspected. However, these rocks may have been associated
with a former chimney or house foundation.
An examination of the 1902 Bastrop, Texas 1/125,000 USGS map shows a cluster of eight houses at the
approximate location of the site. Numerous other clusters of houses are shown at other nearby locations
on the same terrace system that overlooks the Colorado River floodplain. Additionally, many other
houses are shown along dirt roads on the river floodplain. It is probable that the houses on the terrace and
the floodplain are related to intensive agriculture activities in the area called Hills Prairie.
No house structure is shown on the 1948 Bastrop, Texas 15’ USGS map at the site location. However, a
house is shown on the opposite side of State Hwy. 304 at a distance of 60 meters south of the site. A barn
or abandoned house is also shown about 80 meters south-southwest of the site on the 1982 Bastrop, Texas
7.5’ USGS map.
Plum Creek
Plum Creek has overflow channels that have formed sloughs or wetlands on either side, but most
significantly on the west, where the channel appears to b the actual creek. It was possible to travel around
the south edge of the slough and approach the actual creek channel, which is very deep (15-20 ft) and
heavily wooded except under the transmission line, where it is extremely brushy. Both exposed banks of

439

Plum Creek were examined, but there was no evidence of cultural material. Neither bank appears
conducive to near-surface archaeological sites. Because only shredding appears to be necessary, no
shovel tests were excavated. A representative of the landowner on the east side of Plum Creek mentioned
that “arrowheads” had been found on the west bank of the creek, north of the ROW. If it becomes
necessary to bulldoze an access path to the actual Plum Creek bank, then additional cultural resource
work should be considered, but at present it appears that any such path could be made with minimal
disturbance.
Colorado River – north bank
This was walked, but only one shovel test was excavated. Future ground disturbing projects would
require additional investigations.
Summary of Recommendations
There are only three locations on T-153 that are should be considered for avoidance within the scope of
standard maintenance procedures.
1.
Between Hwy 304 and STR 9/3 is an historic cistern within the center of the ROW. It is located
within an open field, and it does not appear that even mowing is needed at this time. However, crews
should take care not to damage this piece of private property.
2.
Nearer Lockhart, there are two small stream crossings that should not be modified if possible.
The first is Pecan Branch of Plum Creek, and the crossing is located about 150 feet west of STR 15. At
present it is drivable without modification. If it is felt significant earth movement is necessary here,
additional archaeological investigations are warranted.
3.
The second is a somewhat larger stream known as Dry Creek. At present it is not drivable, and it
does not appear to have been crossed by vehicles in the past. It is located immediately west of STR 7. If
it is felt significant earth movement is necessary here, additional archaeological investigations are
warranted.
Elsewhere, there is some concern at Plum Creek, which is flanked on both sides, but significantly so on
the south, by sloughs or wetlands. On the south, the actual bank of Plum Creek, east of STR 14, is
approachable only by going around the slough for some distance on either side of the ROW. If significant
earth movement is necessary to access this area, then additional archaeological investigations are
warranted.
Beginning at STR 12 and continuing for a distance of 300 ft west significant earth movement should be
avoided. It appears that only shredding will be necessary here.

Project Description:

T-374 Enhancement

Figure References:

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

LCRA proposes to enhance existing transmission line T-374 located in Kimble County, Texas. This line
was surveyed for cultural resources by PBS&J in 1999 prior to its construction. Twenty-two prehistoric
archaeological sites were recorded along the line, and an additional nine along access roads. Of these
sites, only two, 41KM169 and 41KM179 appear to have any additional research potential beyond that
already recorded. Ground-disturbing activities should be avoided within these two sites.
Site 41KM169 is an open campsite with burned rock and mussel shell present. PBS&J archaeologists
found no evidence that the site extended into the transmission line ROW. The site is located
approximately midway between STRs 18 and 19. It is therefore recommended that all activities
associated with the project be confined to the ROW between these two structures.
Site 41KM179 is a substantial burned rock midden located along a drainage between STRs 32 and 33.
Recent aerial imagery indicates the ROW was not cleared across this drainage at the time of construction,
and topographic maps suggest a stream crossing is not likely. It is therefore recommended that no ground
disturbance take place within this drainage, located about 530 feet south of STR 32 and 930 feet north of
STR 33.
If these two areas cannot be avoided than Archaeology Services should be contacted so that additional
avoidance measures can be devised or potential additional investigations can be conducted.

Project Description:

Wirtz Substation Borrow Pit

Figure References:

3, 190

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services require approximately 1100 cubic
yards of fill for planned construction at the LCRA’s Wirtz Substation in Burnet County. A site has been
chosen on LCRA property 1000 feet north of the substation for the source of this fill. This particular area
has been used as a source of construction fill in the past, perhaps as far back as the early 1950s when the
dam was constructed. No archeological surveys have been conducted in this area and it is not known if
any cultural resource sites are present or even if intact soil still exists. Since removal of the fill will
damage or destroy any unrecorded archeological sites, LCRA’s Archeology Services conducted a field
survey of the project area on July 31, 2008.
The area of the proposed construction fill source lies along the west edge of an existing borrow pit with
gradually sloping walls. Stream-worn cobbles and gravels are exposed near the top of the walls with
coarse granite sand below. The fill for the upcoming construction project will be removed from along the
upper edge of the existing pit, in effect extending it westwards. The level ground immediately outside the
pit has obviously been bladed at one time, removing any topsoil and leaving Pleistocene gravels exposed
on the surface. Because of its location, any cultural material would only be found on or just below the
surface, and be easily visible since vegetation is sparse.
The ground along the upper edge of the borrow pit was inspected for artifacts and features such as hearths
that might have been shallowly buried prior to the blading. No cultural material was found during the
survey and no archeological site is present in the project area.
Based on the results of the field investigation, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends that the
project proceed as planned As with all projects of less than two acres without archeological sites, our
staff will report the results of the investigation in our Annual Report to the Texas Historical Commission
for Calendar Year 2008.

Project No.

44.08

Project Description:

T-153 Revisit

Figure References:

5, 191

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

August 2008

On August 5, 2008, LCRA Archaeology Services staff revisited a prehistoric open campsite located along
a right bank tributary of Plum Creek near the town of Lockhart (Figures 191, 192). The site was recorded
June 24, 2008, by LCRA Archaeology Services staff as part of reconnaissance investigations prior to
standard maintenance activities on the transmission line. As no impacts were expected at the site,
investigations were limited to surface examination, resulting in unknown – and therefore potential –
eligibility for listing as a State Archeological Landmark or nomination to the National Register of
Historic Places. The revisit consisted of additional subsurface investigations within the transmission line
ROW in order to make a determination of eligibility for that portion of the site.
Four shovel tests (STs) were excavated, two on each side of the creek. Shovel Test 1 was placed on a low
rise near the north edge of the ROW on the east bank of the stream. It was excavated to a depth of 60 cm
through dark gray clay (loam) which graded to yellowish-brown clay (loam). Carbonate lenses were
observed between 20 and 30 cm and between 50 and 60 cm, and vertical cracks were present in the walls
of the test to a depth of 30 cm. Recovery (from 20 cm levels) consisted of one flake and three small
burned rock fragments in Level 1, four chips, three small chunks and one small burned rock in Level 2,
and no recovery from Level 3.
Shovel Test 2 was placed about 25 m south of ST 1, near the south edge of the ROW, in an area
containing a light, diffuse scatter of burned rock and lithic debitage. It was excavated through 50 cm of
gray clay which graded to yellowish-brown clay. Level 1 contained one chert spall and one small bone
sliver, Level 2 contained two chert chips, and there was no recovery from Level 3 (to 50 cm).
Shovel Test 3 was placed on the opposite side of the creek near an existing, recent dirt road crossing at
about the centerline of the ROW. It was excavated through 40 cm of dark yellowish-brown clay. Level 1
contained three flake fragments and two chert chunks, while Level 2 contained two burned spalls in the
upper 5 cm. There was no recovery below 25 cm.
Shovel Test 4 was placed about 15 m northwest of ST 3, near the north edge of the ROW. It was
excavated through 30 cm of dark yellowish-brown clay. No artifacts were recovered from this test.
As a result of these investigations it was determined that the data recovery potential from the portion of
the site within the transmission line ROW is too limited to warrant listing as a State Archeological
Landmark or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places. Site integrity has also been
compromised by extensive land clearing and brush burning, as well as obviously recent bone mixed with
possible prehistoric bone within the ROW. Subsurface aspects of the site remain unknown outside the
ROW. Should future work by LCRA include expanding the ROW then additional investigations,
especially to the north, are suggested.

449

Figure 191. Project 44.08, T-153 (41CW118) Revisit, McMahan USGS quad.

Figure 192. Detail of site 41CW118 with locations of shovel tests.
