Project No.

46.08a

County:

Caldwell

Project Description:

Lytton Springs Autotransformer

Figure References:

5, 193

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

August 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Lytton Springs Auto-Transformer Project will consist of
expansion the electrical substation facilities at Austin Energy’s Lytton Springs Substation and
construction of seven new transmission structures to tie LCRA’s existing T-382 and T-534 transmission
lines into the new substation facilities. This memo covers only the substation portion of the project, with
a separate report for the transmission line work to be issued at a future date.
The proposed substation expansion project is located about 0.70 miles east of the Mendoza community in
northern Caldwell County, Texas (Figure 1). The proposed substation additions will include the
construction of two new bays immediately west of the fenced perimeter of the existing substation. These
will cover an area measuring about 75x20 meters and will be constructed in an area not previously
disturbed by the original substation construction. LCRA will also construct a new control house just
outside the substation’s northeast fence in an undisturbed area. Other substation work will occur within
the fence of existing substation in areas heavily disturbed by previous construction.
A file search showed that the original Lytton Springs substation area was not surveyed for cultural
resource sites in 1979 as a part of the Fayette to Lytton Springs Transmission Corridor project by
archaeologists from the University of Texas. No cultural resource sites have ever been recorded at the
location of the substation.
The file search also indicated that there are no previously recorded archaeological sites in the proposed
substation expansion area. Available data suggests that there is a low probability for any unrecorded
Native American archaeological sites to be present in the proposed substation station expansion area. To
assess the potential for historic period archaeological sites, historic maps consisting of the 1910 Austin,
Texas 30’ USGS map (field surveyed in 1895-1896), the 1955 Montopolis, Texas 15’ USGS map, and the
1968 Creedmoor, Texas 7.5’ USGS map were examined to determine if any buildings had ever been
present within or near the project area. While the 1910 map did not indicate any structures in the project
vicinity, the 1955 Montopolis, Texas 15’ USGS map showed a farmhouse that was located within the
southwest part of the property area. That same structure is then depicted as an abandoned house or barn
on the 1968 Creedmoor, Texas 7.5’ USGS map. Although a recent aerial map indicates that the building
is no longer present, there appeared to be some potential that historic period archaeological remains such
as building foundations and artifacts could be present within or near the substation expansion area. For
this reason, a cultural resource field survey appeared to be warranted.
The cultural resource field survey of the areas where the new bays and control house will be built was
conducted on August 18, 2008 by the LCRA Archaeology Services staff. Field survey methods used
consisted of a 100 percent intensive pedestrian survey along with shovel testing. The field survey quickly
indicated that large sections of the area where the new bays will be constructed are covered by thick
grasses. Good ground surface visibility is present though along the outside edges of the existing
substation fence on the eastern edges of the bay construction area. Those areas were intensively
examined. Elsewhere, the archaeologist walked zig-zag transects to find bare and eroded patches of
ground surface that afforded some ground surface visibility. To further evaluate the potential for historic

452

Figure 193. Project 46.08a, b, Lytton Springs Autotransformer on Creedmoor USGS quad.

453

period materials, three shovel tests were excavated in areas with poor ground surface visibility. The
shovel tests measured 40 cm in diameter and were excavated in 20 cm increments. All fill from the
shovel tests was either screened through ¼ inch wire mesh or, in the case of dense clays, was broken apart
with shovel and trowel to search for any artifacts. No cultural resource sites were found in the area where
the new bays are to be constructed.
The archaeologist then proceeded to survey the area where the new control house will be constructed.
Some areas with good ground surface visibility were found along the adjacent substation fenceline and in
areas west of an oil containment pond. No cultural resource sites were found in the control house
construction area.
Since the areas examined for the substation work are less than two acres in total size and no cultural
resource sites were found, a report does not have to be sent to the Texas Historical Commission at this
time. Instead, the substation construction may proceed at this time and our staff will report the results of
the investigation at the end of the year in our Annual Report to the Texas Historical Commission for
Calendar Year 2008. Also, as stated in the first paragraph of this memo, a separate investigation report on
the transmission line work associated with this project will be produced at a later date.

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Archaeology Services staff has completed a cultural

Project Description:

T-367 Maintenance

Figure References:

5, 194

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

August 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct
maintenance work on its existing T-367 transmission line which is also known as the Cedar Hills/
Swiftex/Sim Gideon line. This transmission extends for a total length of 19.88 miles, and it is situated in
Bastrop County, Texas.
A file search showed that the transmission line was surveyed for cultural resource sites in 1995 by
archaeologists from Espey, Huston, & Associates (EH&A) during two separate investigations prior to
LCRA’s rebuilding of the line. As a result of those surveys, a total of 17 cultural resource sites were
recorded and assessed. The file search also showed that three addition cultural resource sites have been
found in more recent years by archaeologists from Texas State University in the portion of the
transmission line right-of-way (ROW) that transverses the Texas Army National Guard’s Camp Swift
training facility.
Review of data generated by EH&A and Texas State University work indicates that 6 of the 20 previously
recorded cultural resource sites on the T-367 transmission line are potentially significant and, thus,
require protection from any damages that might occur during the upcoming maintenance work. These
six sites consist of 41BP197, 41BP386, 41BP387, 41BP388, 41BP389, and 41BP778.
Locational information for these sites is as follows:
41BP197: From 150 ft. north-northeast of STR #276 to 75 ft. north-northeast of STR #277;
41BP386: From STR #188 to 165 ft. south-southeast of STR #188;
41BP387: From 245 ft. north-northwest of STR #187 to 420 ft. south-southeast of STR #187;
41BP388: From 50 ft. south-southwest of STR #264 to 300 ft. north-northeast of STR #264;
41BP389: From 225 ft. north-northwest of STR #192 to 150 ft. south-southeast of STR #192;
41BP778: From STR #219 to 100 ft. south-southeast of STR #219.
Descriptions and maps of these six sites found in previous cultural resource survey reports suggests that
use of a hydro-axe machine would not cause any additional damage to the sites beyond the damages
already caused by the original construction and rebuilding of the transmission line. However, any use of
machinery that causes any subsurface soil disturbance on these five sites is prohibited. Additionally,
since cultural materials at sites 41BP387 and 41BP389 appear to extend to the banks of stream channels,
the LCRA maintenance staff will need to consult with the LCRA archaeological staff prior to constructing
any needed new low water stream crossings adjacent to these two sites.

LCRA proposed a series of land and brush management activities at its Gloster Bend Resource Area.

Commission as necessary.

Project Description:

Double Horn South Fence

Figure References:

3, 197

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is planning to build a fence along a portion of the
southeastern boundary of its Double Horn Preserve starting from Lake Travis and running south
southwestwards approximately 900 meters to an existing cross fence. The proposed fence will pass
through three previously recorded archeological sites: 41BT100, 41BT101, and 41BT389. It will also
skirt the south edge of a fourth site, 41BT364. All these sites contain prehistoric components while
41BT389 also contains a Historic component representing a mid 19th century homestead and a later early
20th farmhouse both lying outside LCRA property. With the exception of 41BT364, these sites are
eligible for State Archeological Landmark designation.
On September 23, 2008, LCRA’s Archeology Services staff made a field survey of the proposed fence
route in order to locate any unrecorded sites in areas where substantial impacts – mainly land clearing will likely occur with this project. The survey found that most of the proposed fence route will run
through a corridor that was probably cleared years ago and is now only obstructed by branches from
adjacent trees. Clearing will probably still be needed at the west end of the proposed fence route and
through sites 41BT100 and 41BT389.
No new sites were located during the survey, however shovel testing was not done and buried cultural
deposits could still be present outside of previously recorded site areas. Areas in the vicinity of 41BT101
where the spring emits from the escarpment are particularly sensitive since the limits of this site have not
been established. Possible burned rock was seen on the surface in the thicket south of the springhead.
To avoid impacting these significant sites and any unrecorded sites, it is proposed that the clearing of
trees and other vegetation be done by methods - such as using mechanical shears attached to a skid steer that will not cause below ground disturbances. Shallow ruts of one to two inches will probably not harm
buried archeological deposits and are permissible. If deeper ground disturbances in any particular area
are deemed unavoidable then LCRA Archeology Services will need to conduct testing at that location
prior to clearing.

Project Description:

T-263 Maintenance and Overhaul

Figure References:

5, 198

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

September 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Archaeology Services staff has completed a cultural
resource file search for the proposed FY09 overhaul and maintenance work on Transmission Line T-263,
which is also known as the La Grange to FPP Transmission Line. The file search consisted of a check of
the Texas Historical Commission’s computized Site Atlas and the LCRA’s internal cultural resource files.
The file search indicated that the LCRA Archaeology Services staff had previously conducted a desktop
review of the T-263 line for proposed maintenance work in FY04. At that time, it was noted that there are
three previously recorded archeological sites, 41FY459, 41FY477, and 41FY518, located within the T263 right-of-way. These archeological sites are all located near the east end of the transmission line
corridor, and all were recorded by LCRA archeologists and contractors in association with the LCRA
Fayette Power Plant project. Current review of the Texas Historical Commission’s computerized Site
Atlas indicates that no new cultural resource sites have been recorded within or near the T-263 right-ofway (ROW) since the time of the FY04 review memo.
41FY459 is a multi-component archeological site as it contains both prehistoric materials and the
reinterred human remains from the historic Biegel-December cemetery. Although the prehistoric
component clearly extends in the transmission line corridor, the prehistoric materials were assessed as
insignificant by the LCRA archeological staff and the Texas Historical Commission in 1985. Thus, the
presence of the prehistoric materials does not place any special restrictions on the overhaul and
maintenance work. A visit to cemetery area in 2006 during a separate project showed that the reinterred
human remains and their associated gravestones lay well outside of the transmission line ROW. Thus, the
proposed overhaul and maintenance work should not have any negative impacts on the cemetery area.
The second site, 41FY477, is a former Charpentier family historic cemetery. In 1975, the LCRA
exhumed the three known graves and reinterred the remains in a cemetery in DeWitt County at the
request of family descendents. The former cemetery should within or very near the T-263 ROW in the
vicinity of Transmission Structure #16/3. According to Doug Marek of the LCRA Transmission Services
staff, the overhaul work will not include the replacement of Structure #16/3. Care should be taken during
any maintenance work if any ground-disturbing activities are included in the unlikely event that any
unmarked graves might have been overlooked.
The third site, 41FY518, was recorded in 1992 by the LCRA Archeology Services staff during inventory
work on the LCRA’s FPP property. This historic site was a rural farmstead that was settled about 1907
according to archival research. Construction of a newer house in the 1960s on the exact same site as the
older structure essentially destroyed the older site component. The Texas Historical Commission
concurred with the LCRA’s assessment that the site is not significant in 1992. Thus, there are no special
restrictions for the clearing and maintenance work in the area of this archeological site.
Aside from the data on previously recorded archeological sites, review of stream drainage and
topographic data would suggest that the only high probability area for unrecorded significant prehistoric
cultural resource sites would be along the terraces that flank Cedar Creek between Transmission
Structures #9/2 and #9/4 near the west end of the T-263 route. Should the LCRA field personnel involved

465

Figure 198. Project 52.08, T-263 Maintenance and Overhaul in Fayette County.

466

in the overhaul and maintenance need to conduct any ground-disturbing activities along stream banks in
the area between Structures #9/2 and #9/4, then the LCRA Archaeology Services staff should be notified
so that the area can be investigated in advance of the dirtwork.
As always, standard emergency discovery provisions are also applicable. Should any presently unknown
buried archeological materials be found, then work should cease in the immediate area of the discovery

Project No.

54.08

County:

Burnet

Project Description:

Dobyville Substation

Figure References:

3, 199

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The LCRA plans to build a new substation on a 2.4 acre tract owned by PEC near the small community of
Dobyville in Burnet County. Since the LCRA will be funding the project, a cultural resources
investigation is required before construction begins. LCRA’s Archeology Services staff first did a file
search to see if previous archeological investigations have taken place in the project area. We found that
PBS&J had surveyed a portion of the current project area in 2004 as part of an LCRA transmission line
rebuild project. The PBS&J archeologists surveyed within the 100’ ROW of the T-109 transmission line
which bisects the PEC property and covers an area of 0.9 acre within this tract. No sites were recorded in
the T-109 corridor. The remaining 1.5 acre of the property has never been investigated archeologically.
Therefore Archeology Services conducted a field survey on October 14, 2008 to determine if any
archeological sites are present and to assess their eligibility for State Archeological Landmark
designation.
All areas within the PEC property were walked over by Archeology Services staff. Ground visibility was
generally good with thick grass cover encountered only in the northwest corner. The property has been
cleared of trees in recent years and the ground probably bladed. Old burned piles appear to have been
compressed into the ground in places. No cultural material was seen other than that associated with
recent PEC use of the property.
Following the Texas Historical Commission’s (THC’s) guidelines for survey-level investigation, six
shovel tests were dug across the project area (Figure 1) to look for buried cultural material. Each test
involved excavating a 30 cm diameter circular hole and screening the dirt through a ¼” mesh to recover
artifacts. No artifacts were recovered during the shovel testing other than a fragment of a modern Coke
bottle in Shovel Test 6. All the tests encountered limestone bedrock or caliche at various depths, the
deepest being Shovel Test 5 in the southeast corner of the tract where bedrock was struck at 44 cm below
surface. The soil in this area was noticeably more clayey and darker in color with a surface zone of ashy
loam containing scattered charcoal, evidence of the recent clearing operations. The thinnest soil cover
was found in Shovel Test 2 on the north edge of the project area where limestone was found at a depth of
15 cm below the overlying pale yellow loam. To some degree, the soils encountered during testing
conformed with those mapped by the US Soil Conservation Service but the thin loamy Brackett soils
were found to cover a larger area of the PEC tract, approximately the western half, with the clayey Doss
soils covering most of the eastern half of the property.
To summarize, no archeological sites were found in the project area. In accordance with our MOU with
the THC, the agency which oversees compliance with the antiquity protection laws, projects less than two
acres in size where no archeological sites were recorded do not require a report and 30 day review period.
Instead, the substation project can proceed by virtue of this memo. Considering the intensity of the
survey and the previous ground disturbances, it is highly unlikely that any archeological features were
missed during our field investigation. However, if construction crews should uncover unexpected cultural
material, they should cease operations at the location of discovery and contact Archeological Services so
an evaluation can be made.

Project Description:

Reserve on Lake Travis, dredging

Figure References:

3, 201

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

December 2008

The owner of the Reserve on Lake Travis development wishes to dredge construction fill from three
coves extending into his property; these coves have been designated as Proposed Dredging North Site,
Proposed Dredging South Site 1, and Proposed dredging South Site 2 (Figure 1). While he owns the
portion of these coves above 670’ amsl, the LCRA owns the land below this elevation. All three coves
below 680’ amsl have not been surveyed for archeological sites so the proposed dredging could harm any
unrecorded archeological deposits present on LCRA land. After inspecting a map showing near-shore
contours of the lake bottom, the sides of the North Site cove were found to be steep with little potential
for archeological sites, however the two southern coves were depicted as having gradual inclines that
could harbor archeological deposits. On December 29, 2008, LCRA’s Archeology Services conducted a
cultural resources survey of the two southern coves to locate archeological sites and assess their potential
eligibility for State Archeological Landmark status. The combined area surveyed of both coves was 1.9
acre.
Proposed Dredging South Site 1 – the western and smaller of the two south coves - was found to contain
little or no sediments along its banks, with mostly bare limestone rock and talus visible. The bottom of
the cove is filled with recent sediments of sand and silt intermixed with driftwood and other debris. No
artifacts were found.
Proposed South Site 2, a somewhat larger cove than Site 1, did contain soil on its western bank near the
end of the cove. Shovel testing this area failed to recover any artifacts and the sediments were found to
contain large amounts of detritus (small rocks and organic matter) that indicate disturbance. A burned
rock scatter in the area was shovel tested and found to be shallow and intermixed with substantial
amounts of ash and charcoal but no artifacts. This feature appears to be the remains of a relatively recent
burn pile.
Since the portion of the project on LCRA property covers less than two acres and contains no
archeological sites, a report does not need to be sent to the Texas Historical Commission for approval and
the dredging can proceed without further archeological investigation. However, if the work crews should
uncover any presently unknown archeological features, dredging should stop in the immediate area of
discovery and LCRA Archeology Services should be contacted at 1-800-7776-5272, ext. 6714.

Project Description:

Calcasieu Point Dredging

Figure References:

3, 202

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

A private developer wishes to quarry sediments on Calcasieu Point to be used for construction fill (Figure
1). The area he proposes to dredge includes lake bottom lying between elevations of 670’ and 666’ amsl
which is owned by the LCRA. LCRA Archeology Services conducted a survey of the LCRA portion of
the proposed dredging area on September 30, 2008 to determine if Site 41TV1431, previously recorded
on the adjacent mainland, extends into that area or if other unknown sites are present there.
The entire point was found to be a gravel bar with a thick bed of sand packed with cobbles mostly of chert
and quartz overlying gravelly clay or limestone bedrock. Shovel testing found the cobble bed to be up
three feet thick in areas. These deposits, especially the upper foot, are loose and appear redeposited by
water currents. The only artifact recovered in the three shovel tests placed on the gravel bar was a simple
flaked tool in the upper, loose sediments. The artifact had a slight surface sheen suggesting it has been
pushed around in the sediments by water current and therefore is not in its original context. No true
archeological sites were found.
Since the portion of the project on LCRA property covers less than two acres and contains no
archeological sites, a report does not need to be sent to the Texas Historical Commission for approval and
the dredging can proceed without further archeological investigation. However, if the work crews should
uncover any presently unknown archeological features, dredging should stop in the immediate area of
discovery and LCRA Archeology Services should be contacted at 1-800-7776-5272, ext. 6714.

Project Description:

Llano County Boat Ramp

Figure References:

On October 6, 2008, LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted a field survey of Llano County’s

County:

Project Description:

T-184 Maintenance

Figure References:

5, 204

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The LCRA plans routine maintenance on their T-184 transmission line between the Lockhart and Luling
Substations. The portion of the corridor this line shares with T-153, running a distance of approximately
six miles east from Lockhart, was surveyed by LCRA’s Archeology Services staff in June, 2008. In
addition a small area where T-184 crosses Plume Creek was apparently surveyed by TxDOT with no new
sites recorded. The rest of T-184 has not been surveyed for cultural resources and may cross unrecorded
archeological sites. To identify these sites and assess their need for avoidance measures, LCRA’s
Archeology Services staff conducted a field survey on October 7 and 8, 2008. The survey was mostly
restricted to stream crossings where embankment modification by maintenance crews could harm any
archeological deposits that might be present. Areas near water sources also have a higher potential for
prehistoric sites. Three sites were recorded.
41CW122 is a prehistoric quarry site located on an escarpment slope overlooking Sixmile Branch, a small
tributary of Plum Creek. Small cobbles and gravels of flint and quartzite cover two prominent lobes
projecting from the escarpment. Similar gravels cover a large portion of the South Texas Plains and are
often referred to as “Uvalde Gravels.” Scattered amongst these naturally-occurring gravels in moderate
densities are artifacts consisting of flakes and cores, the byproducts of stone tool manufacture. Because
of the absence of diagnostic artifacts, use of this quarry cannot be assigned to any particular time period.
The site is shallow as evidenced by erosion on the side of one of the lobes. Further investigation will add
little additional information about this site and no avoidance procedures are required.
41CW123 consists of a single stone grave marker belonging to G. C. Neill (1820 to 1886) located a short
distance west of FM1322. The grave marker does not appear old and probably was erected by his
descendants, perhaps to replace the original marker which is no longer in evidence. Since the marker lies
within the transmission line ROW, the maintenance crews should be aware of its presence. A small
brushy area immediately south of the marker should also be avoided since it might be the actual grave
site. A large limestone boulder - the original marker? - is visible in the brush.
41CW124 is a lithic procurement locale situated on a slight rise south of the Clear Fork of Plum Creek
where Uvalde gravels are exposed on the surface. Cultural material present is typical of lithic
procurement use and includes flint chipping debris, cores, and at least one early-stage biface. This
material is less dense in the ROW than that at Site 1 and like the first site has little research value.
Therefore, this site does not need to be avoided during maintenance activities.
To summarize, three archeological were recorded during the survey of T-184. One of these sites,
41CW123, consists of a grave marker and a small thicket that could contain the actual grave site. Both
the marker and the thicket need to be avoided during the scheduled maintenance activities. This can

477

Figure 204. Project 63.08, T-184 Maintenance in Caldwell County.

478

easily be accomplished since they lie on the east edge of the ROW where clearing is not needed. The
marker lies 20 feet east of the east pole of Structure 34/3 and the thicket is immediately south of the
marker and extends southeastwards outside the ROW. Elsewhere if any unexpected archeological
material is encountered, please cease operations at the find site and contact Archeological Services so an
evaluation can be made.

Project Description:

T-170 Overhaul

Figure References:

5, 205

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct
overhaul work on its existing T-170 transmission line which is also known as the Winchester to Salem
transmission line. This transmission extends for a total length of 34.74 miles, and it is situated in Fayette
and Washington counties, Texas.
A file search showed that portions of the transmission line were surveyed for cultural resource sites in
1989 by archaeologists from Espey, Huston and Associates, Inc. Fourteen archaeological sites were
found by EH&A with three of these, 41FY240, 41FY500, and 41FY504, being recommended for
avoidance and preservation. In 2007, LCRA Archaeology Services staff revisited these three sites prior to
routine maintenance activities in order to assess the potential for the proposed maintenance activities to
impact these sites. At that time the LCRA staff also conducted spot checks at previously unsurveyed
stream crossings and at other areas not previously surveyed that were considered to have moderate to high
probabilities for unrecorded archaeological sites. As a result of that additional work, one new
archaeological site, 41WT72, was recorded and assessed. This new site was recommended for avoidance
and preservation.
During the cultural resource survey for maintenance activities the LCRA staff noted that single
transmission structures are situated within the boundaries of each of the four sites recommended for
avoidance and preservation. The transmission structures associated with each of the archaeological sites
are as follows:
1. STR 7/3 – located within archaeological site 41FY500
2. STR 15/8 – located within archaeological site 41FY504
3. STR 16/2 – located within archaeological site 41FY240
4. STR28/7 – located within archaeological site 41WT72
Although none of the four transmission structures within these archaeological sites will need to be
removed and replaced with a new structure, materials will be replaced or added onto each of the four
structures according to LCRA Transmission Services Engineering staff. Therefore, in each case, one or
two heavy trucks will need to set up adjacent to each pole and utilize outriggers to stabilize the vehicles.
The outriggers could penetrate into the ground, and thus could cause damage to subsurface archaeological
deposits. Our staff recommends that the personnel involved in working on the structures within each site
use 2 or 3 ft square metal sheets or wooden mats on the ground for the outriggers to set on. These metal
sheets/wooden mats are standard safety equipment that operators always have with them in the field. This
should alleviate any damage to subsurface deposits.
Although driving vehicles around these four structures is permissible, it is also recommended that field
personnel try to minimize vehicular traffic as much as possible during work in those areas. The
remainder of the proposed overhaul work may proceed without any restrictions or special conditions.
However, standard emergency discovery provisions are always applicable. The emergency discovery
clause states that should the personnel involved in the overhaul work uncover any presently unknown

Figure 205. Project 64.08, T-170 Overhaul in Fayette and Washington counties.

481

buried archaeological materials or features at other areas, then work should cease in the immediate area of
the discovery. The LCRA Archaeology Services staff should be contacted at 1-800-776-5272 ext. 6714
so that the find can be assessed in consultation with the Texas Historical Commission, and a
determination can be made as to whether any avoidance or mitigation measures are needed. In such a
situation, work may continue in other areas while the emergency discovery finds are being assessed.

Project Description:

T-309 Overhaul

Figure References:

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct
overhaul work on its existing T-309 transmission line which is also known as the Seguin to Cushman
transmission line. This transmission extends for a total length of only 2.02 miles, and it is located in
urbanized areas of the City of Seguin, Guadalupe County, Texas.
A file search showed that the LCRA Archaeology Services staff had previously conducted a cultural
resource assessment of this transmission line in 2006 when the LCRA purchased the existing transmission
line from the City of Seguin. As a part of the purchase project, LCRA was going to rebuild 12 of the
structures located at the north end of the line. At that time, LCRA was also proposing to conduct some
maintenance work, primarily trimming of tree limbs. The LCRA learned that some of the tree limb
trimming would include a very large oak tree that was considered to have potential historic importance to
some citizens in Seguin. The LCRA Archaeology Services staff researched the issue, and then asked the
Texas Historical Commission for a determination. The THC responded that the tree was not historical
significant and that the maintenance work could proceed.
In that letter to the THC, the LCRA Archaeology Services also provided data on the fact that there were
no previously recorded cultural resource sites elsewhere within the line right-of-way, and that there was a
low probability that any existed given the lack of any stream channels in the immediate vicinity. Further,
the LCRA noted that the vast majority of the ROW lay within previously disturbed areas where city
streets, drainage culverts, buried utility lines, and driveways were present. The THC concurred with the
LCRA’s assessment.
Based on the previous review in 2006, the currently proposed overhaul work may proceed at this time.
However, standard emergency discovery provisions are always applicable. The emergency discovery
clause states that should the personnel involved in the overhaul work uncover any presently unknown
buried archaeological materials or features at other areas, then work should cease in the immediate area of
the discovery. The LCRA Archaeology Services staff should be contacted at 1-800-776-5272 ext. 6714
so that the find can be assessed in consultation with the Texas Historical Commission, and a
determination can be made as to whether any avoidance or mitigation measures are needed. In such a
situation, work may continue in other areas while the emergency discovery finds are being assessed.

Project Description:

T-208 Overhaul

Figure References:

5, 207

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct
overhaul work on its existing T-309 transmission line which is also known as the Plum to La Grange
transmission line. This transmission extends for a total length of 8.39 miles, and it is located in Fayette
County, Texas.
A file search showed that the original T-208 transmission was not served for cultural resource sites prior
to its construction due to the fact that the construction occurred before to the date of the enactment of the
State Antiquities Code. However, in 1980 during a Texas Department of Transportation (TxDOT)
cultural resource survey of alternate routes for a proposed rebuilding of State Highway 71, newly
discovered site 41FY195 was found to partially extend into the T-208 right-of-way (ROW). Finds at this
site included hearth features and a Late Prehistoric arrowpoint. TxDOT archaeologists assessed the site
as potentially significant and stated that test excavations would be needed should the site be threatened by
highway construction. However, TxDOT later chose a different route for the highway construction.
In 2004, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff conducted a cultural resource assessment of the T-208
ROW prior to proposed transmission line maintenance work. Site 41FY195 was revisited and one new
archaeological site 41FY539 was recorded during that fieldwork. The LCRA staff found site 41FY195 to
be intact aside from one bulldozed area that had been previously noted in 1980. Cultural materials found
by the LCRA staff included chert chipping debris, burned rocks, and mussel shells. A cutbank exposure
indicated that these types of materials were distributed below ground surface to a depth of 112 cm. The
site was interpreted as a multi-component prehistoric campsite, and the previous assessment of the site’s
potential significance was confirmed. Special restrictions were placed on the maintenance work within
the site boundaries.
The second site investigated by the LCRA staff during the 2004 survey is 41FY539. This site is situated
near State Highway 77 on a high bluff overlooking the east bank of the Colorado River. This site was
determined to be a prehistoric lithic scatter/procurement site with a dense accumulation of chert chipping
debris, cores, and tested cobbles. Depth of cultural deposits was found to be only 30 cm, and no
diagnostic artifacts were found. The LCRA staff assessed the portion of the site within the transmission
line ROW as not significant, but noted that more substantial parts of the site could be present outside of
the transmission line ROW.
The THC concurred with the LCRA’s assessments for 41FY195 and 41FY539. A search of the THC’s
computerized Site Atlas indicates that no additional cultural resource sites have been recorded within the
T-208 ROW since the time of LCRA’s 2004 survey.
As a result of the LCRA’s cultural resource survey for the maintenance project, staff had placed
restrictions on the maintenance work at 41FY195, and had allowed work within the boundaries the second
archaeological site 41FY539 to proceed without any restrictions. Those same recommendations are
applicable for the current overhaul work.

Project Description:

T-132 Overhaul

Figure References:

5, 208

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority’s (LCRA) Transmission Services staff is proposing to conduct
overhaul work on its existing T-132 transmission line which is also known as the Flatonia to Hallettsville
transmission line. This transmission extends for a total length of 15.71 miles, and it is situated in Fayette
and Lavaca counties, Texas.
A file search showed that the line was not surveyed for cultural resource sites prior to its original
construction due to the fact that the construction predated the enactment of the Texas State Antiquities
Code. However, portions of the transmission line were surveyed for cultural resource sites in 2007 by the
LCRA Archaeology Services staff in anticipation of maintenance activities. As a result of the LCRA
Archaeology Services staff’s work in 2007, one new archaeological site, 41FY550, was recorded and
assessed. This new site was recommended for avoidance and preservation. Specifically, it was
recommended that the area between existing transmission structure 4/6 and the south bank of Big Rocky
Creek not be subjected to any ground-disturbing activities. That recommendation is applicable for the
current overhaul project.
The memo from the LCRA staff work for the maintenance project also noted that there is a potential for
unrecorded cultural resource sites to exist on the south side floodplain of the Lavaca River and on the
terraces adjacent to Long Branch Creek. On the south side floodplain of the Lavaca River unrecorded
cultural resource sites could be present between transmission structures 11/1 and 11/9. Along Long
Branch Creek, unrecorded cultural resource sites could be present between transmission structures 8/6 and
9/8. These portions of the Lavaca River floodplain and the terraces adjacent to Long Branch Creek were
not investigated by the archaeologists for the maintenance project because it did not appear that any
maintenance activities in those areas would require any ground-disturbing activities. In both of these
stretches of the transmission line, previous cultivation and the previous transmission line construction
activities may have damaged any unrecorded sites. Given this fact, any currently proposed overhaul
work would not further damage such unrecorded sites if it is conducted during dry weather conditions so
that tires of heavy machinery will not create deep ruts in undisturbed subsoils. Therefore, it is
recommended that all proposed overhaul work activities in these two areas be conducted during dry
weather conditions as a precaution to avoid any further impacts to potential sites.
The remainder of the proposed overhaul work may proceed without any restrictions or special conditions.
However, standard emergency discovery provisions are always applicable. The emergency discovery
clause states that should the personnel involved in the overhaul work uncover any presently unknown
buried archaeological materials or features at other areas, then work should cease in the immediate area of
the discovery. The LCRA Archaeology Services staff should be contacted at 1-800-776-5272 ext. 6714
so that the find can be assessed in consultation with the Texas Historical Commission, and a
determination can be made as to whether any avoidance or mitigation measures are needed. In such a
situation, work may continue in other areas while the emergency discovery finds are being assessed.

Project Description:

South Shore Park Maintenance Yard

Figure References:

5, 210

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

February 2008

LCRA proposes to enlarge an existing maintenance yard facility located at its South Shore Park on Lake
Bastrop in central Bastrop County, Texas. The existing facility is approximately 0.4 acres in size, and
abuts a septic field. A 35’ x 95’ area to the west of the facility and a 35’ x 100’ area to the south will
have vegetation removed by bulldozer and then covered with compacted roadbase material. Total
proposed impacts total 0.164 acres.
The existing facility and proposed improvements are within previously recorded archaeological site
41BP354, a part of the Camp Swift Demolition Area (Tomka and Crouch 1996). Materials found within
the site included inert land mines and small arms ordnance, along with screw pickets and barbed wire
entanglements. Most of the material and apparent activity area was located in the southern portion of the
site. Recommendations were for additional study, and/or preservation and erosion control.
More recent work in the area has included non-published inventory assessments as well as a trail system
running near the facility (Malof 2006). In addition, an assessment of Camp Swift by the U.S. Army
Corps of engineers included a magnetometer sweep along a transect through the southern improvement
area (Parsons Engineering 2003).
In the opinion of LCRA Archaeology Services staff the scope of the present project is unlikely to impact
any unknown significant archaeological materials. It is therefore recommended that the project be
allowed to proceed as described above.

Project No.

9.08

County:

Bastrop

Project Description:

River Front Drive Monitoring (report of results is in Part 4)

Figure References:

5, 212

Type of Investigation(s):

Monitoring

Date of Investigations:

March 2008

On February 19, 2008, the Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) Archaeology Services staff provided
information to you by letter concerning a discovery of prehistoric materials associated with site 41BP821
during construction of LCRA’s river access road at River Front Drive in Bastrop County, Texas. That
same day Dan Prikryl of the LCRA staff also discussed the finds with Mark Denton of your staff and
began to construct a remedial plan that would involve either the excavation of a 1x2 meter unit or the
capping of the materials exposed on the slope.
Buried prehistoric materials found during the earlier 2007 cultural resource survey of the project area
included a distinctive Toyah Phase beveled knife. Based on the elevations of subsurface artifacts found in
various cutbanks and backhoe trenches, it also appeared that the materials at 41BP821 might represent a
single Toyah Phase component of 15 to 20 cm thickness. During the initial trip to the site after it was
learned that construction had already started, large bison-sized bone fragments were found among the
cultural materials exposed on the bulldozed slope west of the proposed road bed. Although much dirt had
been removed on the slope, it was assumed that the elevation of the bones corresponded to the same depth
as the previously documented buried component. Because numerous bone fragments were present in a
discrete area covering about 170x70 cm, it seemed possible that the articulated portions of a buffalo
skeleton not removed during butchering could be eroding out of the slope.
Last week, the LCRA staff returned to the site to gather additional data for the remediation plan. Since a
vertical and horizontal datum had been established during the previous 2007 survey investigation, an
engineering transit was used to establish the vertical relationships of the exposed cultural materials to
those previously found. Elevations on the approximately 170x70 cm cluster of bison-sized bones and
several pieces of chert debitage and burned rocks that comprised the bulk of the materials exposed on the
slope showed that the cluster lay about 1.3 meters lower in elevation than that of the previously
documented component. Staff also observed that a very small number of tiny bone fragments, a burned
rock, and a chert flake were exposed further upslope at elevations compatible with the previously
documented component.
Staff also sent some time reassembling bone fragments within the cluster and included other small
fragments that had been pushed further downslope from the main cluster. A very minor amount of dirt
was removed within the bone cluster to more fully expose the bones. As a result of this work, two bisonsized bones were identified. These consist of a scapula and rib fragment. It was also noted that the thin
crusts of dirt adhering directly to the bones were the dark grayish-brown color of the Zone 3 sediment in
which the prehistoric component had been previously documented. However, most of the other soil on
the slope surface in this immediate area is the reddish-brown color of Zones 5 and 6 which were
documented the previous year in profiles as laying deeper than the cultural component. Importantly, too,
it does not appear that any additional bones or bone fragments are eroding out of the terrace slope at this
location.

496

Figure 211. Project 9.08, River Front Drive Monitoring on Utley USGS quad.

497

Our staff concludes that the scapula, rib, and several artifacts near these two bones mostly likely either
moved downslope from their original locations within the buried component back during prehistoric times
or were pushed downslope by the bulldozer during the recent construction activities.
At this point, staff has concluded that if a 1x2 meter unit is excavated, it should be placed upslope from
the main bone cluster in the area where other minor amounts of prehistoric materials are eroding out of
the slope at the same elevation as the previously documented component. However, we feel that if we
excavate a 1x2 meter unit, there would still be an erosion problem in future over an approximately 20
meter wide area where other materials related to the buried component could erode out of the slope.
Further, even if we tap down the backfill in the unit, it would likely come loose and the walls of the
excavation unit could erode.
At this point, it appears that covering the site area with protective material is the best course of action as
opposed to excavation of the 1x2 meter unit. I have spoken with Michael Rainey of the Bastrop County
NRCS (formerly the Soil Conservation Service), Barrie Coburn the Director of the TxDOT Landscape
Design and Enhancement staff, and Jodie Hermes of LCRA’s Parks construction staff about erosion
control materials for soils and slopes like those at the site.
Options considered included the use of Curlex, which is the green colored material that commonly used
on road right-of-way slopes and at new subdivision developments. Curlex is a fabric that eventually
breaks down as vegetation grows and takes root within the mesh. It is normally used to halt erosion of
slopes during the fall and winter months when there is little expected growth of new grass. A second
option examined is a more permanent plastic mesh product such as Enkmat, but Mr. Rainey said that his
experience with those types of products is that rainwater eventually gets under the plastic and starts
erosional channels.
A third option examined is the use of hydromulching, which involves spraying a mix of compost and
wood/paper fibers over the slope surface and then spreading grass seeds on the mix. Hydromulching is
used effectively in the Spring when new grass starts its growth cycle. Based on seasonal conditions,
LCRA proposes the use of hydromulching.
LCRA also proposes to remove the bison scapula and rib that have been pieced back together prior to
laying down the hydromulch, since it does not appear that the non-butchered portion of an articulated
bison skeleton is present. Because the buried component appears to be only 15 to 20 cm thick, LCRA
does not feel that covering the area where the component surfaces on the slope with 6 to 12 inches of fill
material is necessary before laying down the hydromulch material and seeding the slope surface. The
current slope surface has been packed down by bulldozer
Traffic and any loosely packed introduced fill material and the vegetation that would start to grow on it
would probably erode given the sharp 10 to 12 degree angle of the slope. Runoff water on the upslope
side of the hump created by the fill material might also create a runoff channel to the left or right lateral
sides of the hump producing an erosional cut.
LCRA Archaeology Services staff will also monitor the results of the hydromulching to determine its
effectiveness. Should the hydro mulching not halt the erosion, then our staff will consult with you to
determine needed actions. We ask for your concurrence with this assessment and recommendation.

498

Project No.

12.08

County:

Guadalupe

Project Description:

Galle Substation

Figure References:

5, 212

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

February 2008

LCRA proposes to construct a new electrical substation south of the community of Zorn, adjacent to State
Highway 123, in northern Guadalupe County, Texas. The substation will be within a 4.85 acre portion of
an agricultural field and will be accessed by a 60-ft wide road that will connect to SH 123. The substation
will tap into existing LCRA transmission line T-203.
LCRA Archaeology Services has conducted a file and map review of the proposed project. The project
area is located on a broad upland divide dissected by tributaries to York Creek, which it overlooks to the
north. It is within the mapped Cretaceous-aged Navarro Groups and Marlbrook Marl, undivided
formation, which consists primarily of marl, clay, sandstone and siltstone. Soils are mapped as Houston
black gravelly clay. There are no structures presently mapped within the project area, nor on the 1911
USGS map.
The adjacent transmission line T-203 was selectively surveyed for cultural resources in 2006 by LCRA
Archaeology Services staff in advance of a proposed maintenance (clearing) project (Hixson et al.
2007:308). Six archaeological sites, 41GU94-41GU99, were recorded at that time. Two of these,
41GU97 and 41GU99, were recorded about 700 m both south and north, respectively, from the project
area. Both were within drainage systems. The following site descriptions are from the internal memo and
2006 Annual Report.
Site 41GU97 is located between STRs 15-17 and also contains both a prehistoric and historic component.
The prehistoric component consisted of a very low density lithic artifact scatter amongst ancient gravels
in the plowed fields to the north and on the hill and plowed field to the south of a York Creek tributary
channel. There is also a low density scatter of burned rock located atop the hill near STR 17.
Considering that plowing likely churned up the upper 30cm of topsoil, part of the site is located on a
hillslope, surface visibility is very good, and that the lithic scatter here was extremely low, no shovel tests
were dug. Due to all these factors this site is assessed as not significant. It is further recommended that
the maintenance and creek crossing repair work be allowed to proceed.
The historic component of Site 41GU97 consists of a large barn constructed of corrugated tin with an
associated trash scatter. The trash scatter consists of some purple glass, whiteware, various metal cans,
and scrap metal. From talking with the landowner’s grandson we learned that his grandparents moved
onto the property in the 40s or 50s and built the barn and what he thought was a house (though it looked
like a long garage and/or carport). The house/garage/carport later blew down in a storm; the barn remains
standing. The barn and remnants of the other structure are outside our ROW and therefore shouldn’t be
impacted by any maintenance-related activities.
Site 41GU99 is a prehistoric camp site located along York Creek approx. 200 m east of the Zorn
cemetery. Numerous flakes, 3-4 early-stage bifaces, and an Ensor-like point base were found at this site.
Three shovel tests were dug at this site. ST 7, located within the ROW, 75 m south of STR 9 and 21 m
north of the York Creek channel, was dug down 50 cm through dark gray clay loam, stopping when the
soil became very clayey. One quarter-sized tertiary flake with heavy patination was recovered from Level

499

Figure 212. Project 12.08, Galle Substation on Geronimo USGS quad.

500

1. ST 8, located within the ROW, 50 m south of the same creek channel (70 m south of ST 7) and 40 m
north of STR 10, was dug down 50 cm through brownish-gray clay loam, again stopping when the soil
became very clayey. One flake and six pieces of burned chert were recovered from Level 1; three pieces
of burned chert were recovered from Level 2, and nothing was recovered from Level 3. ST 9, located
within the ROW, in a plowed field 35 m south of STR 10 (75 m south of ST 8), was dug down 60 cm
through dark gray clay loam, again stopping when the soil became very clayey. Nothing was recovered
from this shovel test. North of Site 41GU99, within the ROW (across the road that leads to the
cemetery), is a plowed field with a low density scatter of lithic debitage and burned rocks. South of Site
41GU99, within the ROW bordering two York Creek tributary channels are more plowed fields
containing ancient gravels and a very low density lithic debitage scatter. Because this site has some
potential for additional diagnostic artifacts it is recommended that no soil removal or subsurface activities
be conducted at this site, otherwise transmission line maintenance may proceed.
Although it cannot be conclusively demonstrated that the transmission line adjacent to the present project
area was surveyed, it is clear that it was considered a low probability area for containing intact
archaeological materials. The present map and files review supports this conclusion, and it is
recommended that the project be allowed to proceed without additional cultural resource investigations,
with the understanding that standard emergency discovery procedures will be in place.
LCRA requests concurrence from your office with these recommendations.

The Texas Department of Transportation (TxDOT) is proposing to widen U. S. Highway 290 between
Ranch Road 12 and Nutty Brown Road in northern Hays County, Texas. The road widening project will
require the Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) to relocate and improve existing waterlines within
the TxDOT right-of-way (ROW). The required work consists of relocation of approximately 300 linear
feet of 8-inch diameter PVC waterline from the existing TxDOT ROW into a newly designated LCRA
waterline easement adjacent and parallel to the existing ROW (see attached map). The wateline
relocation will require the excavation of a backhoe trench to a depth of 4 feet below ground surface for
the subsurface placement of the waterline.
A file search conducted by the LCRA Archaeology Services staff showed that there are no previously
recorded cultural resource sites within or near the proposed relocation area. A cultural resource
assessment of the original waterline project by PBS&J in 1999 had concluded that the original waterline
easement in this vicinity was a low probability area that did not require a field investigation. The Texas
Historical Commission concurred with that assessment. The currently proposed relocation area is situated
just upslope to the northwest in a similar low probability area. Therefore, the LCRA recommends that the
proposed construction be allowed to proceed without any additional cultural resource investigations. We
request your concurrence with this recommendation, with the understanding that standard emergency
discovery provisions are applicable.

Project Description:

Wirtz Substation Expansion

Figure References:

3, 214

Type of Investigation(s):

Date of Investigations:

June 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to expand the Wirtz Electrical Substation,
which is located immediately below (east) of Wirtz Dam in Burnet County, Texas. The expansion will
consist of moving the boundary approximately 88 feet northward and as much as 132 feet on the northeast
side. Some new substation equipment will be placed within the expansion area and a new drainage ditch
will be excavated within the outer edges the new north and east boundaries. The ditch, which will be
excavated to variable depths ranging from 6 inches to 8 feet, will channel water from heavy rainfall
events around the substation southeastward into an existing drainage cut into the Colorado River.
Additionally, a new chain link fence will be constructed on the perimeter boundary of the additional
acreage and a section of new road will be constructed on an east-west line into the eastern part of the
expansion area.
A file search conducted by the LCRA Archaeology Services staff showed that there are no previously
recorded cultural resource sites within the proposed project area. One previously recorded site, 41BT266,
is located about 100 meters south of the existing substation near the north bank of the Colorado River.
This site was recorded in 1993 by Bruce Nightengale of the LCRA staff during a cultural resource survey
for LCRA’s proposed flood damage repair and slope stabilization project. A total of only three flint flakes
were found, and Nightengale stated that the soils had previously been disturbed to such an extent that site
interpretation was impossible. The site was assessed as not significant.
The project area is within the area surveyed by Humphreys (1951) for the construction of the Wirtz Dam.
No cultural resource sites were found in this area by Humphreys. The records review also indicated that
no cultural resource survey had been conducted prior to the original construction of the substation. A
review of photographs at the LCRA Archives office suggests that the project area was heavily disturbed
by the dam construction and is within a refilled borrow pit. Given this fact and the results of
Nightengale’s nearby survey, the LCRA recommends that the proposed construction be allowed to
proceed without any additional cultural resource investigations. We request your concurrence with this
recommendation, with the understanding that standard emergency discovery provisions are applicable.

Project Description:

Elgin Wastewater Rehab

Figure References:

5, 215

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

May 2008

In 1997, LCRA acquired much of the city of Elgin’s wastewater infrastructure, including existing
underground lines that have since been found to be suffering from infiltration and leakage. As a first
phase of rehabilitating the line, LCRA proposes to take advantage of street work already planned by the
city to replace approximately 3700 ft of line and 18 manholes. The work will be conducted by the city of
Elgin, and LCRA will provide engineering and other oversight. All work will be conducted within
existing roads or road ROWs, and the new pipe will be placed within the previously excavated trenches.
A review of the Historic Sites Atlas shows no recorded archaeological sites within the areas presently
proposed for construction, nor are any recorded within the main part of the city. Given the nature of the
proposed project, LCRA Archaeology Services recommends that no cultural resources survey is
necessary, and further recommends that any modifications and/or future wastewater refurbishment
projects in Elgin that fall under the same general parameters be allowed to proceed without consultation
with your office, as it is felt that it is highly unlikely any archaeological sites eligible for formal listing as
State Archeological Landmarks or nomination to the National Register of Historic Places will be
impacted by any such work.

506

Figure 215. Project 30.08, Elgin Wastewater Rehab on Elgin East and Elgin West USGS quads.

507

Project No.

39.08

County:

Burnet, Travis

Project Description:

Muleshoe Bend Cisterns Clean-Up (report on monitoring is in Part 4)

Figure References:

3, 216

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

July 2008

The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) has an ongoing hazardous materials remediation and
removal program for its properties. Program activities proposed for the current fiscal year include
removal of hazardous materials from underground cisterns at historic sites in LCRA’s Muleshoe Bend
Recreation Area. Individual underground cisterns have previously recorded by LCRA archaeologists at
Muleshoe Bend archaeological sites 41BT283, 41TV250, 41TV507, 41TV508, and 41TV1779.
Additionally, two underground cisterns have been previously recorded at Muleshoe Bend archaeological
site 41TV499. Importantly, the remediation/removal project will involve the removal of most of the
underground cisterns and their contents by utilizing a backhoe. The proposed program activities also
include the removal of some potentially hazardous materials at trash dumps associated with sites
41BT283 and 41TV498. These trash dump materials mostly consist of large metal objects such as
barrels, car parts, and wash tubs which are visible on ground surface.
With the exception of site 41BT283, all archaeological sites listed in the previous paragraph were historic
tenant farmer house sites that were components of the Turner Farm. Previously, historian Dan Utley
conducted archival research and oral history work on the history of the Turner Farm and its associated
tenant farmer house sites for LCRA. That work was reported in Utley’s 1995 publication entitled
Through Boundary Waters. Although Utley’s research was not been able to precisely date original
construction of these tenant farm houses, according to one informant at least some of the tenant farm
houses were present by 1881. Artifacts found at these sites range in age from the late 19th through the
early 20th centuries. The tenant farming system operated until LCRA purchased the Turner Farm during
the construction of Marshall Ford Reservoir (now called Lake Travis) in the mid 1930s. LCRA
subsequently used heavy machinery to raze these farm houses, but left the underground cisterns intact.
Site 41BT283 was not a part of the Turner Farm property. Utley noted that a contour map drawn of the
impoundment area of the Marshall Ford Reservoir in 1936 depicts this house and labels it as the Jack
Hays family house. Artifacts ranging of late 19th and early 20th century ages were noted at the site by
LCRA staff archaeologists during subsequent visits to the site.
In late 2007 and early 2008, LCRA archaeologists conducted an intensive cultural resource survey for
LCRA’s proposed Muleshoe Bend Capital Improvements Project. Sites 41TV498, 41TV499, 41TV507,
and 41TV508 were revisited and assessed during that project. As a result of that survey, sites 41TV498,
41TV507, and 41TV508 including their underground cistern features were assessed as not eligible for
inclusion in the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) and not meriting formal designation as State
Archeological Landmarks (SAL). A portion of 41TV499 containing evidences of an early historic
component that may predate the tenant farm house system was assessed as potentially significant, and that
part of the site was recommended for avoidance during the construction of the capital improvements
project. However, the two cistern features at 41TV499 are not located within the portion of site 41TV499
that was assessed as potentially significant. The Texas Historical Commission (THC) concurred with
these assessments on February 21, 2008.

508

Figure 216. Project 39.08, Muleshoe Bend Cisterns Clean-Up on Pace Bend USGS quad.

509

Based on the THC’s concurrence with these assessments the LCRA proposes to move forward with the
total removal of the cisterns at sites 41TV499 and 41TV508 along with the trash removal at site
41TV498. Since site 41TV507 has an intact house foundation and root cellar pit, and it is located
adjacent to one the hiking trails associated with the Capital Improvements Project, the site has future
interpretative potential. LCRA proposes to excavate into this cistern to remove hazardous materials and
then restore this cistern feature so that it can be used a potential exhibit along one of the future hiking
trails.
Since sites 41BT283, 41TV250, and 41TV1779 are not located within the area surveyed for the Muleshoe
Bend Capital Improvements Project, the NRHP and SAL status of these sites has not been previously
determined. These sites have recently been revisited by the LCRA Archaeology Services staff with
emphasis on gathering additional data on their cistern features. As with many of the other cisterns,
various types of trash debris are visible within the cisterns. It is assumed, however, that the trash debris
dates to the time of the abandonment of the house sites in the mid 1930s and later. Thus, it is doubtful
that the materials within the cisterns have any historic value. Inspection of the mortar on the collars of
these cisterns suggests that it is all of late 19th century date. Based on these data, the LCRA Archaeology
Services staff assesses the cistern features at 41BT283, 41TV250 and 41TV1779 as insignificant. The
LCRA Archaeology Services staff recommends that the removal of the cisterns at 41BT283 and 41TV250
be allowed to proceed. Inspection of the layouts of these sites and the presence of existing nearby gravel
roads indicates that the cistern removal at these two sites can be completed without damaging other
features such as the house foundation remnants. Inspection of the trash scatter located west of the cistern
at 41BT283 revealed several large metal objects such as wash tubs and barrels. Removal of these objects
is also recommended.
The field visit showed that cistern at 41TV1779 still holds water and has an intact above ground collar.
LCRA proposes to attempt to leave this cistern intact as a representative example of the cisterns at the
former tenant farmhouse sites. Remediation work at site 41TV1779 by the LCRA hazardous materials
staff will involve use of a rope with grapple hook to remove a barrel that is floating within the standing
water at the bottom of the cistern. LCRA will also process a sample of the water within the cistern to
ensure that it does not contain any hazardous chemicals. Should the water sample analysis indicate that
harmful chemicals are present and thus necessitate the removal of the cistern, LCRA will further consult
with the THC.
The LCRA Archaeology Services staff will also conduct spot monitoring during the excavation and
removal of the other cisterns described above. One purpose of the monitoring will be to ensure that pre1870 era artifacts of significance are not present within some of the cisterns. Further, during the
monitoring the LCRA staff proposes to measure the dimensions of the underground portions of the
cisterns to determine whether they are all one standard size or, instead, are of variable dimensions.
We request your concurrence with these assessments and recommendations for the proposed hazardous
materials remediation and removal at Muleshoe Bend.

510

Project No.

53.08

County:

Wharton

Project Description:

Jarvis Creek Bridge

Figure References:

5, 217

Type of Investigation(s):

Field survey

Date of Investigations:

September 2008

LCRA proposes to remove an existing wooden bridge that crosses Jarvis Creek just west of the
community of Lane City in southeastern Wharton County, Texas. The bridge is part of a road owned by
LCRA that is used to access LCRA’s Gulf Coast Irrigation Pump Station, which is located on the
Colorado River about one mile west of the bridge. Due to the deteriorating condition of the bridge, it is
no longer a viable means of accessing the pumping plant. A nearby low water crossing is subject to
flooding. LCRA therefore proposes to place two large (ca. 8 ft) culverts and fill at the bridge location to
allow continued access to the pumping plant.
A cultural resources investigation was initiated in late August, 2008, when LCRA Archeology Services
was notified of the project. The investigation consisted of a map and files review combined with historic
research, photo and sketch documentation and assessment of impacts to potential archaeological
resources.
Historic Context
The bridge is located on a former county road (Wharton CR 120) that was originally a spur of the Cane
Belt (later Gulf, Colorado and Santa Fe/Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe) railroad. The railroad spur
appears to have been built between 1902 and 1919, based on a map of the irrigation system below
Wharton (Taylor 1902:40) and a USGS map from 1919 (revised 1929) (Figure 218), and runs parallel and
about 100 feet south of the primary irrigation canal from the pumping plant. The Cane Belt Railroad
eventually ran from Sealy to Matagorda, and was chartered in 1898 to facilitate sugar cane and other
industries (Reading 2002). The town of Lane City, named after railroad president Jonathan Lane, appears
to have been a true railroad town, and was established in 1901 around a store and post office, and was
soon a commercial and social center (Hudgins 2002a). The Cane Belt Railroad itself was short-lived,
however, and following financial problems was sold to the Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe in 1903, which
in turn leased it to the Gulf, Colorado and Santa Fe in 1905.
The Bay Prairie Irrigation Co, (aka Southern Irrigation Co. and Lane Irrigation Co.) established the
pumping plant near Lane City probably in the 1890s (Hudgins 2002b). In 1904 the plant was described as
the “largest of its kind in the world” (Hudgins 2002a). In 1909 a large storm (probably the fourth
hurricane of that year [Wikipedia Contributers]) destroyed much of Lane City and severely damaged the
pumping plant. This seems a logical time for the railroad spur to have been built, in order to aid in
reconstruction of the plant. The USGS map from 1919/1929 also shows an apparent community called
Hoid at the location of the pumping plant. No additional information on this place name as been located.
About 1940 the railroad spur was removed and a county road was placed upon the rail bed (Hudgins
2002a) (Figure 219).
Present Condition
The bridge is an all-wooden, creosote treated trestle very close to 100 ft in length and 16 ft in width
(Figure 220). It is built upon six sets of three-member piers (pylons) placed approximately 16 ft apart

511

(Figure 221). The bridge has a maximum height of perhaps 30 ft above the channel of Jarvis creek,
which is about 70 ft from the west end. The piers range from about 10 to 16 inches in diameter. Near
each set of piers are the remains of earlier four-member piers (Figure 222). It is unknown when these
were replaced. Upon each set of piers are 10 x 10 in beams (headers) that span the width of the bridge.
The main bridge rails (joists) are 6 x 17 in, and overlap the headers by 2-3 ft. Seven joists are used to
create the primary linear support network. The bridge deck (Figure 223) is composed of tightly spaced
stringers that are about 2.75 x 7.75 in, each about 16 ft in length. Running lengthwise along the bridge
are two treads, each comprised of three parallel boards measuring 3.75 x 13.5 in. These are staggered in
position, and the treads are spaced to accommodate car and truck traffic. Two low rails (parapets)
constructed of the same size material as the stringers are on either edge of the bridge.
The bridge is anchored on the west end by four pylons that also form the bracing for an abutment. The
abutment appears to be a continuation of the bridge tread, in that the lumber is of the same dimension as
the stringers. The abutment is about 10 ft high on the western end of the bridge, and about 3 ft high on
the eastern end. On the west end of the bridge are two wing walls constructed of boards very similar in
dimension to the secondary (linear) bridge tread. Both wing walls are bulging from pressure of the fill
behind them, and the one on the north side has partially collapsed, and on the southern side the pylons
used for support have rotted and collapsed (Figure 224). The east end of the bridge is of similar
construction but lacks the wing walls.
In addition to the new pier arrangements, there are other signs of rebuilding and recycling of materials.
The main joists consistently have visible holes in the overlapping ends (Figure 225), indicating bolts or
other fasteners that are no longer necessary. One of the joists has a large notch mid-span, with a large
galvanized nail, the result of a previous toenail fastening, protruding from it (Figure 226). One of the
piers at the western abutment has remnants of barbed wire and fence staples very near the abutment wall,
at such an angle as to indicate the pier, at minimum, was twisted since placement (Figure 227). It more
likely came from a different location, as the remaining three piers have no similar fasteners. The
secondary linear tread boards (runners) have consistent wide shallow notches near the ends (Figure 228),
suggestive of previous use in another capacity, such as rafters laid across a building header. Metal
fasteners are a combination of galvanized bolts, flat iron brackets, and cast iron washers (Figure 229).
These last have the greatest appearance of age, but are placed in seeming random fashion across the
bridge supports. Felt paper is present on top of the four pier at the western abutment, and is fastened by
tacks that also appear fairly old (Figure 230).

Archaeological Considerations
Because removal of the bridge and the existing low water stream crossing will cause some earth
disturbance, an archaeological investigation was also undertaken. The investigation consisted of a file
review for previously recorded sites (none are present at the bridge location) and a field assessment of
impacts. The bridge removal and subsequent construction of a large culvert crossing will be entirely
within the heavily disturbed and built up railroad bed and stream embankments. Exposures and
disturbances were examined for both prehistoric and historic materials. A single crude iron fastener was
seen on the rail bed past the west abutment (Figure 231). No other types of artifacts, other than remnant
railroad gravel, were seen. The existing low water crossing that will be removed is within a low lying
area of the creek, and does not impinge on higher, more stable banks. No shovel tests were excavated, as
there was no indication of previously undisturbed deposits that might contain historic or prehistoric
materials.

512

Summary and Recommendations
LCRA proposes to remove a decaying and dangerous wooden trestle bridge that spans Jarvis Creek near
the community of Lane City in Wharton County. The bridge was built sometime after 1902, with 1909 or
1910 being a possible date of construction. The bridge was built as a spur of the Gulf, Colorado and
Santa Fe (Cane Belt) Railroad, apparently to service the existing pumping station of the Bay Prairie
Irrigation Company. The railroad spur was discontinued about 1940, and served thereafter as a county
road. At some point, possibly after LCRA purchased the irrigation system in the early 1970s, the road
was privatized. The bridge is now only accessible through a series of locked gates.
An examination of the bridge indicates at least one, and quite probably several, rebuilding episodes.
There is widespread evidence of movement and recycling of materials throughout the bridge. A four-pier
support network was replaced by a three-pier system sometime prior to 1985.
Although the bridge was part of a regional system that supported agricultural, industrial and other
economic pursuits during the first half of the twentieth century, it appears to lack the structural integrity
that might otherwise allow it to be determined potentially eligible for listing on the National Register of
Historic Places. LCRA therefore requests a finding of “No Effect” on eligible properties and concurrence
that the bridge can be removed. (The History Programs Division of the Texas Historical Commission and
the National Register Coordinator have conferred and are in agreement that the bridge is not eligible for
listing on the National Register of Historic places – see attached copy of e-mail).

Project No.

57.08

County:

Comal

Project Description:

Landa Mills Remediation (historical report is in part 4)

Figure References:

3, 232

Type of Investigation(s):

Historic research

Date of Investigations:

August 2008

LCRA continues to own approximately six acres in the city of New Braunfels that was once part of Landa
Industries. The parcel is located north of Fredericksburg Road, south of the Wurstfest grounds, and east
of the old Comal Power Plant (Figure 2). The property was used primarily for power production, and
contained both steam and hydro generation facilities. Structures that remain are the final hydro plant,
constructed in 1928, and the remains of the steam plant, built in 1917. Neither of these structures has
been formally assessed for eligibility for listing on the National Register of Historic Places.
As part of ongoing, proactive environmental stewardship, LCRA is assessing the property for
contaminated materials. This assessment will include a series of backhoe trenches and core samples
designed to locate buried potentially hazardous materials, primarily the waste byproducts of coal burning
(clinkers). Other assessments may include testing for asbestos, lead, and petroleum products.
The area proposed for backhoe trenches consists of approximately 0.55 acres that is between the present
forebay, Landa Park Drive, and the old steam plant. Backhoe trenches will be excavated to the final
depth of any contaminants, which is not expected to b below 2-3 feet (60-90 cm). The number of
trenches will be at the discretion of the project manager, but is not expected to exceed four of five.
Sampling in other areas will almost certainly be limited to soil cores. Areas that are identified as
contaminated will have the soil removed and disposed of appropriately.
LCRA Archaeology Services has completed a historical review of development in and around the LCRA
property for inclusion in an internal remediation report. This review was based in large part on
examination of Sanborn Fire Insurance Company maps and period photographs. This review indicates
that the area proposed for backhoe trenching has been severely impacted and modified through time. It
considered to be extremely unlikely that intact prehistoric deposits are present, and that historic materials
will be limited to industrial byproducts with little interpretive value.
LCRA Archaeology Services therefore recommends that the backhoe trenching be allowed to proceed,
with the understanding that if unexpected prehistoric or historic materials are discovered during trenching,
then trenching will cease at the location of the discovery until a professional assessment of the discovery
can be made.
A copy of the historical investigation is included for your information.

Figure 232. Project 57.08, Landa Mills Remediation on New Braunfels West USGS quad.
