Project No.

61.08

County:

Llano

Project Description:

Lake Buchanan Water Tank

Figure References:

3, 233

Type of Investigation(s):

File search

Date of Investigations:

October 2008

The LCRA is planning to construct a water storage tank to supply water to an existing pipeline on the
south side of Lake Buchanan in Llano County. The tank will be built on a y.; acre site at the upper end of
the northwest slope of Long Mountain. A private developer will construct the pipe system tying the tank
to the existing waterline on Hwy 29 along a path that the developer has already cleared of vegetation.
A file search showed that no archeological sites have been previously recorded at the location of the
proposed tank. The file search also indicated that the area has a low potential for prehistoric sites because
of the slope and distance from water sources. In any case, the thin soils (Roughcreek-Rock outcrop
complex) and exposed rocks would preclude the presence of intact, buried cultural material. Review of
the 1909 Burnet, Texas 15' USGS map and the current 1967 Kingsland, Texas 7.5' USGS map do not
show any houses or farmsteads at the project area location.
Based on the results of the file search, the project area is assessed as having a low probability for cultural
resource sites. An archaeological survey does not appear to be warranted, and the LCRA Archaeology
Services staff recommends that the proposed construction be allowed to proceed. We ask for your
concurrence with our assessment and recommendation for this project.

Part 4. Results of Monitoring and other Projects not Previously Reported to THC.
This section contains the detailed results of two monitoring projects, as well as the results of archival

528

Archaeological Monitoring at 41BP821 for the Lower Colorado River Authority’s Proposed
Rio Vista River Access Road Project, Bastrop County

By Daniel J. Prikryl
Principal Investigator

TAC permit #4479
Project 9.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Parks and Resource Conservation
April 2008

529

Introduction
The Lower Colorado River Authority (LCRA) is proposing to construct the new Rio Vista river access
site on the Colorado River upstream from their McKinney Roughs Nature Park. This new access point
will be utilized for putting rafts and canoes into the river for guided river excursions provided by
McKinney Roughs staff for school groups and the general public. The project area is located on the south
side of the Colorado River adjacent to River Front Drive in the Pope Bend area (Figure 234). In February
and March 2007, the LCRA Archaeology Services conducted an intensive cultural resource survey of the
area to be affected by the proposed construction of the new river access site. The principal grounddisturbing activity is the construction of a road which involves the widening and deepening of an existing
gully that cuts into the first terrace above the Colorado River floodplain (Figure 235). The purpose to the
earth removal is to create a beveled slope from the terrace top down to the active river floodplain. One
new cultural resource site, 41BP821, was recorded and assessed during the investigation (see Figure 235).
A bipointed, beveled knife diagnostic of the Toyah Phase was the only diagnostic artifact recovered at
41BP821 during the survey. The fieldwork did not identify any significant or potentially significant
archaeological deposits associated with 41BP821 that would be affected by any proposed construction.
However, because potentially significant buried cultural deposits at 41BP821 were found in the far east
end of Backhoe Trench #2 approximately seven meters east of a proposed access road, archaeological
monitoring of earth-removing activities was recommended (Prikryl 2007). The Texas Historical
Commission (THC) concurred with that recommendation in March 27, 2007.
On February 18, 2008, the LCRA Archaeology Services staff was informed that earth-moving activities
related to the construction of the access road had started during the week of February 4-8, 2008. It
appeared that the need for archaeological monitoring was overlooked due a change in project managers
for the project. The LCRA Archaeology Services immediately made a field trip to the project area. The
following report describes the results archaeological monitoring that was subsequently conducted by the
LCRA in coordination with the THC. Personnel who conducted the monitoring consisted of LCRA staff
members Daniel Prikryl (Principal Investigator) and Andrew Malof and contracted archaeologist Cody
Kiker. The investigation was conducted under TAC Blanket Permit #4779, which was issued to the
LCRA by the Texas Historical Commission (THC) for Calendar Year 2008. All artifacts recovered
during the monitoring along with associated documents will be curated at the Texas Archeological
Research Laboratory.

Monitoring Program
An initial field trip to the project area was conducted on February 18, 2008 to make a general assessment
of any impacts to site 41BP821. Since a pause in construction had already been planned for February 18
and 19, there was ease of access to the road construction area and time available to inspect the impacted
areas. The field inspection suggested that cultural materials had been minimally affected on the east flank
of the road construction area where Backhoe Trench #2 had been previously excavated. However,
bulldozer scraping of the western flank of the road construction area had exposed a cluster of large
mammal bones (Figure 235). Several chert flakes and burned rocks lay in and around the bone cluster.
This cluster of prehistoric materials covered an area measuring about 170 cm on a north-south line by 70
cm on an east-west line (Figure 236). The artifact cluster also lay about 3.65 m west of the proposed
access road at an elevation of 80 cm above the current access road bed (Figure 237). Based on a previous
find of a beveled knife characteristic of Late Prehistoric Toyah Phase nearby in the wall of a minor road
cut previously constructed by the former landowner, it was deemed probable that the large mammal bones
were bison bones.

530

Figure 234. Section of the Utley, Texas 7.5’ USGS Map Showing the Rio Vista River Access Site Project
Area in Dark Blue.

531

Figure 235

On February 19, 2008, the LCRA staff met with the THC to describe the results of the LCRA’s field trip
to the site. Photographs showing the concentration of cultural materials on the west flank of the access
road and the location of find were provided to the THC during the meeting and later as an e-mail
attachment. Since the cut for the access road was already at an elevation below the archaeological finds,
it was concluded that construction of the narrow road bed area could

532

Figure 236. View of mammal bones and associated artifacts as first seen during initial visit to project
area.

Figure 237. Archaeologist standing at location of bone cluster. Note depth of road bed cut is below the
elevation of the bone cluster.

533

proceed with the provision that LCRA Archaeological staff be present to monitor those earth-removing
activities. The THC also concluded during the meeting that LCRA would need to produce a remediation
plan which would involve either the excavation of a 1x2 meter unit at the location of the bone
concentration to gather additional data or provide the THC a preservation plan that would effectively cap
the archaeological materials with 6 to 12 inches of soil and special materials such as geo-textile fabric to
preserve/protect the archaeological materials and ensure that the materials will not be adversely affected
by erosion.
The LCRA Archaeology Services staff then conducted the monitoring for the additional scraping and
removal of 20 cm (8 inches) of soil to finish the access road bed on February 20, 2008. Heavy machinery
used by construction personnel for this work consisted of a bulldozer, a front-end loader/backhoe, and a
heavy roller machine to compress the roadbed surface (Figure 238). Soil was removed in one to two inch
increments. The archaeologist watched each pass from a safe distance on the adjacent higher slopes to
determine if any features or artifacts were being exposed as the soil was removed. Additionally, the
archaeologist then walked over each newly exposed surface and also examined the backdirt generated
from each pass of the machinery. No cultural materials were found during the monitoring of the
excavations of the road bed.

Figure 238. View of continuation of construction of the road bed during archaeological monitoring. Note
that gray plastic covers the bone cluster.
The LCRA staff returned to the site on February 26, 2008 to gather additional data for the remediation
plan. Since a vertical and horizontal datum had been established during the previous 2007 survey
investigation, an engineering transit was used to establish the vertical relationships of the newly exposed
cultural materials to those previously found. Elevations on the approximately 170x70 cm cluster of
bison-sized bones and several pieces of chert debitage and burned rocks that comprised the bulk of the

534

materials exposed on the slope showed that the cluster lay about 1.3 meters lower in elevation than that of
the previously documented location of the buried cultural zone (Figure 239).

Figure 239. Cross-section diagram of the terrace slope showing the location of the bone cluster and the
previously documented buried cultural zone based on transit elevation readings.
Staff also sent some time reassembling bone fragments within the cluster and included other small
fragments that had been pushed further downslope from the main cluster. A very minor amount of dirt
was removed within the bone cluster to more fully expose the bones. As a result of this work, it appeared
that the bone cluster consisted of a scapula and one or two rib fragments (Figure 240). Importantly, too, it
did not appear that any additional bones or bone fragments were eroding out of the terrace slope at this
location.
Staff also observed that a very small number of tiny bone fragments, a burned rock, and a chert flake were
exposed further upslope at elevations compatible with the previously documented prehistoric component
(see Figure 239). Staff concluded that the scapula, rib(s), and other associated artifacts near these two
bones may have been moved downslope from their original locations within the buried component back
due to erosion during prehistoric times. Another possibility was that the bones and nearby artifacts were
pushed downslope by the bulldozer during the recent construction activities.
At that point, staff concluded that if a 1x2 meter unit was excavated for the remediation of damages to the
site, it should be placed upslope from the main bone cluster since other minor amounts of prehistoric
materials appeared to be eroding out of the slope both at the same elevation as the previously documented
component and also near ground surface at the top of the

535

Figure 240. View of exposed bison bones with two rib fragments at top center of photo and scapula in
center of photo to immediate right of photographic scale.

536

slope. To gather additional data on these possibilities, staff then excavated two additional shovel tests
(ST #2 and 3). Shovel Test #2 was excavated on the terrace top beginning at the original ground surface.
This shovel test was dug to a depth of 90 cm with no prehistoric artifacts being recovered. Shovel Test #3
was excavated about 1.5 m upslope from the artifact labeled as “minor bone fragment” on Figure 239 in
order to determine if any artifacts related to the buried cultural zone would be uncovered. The upper 35
cm of this shovel test consisted of the same very dark grayish brown sandy clay loam in which the buried
cultural materials had been found during the 2007 survey. No artifacts were found, however, in this
deposit during the excavation of the shovel test. Deeper, the shovel test penetrated a reddish brown clay
loam that was also sterile. Shovel Test #3 was terminated at a depth of 60 cm.
Based on the negative results of these shovel tests and the fact that staff was concerned about the erosion
potential that would still exist on the slopes if a 1x2 meter unit was excavated, staff decided to
recommend to the THC that covering the site area with protective material was the best course of action.
To gather information for a plan to cover the site area, the LCRA staff spoke with Michael Rainey of the
Bastrop County NRCS (formerly the Soil Conservation Service), Barrie Coburn the Director of the
TxDOT Landscape Design and Enhancement staff, and Jodie Hermes of LCRA’s Parks construction staff
about erosion control materials for soils and slopes like those at the site.
Options considered included the use of Curlex, which is the green-colored material that commonly used
on road right-of-way slopes and at new subdivision developments. Curlex is a fabric that eventually
breaks down as vegetation grows and takes root within the mesh. It is normally used to halt erosion of
slopes during the fall and winter months when there is little expected growth of new grass. A second
option examined was a more permanent plastic mesh product such as Enkmat, but Mr. Rainey said that
his experience with those types of products is that rainwater eventually gets under the plastic and starts
erosional channels.
A third option examined was the use of hydro mulching, which involves spraying a mix of compost and
wood/paper fibers over the slope surface and then spreading grass seeds on the mix. Hydro mulching is
used effectively in the Spring when new grass starts its growth cycle. Based on seasonal conditions,
LCRA staff proposed a remediation plan that consisted of applying hydro mulch and grass seeds to
stabilize the slopes to halt erosion of archaeological materials.
LCRA then provided a letter with this recommendation to the THC on March 3, 2008. In that letter,
LCRA also proposed to remove the bison scapula and rib that have been pieced back together prior to
laying down the hydro mulch, since it appeared that these were the only bones present. Because the
buried component appeared to be only 15 to 20 cm thick, LCRA stated that it did not feel that covering
the area where the component surfaces on the slope with 6 to 12 inches of fill material was necessary
before laying down the hydro mulch material and seeding the slope surface. The current slope surface
had been packed down by bulldozer traffic and any loosely packed introduced fill material and the
vegetation that would start to grow on it would probably erode given the sharp 10 to 12 degree angle of
the slope. Runoff water on the upslope side of the hump created by the fill material might also create a
runoff channel to the left or right lateral sides of the hump producing an erosional cut.
In the March 3, 2008 letter to the THC, LCRA Archaeology Services staff proposed to monitor the results
of the hydro mulching and seeding to determine its effectiveness. Should the hydro mulching and seeding
not halt the erosion, then our staff would consult with the THC to determine needed actions. On March
27, 2008, the THC concurred with the recommendations as described in the LCRA’s March 3, 2008 letter.
A Final Note on the Research Potential of 41BP821
The LCRA staff subsequently removed the scapula and rib fragment prior to the hydro mulching and
seeding work. Removal of the bones, confirmed that a second rib fragment was present. It is not certain

537

if the two rib fragments are parts of one individual rib or represent two separate ribs. During the removal
of these bones, a utilized chert flake was found near one of the rib fragments. One the same date of the
removal of the bones, staff also found a chert drill fragment on the east bank of the road cut. This artifact
is a unificial tool that can be classified as a flake drill as previously described by Shafer (1977). Flake
drills are frequently found in Toyah Phase components in Central Texas. It should be noted, too, that
during the previous February 26, 2008 visit to the site by LCRA staff, a pottery sherd had been found on
the east slope of the road cut. This sherd is tempered with a calcareous material that may well be shell. If
not shell, then the temper is probably bone. In either case, calcareous-tempered sherds commonly date to
the second half of the Late Prehistoric Period in Central and North-Central Texas.
A visit to the University of Texas Vertebrate Paleontology Laboratory to compare the scapula to bison
specimens confirmed that the scapula recovered at 41BP821 is that of a bison. The identification of this
bone fits well with the recovery of Toyah Phase diagnostic artifacts. The absence of any diagnostic
artifacts from other time periods further strengthens the hypothesis that the cultural materials at 41BP821
represent a single component. The cultural materials found on the west flank of the new access road cut
may represent a butchering area with the main campsite area being located on the east flank of the access
road where BHT #2 had previously been excavated in 2007. Overall, site 41BP821 continues to be rated
as a potentially significant cultural resource site.

Results of Monitoring of Cistern Demolition at the LCRA’s Muleshoe Resource Area in
Burnet and Travis Counties, Texas

By Charles A. Hixson

TAC permit #4479
Project 29.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Parks and Resource Conservation
October 2008

540

Introduction
In late October and early November, 2008, LCRA’s Archeology Services staff monitored the partial
demolition of five of the six cisterns (Cisterns A, C, D, E and G) slated for demolition at LCRA’s
Muleshoe tract in Burnet and Travis Counties (Figure 241). Cistern F was scheduled for demolition as
well but was found to be a stone-lined well when its concrete cap was removed. The cap was replaced
and the well left undisturbed.
The Muleshoe cisterns were demolished in order to facilitate removal of trash that had accumulated in
their underground chambers after they went out of use. This trash could contain potentially hazardous
materials that could contaminate the surrounding soil, necessitating its removal. Before the dismantling,
each cistern lay completely underground except for a masonry collar rising up to two feet above the
surface. All excavation was done by a backhoe operated by LCRA personnel.

Methodology
The intent of the monitoring was to record the cross-section of each cistern in order to learn construction
methods and depositional history of their contents.
The demolition and recording procedures were generally the same for each cistern. First a wide trench
was excavated along one side of the cistern, exposing the exterior walls from ground level down to the
base. After photos were taken, the backhoe bucket was used to break open the exposed wall and remove
it in large chunks, more or less bisecting the cistern from top to bottom but not removing the contents.
Photos were again taken and a profile drawn of the walls and contents. Fairly accurate measurements
were made of the collar and depth of the cistern chamber as well as for the wall thicknesses from the
pieces that were removed. Because staff could not enter the excavation pit for safety reasons, only
approximate measurements could be made for the width of each cistern’s chamber.
The cistern’s contents were then taken out in backhoe bucketfuls and placed on plastic tarps laid out on
the ground outside the excavation area. A brief description of the contents and their approximate depth
was then recorded. Where soil sediments were removed, and if deemed nonhazardous, a large sample
would be screened for artifacts.

Results
Cistern A
Cistern A (Figure 242) was different in construction from the other four that were dismantled, in that (1)
the chamber was excavated out of solid bedrock, (2) the chamber had a substantially different profile and
dimensions, (4) the walls had a different type of interior coating or sealant, and (5) the collar was of
masonry instead of brick Figure 243.

541

Figure 241. Location of cisterns and affected archaeological sites in Burnet and Travis Counties, Pace
Bend quad. From west to east: 41BT281, Cistern A; 41TV1779, Cistern B; 41TV508, Cistern D;
41TV507, Cistern D; 41TV499, Cistern E, F; 41TV 250, Cistern G.

542

Figure 242. Cross section drawing of Cistern A.

543

Collar
The above-ground portion of the collar prior to excavation consisted of two rows of roughly hewn
limestone rocks laid in a lime mortar and rising eight inches above the ground surface. The collar’s inside
diameter was 28 inches (72 cm) and outside diameter varied depending in the width of the rocks,
averaging around 47 inches (120cm). Several courses of the collar are probably missing. A rock slablined drain enters the cistern through a rectangular hole two inches underground. Outside the collar, this
drain is still intact for at least several feet, heading in south southwest direction presumably towards
where the house once stood.
The masonry collar continues underground for 18 inches (40cm) apparently intended to retain the sandy
loam soil that overlies the limestone bedrock. This opening extended straight down another two feet
through solid rock before it began to widen out.
Substructure
The below ground chamber of the cistern forms a giant teardrop-shaped void 16 feet (5 meters) deep and
12 feet (3.5 meters) deep at its widest point three-quarters of the way down. The bottom is slightly
concave. Except for the upper 18 inches of soil the entire cistern was carved into solid limestone bedrock.
Plaster
The interior surface of the cistern is coated with two layers of plaster plus a thin outer coating of a black
substance, possibly asphalt. The first layer of plaster applied to the bare rock appears to be lime mortar
light brown in color. Over this a second coat of pale gray mortar was applied and over that the black
material. Together they form a lining that varies from 1.5 to 3 cm thick on the walls and up to 5cm thick
on the bottom.
Contents
Only the bottom 60 to 70 cm contained trash. The bulk was made up of rubber hoses associated with
illicit copper smelting that occurred in the area in recent years. In addition there were moderate amounts
of household trash (glass and plastic bottles). All items appear to be mid 20th century or later. A small
amount of sediment was present in the very bottom of the cistern; this was screened and found to contain
similar household trash.
Cistern C
Cistern C, as well as Cisterns D, E, and G were constructed in a similar fashion with a brick dome used to
constrict the upper portion of the subterranean chamber merging seamlessly into an above-ground brick
collar completely coated in concrete (Figures 244, 245, 246). These cisterns only differed slightly in their
depth. In addition, all were excavated into the cobbly clay sediments that form the high terraces along
this stretch of the Colorado River. The interior walls coated with concrete and plaster.
These cisterns were probably constructed by digging a large circular pit down into the clay sediments. A
narrow shelf was dug into the wall of each pit approximately 2/3s the way up from the bottom to serve as
the foundation for the brick dome. Once the bricks were laid in concrete to form the dome and collar, the
interior walls and floor of the cistern were coated with a layer of concrete and then sealed with a second
layer of white plaster. The space above the dome was then filled in with backdirt to make it level with the
surrounding ground surface.
Collar
The above-ground portion of the collar of Cistern C was completely intact prior to excavation. It was
constructed of approximately six courses of yellow bricks laid in concrete - Portland concrete and sand to form an open cylinder-shape two feet high (60cm) with an opening 52cm wide. All surfaces of the

544

collar were coated with the concrete as well, obscuring any trace of the brick interior. There is no drain
opening so presumably the rain water flowed through a gutter straight into the cistern. This collar was
removed in one piece and hidden nearby so it could eventually be displayed at another, more intact,
homestead site (that of Cistern D).
Substructure
Immediately below ground the cistern begins to flare outwards for a depth of one meter, reaching a
diameter of 170cm. This upper part of the substructure was constructed of courses of yellow bricks laid
in concrete to form a dome. Below the brick dome the cistern descends another 2.5 meters into clay
sediments, widening slightly to its maximum diameter of 2.3 meters just above the slightly concave floor.
The upper 30 to 40 cm of the cistern was excavated through gravelly, sandy loam, the rest excavated
through cobbly clay.
Plaster
The interior walls of the cistern - the brick dome and the clay walls below - were coated with concrete
over which a thin layer of white plaster was applied. The thickness of both coating together ranged from
two to four centimeters.
Contents
This cistern was nearly full with trash, all from the 1970s and 1980s, most of it household trash consisting
of bottles and plastic containers. A half dozen or so large pesticide bottles were present. Half of a Ford
Model T ford hood was mixed in with the trash – the other half was seen lying on the ground outside the
collar prior to demolition.

Figure 243. Beginning the removal of Cistern A.

545

Figure 244. Cross section drawing of Cistern C.

546

Figure 245. Exposing Cistern C.
Cistern D
This cistern was constructed in a similar manner and using similar materials as Cistern C. The collar was
nearly intact except for some missing concrete coating on the rim. The width of the reservoir was the
same as Cistern C but its depth at three meters below the surface was somewhat less. The lower third of
its interior was filled with 1970s and 1980s household trash.
Cistern E
This cistern’s chamber has the same depth as Cistern D but is perhaps slightly narrower with an interior
width of approximately two meters. It also appears more cylindrical in shape. The collar was completely
missing and it had been capped with concrete by LCRA personnel in the early 1990s. This cistern was
filled nearly to the top with baling wire over 1980s-era household trash.
Cistern G
Like Cistern D, Cistern F appeared somewhat more cylindrical than Cisterns A and C. Its chamber was
slightly more than 3 meters in depth and slightly more than 2 meters in maximum width near the base.
The collar was missing several courses of bricks on its north side. The chamber was filled about a third
of the way up with mostly old metal sign posts, some recent household trash, and a water heater. The
entire brick dome with what is left of the collar was removed in one piece by the backhoe and still rests
upright at the site (Figure 247).

Figure 246. Detail of collar construction, Cistern C.

Power Production at Landa Industries, New Braunfels, Comal County, Texas: A Basic Chronology

By Andrew F. Malof

TAC permit #4479
Project 57.08
Lower Colorado River Authority
Parks and Resource Conservation
September 2008

549

Introduction
The area surrounding present-day Landa Park, in the vicinity of the Comal Springs at the edge of the
Balcones Escarpment in central Comal County, has been a major attraction and resource base for
thousands of years. American Indian peoples camped around the springs for at least 12,000 years, leaving
evidence of their activities in the form of stone tools, food remains, and cooking debris (Malof et. al
2007). Early Spanish explorers passed through, remarking on the fresh water and abundant resources.
German immigrants settled the area permanently in 1845, and by 1847 William Meriwether was
harnessing the flow of the springs for milling operations (Utley 1995). A channel dug by slaves provided
the necessary motive force, and this canal eventually became commonly known as the Comal River.
Joseph and Helena Landa purchased the operation in 1860 and began (slowly) expanding the operations
(Foster and Victor 1999). A flour mill was constructed in 1875, and by 1891 an additional warehouse
was added. Contrary to some sources, documentary evidence shows that electric generation was not
started until 1895, a direct result of the management of the property by Joseph Landa’s son, Harry, who
took over after his two brothers, Isadore and Morris, lost interest during the 1880s. Harry began acquiring
additional property along the river, and by 1902 had expanded operations into the property now owned by
LCRA. His original light and power plant, located next to the flour mill south of the property, apparently
provided electrical power for at least portions of the city. A much smaller water turbine and dynamo
system, located very near the present hydro plant, appears to have been dedicated to providing power to
the new cotton processing plant, which emphasized the extraction of cotton oil.
Harry Landa continued making improvements and reconfiguring his holdings, adding a steam plant and
increasing hydro production. The original light and power plant was eventually shut down, and primary
production was moved north, onto LCRA property. Coal was used to fire the boilers for the steam plant
(which was short-lived), and diesel engines were brought in to augment production at the hydro plant.
The diesel engines appear to have been fueled by railroad tanker cars parked on nearby sidings, and later
by underground piping from the Comal Power Plant (Gene Lopanec, personal communication 2008).
There is no evidence of underground fuel storage.
Sources vary on when Harry Landa sold the property. Local historian David Hartmann’s is the most
authoritative (Hartmann, in preparation). Harry’s mother, Helena, had stipulated that all the Landa
properties be sold exactly 10 years after her death, which occurred in 1922. Apparently Harry had an
option to buy, but chose not to, due to potential conflicts with his siblings. The properties were purchased
by investors in San Antonio (later San Antonio Public Service Company, or San Antonio Power and
Light). This group went bankrupt, and the properties went to an insurance company, which likewise went
bankrupt in 1929. The properties, including the park, were fenced off from the public until 1936, when
Peter Nowotny, representing the City of New Braunfels completed a purchase. The public facilities were
reopened shortly thereafter.
In the 1940s, the Comal Power Plant was leased to LCRA, although in 1949 the hydro plant area was still
shown as being owned by San Antonio Public Service Company, even though the actual owner was the
City of San Antonio. Hydro plant operations continued until about 1950, when the plant was mothballed
(Foster and Victor 1999:4). In 1971 LCRA purchased the Comal Power Plant, and presumably the hydro
plant area as well. It was at this time that the earlier brick hydro plant was dismantled. In 1978 New
Braunfels Utilities began rehabilitating the facility (Brune 1981). The diesel generators had been
removed, reportedly sold to interests in Mexico. The plant, now known as the Ruppel Hydro Plant,
remained in operation for about 10 years (Gene Lopanec, personal communication, 2008).

550

Chronology
Much of the following chronology has been obtained through close examination of Sanborn Fire
Insurance Company maps, available online through library systems and on file at the Center for American
History in Austin, Texas. The Sanborn maps offer line drawings of structures and other details along with
keys and occasional notes providing additional information. The available Sanborn maps for New
Braunfels range in dates from 1877 to 1949. Relevant information is summarized below, and Figures
248-250 illustrate periods of early development and the following improvements (and abandonments)
during the critical years between 1912 and 1930. Other sources of information are sparse to nonexistent,
with inquires at LCRA, New Braunfels Utilities, Comal County, the City of New Braunfels and San
Antonio City Public Service resulting in little additional information.
1877 – This single page map shows only the central portion of New Braunfels. The original Landa mill
was present at this time, and the fact it is not represented indicates perhaps both its distance from the main
city and it relative insignificance.
1885 – In 1885 the area is marked as J. Landa’s Flour Mill. The tail race has been constructed, and there
appear to be two connected structures, one of stone and one of frame construction. Portions of the mill
are marked as being under repair. Power is from water, there is no heat, and lights are from kerosene
lanterns.
1891 – By 1891 the frame buildings are covered by corrugated iron and an additional warehouse has been
constructed. Heat is now provided by stoves, lights are described as closed lanterns, and a fire pump has
been added. A rail line spur has been brought in to service the mill.
1896 – There are now two dynamos (generators) shown next to the water wheel or turbine and north of
the tail race, which provide the power for the now electric lights. The “electric power plant” is under
construction to the south of the mill – the foundation is present, but the remainder is “to be as shown,”
including a “tunnel for shaft” that extends to the tail race (Power House No. 1).
1902 – Between 1896 and 1902 there is major expansion of the Landa enterprises. J. Landa’s Flour Mill
is now referred to as Landa Roller Mills, Landa Electric Light and Power Company (Figure 251), Landa
Elevator, and Landa Ice Factory. The Landa Ice Factory and Elevator are new components. Power is still
water, lights are electric, fuel is coal and there is steam heat. The electric plant and ice factory are of
brick construction, while the new elevator is a frame building.
It is during this time that Harry Landa began his expansion east of what is called the Comal River, into
what is now LCRA property and Wurstfest grounds. The Comal River (actually canal) is reconfigured to
create a “mill pond” (forebay) which is channeled through a mill race to a turbine that appears to be
contained within a minimal (if any) structure (future Power House No. 2). The forebay and turbine
discharge flow into a new, two-pronged tail race that feeds the true Comal River. The turbine appears to
be dedicated for electrical production for the new and extensive Landa Cotton Oil Company, which is fed
through an “iron clad power wire shed.” Portions of this enterprise are on LCRA property, notably a
small room labeled as a “lint press.” A substantial water closet (bathroom) is on the northern edge of
LCRA property. The rail road spur has been extended to the cotton oil plant, and crosses between the
forebay and the tail race. All buildings associated with the oil plant are of frame construction. The oil
plant has electric lights, uses coal for fuel, includes boilers for cooking (presumably oil processing) and
oil pumps. Water remains the power source. A set of brick-lined double boilers is mapped along the
canal in line with the wire shed, and includes a 50 foot tall iron chimney.

551

1907 – There are few changes between 1902 and 1907, however the ice factory is enlarged significantly.
A small frame structure is added to the turbine portion of the oil plant, and a third boiler was added to the
pair present in 1902, but by this time it is already out of commission. The original two boilers are still in
operation.
1912 – A grain storage building was added next to the elevator in the original mill area. There is
additional information available on the Electric Light Plant, which has a 200 kW dynamo, two 22 kW
dynamos, a 75 kW dynamo and a 30 kW dynamo, for a total of 349 kW in available production. This is
still known as the Landa Electric Light and Power Company. The cotton oil plant turbine area (future
Power House No. 2) now has a more substantial frame building, and contains a 200 kW dynamo. A
building containing shakers has been added to the LCRA property, as has a 100 horsepower engine, the
function of which is unclear. Another gasoline engine is located just past the northwest corner of LCRA
property, and is associated with the elevator at that location. This may be a backup power source for fire
pumps. Most notably, a new single boiler with a 40 foot iron chimney has been added to the north of the
turbine area. This location coincides closely with the location of later steam plant (future Power House
No. 3). The third boiler that was mapped in 1907 is no longer shown. Power in this area is now
described as both water and steam, with the steam derived from coal fuel. There is no indication that the
power production in this area is other than for the oil plant. The large bathroom is not shown on this or
subsequent maps.
1922 – The ten years between 1912 and 1922 saw significant alterations in what is now referred to as
Landa Industries. The emphasis has shifted away from the original flour mill location and towards the
area by the cotton oil plant. The ice factory is no longer present, and the buildings have been converted to
flour storage warehouses. A light plant is still present, but appears secondary to the enlarged system to
the north. The flour mill is still using water power, and the elevator is operated electrically. There is no
longer heat available in this complex.
Within the LCRA property there have been substantial improvements. The original small turbine area has
been reconfigured, with a vastly enlarged tail race serving the large brick building (Power House No. 2)
that is now referred to as a “Light and Power Plant” (Figures 252, 253). This enlarged tail race section
required a rerouting of railroad track in that area. The turbines (Figure 254) are augmented by two 300
horsepower oil engines, and there are three 250 kW dynamos. A large warehouse is located immediately
northwest of the power house. The cotton oil plant is described as being powered by the enlarged steam
plant (Figures 255, 256), suggesting dedicated use. The steam plant, built of both cement and brick,
contains three brick encased boilers and the tall cement chimney (Utley 1995) is now present. The
cement portion was built in 1917, according to a notation on the 1930 map. It is assumed the brick
portion was built about the same time. The boilers use oil for fuel and power a turbine that operates a 50
kW dynamo. The turbine and dynamo are in the southeast quadrant of the building, and an office is in the
southwest quadrant. The cotton processing rooms and the gasoline engines are no longer present,
replaced by two offices and an auto shop.
1930 – The original flour mill is still present, but the light plant has been converted to a warehouse. What
appears to be sweet (molasses enriched) feed mill has been added. Importantly, the tail race is now gone,
and the mill is run entirely by electricity. The entire cotton processing plant is now out of commission,
and the steam plant is no longer used. Although still mapped as Landa Industries, the hydro and steam
plant areas, as well as the recently completed Comal Power Plant are owned and operated San Antonio
Public Service Company. The brick power house is now described as a “Lecture Hall,” (and elsewhere as
a Club Room”). A new three-story cement structure has been placed immediately to the east – this is the
new hydro plant, and according to the map was completed in 1928 (Figures 257, 258). This is the same
date as the Comal Power Plant extension project, which created what was essentially a millrace from a
large tunnel that extended from the 1927 Comal Power Plant under the Comal canal (Figures 259, 260).

552

This fed the feature now referred to as the “well.” The generator is powered by both water turbines and
oil engines. Numbers of engines, their horsepower ratings and final electrical output are not stated. The
new building has gas heat. Movement of the hydro plant eastward once more required reconfiguration of
the forebay. It is channeled more directly into the new plant, and the large tailrace is now underground.
The railroad spurs were also adjusted, and one spur came to a termination directly behind the new plant.
Another spur appears to service the auto shop, although that building is no longer designated as such.
1949 – The 1930 map was revised in 1949. There are few changes. The flour mill is now the Dittlinger
Roller Mill and has been expanded in size. The hydro plant appears to still be in operation, and is still
owned by San Antonio Public Service, although by now the Comal Power Plant is shown to be operated
by LCRA.

Summary
The Landa enterprises, begun in the 1870s, continued as viable economic pursuits for several decades.
The favorable setting near constant-flowing, constant-temperature spring vents created opportunities for
major development of additional power sources during the 1920s. Hydro generation continued
intermittently through the 1980s (Figures 261, 262), actually outliving the larger and more modern gasfired Comal Power Plant. At present, all industrial use of the old Landa properties has ceased, replaced
by recreational and residential activities (Figure 263).
